BRIGHAM YOUNG.

On the first day of June, eighteen hundred and
		one,
	The fair month of perfume and roses,
In the County of Windham, the State of Vermont,
	Where Whitingham village reposes
A prophet was born; how proudly the sun
	Beamed down on the nations that morning,
His glances of love lingering over the town,
	The town that our prophet was born in.

No glittering pageant to emblazon his way,
	No herald to tell of the glory
He would bring to his nation at some future day;
	A hero of song and of story.
Not a hint to the world that a seer was sent,
	An ensign to raise to the people;
That an era was dawning of peace and good will,
	The goal of the ages, the sequel.

Brigham Young thy achievements we witness to-
		day,
	In these green vales of the mountains;
For thou, under God, didst cause deserts to bloom,
	Parched ground to burst forth into fountains,
As far as our vision extends either way,
	Yea, even from centre to border;
On mount, valley and stream thy name is en-
		graved,
	A witness to ev’ry beholder.
Magnificent piles have been reared to his name,
	A people united together,
Called from many a land by the power of God,
	Inspired by the faith of their leader.

O, sons of the prophet, his memory revere,
	Seek naught but to add to his lustre,
As down through the ages it triumphantly rolls,
	Rare gems round his name may you cluster,
When your little ones gather around you at eve,
	Ye daughters, O tell them the story
Of his valor, his wisdom, his honor of God,
	Yea, e’en till your locks have grown hoary.

Today a great people delight in his name,
	Their Moses, whom God did rely on
To lead forth His children from bondage and
		sin,
	To establish a Latter-day Zion
Upon this glorious land, blest home of the free!
	Where forever Thy praises will ring;
For a church with a prophet and seer at its head,
	Whilst Thou art our God and our King.
						RUTH M. FOX.
	Salt Lake City, June 1, 1900.


[Column 1]
DEAR OLD NEW ENGLAND.
	Among the New England hills memor-
ies come back to me that I never recall any
other time or in any other place. There is
in some of us a sort of heredity, perhaps,
that makes us cling with loving and long-
ing to the haunts of nature in which child-
hood has been passed. The time of year
may make some difference in one’s feelings
and the springtime with its buds and early

[Column 2]
blossoms is especially significant of youth,
as well as of beauty in the landscape.
	Journeying from New York, with its
noise and bustle, up through the Connecti-
cut valley and into the heart of New Eng-
land among the beautiful villages nestled
among the green hills is charming in itself
as a delightful ride; and all along the way
one appreciates the quiet that seems so rest-
ful when coming from the hurry and worry
of the modern Gotham, to where time seems
almost to stand still.
	The trees so dear, so fresh in early leaf!
(trees are almost companions to me) and
the brooks so musical and all the blossom-
ing shrubbery on every hand awakens such
tenderness that one must needs be careful
not to betray emotion. Feelings are so in-
tense at such times that one lives richly in
the luxury of nature for the passing
moments, and can scarcely believe how
time passes: the air is full of sweetness and
the hum of insects on the wing and the
bird-songs mingle with the murmur of the
lazy brooklets that glide so smoothly over
the pebbly beds and seem to tell of the hills
from which they come, as in passing one
does exchange a word of greeting, perchance
with even a stranger.
	The day was delightfully warm and the
glowing life imparted by the warm sunshine
and the unalloyed joy of every note in the
melody of nature gave one a serene repose-
fulness that was not unlike a pleasant dream.
Such hours absorb the soul and fill it with
an infinite charm. One could not help feel-
ing the truth of Wordsworth’s lines on such
a day:
	“All bright and glittering in the smokeless air,
		Never did sun more beautifully steep
	In his first splendor, valley, rock or hill.
		Ne’er saw I, never felt a calm so deep!
	The river glideth at its own sweet will,
		Dear God! the very houses seem asleep
	And all that mighty heart is lying still.”
	Flowers and trees and the long tangled
grass are all a part of the pano-
rama. Trees appeal to me with a
power and significance so wonderful that I
can find no words to give expression to the
sentiment. The leaves have a meaning we
have never interpreted, there is a grandeur
about even the gnarled and rugged oaks,
that have withstood the storms of centuries
and remained unmoved, as it were, through
long periods of change.
		“The groves were God’s first temples,”
	This is a theme so delightful that one
would fain linger leisurely over it, but in
this practical, busy life one must tell some-
thing, or the impatient, busy man or
woman of the world will pass it by. We
forget when we are ourselves absorbed that
other people are not so overcharged with
sentimentality.
	Arriving at one of the most picturesque
country villages in the old Bay State,
the welcome given me was of the same
stately fashion of the olden time, and one
could easily imagine time had gone back-
ward. Yet there were railroads everywhere
and electric cars, bicycles and all the
modern electrical agencies in operation,

[Column 3]
but none of the brusqueness and feverish
excitement of the West.
	The gentleness of manner, the mildness
of expression and the sweetness of tone, all
harmonize with the beauty of the hills and
groves and brooks of which the poets have
sung thro’ the century.
	But one hastens on from place to place
to take just a look at familiar scenes
of the long ago, and as neither trains nor
streetcars run in out of the way places one
must have a carriage, so with a surrey (not
an “old-fashioned shay”) five of us rode
through half a dozen sleeping villages,
nestled down among those dear old hills.
What a wonderful ride that was, one never
to be forgotten, and how we all plied Sister
Pallas with questions about this place and
that and how many times I said to her,
“dear, you ought to know, for you have
been here all these years and I have been so
far away.” The once familiar scenes
brought old memories long since stored
away in some remote corner of the brain,
but not lost, for they come back with such
force that they almost awaken the old
echoes among the hills. We went up and
down and round about, gazing into the
ponds where the water-lilies grew, and
where we went rowing and skating, long
ago, and plunging into groves of the sweet-
scented hemlock, stopping at the same old
schoolhouses where we were taught when
children, and the meeting house with its
long rows of stalls for the horses at the
back, standing just as they did of yore, but
inside the Church was remodelled, the high
pulpit and cushioned pews with doors, had
been taken out and the gallery where we
used to sing from the old Watt’s hymn
book, when the choir leader used a tuning
fork. Now the whole appearance is modern;
but we wandered about and finally to the
home I left when starting for Nauvoo: the
place desolate, the well still the same, tho’
the old sweep had gone within the last few
years, but the neighbors still think it the
best and purest water in the village: the
brook near the well and the old home, still
runs through the hemlock groves, where
on the first day of March when a hole had
been cut in the solid ice, I went down into
the water and was baptized, the last and the
youngest of eight who received that or-
dinance of the Gospel on that day.
	The apple trees were budding into bloom
and the magnificent juniper tree which has
been the home of the humming bird for
three score years, still stands in the front
yard in its stately pride.
	The roses and lilac bushes were all gone
and only a few herbs in the kitchen garden
have lived like the trees without care.
Deserted and desolate are many of the
homes from which the Latter-day Saints
emigrated to Nauvoo early in the Forties.
	The many that we knew were nearly all
gone, the family Doctor who lived next
door with only a lane between our houses,
has gone to his rest; the minister’s house
just beyond, black with age like many
more; the old Academy with its grove of
pines, has been better kept, the boys still
play ball on the common. We drive on to-


wards another village, and espy some dis-
tance from the road the Pond House, where
many. Mormon meetings were held and we
plainly discern the pond where a great many
baptisms were performed in the years ’41
and ’42 The house, the farm and the
pond belonged to Stillman Pond who died 
some years ago in Cache Valley, and who
has left a large posterity to honor his name,
the grandfather of Sister Almira Merrill,
wife of (Apostle M. W. Merrill) and great
grandfather of Professor Joseph Merrill,
of the Utah University. One of his young-
er sons is president of Bannock Stakes of
Zion in Idaho, and one or more are now
filling missions abroad. From this part of
Massachusetts come also the Woodbury’s,
well known for their zeal in the Gospel, the
Haskells, the Aikens, the Pomeroys and
others who have been true and faithful in
the Church.
	We drove to Whitemore’s corner,
where Sister Pallas had said “good bye” to
our blessed mother when she forsook her
home she loved so well, her children dearer
than life itself, and with the three youngest
began the long and then tedious journey to
Nauvoo, bidding those she left behind the
last Farewell though she knew it not, for
her faith was great, and her hopes were
bright.
					AUNT EM.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
GENERAL CONFERENCE RELIEF SOCIETY
			(Conclusion.)
	Counselor Ellen Thueson, of Sevier Stake,
said they had held their last conference in
Grass Valley and the sisters in that locality
turned out well and they had a good time,
also on the 17th of March the meetings
were very enjoyable.
	President B. W. Smith spoke very im-
pressively about sustaining the EXPONENT,
felt it was the duty of the sisters, all who
were able, to take it and read it. She had
taken it from the beginning, and could not
do without it, it contained many matters
necessary for the sisters and much historical
data. Urged the sisters to labor in the
Temple, not to neglect their dead, if they
did their sorrows would be great in a day to
come.
	President Jane S. Richards: “Our
meeting is never long enough. I hope we
shall all remember what Sister Zina has
told us today, let us pray that she may live
and yet visit many Stakes of Zion. Brother
Hammond has said the Relief Society 
meetings are the best he has ever attended.
I feel that they are the best for us.” Sister
Richards spoke very sweetly about the
children and babies, told a story of being in
a meeting when the brothers or Bishop
asked those who had babies to stay down
by the door. She wanted the babies to
come up around the stand. We want to
begin with the children while they are small
and train them to go to meeting and love
it, and we who are older can begin with our
grandchildren. Let us be diligent in all
our duties, pay our donations, now this ten
cent donation is not one cent a month, there
has to be money to carry on this work,
you cannot have a celebration everywhere,
but we can do whatever is practicable in the
locality. “In Ogden we always have a
good sized conference, the people turn out
well. We do want to have our young girls
come and join the Relief Society. If they
are young girls now they will grow older
and have to take their part as the older sis-

[Column 2]
ters do, and how much better it will be to
begin young. You will be glad when you
meet the Prophet Joseph, who organized
this society, that you are among those who
carried out his instructions. He was like
an angel when he spoke to us, many times
his face was transparent.”
	President F. A. Hammond: “Time is
very precious to us. Society meetings are
among the very best I have ever attended.
The sisters in Moab had a good time at
their anniversary meeting, March 17. I
realize the necessity of sustaining the EX-
PONENT, I have felt many times that it was
worthy better support than it received from
the sisters. In my travels and journeyings
all the country round, I meet with the sis
ters in their organizations and try to en-
courage them in their duties; and storing
up wheat is one of the obligations of the
society, some have done more than others.
These aged sisters who preside some of
them I have known over fifty years. I re-
joice to see them here today and to behold
their faces, and I pray the Lord to bless
them and to give them life and health as
long as they desire it on the earth.
	Counselor Angeline Hinckley, of Millard
Stake, spoke of the long trips they had to
make by team when visiting the country;
yet they always felt blest and richly repaid
for any weariness or inconvenience. They
held conferences in the different parts of the
the Stake.
	President Zina D. H. Young blessed the
sisters.
	There was a motion made to thank the
choir and all those who had taken part in
the music, which carried unanimously.
	The choir sang “Should you feel inclined
to censure.’ Conference adjourned for six
months. Benediction by President Zina D.
H. Young.
			E. B. WELLS, Secretary.


U. W. P. C.
	The regular meeting of the U. W. P. C.
was held in the EXPONENT office, April 30,
1900, the president in the chair. Opening
prayer by Dr. Romania B. Pratt. Quota-
tions from Ruskin. A communication say-
ing that badges could be obtained by mem-
bers of the club was read; also a communi-
cation from the General Federation con-
cerning our dues. It was decided by the
club that since we have withdrawn from
the General Federation our dues will not be
sent. A communication was read concern-
ing our delegate to Milwaukee. The club
decided to send no delegate. The presi-
dent reported from the club council. The
council asked the club ladies of the city to
pay ten cents each to bring some noted
lecturer here to the next Annual State
Federation to be held in October. Mrs.
Emma J. McVicker spoke in favor of hav-
ing some one come. Dr. R. B. Pratt moved
we pay the ten cents; Ruth M. Fox
seconded—carried. The subject of estab-
lishing a free Kindergarten was also re-
ferred to, after which the program was
carried out as follows:
	Bessie Shirley gave a talk on mines and
mining and a chain discussion was entered
into by all members present on the best way
to obtain obedience from children.
	Closing prayer by Ruth M. Fox.
			GRACE ANDERSON, Rec. Sec.


MISS MARY E. WILKINS has just started
on her first journey to Europe.


R. S. REPORTS.
OAK CREEK ANNIVERSARY.
	Minutes of the Oak Creek Ward Relief
Society meeting, held March 17, 1900,
President Caroline E. Lyman presiding.
Singing by the choir. Prayer by Joseph
A. Lyman. Continued with singing.
	Bishop Peter Anderson made some
timely remarks. Song entitled “Daughters
of Zion, the Friends of the Poor,” by
Susan Finlinson, Rebecca Jacobson and
Annie L. Henderson. Stake President
Elizabeth Yates then spoke to us on our
duties as members of the society. Bishop
Yates, of Scipio, also made a few remarks,
and President Caroline E. Lyman spoke a
short time. Mary M. Lyman read the
minutes of the first Relief Society meeting
held in Nauvoo. Singing by the choir and
benediction by Brother George Finlinson.
	Refreshments were then served and a
nice, sociable time was enjoyed by all.
		ANNIE L. ANDERSON, Sec.
UTAH STAKE 
	On the 27th of April we held our quart-
erly conference of the Relief Society of this
Stake at Manilla Ward, near Pleasant
Grove. We had a fine time with a very
large atten[-]ance of brethren and sisters.
They made some changes in their Board of
Directors at their annual meeting in Feb-
ruary, which they will forward you in the
near future.
	Since the above date we have visited
Springville, Mapleton, Spanish Fork,
Salem and Payson. We had a profitable
time at each place, laying before the sis- 
ters their duties in the Reief Society as
members, and our obligations in this
Latter-day work, advising them to be
charitable and liberal, etc. Some are very
enthusiastic in building granaries to store
more wheat this coming season. There are
two new granaries in this county dedicated
to the Lord this year, and one nearly
finished and others anticipating to build in
the near future, by harvest time, if possi-
ble, and getting houses for meetings also
is their theme.
	I believe all are advancing in Relief
Society work and desire to learn more of
the work of the Lord. We are having
new bishopric in the Stake, hence new
Relief Society branches. A new Bishop
in the suburbs of Spanish Fork called upon
me inviting the presidency of the Relief
Society of this Stake to visit the new ward
to help organize a Relief Society in Leland
Ward. Brother Markham being Bishop, he
was present with his counselors. The
officers are: Maria Scoville, president;
Margaret Chishlom, first vice-president; Ruth
Bowen, Sarah Markham and Emma Cam-
mack, directors; Mrs Margaret Davis, Jer-
ome Jensen and Mary A. Thomas, trustees.
	They were very united on the nomina-
tions and I think they will work with en-
thusiasm. There has been several changes
in the Relief Society in the last annual
meeting which will be forwarded to you
shortly.
		Yours very respectfully,
						M. JOHN.


EX-QUEEN LILIUOKALANI has com-

book as The Hawaiian Tradition of the

WOMAN’S EXPONENT


A CAPTIVE OF TWO WARS.
I live in prison, though my open door 
	Leads out upon the sunny fields and glens;
I feel the clank of chains that evermore
	Forged by the cruel fates beyond my ken,
The spirit chafes, the heart keeps sad and sore.

Burdens of sorrow might imprison me.
	Though leaving windows light and open door;
I live alone with tasks of Liberty,
	And weep for Fillippino and the Boer;
But I would up, and striving, helpful be!

Comes the dread message of the winds and waves,
	From tropic shores the wail of anguish riven
From desperate souls that will not be the slaves
	Of any tyrant ’neath the vault of heaven;
The cry of mothers by ensanguined graves!

Tears, though of blood, full of swift heart appeal—
	Would dazzled eyes see only fields of gold:
While such as I can only think and feel, 
	Who listens to Great Freedom’s mandates old?
What strife victorious would relent to heal?

I am not free while they my dark, skinned brothers
	Their rightful freedom nobly strive to win;
While sordid greed their aspiration smothers,
	The patriot’s aim denounce as mortal sin;
Who dares be happy through the loss of others ?

’Neath prison walls I wait the gleaming token
	Of blest deliverance eye and heart shall greet;
The healing touch upon the heart nigh broken,
	God’s messenger of peace with hallowing feet!
Who brings the ringing word of Freedom spoken!
					CORA WILBURN,
	EX.


[Column 1]
DAUGHTERS OF THE REVOLUTION.
	Undoubtedly the ladies have read the
account of the eighth annual meeting of the
D. R. that appeared in Harper’s Bazaar
of May 6. This article is written by Miss
A. W. Sterling and deals principally with
the question of the union of the D. R. and
the D. A. R.
	Then Miss Snow has told you in a very
eloquent manner of the journey of the dele-
gation to the place of meeting, and of their
reception in Philadelphia. Consequently it
will be unnecessary for me to say very
much on either of the above mentioned
subjects. I shall try not to repeat the
things already told, and shall, as nearly as
possible, confine myself to a brief descrip-
tion of the places visited, which are all of
the greatest importance to the Daughters of
the Revolution. And there is so little in-
formation that can be obtained about many
of the sites, except by a visit to the spot it-
self.
	Washington, our nation’s capital, is cer-
tainly rich in historic relics, as well as in
its artistic beauty, though its foundation
dates from a period after the Revolution.
All of the Government buildings, the
Library of Congress, the Museum and Art
Gallery are a credit to any country, but as
Daughters of the Revolution the places and
characters that figure so conspicuously in
our Revolution are the dearest to us. We
are told that Washington himself selected
the site of the White House and planned
its erection, but John Adams was the first
occupant. Perhaps there is not another
spot in America that appeals to us in just
the same way as does Mt. Vernon—and I
wish to speak of this particularly, as it is to
a woman’s courage and energy that we owe
the preservation of this “shrine of patriot-
ism.” This property we know belonged to
Lawrence Washington, a half brother of
George’s, and several years his senior.
Lawrence built the Manor House and called
it after Admiral Vernon, under whom he
sailed and for whom he cherished the
warmest friendship. On the death of
Lawrence and his only daughter, George
inherited Mt. Vernon. It was from this

[Column 2]
spot he was called to the field of battle,
from this spot he was called to be president
of the United States and from this spot his
magnificent soul took its flight to higher
regions. I was much impressed by the
following quotation which is printed in the
library at Mt. Vernon, and was written by
Dr. Andrew Reed, an eminent English
philanthropist, on visiting the tomb of
Washington in 1833:
			Washington
The Brave	The Wise		The Good
			Washington,
	Supreme in War, in Council and in Peace,
Valiant		Discreet		Confident
			Washington,
Without		Without		Without
Ambition		Fear			Presumption
			Washington,
In disaster calm, in success moderate, in all, Himself,
			Washington,
The Hero		The Patriot	The Christian
The Father of Nations the friend of Mankind,
			who,
	When he had won all, renounced all
			and sought
	In the bosom of his family and of nature
			Retirement,
		And in the hope of religion,
			Immortality.
	Every spot of ground and every article in
any way connected with our hero is sacred
to us, but of all the things that were con
nected with Washington, from the cradle to
the grave, there was nothing else that ap-
pealed to me so strongly as did his swords,
which I knew had never been drawn in any
cause but that of right and humanity. Three
of these hang in a case in the main hall at
Mt. Vernon, and beneath the case contain-
ing them is the following clause taken from
his will:
	These swords are accompanied with the injunction
not to unsheath them for the purpose of shedding
blood except it be for self-defence, or in defence of
their country and its rights; and in the latter case
to keep them unsheathed and prefer falling with
them in their hands to the relinquishment thereof
	In 1855 John Augustine Washington was
unable to keep the estate of Mt. Vernon
and was therefore compelled to offer it for
sale. Miss Ann P. Cunningham, of South
Carolina, decided to save this sacred spot as
a possession of the nation, if possible. In
1858 the Mount Vernon Ladies’ Association
of the Union was organized Miss Cun-
ningham was made Regent and twelve states
were represented by vice-Regents. The
ladies were aided in their splendid work by
some of the most prominent men in our
country, such as Edward Everett and
Washington Irving. The sum required for
purchasing the property was raised and
each state seems to have vied with the
other in refurnishing the rooms, supplying
relics and preserving with remarkable ac-
curacy and faithfulness every detail of our
Washington’s home. This magnificent
work should be an object lesson to the
Daughters of the Revolution, and certainly
we cannot be too grateful to this band of
noble women.
	Perhaps the most interesting church to
be visited in the East is Old Christ’s Church,
in Alexandria, Ga., for George Washington
was one of the first vestry-men of this
church and his pew remains just as he left
it, with a fac-simile of his autograph on a
steel plate on the door of the pew. The
pastor from this church, Rev. Charles
Davis, preached the funeral sermon at
Washington’s funeral.
	We reached Philadelphia on Saturday
afternoon and were fortunate enough to
secure very pleasant quarters at the Strat-

[Column 3]
ford Hotel, the D. R. headquarters.
	Miss Emily H. Cannon came from Bos-
ton Saturday evening, to our intense de-
light, and Utah’s delegation was full, and
I may say, we were decidedly the youngest
delegate present. We had expected to be
kindly received by the Eastern D. R’s.,
for it is undoubtedly true that in no other
association of women is there manifest the
cordiality, nor the bond of sisterhood so
strongly felt, as in these patriotic societies,
but we could scarcely have anticipated the
reception that met us. Miss Snow has so
admirably described it that it would be
superfluous for me to add anything; but I
must say that from the President-General
and her two “vices,” as Miss Sterling and
Miss Hunt style themselves, all the officers
and Board of managers and the D. R’s.
from most remote parts are women to be
admired, respected and loved.
	Miss Sterling has graphically described
the Old Swede’s Church, on Sunday, April 23.
Monday, April 24, the business meeting
was held. This occupied the entire day
and a part of the evening. The order of
business as set forth in the program was
closely followed. Every member of the
society will receive, as soon as it is printed,
a complete account of the business meeting.
The question of a union of the D. R. and
D. A. R. was considered in a dignified and
decisive manner and unless a better plan
of uniting the two can be suggested than
any yet proposed, there will certainly never
be a union, for the D. R’s. would gain very
little for what they would have to surrender.
There is not a society in America that has a
stronger, more liberal, exalted
and progressive organization than
the Daughters of the Revolution, who work
under the colors chosen by George Wash-
ington himself, and have as their motto
Liberty, Home and Country. The nomin-
ating commitiee to nominate seven ladies to
fill the vacancies that would occur in the
Board of managers, consisted of one mem-
ber from each state. I had the honor of
representing Utah, and the ladies nomina-
ted were all elected.
	The Washington Memorial Fund was
spoken of, also the Prison Ship Martyr’s
Association, and the Daughters were urged
to assist both. The organization of the
Junior Auxiliary in every state was strong-
ly recommended. The reports from the
different states were very interesting, and I
realized more than ever the necessity of
having some organized plan for study and
of doing something in a public way when
possible.
	You know the order of our excursions as
the program mapped out was closely fol-
lowed. The first place visited in “Old
Philadelphia” was Carpenters Hall. This
place was built in 1770 by the Carpenter’s
Guild, and remains just as it was when just
concluded. Within this building on the
5th day of September, 1774, was convened
the first Continential Congress, with Pey-
ton Randolph as president, and delegates
from the thirteen colonies. Over one of
the large doors, just opposite the street en-
trance, is this inscription: “Within these
walls Henry Hancock and Adams inspired
the delegates of the colonies with the nerve
and sinew for the toils of war.”
	From here we went to Independence
Hall. This building is very carefully kept.
There are many fine old pictures to be
seen, but the objects of greatest interest are
the table and chair used in signing the
Declaration of Independence.

	SALT LAKE CITY, UTAH, JUNE 1, 1900.


PIONEER WOMAN’S PAPER.
	This number of the paper, June first,
nineteen hundred is the commencement of
the Twenty-ninth volume of the WOMAN’S
EXPONENT. It is the oldest woman’s
paper in the West, and the first established
except the New North West in Portland,
Oregon; that was discontinued several
years since. At the time this paper was
first issued, there were few women in any
of the professions, and even women teachers
received very small salaries and hardly
ventured to speak up in meeting; but
wonderful developments have been made
in woman’s work and woman’s sphere dur-
ing the last thirty years Those who have
watched the progress of affairs must be
aware how marvelously woman herself has
kept pace with the spirit of the age, not
only in the East, where every facility for
advancement is easy of access, but in the
far West, where all was very crude and
new but half a century ago.


[Column 1]
A TRIBUTE OF LOVE.
ZINA SMOOT WHITNEY.

“There is no death—the thing that we call death
	Is but another sadder name for life,
Which is itself an insufficient name,
	Faint recognition of that unknown Life
That Power whose shadow is the universe.”

	When we think of the beautiful woman
whose name is mentioned here, we cannot
feel that she is dead, and as we sat pen in
hand meditating, the poet’s words we have
quoted came fresh to mind, although we
had not thought of them for years. Zina
has passed from earth to another sphere,
she has gone home to her Father and
Mother in heaven, and her little babe,
Heber, and to her earthly father and
mother, and many more whom she knew
and loved while here. We who loved her—
children, husband and friends—cannot call
her back, do what we may, but we can
cherish her memory, emulate her good
deeds, her many virtues, her tender, sweet
words and ways, her gentleness, her purity.
We could not wish to have her live and
suffer longer; she has gone where there is
no pain nor sorrow, nor will there be any
more sad partings for her to endure, and
by and by we shall go to her if we are
worthy.

[Column 2]
“So—through fierce winds blowing.
	Through rough desert ways,
Through long nights whose dreary darkness
	Reaches o’er the days,
So He brings them home at last
Safe from every stormy blast!

So—through cares and trials,
	Through temptations strong,
Through dead hopes whose joyous blossoms
	Have been wasted long;
So He brings His chosen Home
Nevermore to sadly roam!”

	Sister Whitney’s many attractive char-
acteristics endeared her to those with whom
she was associated; and she had a large
circle of intimate friends, as well as
relatives whose hearts were deeply touched
when they realized that she had gone for-
ever from their view. But her children
live and whatever affection or tenderness
may be bestowed upon them the mother
will appreciate as if it were done to her,
and even more, for now her capacity for
love will be greater than before. She
“wore the badge of motherhood with
most becoming grace,” and now that she
cannot minister to the little ones she
loved so tenderly, will watch her little
flock with even greater solicitude. And
we who remain count not those lost who
died loving us.
	Zina Smoot Whitney, beloved wife of
Bishop O. F. Whitney, was born February
20, 1859, and was the daughter of the Hon.
Abraham O. and Emily H. Smoot. Sister
Whitney passed peacefully away at her
home in this city, on Sunday afternoon,
May 20, 1900, aged forty-one years and
three months.
	The funeral was held at the family
residence on Wednesday, May 23. The
rooms were beautifully decorated with
flowers, the loving gifts of her dear friends,
who with their own hands arranged them
and also made the dainty garments in
which she was robed and laid away.
	The funeral services were held at 2 p.
m. and were tender and impressive, the
music being particularly fine, and the
speakers were sympathetic as well and in-
spirational. Her grave was literally
covered with exquisite floral tributes. Her
last resting place was dedicated by Elder
Rulon S. Wells, and the quartette of sing-
ers rendered the beautiful hymn “Rest on
the Hillside, Rest.”


EDITORIAL NOTES.
	Mrs. Potter Palmer will entertain dur-
ing the Paris Exposition in the house of
the Marquis de Montgomery, near the
Trocadero, which she has rented. It is
said that she is paying for it $4,000 a
month rental.
	The anniversary of the birthday of
President Brigham Young is being cele-
brated today, June 1. The children of the
city have brought beautiful flowers in
great abundance with which the statue of
the great pioneer and the monument are
being festooned and garlanded; bands are
playing and cannons have been fired by
the veteran artillery men early this morn-
ing. Services in the Tabernacle are a part
of the program, including an address by
President Lorenzo Snow, and in the after-
noon an excursion to Saltair, in which the
public are invited to participate. This is
one of the notable days for Utah when
they delight to honor this great pioneer
and leader, certainly one of the foremost
men of the nineteenth century.


Mrs. Martha G. Wells, mother of the
Governor, gave a Kensington at their resi-
dence on First Street, on Friday, May
18, from two to six p. m. About sixty
ladies, mostly intimate friends and relatives,
were present. The handsome rooms were
artistically decorated with roses, carnations
and choice flowers, and the elegantly
dressed ladies with their gaily colored
embroidery and needlework, made a bril-
liant picture, the brisk flow of conversa-
tion, the greeting of old friends and the
hearty good feeling that pervaded the
company was most enjoyable. Delicious
refreshments were daintily served and the
afternoon seemed only too short for such a
happy gathering.
	The D. R. Society gave a musical on
Monday evening, May 28, at the residence
of Mrs W. J. Beatie, for the benefit of the
Scofield sufferers. The large and com-
modious parlors were beautifully decorated
with the stars and stripes and the D. R.
colors, blue and buff, and lovely flowers
made the place fragrant with sweetest per-
fume. Helds band played choice national
selections on the lawn which was brilliant
for the occasion with electric lights.
General Charles Burton was Master of
Ceremonies, and the young and talented
Arthur Shepherd took charge of the piano,
playing the accompaniments for the sing-
ers, which the ladies felt was a very great
favor. Mrs. Willard Croxall rendered a
song in French and also one in English.
Miss Mabel Cooper, whose name is well
known in Salt Lake, also favored the com-
pany with one of her finest pieces, and Mr.
Walter Wallace, who has a fine bass voice,
also sang one of his best solos. Refresh-
ments were served to all guests, and the
ladies of the society feel the affair was a
success both socially and financially.


CANADA.
	The Alberta Stake Relief Society met in
conference at Cardston, May 12, 1900,
Stake President Mary L. Woolf presiding,
Meeting opened by singing “Come, come
ye Saints.” Prayer by Counselor Jane
Hinman. Singing, “How firm a founda-
tion.” Roll called, fourteen officers present.
Minutes of forenoon session of previous
conference were read.
	President M. L. Woolf rejoiced to see so
many young mothers with their babes
present. Felt that the minutes just read
were full of inspiration. We must culti-
vate the spirit of God continually, that our
influence may always be felt. Knew the
Lord would bless us and we would feel
doubly paid for the little sacrifices made to
attend our conference.
	President Sarah B. Daines, of Cardston,
felt the work of the Relief Society was a
noble mission for the daughters of Zion.
Reported the Cardston society. The sisters
are united and faithful in their duties, but
the attendance at meeting was not so large
as she desired.
	President Elizabeth Webb, of the
Mountain View society, reported their
society as being in good condition. The
sisters are united and trying to do their
duty. She had enjoyed her visit in Utah
very much, had seen the hand of the Lord
made manifest to a great extent in the heal-
ing of the sick.
	Counselor Eva Hansen reported the
Aetna society as being in an improved con-
dition, although scattered. The sisters are
united and energetic in their duties.


[Column 1]
	President Annie Baker gave a favorable
report of the Leavitt society.	Some of the
sisters walk three miles to attend their
meeting. We are blest with good weather.
Felt we would have a prosperous year and
we should be grateful for our blessings.
	Singing, “Zion stands with hills sur-
rounded.”
	President Maria Bennett, of Magrath,
society, had been called with her husband
to settle in the country, and had been hap-
pily surprised. The sisters are energetic
and faithful in their labors and have a very
prosperous society. Desired the spirit of
her calling that she might be able to per-
form her duty.
	Stake Counselor Jane Hinman bore her
testimony to the blessings received through
fasting and prayer. Knew the sick had
been healed. “We are progressive, our
president is alive to her duties and has the
spirit of her calling.” Spoke on the princi-
ple of obedience. If we perform our duties
as mothers our children will respect and
obey us. We should teach our children 
patience and self-denial.
	President M. L. Woolf endorsed the re-
marks in regard to fasting and prayer, and
added her testimony.
	Minutes read and approved. Singing,
"We thank Thee, O God, for a prophet.”
Benediction by Sister Sarah Gibbs.
	Afternoon session convened at 2 p. m.,
Counselor Jane Hinman presiding. Sing-
ing, “Guide us, O Thou great Jehovah.”
Prayer by Patriarch Henry L. Hinman.
Singing, “Down by the river’s verdant
side.” Minutes of afternoon session of
previous conference read.
	Counselor Rhoda E. Hinman said: “We
are living in a day of progress, when we
cease to progress we cease to perform our
duties. We are living in an eventful time
when the prophecies are being fulfilled, we
should be prayerful, we worship a God who
hears and answers prayers.
	President Catherine Pilling of Ætna:
“We have many things to be thankful for
and above all we should be grateful for the
Gospel and live up to its requirements. We
should as sisters overlook each others failing.
	President M. L. Woolf said the General
Board desires a representation from each
Stake at the General Relief Society Confer-
ence at Salt Lake City. She presented the
name of Sister Zina Y. Card as an aid to
the Alberta Stake. Carried unanimously.
“Mothers should always be able to govern
themselves and seek for power to train their
children properly that they may grow up
useful in the kingdom of God.”
	Sister Zina Y. Card said she had attended
the General Relief Society Conference and
gave instructions received. The 17th of
March should be remembered as the day of
the first organization of the Relief Society by
the Prophet Joseph Smith. It should be ob-
served as a ten cent fund day and could be
celebrated as desired, the president of each
branch being responsible for the fund. The
Stake officers should visit each society once
a year, and twice if convenient. A class
should be called to be trained as nurses in
each Stake, we should study to care proper-
ly for the sick.	The Stake officers’
meeting should be held quarterly; local
officers’ once a month.	The sisters should
build houses and granaries, as their means
will allow after the poor are properly taken
care of. Spoke of our average attendance
not being as large as it should be according
to the members enrolled.

[Column 2]
	Duet by Mamie Ibey and Rhoda Duce,
“God be With You till We meet Again.”
	Counselor Elizabeth Hammer said the
prayer offered up at the beginning of the
meeting had been answered and we had
been strengthened. In reading the confer-
ence news she had felt the same spirit that
was with the Apostles and with the priest-
hood in this land.
	Patriarch John A. Woolf addressed the
conference: “The labors of the sisters run
in a different channel to those of the breth
ren, but all is for the building up of the
kingdom of God.” Spoke upon the law of
tithing, we would be judged from the
books and we want our names written in
the Book of the Lord. Our children should
be taught to pay their tithing that they may
not neglect this duty when grown, they
should also be taught obedience to the
priesthood. Mothers have a great deal of
influence with their children, also with their
husbands.
	Brother C. O. Card spoke upon the law
of tithing, ten thousand members of this
Church had cut themselves short of the
blessings of God. Advised the Saints to
pay their debts and endeavor to keep out.
Spoke upon the product of cheese and but-
ter making, advised the Saints to be self-
sustaining in these and other things, as far
as possible, and not depend altogether on
imported goods. Gave many good in-
structions upon temporal matters. If we
are united, our labors will be light; if we
keep the commandments of God, we will
soon have a Temple in our midst.
	Singing, “O, my Father.” Benediction
by Counselor Thomas Duce.
			JANE W. BATES, Sec.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
CURRENT EVENTS.
(Read at the U. W. P. C. May 31, 1900.)
Madame Chairman and Ladies:
	In order to get over the worst part first, I
will commence this paper with the awful
calamity at Scofield, by which a large num-
ber of women and children were
left homeless and helpless. A calamity like
this is unparalleled in the United States.
	On the morning of May 1st over two hun-
dred men went to work as usual in the
mine. In a few hours their mangled re-
mains were being brought to the surface. A
large proportion of these were heads of
families. My sympathy went out to the
poor Finn women, who could speak no
English, and could only sit and moan.
Imagine ourselves where we could not
speak the language, and our loved ones
buried in a debris of fallen earth and tim-
ber.
	The disastrous results of this accident
have only been exceeded by one, which oc-
curred in Wales some years ago, in which
five hundred were killed in a mine explo-
sion. The largest one in the United
States, previous to the Scofield accident, oc-
curred in Pennsylvania, in which over one
hundred men lost their lives.
	We should be proud of the citizens of
Utah, and also citizens of other states, who
responded so nobly to the call for finance.
It is well, for we know not the hour that
we may need help ourselves.
	It is not war alone that makes heavy
drafts upon the courage and happiness of
women, since in many of the hazardous
vocations “Men must work and women
must weep.” The work of the world calls

[Column 2]
for sacrifices every hour. Every day lives
are snuffed out, and every day the world is
engaged in burying its dead. After the
funeral, the work has to be picked up and
carried on, so it is an endless chain, and life
itself is but a long funeral procession, until
it is closed at the grave.
	Almost heart-rending reports come from
India, bringing news of the pitiful condi-
tion of the country more than ever to the
public view. Cholera is gathering in its
deadly hold ninety millions of people, who
are living in the midst of pestilence and
misery.
	The government is exerting itself to
meet the emergency, but not more than five
million can be relieved. The roads are
crowded with ghastly bands seeking to es-
cape from stricken territories, but for lack
of food and water succumb in the attempt,
and the roads are dotted with heaps of dead
and dying. In some parts of India the
bodies lie exposed to the sun, as the inhabi-
tants are dying more rapidly than the sur-
vivors can provide for the dead.
	An appeal from London says that the
United States ought to at least contribute a
million of dollars toward a relief fund. An-
other from Calcutta says that owing to the
fact that the English are engaged in the
somewhat expensive task of reducing the
South African Republic to the same Colon-
ial vassalage, the government has not been
able to do as much for the succor of the
people of India as it might under other
circumstances.
	Every American sympathizes with these
sufferers in far off Asia. The deplorable
condition produced by crop failure has been
intensified by the financial system enforced
on the people of the unhappy land by the
London bankers. No doubt the United
States would raise the one million, without
much trouble, but while we are sending
money into this British colony, the British
are sending money out to aid in the spoila-
tion in other lands.
	The Philadelphia Ledger has this to say
about England.
	She is sending millions out in carrying out an
aggressive and unjust war against South Africa,
while neglecting the manifest duties towards sub
jects who being under paternal sway, should be
equally the subjects of paternal solicitude. If
England can afford to spend so much money for
the taking of the lives of the Boers, she should be
able to spend a tithe of the sum to save the live
of her starving people in India. If England in-
sists upon spreading death and desolation in South
Africa and squandering her millions in this way,
what is left for humanity to do, but to feed and
care for her starving subjects and colonists in
Asia?
	The Paris Exposition of 1900 was formal-
ly opened on April 14, M. Milerand, the
Socialist Minister of Commerce, was chosen
to deliver the inaugural address. Like all
expositions the present one was opened
some weeks before the buildings were
ready. The number of American exhibi-
tors is second only to those of France, and
far ahead of all other competitors. Where
England has only one thousand, Germany
three thousand, the United States has seven
thousand exhibitors.
	The American section occupies three
hundred and twenty-nine thousand, and
fifty-two square feet, with forty-seven dis-
tinct exhibition spaces. There is every
reason to believe that the exhibition will
have good effect beyond its commercial suc-
cess.
	After three years of almost incessant tur-
moil, it need hardly be said that the Fre


(Column 1)
people have put forth every effort to make
the Exposition successful. The number of
French exhibits reach thirty thousand,
twelve thousand more than all foreign
competitors put together. The grounds
have been laid and the buildings erected in
accordance with the French taste which is
so universally admired.
	Germany has shown particular friendli-
ness, and the Russian section was opened
with special ceremonies, the occasion being
the presentation of a personal gift from the
Czar to France.
	The Queen of Saxony visited the Exposi-
tion, and set an example that will probably
be followed by all the Royalty of Europe.
	One of the most delightful places to live
in is the little village of Ætenburg, Europe.
It is ruled by no monarch, has no soldiers,
no police and no taxes. It is where four
countries meet, but belongs to none.
	The railway system in the Phillipine
Islands consists of a single line of antiquated
cars running over a road of less than 125
miles. To give us an idea of the form of
travel and transportation under the long
Spanish regime: This road connects Manil-
la with Dagupan, a town on the east coast
of the Island of Luzon. The gauge of the
road is three feet six inches, the ties of the
finest hardwood, the locomotives are insig-
nificant. The speed averages less than fif-
teen miles an hour. When the American
troops took possession of the Island, their
railroading astonished the natives, the speed
being frequently doubled. Compartment
coaches are used, each apartment seating
eight people. The first class are provided
with cane chairs: the second class, rough
wooden benches; the third class, entirely
bare and are usually crowded with natives
carrying baskets and bundles of all de-
scriptions.
	The recent visit of Queen Victoria to the
Emerald Isles was an occasion of rejoicing
and the heartiest welcome possible. All
accounts agree that as a mere matter of
artistic display, the Queen’s reception sur-
passed the Jubilee celebration of 1897. And
far more remarkable was the irrepressible
delight of the Irish people at having the
Queen again among them, after an absence
of nearly forty years. The visit was
undertaken in recognition of the bravery
of the Irish soldiers in South Africa, and no
attempt was made on either side to give it a
political coloring.
	On the 24th of this mouth was the 81st
birthday of this noble queen. For sixty-
five years she has dignified the English
throne with the noblest attributes of en-
lightened womanhood. As wife, mother
and monarch she has presented an example
of honor, virtue, piety and patience which
has served as a constant inspiration to her
subjects. The world has seen few such
queenly women, never a more womanly
queen.
	Alaska promises to be of more service in
other ways than producing gold. A curi-
ous and interesting part is the effort to save
our wild animals from extinction. Thirty-
five small, bare Alaskan islands are occupied
by fox farmers who are breeding wild foxes.
They pay the United States $100 a year for
these islands. The blue fox has been in
line of extinction with the buffalo, wild
pigeon and the ruffed grouse. The fox
farmers pay from $150.00 to $200.00 per
pair for breeding animals, and mnst wait a
dozen years for profit, then the profits are
large.	The animals are fed on unmarket-

(Column 2)
able fish, blubbers and cornmeal. Each
island is in charge of a keeper and two or
three native assistants. The Island of
Kadiak has 1275 foxes bred by an enter-
prising Long Islander, who believes that
this will lead to the domestication of many
other fur bearing animals.
	The Supreme Court decision just handed
down in the Kentucky gubernatorial con-
test puts an end to a disgraceful controversy.
In Kentucky for over a century the ques-
tion of disputed governorship has been one
for the legislative assembly to decide. 	It
did decide who was Governor and there
the case should have rested. 
	The present season has witnessed an epi-
demic in strikes, unlike anything the coun-
try has seen for years. 	Chicago gets her
share of strikes especially among the
machinists. 	A short time ago three thou-
sand machinists were out in Chicago, and
two thousand more in Cleveland and Colum-
bus, Ohio.
	The unrest in the trade threatened the
suspension of work among the Union
machinists all over the country. In Chicago
the violence which the strikers have resorted
to has not been beyond the power of the
officials to control. In New York the
militia have been called upon to preserve
order.
	Another strike a few days ago, in St.
Louis produced blood shed and death.	A
street car strike in Kansas City is also re-
corded. The car strikes are disastrous to
people living miles out of town, and who
are obliged to depend on car service, especi-
ally working people who are liable to lose
positions if not in time for work.
	Japanese emigration continues to excite
the laboring men in this country, and no
wonder. Hundreds of the Orientals are
coming in on every ship, some to take the
place of section hands on railroads and
others to work in factories.	It is reported
that the Japanese are leaving their country
by thousands because of the danger of a
conflict between Japan and Russia. It is
estimated that ten thousand Japs landed on
the Pacific coast last month.  They dis-
embarked in British Columbia and made
their way across the border to get employ.
ment when and where they might.
	The Bubonic plague seems to be a serious
matter. The Chinese are in mortal terror
of innoculation. They gather in mobs and
the Federal authorities are obliged to take a
hand in the disturbances.	The Chinese
highbinders have threatened to assassinate
any Chinese who submit to innoculation
against the plague. There are twenty-three
thousand Chinese in the Chinese quarters
in San Francisco, and they are terrorized at
the prospect of vacination. With the
“Haffkine Brophylactic” most of the
Chinese were at first willing to submit to
the innoculation, until the  highbinders
issued the ultimatum that the whites wanted
to kill off the Chinese and took this method
of doing so.	The plague is a high fever,
indisposition one day, collapse the next and
the following day death. There seems to
be no medical treatment for it but the serum
which is injected into the blood, and in that
way destroys the food of the bacilli and the
fever is abated and recovery assured.	Rats
are a ready victim to the disease and die
in numbers, they are the most dangerous
means of carrying the infection. In the
last dispatches from San Francisco the
board of health had quarantined China-
town, and all that section of the city is sur

(Column 3)
rounded by police who refuse to let any one
pass in or out. The quarantine also in-
cludes street cars, which are not allowed to
pass through the district.
	A session that we have been much inter-
ested in this month is the Mothers’ Con-
gress. The papers and oral arguments
gave subjects for consideration.	An inter-
change of thoughts and ideas is good for us
all.	After the reception of Professor Cris-
mon,s paper at the Mothers’ Congress at
Des Moines, Iowa, I am inclined to think
that the gentleman will not be so free to
offer his valuable information.
	The resolution that was passed at our
Congress to prevent young girls from enter-
ing wine rooms and the sale of cigarettes to
minors, deserves the thanks of every mother
and father in Utah. We have laws here
against these very things and every one
should pay the penalty for disregarding
these laws. If the City Council pays no at-
tention to the appeal of the mothers, then
it is time for them to take a hand in the
municipal affairs of our fair city.
	Last, but not least, in our current events
of this month, is Decoration Day.	This
day is for a tribute of love and honor to
the nation’s dead.	No other government
has a day like this, others have achieved
their independence, at the cost of blood,
but they have never thought of setting
apart a day for the decoration of the graves
of the dead soldiers.
	In Utah and in other states new graves
have been made in the last two years. The
brave lads who heard the call for soldiers
sprang to arms in response. Some of these
were boys who but a little while ago were
in our public schools, and when they re-
turned to us were hidden away in their final
homes and were given back dust to dust.
Many a mound which covered the crumb-
ling dust of heroes was decorated yesterday
with flowers and wreaths, symbols of a sor-
row with which we would not part; tributes
of a love that lives while memory lasts.
				LUELLA RHODES.


6          WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


OBITUARY.
SARAH WILLIAMS DAW.
It is with feelings of sorrow that we record the
death of Sister Sarah Daw, wife of Isaac Daw,
and daughter of George and Mary Williams, who
peacefully passed away at her home in Cedar
Fort, Utah Co., Utah, April 11, 1900, of Bright’s
disease, aged sixty-six years and twenty days.
She embraced the Gospel in England and emi-
grated to Utah in the year 1856, crossing the plains
in Captain Millen Atwood’s handcart company.
Deceased was married to Isaac Daw the same
year that she arrived in Utah, they lived at West
Jordan for six years, then settled at Cedar Fort,
where she resided till the time of her death. She
was the mother of seven children, two sons and
five daughters and thirty-five grandchildren. Sis-
ter Daw held the office of director and teacher in
the Cedar Valley ward Relief Society at the time
of her death. 							to
We her colaborers deem it a pleasant duty
testify to her faithfulness in the Relief Society.
RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT.
Whereas, God has seen fit to take unto Himsel
our beloved director and teacher, Sister Sarah W.
Daw,
Resolved, That we mourn in her death the loss
of a faithful worker, a comforter in the hour of
trouble, devoted wife and mother and a true
Latter-day Saint.
Resolved, That we esteem her faithful labors
worthy the emulation of every member of our
society.
Resolved, That we sympathize and mourn with
the aged and bereaved husband, and the children,
grandchildren and friends.
Resolved, That we send a copy of these reso-


[Column 1]
lutions to the WOMAN’s EXPONENT for publication,
a copy be placed on the record of the Relief Soci-
ety and one be sent to the bereaved family.
Good bye, Sister Daw, we miss thy face,
Thy loving smile and gentle grace;
Gone to the Father on that happy shore,
Where pain and sorrow thou will share no more,
ELIZABETH BERRY, President.
MELISSA A. WEEKS, Vice-President.
FANNIE N. BERRY, Secretary.
MARGARET BENNETT, Treasurer.
ELLIS PETERSON LUTHAN.
Died at her home in Haden, Idaho, April 14,
1900, of pneumonia, Sister Ellis Peterson Luthan.
Deceased was born December 2, 1840. She emi-
grated to Utah in 1853 and has ever been a faith-
ful Latter-day Saint. She was a loving wife and
mother and leaves a husband and eight children
and a host of friends to mourn her loss.
Sister Luthan was first counselor to the president
of the Relief Society (Martha Little) in the Haden
ward, having occupied that position from the time
of the organization, now five years. She always
gave good counsel and donated liberally, was
faithful in all her duties. She was filled with the
spirit of the Lord when testifying of His goodness
and power, and always urged the sisters to love
each other and be faithful to their duties. She had
no fear of death and said it would be joy to meet
her mother and those who had gone before; and yet
she felt she would like to live for the sake of her
family. By her request the sisters fasted and
prayed, but it seemed that her time had come and
her spirit took its flight in peace and joy. She
passed away like a babe falling asleep, she had
been ill just six days.
The whole community mourn her loss, for she
truly was a mother in Israel to all who needed her
loving care, visiting the sick, ministering to the
poor, comforting the afflicted and cheering all who
came within her presence. She was of a most
amiable disposition and by her smiles and cheer-
fulness would dispel all the gloom around her.
The funeral was largely attended, the Society
house was crowded to overflowing, and a long
procession of seventy conveyances followed the re- 
mains to the grave and the greatest respect was
shown to the family.
MARTHA LITTLE, President.
MAY SMITH, Secretary.
MARY SMITH ANDERSON.
Sister Mary S. Anderson, of Rexburg, Idaho,
departed this life May 8, 1900, at twenty 
minutes past one and the funeral was held on the
the 10th at one o’clock p. m. Deceased was great-
ly beloved and many sad and aching hearts are
left to mourn her loss here upon the earth.
Sister Anderson was born February 23, 1841, in
Dundee, Scotland. She embraced the Gospel at
an early age and left her home in Dundee in com-
pany with her mother. her brothers and three sis-
ters, April 30, 1856.
They sailed from Liverpool, England, in the
ship Thornton, May 4 of the same year, arrived in
New York May 14, continuing their journey by
railway and steamboat, arriving at Iowa City,
June 26, and started to cross the plains in the
fourth handcart company, July 15th, Captain J.
G. Willey. Millen Atwood, was Captain of the
hundred in which they traveled to Salt Lake City;
they had a very tedious journey across the plains.
Miss Smith was married to Andrew A. Ander-
son, July, 1857, and lived in Lehi until 1860, when
with her husband and one child she moved to
Smithfield, Cache Co., where she lived until the
fall of 1884, when she with her husband and fam-
ly moved to Rexburg, Idaho. She filled the office
of first counselor to the president of the Relief
Society of Fremont Stake, and she traveled around
the Stake, giving good counsel to elevate the sis-
ters up to a high standard of love and good works,
and many sought her counsel and it was said of
her at her funeral that she was not only a mother
to the whole town and Stake, and that no other
woman in the county would have been missed
more or was loved more, gems of precious thought
were always coming from her lips. Her death
cast a gloom over all, she made the weary take up
their burdens and praise God for his wonderful
love and care for His people. She has left a hus-
band and seven children, three sons and four
daughters to mourn her loss; three sons and one
daughter preceded her behind the vail. One of
her sons, A. S. Anderson, was a member of the
State Convention that drafted the Constitution, and
she was also the mother of J. A. Anderson of the
Utah Volunteers, who made his name honored by
his nobility of character portrayed while in
Manilla.

[Column 2]
Sister Anderson did considerable work in the
Manti Temple for her dead relatives and also
officiated for them two or three months in the
Temple at Logan,
In all the trials through which she was called to
pass she was never heard to murmur or complain,
but al[-]ays had a word to strengthen others in
their faith and encourage them to press onward
and upward. The writer has often heard her
speak in our General Conference of the Relief
Society and in officers’ meetings, and felt to sym-
pathize with the bereaved who so sadly miss her
in the places she occupied. May the Lord abund-
antly bless them that they may emulate her wise
example and carry out the precepts she taught
them. that they may meet her hereafter never
again to be separated nor to know the grief of
parting.
RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT.
Whereas, In the dispensation of providence
God has seen fit to call home one of His daughters
who labored long and well as first counselor to
the Stake president of the Relief Society of the
Fremont Stake, and who also traveled many hun-
dred miles visiting the different branches of the
society in sunshine and showers when Fremont was
united with Bannock Stake; and
Whereas, She has proved herself an efficient
worker in all her various duties, and was one
whose kindness and generosity of spirit brought
sunshine to many hearts, visiting the sick and
afflicted and helping the needy in time of want;
and we can truly say she was a peacemaker and an
administering angel, her memory will ever live
with her many friends, as her bright gems of
thought have been scattered broadcast to live in
the hearts of men,
Resolved, That we her sisters, united with her
in the presidency of the Fremont Stake of the
Relief Society, do deeply feel her loss in connec-
tion with her earnest efforts and wise counsel, as
to know her was to love her, as we know she was
one of God’s choicest spirits; nevertheless in this
our loss we feel to bow to the mandates of an Al-
wise Creator who doeth all things well.
Resolved, That a copy of these resolutions be sent
to her family. a copy be placed on the Stake
record, also one be sent to the WOMAN'S EXPONENT
for publication.
TEMPERANCE HINCKLEY, President,
SARAH A. BARNES, Secretary.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.                         7


THE MOTHERS' CONGRESS.
	The Third annual session of the Mothers’
Congress convened in the Assembly Hall at
10 o’clock a. m., May 18. Those present
were bright, representative women
	The speakers’ stand was beautifully dec-
orated with flowering plants and jardinieres
filled with snowballs, making a bright
picture.
	The meeting in the morning opened with
prayer by Mrs. M. Isabella Horne and fol-
lowing this the president, Mrs Nellie Little,
read a note from Mrs. Antoinette Kinney,
tendering her resignation as recording
secretary of the Congress. The resignation
being accepted, Mrs. Augusta Grant nomi-
nated Mrs. Ella Stewart, who was elected
to fill the vacancy.
	President Nellie Little then read an ad-
dress: ‘‘The Utah Mothers’ Congress has
met this morning to hold its third annual
meeting, since its organization a little more
than two years ago, a gradual interest in the
work has been awakened.
	“At the previous annual meetings held
in ’98 and ’99, the promoters of the
good cause in Utah were fortunate in se-
curing as speakers eminent women from the
East. They, with our citizens men and
women, talked on subjects relating to the
nature, the home and the parent of the
child. At this present annual meeting the
subjects will be treated by residents of
Utah, and with the past two years’ exper-
ience as a guide we feel confident that the
Congress now in session will be a success.
	“The scope embraced by the Mothers’
club is a wide one; unlimited, unrestricted.
While some organizations of women are
established solely for literary, social or
philanthropic purposes, mothers’ clubs may
properly consider any subject pertaining to
the condition of the human family.
	“Where the surroundings of children are
undesirable and create immoral tendencies
the co-operation of the mothers in changing
the condition is very effectual. The true
mother will consider the welfare, not only
of her own child alone, but will be inter-
ested in improving the condition of all chil-
dren in her locality. The woman who
takes into her heart her own children may
be a very ordinary woman, says the novel-
ist, but she who takes into her heart the
children of others, is one of God’s mothers.
The mother, too, receives her immediate
reward in helping to improve the condition
of the youth in her community, for what-
ever elevates the moral standard of children
collectively will elevate the standard of the
individual child.
	“Here in the west she has greater power
than her sister in the east. She can vote
for men to go into office who will make just
laws, laws which favor the many and not
the few, and influence and modify legisla-
tion to the good of the community.
	“Woman’s influence on the child, on the
home and on society, is recognized and
truly appreciated by our fathers, brothers
and husbands. A minister well known in
 our city has been quoted thus: ‘A child
may get along with an inferior kind of a
father, but God pity him if he has a poor
excuse for a mother.’ It is these poor ex-
cuses of mothers that the Mothers’ Club
should endeavor to aid. They are not al-
ways poor excuses intentionally, but through
ignorance, through lack of education. They
received no training, no teaching in their
youth for the responsible position of parent-
[-]ood, in consequence of which they have
no system, no standard to guide them in
the training of their own children.
	“The work of mothers’ clubs is devoted
to the good and elevation of others. Very
seldom is self-culture the primary object.
Necessarily the mothers are benefited by
their associating together, but their first
thought is to bestow a benefit on others,
their aim is wholly altruistic. If, as is
claimed, all the great evils of life have their
root in selfishness, mothers’ clubs with an
altruistic motive must accomplish results
just the reverse of evil. We do not expect
to revolutionize the existing order of things
but we can aid our fathers, brothers and
husbands in promoting a better condition
of society. If we do this, our efforts will
be rewarded "
	Mrs. Anderson led in the hymn, “Nearer
my God.to Thee.”

	MRS. DUSENBURRY’S PAPER.

	The time has arrived when co-operation
is fast taking the place of in[-]ividual labor,
that is, labor where one man or woman
makes an effort to carry on successfully a
number of occupations alone. For many
years the male portion of our population
have seen the necessity of specializing their
part of the labor; but the mother is still
toiling and wondering why she cannot be a
success at child raising, cooking, washing,
ironing, sewing, sweeping, dusting, calling
and entertaining. She little realizes that
any one of these occupations would furnish
sufficient employment if done in a profes-
sional way, by one person. She does not
stop to think that the world means many
people and she is wearing her life out for


but the few who are especially near and dear
to her. She endeavors for a few moments
each day to teach her child something of
the life of Christ, never realizing that the
little one can have no conception of the
Savior unless he has present living exam- 
ples upon which to buil[-] his ideals. She
tells him Christ spent His life toiling for
His fellowmen. She relates to him stories
of the never-tiring labor of Jesus for the
rich and poor alike. She pictures to the
little mind the beauties of such a life, yet
day by day, year by year, passes and she
toils on; for the few who surround her
hearthstone. It is said, “to work for one’s
self is the lowest kind of labor. To work
for one’s family is but one step better.”
Then there is but one kind of service that
will answer the present needs of the human
family and that is service for all the world.
How are the mothers and women to assist
in this struggle for a broader field of action
if they are not awakened to the necessity of
co-operation ?
	The world today is much exercised over
the child-study question, but comparatively
little is written or said about mother-study.
Fewer punishments and less scoldings
would take place if we stopped to think
that the fault for which we are punishing is
only a reflection of that same trait in our-
selves.
	If the parent would begin with reforma-
tion of self, the desire for self-improvement
would grow greater, while the unnatural
warping of child character would grow less.
What we want is mothers not alone in a
physical sense, for this attribute we hold in
common with the lower animals—but in-
tellectual, broad-minded, spiritual, social
mothers—mothers who are willing to meet
bravely the world, and battle with its diffi-
culties; mothers whose names will even
stand with honor in the lives of their poster-
ity. These are the kind of women we must
have, but the kind we will never get until
the women of the century are fully awaken-
ed to the importance of organization and
co-operation.
	We must have broader-minded mothers
and there is no better way to develop them
than through interest shown in their little
ones. Just let the mother feel that you are
unselfishly laboring for her children and
she will give you her hearty co-operation.
This is the only successful way of obtain-
ing the support and assistance of the greater
number of our mothers today, I have found
it to be the only thing that will make
mothers’ clubs successful organizations. It
has been the custom for years for mothers
to remain home days, weeks, yes, years,
ceaselessly laboring at everything, for the
benefit of her loved ones; she has never
stopped to think “that love grows by being
spent,” and she is never requiring her little
ones to labor for her. Her love each day may
grow brighter and stronger for them while
they are seeking other things to love. We
must, as mothers, mingle with the world;
for it is the only way to develop that love
for humanity which is character building.

NOTES AND NEWS.

	THE leading social event of the Mothers
Congress, which recently convened in De
Moines May 21, was the reception given
by the Governor and Mrs. Shaw in the
Governor’s apartments in the Capital.

MISS JANE ADDAMS, of Hull House,
Chicago, sailed for Paris last week. She 
has been appointed a member of the Jury of
Awards for the Paris Exposition. She will
serve also on a Committee on Exhibits in
Social Economics.

	MISS ANTHONY, after rendering good ser-
vice at the Annual meeting and festival of
the New England W. S. A., was abducted
by the Rev Anna H. Shaw and Miss Lucy
E. Anthony and carried off by them to rest
at their cottage on Cape Cod, which Miss
Anthony had never seen.

	“MR. DOOLEY” has now been discoursing
on the relation of marriage to politics. He
takes the ground that “a man in pollytics
has got to be marrid,” aud that an un-
marrid man don’t get along in pollytics be-
cause he don’t need th’ money.” Mr.
Dooley tells Mr. Hennessy that “ye’ll find
all th’ big jobs held be marrid men, and all
th’ temp’ry clerkships be bachelors.”

	MISS KATHERINE REED, daughter of
ex-Speaker Reed, was among the forty-
eight young women who took degrees in the
Women’s Law Class of New York Univer
sity last week. Mrs. John P. Munn, Presi-
dent of the Woman’s Legal Education
Society, presided and announced that the
$25,000 needed to endow the chair of the
lecturer to the Woman's Law Class had
been raised in full.

MRS. MARY HARRISON MCKEE, daugh-
ter of expresident Harrison, is one of the
active working commissioners to the Paris
Exposition from New York State. It was
only after a special act of the Legislature,
in which the commission was created, that
Mrs. McKee, Mrs. Clarence Burns and Mrs.
N. Metcalf received their appointments.
They have an equal vote with the men on
all matters, and do active work in the
selection of exhibits. Mrs. McKee has
proved her helpfulness to the commission
by advancing many practical ideas.

SUSAN B. ANTHONY will lecture at
Chautauqua this summer. Shades of the
past, what has happened to Bishop Vin-
cent? All roads lead to Rome with Susan,
and no matter what her subject may be,
you will discover that it bears a vital and
thrilling relation to woman suffrage
Wherein Susan is wise.
