[Column 1]
OUR SCANDINAVIAN WOMEN.
A TYPE. 
I sat in father's cosy room one day, 
Perusing dreamily a favorite book,
Engrossed in Hamlet Prince of Denmark's dreams 
And wondering how Prince Hamlet used to look,
An upward glance dispelled my bookish thoughts, 
Awoke the real in my wakeful dream, 
Revealed a life which I had never known, 
A life which I so truly noble deem, 
I fain would make my life what that doth seem.
My grandma's picture hung upon the wall,
And as I looked upon the kindly face 
With lips compressed, yet wreathed 
In smiles which time and age could not efface, 
I lost myself and woke on Denmark's Isle-
And see! in middle age and passing fair, 
Calling her little sons about her knee,
To teach them honesty, love, faith and prayer, 
A woman struggling with her load of care.
And lo! a weary traveler stops to rest.
She bids him welcome, shares her frugal meal—
“One of the least of these my little ones," 
Her lips repeat, and blessed does she feel.
She hears his message, life and light divine, 
Calmly and peacefully it floods her soul; 
Her heart rejoices in the light and life 
Which shall endure while endless ages roll:
She recognizes truth, loves and accepts the whole.
A struggle follows: he who has vowed 
To love and cherish her while life shall last, 
Though he believes cannot endure the scorn 
That comes to those 'mong whom her lot is cast, 
He hears but answers not the Shepherd's call; 
He sees but follows not the heavenly light; 
He fears but does not shun the demon dark
That drags him onward in its tireless flight, 
To hurl him down to dreary, awful night. 
And so she stands alone, with only God. 
Her chosen, neverfailing, powerful friend; 
And now leaves husband, home, her native land, 
All things on which a woman’s joys depend—
And guided by the beacon light of faith, 
Goes forth in search of Zion's holy hill,
Leading her youthful sons from vice and sin,
To the pure fountain of her Father's will-
Praying and weeping-Praying and trusting still.
I cannot trace her o'er the trackless plain-
My eyes anon fill up with scalding tears-
A hopeful pilgrim in a pilgrim band, 
Footsore, aweary, prey to constant fears 
From savage hordes; or fainting by the way, 
From nightly vigil o'er some sufferer kept: 
Who ere the morn by plague is snatched away, 
His requiem sung, hot tears of anguish wept, 
E'en though at eve so peacefully he slept.
What wonder that her woman frame gave way?
What wonder that her spirit took its flight? 
And yet she lived to see this blessed land,
Saw darkness bursting into glorious light: 
Saw friends, saw home, the city of the Saints--

[Column 2]
Saw and rejoiced, rejoiced and was content-
Nor deemed the sacrifice she made too great 
For this one blissful hour by heaven sent, 
In which the fulness of her soul was spent. 
HEROINES.
So I move from my father's study, 
Where grandma's picture is hung, 
And I think of the hundreds of women 
That I live and move among-
Women who, just like grandma, 
Have breasted the stormy wave, 
Women with souls as noble, 
Women with hearts as brave. 
And I see their trials and hardships,
Their doubts and sickening fears, 
Their struggles and privations, 
Their heart-aches and their tears;
And I see them enter a valley,
That is desolate, bleak and bare, 
And gladly accept the burden 
Which it is their lot to share.
And whether with loved companions 
They greet the barren soil; 
Or whether for son or brother, 
They harden their hands by toil, 
They are earnest, sober, truthful, 
With a constant great delight 
In the overthrow of Error, 
And the victory of right.
So with men of might and valor, 
Hand in hand these women go, 
Toiling on 'mid heat and hunger, 
Or 'mid winter's drift of snow; 
So heart and soul with their brothers, 
They battle 'gainst every sin, 
With a struggle that defies submission,
With a vigor that's bound to win.
TRIUMPHS.
A lapse of a few brief seasons now, 
And I look again at the lonely vale; 
The changes made by the faithful plow, 
Would read, methinks, like a fairy tale; 
But those women are there and are still the same, 
Though not renowned or known to fame,
And still they come from those northern climes,
And are welcomed still to our blessed land 
In the mountain-tops, where in ancient times, 
God's prophets saw the faithful stand.
And the vale is filled with happy homes, 
With meadows, orchards and growing wheat, 
With chapels where, when the Sabbath comes, 
These honest people for worship meet,
And praise their Maker in prayer and song,
And approve what's right and condemn what's 
wrong, 
And always these noble sisters live, 
By the light of the Savior's plan,
And ever their hearts and souls will give 
For the betterment of man. 
Thank heaven for all these sturdy ones,
These men of toil, these women true,
Who, working on through storms and suns,
Found naught too hard for their hands to do,
And whether on earth or whether above,
We open the records of life and love,
By the side of the names of our Danish sires.
Whose hearts grew warm in the Gospel light 
Will our mothers be, for those living fires 
Have kept them as truly bright.
ALOFA.


[Column 1]
	DAUGHTERS OF THE REVOLUTION. 
                                               
                     (Concluded.)

	Liberty Bell, which bears the inscription:
“Proclaim Liberty throughout the land to
all the inhabitants thereof.”   And as I
stood by the old bell the words of the dear
old poem came back to my mind:

[Column 2]
	    That old state-house bell is silent,
		Hushed is now its iron tongue,
	    But the spirit it awakened,
		Still is living, ever young.
	    And while we greet the smiling sunlight.
		On the Fourth of each July,
	    We will not forget the bell, 
		Which twixt heaven and earth,
	     Rang out loudly, Independence!
		Which, please God, shall never die !”

	The next place visited was the Betsy
Ross House, the birthplace of “Old
Glory.”   From here we went to Christ’s
Church, a most historic spot; for many
prominent people—among them General
Washington, Franklin and the Penn family
—used to worship here.    The Continental
Congress appointed July 20, 1775, as a day
for prayer and fasting, and as a body at-
tended this church, and after Independence
bell ceased ringing on July 4, 1776, the
chimes of this church caught up the strain
and rang out the message anew.
	The last place visited on Tuesday morn-
ing was old St. Peter’s Church, where
Washington used to attend service. There
are many old interesting inscriptions and
tablets, one of the most important of which
is erected by the Pennsylvania Sons of the
Revolution, in honor of Captain William
Shippen, who was killed at the battle of
Princetown January 3, 1777.
	On Tuesday afternoon a most elaborate
breakfast was given the retiring delegates
by the General Society D. R. I will again
refer you to Miss Sterling and Miss Snow for
a very complete account of that delightful
event, for they have both told it so well.
	Wednesday, April 26, we went to Valley
Forge, and I am sure it was a day never to
be forgotten by any of us. We were met at
the Valley Forge R. R. Station by large
coaches in which we were driven all over
the historic spots.    Captain I. Heston
Todd, who owns the greater part of Valley
Forge, led the party and pointed out and
explained the points of interest. We know
that the American army went into camp at
this place on the 19th of December, 1777.
and remained there until June 19, 1778.
This is an excellent place for an encamp-
ment, as the natural features of the country
would enable the entrenchments to be made
very strong. The Schuylkill river bounds
it on one side, Valley Creek—a large
stream—on the other. These two streams
really take such a course as to encircle
Valley Forge on three sides, the other side
is protected by the earth works, planned by
one of Lafayette’s aid de camps. Modern
engineers are puzzled by some of the fea-
tures in these earth works. General
Washington’s headquarters are at the point
where the Schuylkill and Valley creek flow
nearest together.  Fort Washington is
directly opposite the Schuylkill, and the
headquarters of Lafayette and General
Knox were at the end nearest Valley creek,
while those of General Wayne were at the
other extremity of the Fort. Fort Hunt-
ingdon is almost directly opposite Wash-
ington’s and Wayne's headquarters, so that
the four headquarters really represent the
four extremities of the army encampment.
	Nothing remains of the Forts except the


[Column 1]
earth works, and the point that would have
been most accessible to an attacking force
was Fort Washington; so that the earth-
works are higher and steeper than at any
other place. The first name of this historic
spot was Mount Job, but there was, I think,
in 1757 a forge built on Valley creek, and
f[-]om that the place came to be known as
Valley Forge, where the raw recruits from
thirteen colonies were so ably drilled by
Baron Van Steuben, that they were able to
defeat the best English soldiers. We are
all familiar with the account of the suffer-
ings of our soldiers during that “terrible
winter” at Valley Forge, but Captain Todd
told me that the mortality among the
soldiers from the south was much greater
than among the northern soldiers, because
in building their huts they would not dig
deep enough into the earth, and were,
therefore, not protected against the cold.
Though the ground was covered by
graves at one time the only one that is now
marked is that of Captain John Waterman.
He is buried on a part of Captain Todd’s
property, and the Captain offered to give to
the Daughters of the Revolution that spot 
of ground, on condition that they would
erect a monument in honor of the brave
dead. The offer was accepted and the
monument will be erected.
The only buildings of Revolutionary fame
remaining are General Washington’s head-
quarters and the old camp schoolhouse. We
had lunch at a modern hotel called “The
Washington Inn.” The headquarters
house is in charge of the Centennial
and Memorial Association of Valley Forge.
This society is composed of ladies and
gentlemen. The present Regent is a lady,
Mrs. Anna M. Holstein, of Bridgeport.
The following statement is made of the
Headquarters House: “From cellar to
attic it is in good preservation, and appears
today precisely as it did when Washington
was domiciled within it. The doors, with
bells and locks, are the very same his
hands have moved; the floors, except a por-
tion of the one in the office room, are those
over which the Great Chieftain has walked
in many a weary hour; the window glass
and sash are unchanged since the days
when anxious eyes looked through them at
the soldiers’ huts upon the hills.”
In speaking of Valley Forge Thackeray
tells how easy it would have been for Howe
with his great army in Philadelphia to have
utterly defeated the American forces and
put a period to the Revolution at this time,
he says. “But it was ordained by heaven,
and for the good, as we can now have no
doubt, of both empires, that the great
Western Republic should separate from us;
and the gallant soldiers who fought on her
side, their indomitable chief above all, had
the glory of facing and overcoming, not
only veterans amply provided and inured
to war, but wretchedness, cold, hunger,
dissensions and treason in their own camp,
where all must have gone to rack but for
the pure, unquenchable flame of patriotism
that was forever burning in the bosom of
the heroic leader. What a constancy!
What a magnaminity! What a surprising
persistency against fortune! Washington
before the enemy was no better nor braver
than hundreds who fought with him or
against him; but Washington, the chief of
a nation in arms, doing battle with distracted
parties: calm in the midst of conspiracy;
serene against the open foe before him and
the darker enemies at his back; Washington
inspiring order and spirit into troops hun-

[Column 2]
gry and in rags; stung by ingratitude yet
ever ready to forgive; in defeat invincible,
magnaminous in conquest and never so
sublime as on the day when he laid down
his victorious sword and sought his noble
retirement. Here indeed is a character to
admire and revere, a fame without a flaw.”
On Wednesday evening the Pennsylvania
Society D. R. gave their delightful recep-
tion. A number of Colonial pictures were
shown, illustrating a lecture. The speaker
gave us a part of Paul Revere, but I did
not like his rendition of it nearly as well as
I did that of our Vice-Regent, Mrs. Wil-
liams.
Thursday morning we went to German-
town, saw the house where Gibbul-Stewart
painted the pictures of George and Martha
Washington, also Robert Morris’s house,
that Howe used as his headquarters while
in Philadelphia. We also visited most of
the scenes spoken of in Hugh Wynne, but
the principal places of interest were the
Germantown Academy, a boys’ school
founded by Franklin, and frequently visited
by Washington and the Chew House. This
house is still owned by the Chew family,
one of the wealthiest and most exclusive in
Philadelphia, and is not open to the public,
but as a special courtesy to the D. R., Mrs.
Chew threw her house open for their in-
spection. Chief Justice Chew was a great
friend of Washington’s, but on the morning
of October 4, 1777, some English soldiers
entrenched themselves in the house and the
battle of Germantown occurred right on
this spot. Mrs. Chew was so charming
and gave us a full history of the event.
The D. R’s. were officially represented
in the services of the unveiling of the Grant
monument on April 27, 1899, and our
President-General placed a laurel wreath
tied with the D. R. colors.on the monument.
On Thursday afternoon we visited the
University of Pennsylvania and were shown
about by Mr. Penniman, Dean of the Col-
lege department.
All of Friday was consumed in the drive
through Fairmount park. This ended the
week’s excursions, and Miss Sterling said
she considered it the cream of the enjoyable
events. We visited the Penn House, the
first brick house built in Philadelphia. It
was built by William Penn in 1682; also the
Belmont Mansion. The home of Judge
Richard Peters stands on George’s hill and
overlooks the city of Philadelphia. Wash-
ington used to ride out early in the morn-
ing alone. Other guests were Jefferson,
Adams, Morris, General Steuben, Louis
Phillipi and Franklin. Mount Pleasant,
Benedict Arnold’s home, later Baron Steu-
ben’s, was the last spot visited on the
drive.
JULIA A. FARNSWORTH LUND.


10		WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]	
	LADIES’ SEMI-MONTHLY MEETING.
	The Ladies’ Semi-Monthly Meeting was
held on Saturday, May 12, 1900, in the
Fourteenth Ward Hall, President M. I.
Horne presiding. Opened by singing,
“God moves in a mysterious way.’’ Prayer
by Sister Clara C. Cannon. Singing “Let
us pray, gladly pray.’’ Minutes read and
approved.
	President Horne said it seems necessary
that we assemble ourselves together often
and be stirred up in the principles of the
Gospel. Spoke of the calamity of the
Scofield explosion that had recently hap-
pened, causing so much death and sorrow

[Column 2]
among us, and that she would like to give
every woman in Salt Lake Stake a chance
to contribute to their relief.
	A sister from the Eleventh Ward spoke
of the terrible calamity, said their society
had made a collection for the widows and
orphans.
	A sister in the congregation bore her
testimony and related her experiences in the
missionary field in Holland. Another sis-
ter bore a testimony of the truth and told of
her interest in Temple work.
	The congregation sang “Redeemer of
Israel.’’
	Sister Zina D. H. Young said the spirit
we partake of here refreshes us in body and
in mind, begged all to be humble and meek
and true to their obligations and never
speak evil especially of the priesthood.
	Sister E. B. Wells spoke of the wonder-
ful history of the building we are now in,
and of the spirit of God that people had been
endowed with who had spoken here. She
thought we ought to keep good records in
our Relief Society, felt that the history of
our work should be written and kept. Spoke
of the difficulty that the Young Ladies’
Society was having in trying to collect the
early history of their organization. Re-
ferred to her recent trip to New York and
how she enjoyed meeting with the brothers
and sisters there. Testified that the Lord
had always preserved her from accident in
her travels. Miss M. Snow and herself
were well received by the women of the
Daughters of the Revolution. Told us she
was preparing a complete file of the EX-
PONENT for the Paris Exposition; that it
was the oldest woman’s paper in the West.
She had often heard it said theEXPONENTwas
a good missionary, and a number of our
missionaries had told her it was. Thanked
the Lord for the organization of the Relief
Society. When the Prophet Joseph turned
the key for women in 1842, he organized
the first society of women in the world, not
till that time were women organized and
the women of the world were adopting
many of our plans of organization in their
societies. She believed our influence was
being felt for good among the women of
the world. Spoke of their good work, she
felt the Relief Society was the high school
of the womens organizations. Said she felt
it was proper that the Salt Lake Stake
Relief Society should give fifty dollars to
the Scofield sufferers.
	Sister Barnes spoke of the influence of
the EXPONENT and the good she believed it
would do in the world, and spoke of thejndg-
ments that would begin in the house of the
Lord.
	Sister Langton said she desired to live to
do good and be filled with charity.
	A sister in the congregation bore a testi-
mony of the truth.
	President Horne said she thought the
price of the EXPONENT was within the
reach of all, and thought we ought to
support it.
	Meeting adjourned. Singing, “The
God we adore.’’ Benediction by Sister
Julia C. Howe.
		M. Y. TAYLOR, Act. Sec.

of all the meetings.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


‘WHAT IS SO RARE AS A DAY IN JUNE."
To Mrs. Pallas Eliza Clark.
BY L. M. H.
Fond greetings and congratulations,
Sister, beloved, my own!
On this anniversary,
That counts thee eighty-one.
This day of bright, glad summer,
Radiant, “Merrie June,”
Arrayed in pristine beauty,
Laden with sweet perfume.
On mountain, hill and valley,
Queen June is holding sway,
All nature’s forces rally,
In gorgeous array,
Grand musicales are given,
In melody of song, 
That echoes are repeating,
And joyously prolong. 
Hearts now are thrilled with music,
Wakened anew to praise. 
The magical enchantment,
Of glorious June days,
Out from her groves and bowers,
With offerings we have come,
Of fairest, sweetest flowers,
To crown thee eighty-one! 
We wreathe thy brow with garlands, 
Perennial in bloom,
Fadeless flowers from gardens,
Of love’s eternal June.
Time lead thee kindly onward,
Through twilight’s eventide, 
Into a perfect morning,
Of love-light glorified.


MUSINGS.
I stood in the Valley of Mesa, 
	Neath palm and clustering vine,
Then gazed on the beauties of nature,
	My thought most near the Divine; 
With orange groves breathing their fragrance
	Roses exhaling their sweets
The chirping of birds in the distance,
	Making earth’s gladness complete.

My being was thrilled with the splendor,
	My soul enrapt with an awe,
Earthly joys I seemed to surrender,
	To Heaven’s superior law;
And e’en while I lingered in musing,
	A glorious mantle was spread,
O’er the world, a halo diffusing,
	As promise to those long since dead.

That glow that comes after sunset,
	Ere shades of night shroud the earth;
Those pale purple mists, soft and mellow,
	Assure new day and new birth.
Our hearts keep time in perfection,
	In rhythm to nature’s sweet song,
We dwell on the great Resurrection-
	Ask why this struggle prolong ?
	ELLIS R. SHIPP.


[Column 1] 
IN MEMORIAM.
MARY ANN PRICE HYDE. 
"The journey is over, the fight is fought, 
She has reached the home of her love, 
And the smile on her beautiful face is caught, 
From the land of smiles above." 
"After the burden and heat of the day, 
The starry calm of night, 
After the rough and toilsome way, 
A sleep in the robe of white." 
"The silent chamber faceth the East,
Faceth the dawn of day,
And the shining feet of our great High Priest, 
Shall break through the shadows grey." 
From time to time we are called upon to 
chronicle the demise of one after another of 
the veteran sisters and mothers in Israel, 
whose lives have been shining examples 
among the Latter-day Saints. Many of 
those women who helped to build the 
foundations of the Church have gone to a 
higher sphere and those who remain of this 
shining galaxy of the sisterhood are fewer 
than those who have departed. 

[Column 2]
Sister Mary A. Hyde was one to be re-
membered among those who helped to 
establish plural marriage. The principle 
of this holy order of matrimony was taught 
her by the Prophet Joseph Smith himself,
and he performed the ceremony that united 
her to Apostle Orson Hyde in the city of 
Nauvoo, Illinois, in the year 1843. Sister 
Hyde honored the principle and was her-
self an honor to the family into which she 
was received at the time of her marriage 
and ever afterwards to the time of her de-
cease. She lived to a good old age, eighty-
four years the 5th of June, 1900. She de-
parted in peace at 6:30 on the morning of 
the 16th of June, 1900. She had suffered 
intensely from various causes for the last 
three weeks previous to that time, though 
her faith in the gift of healing and the ad-
ministration of the laying on of hands never 
wavered in the least, but she longed to go 
and be at rest, and meet with her husband 
and those she knew and loved while they 
tabernacled in the flesh.
Deceased was born at Lea, Gloucester-
shire, England, June 5, 1816, and had a 
very happy childhood in the bosom of a 
united and prosperous family. Her father 
was an honorable business man, and her 
mother educated, cultivated and refined.
Her maiden name was Price. Miss Price 
was educated at Broad Oaks Academy, a 
select school for girls, not far from her 
home in Gloucestershire. She was the first 
of her family to receive the Gospel as taught 
by the Elders of the Latter day Saints, and 
with a younger sister, Emma, a very 
beautiful girl, preceded the other members 
of the family to Nauvoo, arriving in 1841.
Her brother had sent a present to the 
Prophet Joseph Smith, which gave Miss 
Price an opportunity to introduce herself 
to the Smith family. She was greatly im-
pressed with the noble bearing and gentle 
manners of this great and good man, whom 
she afterwards met and became well ac-
acquainted with. 
The following year. 1842, when Apostle 
Orson Hyde was returning from his mis-
sion to Palestine the Price family, mother,
brothers and sisters traveled with him 
across the sea and to Nauvoo. 
When the Saints were driven from Nau-
voo, Sister Mary Ann Hyde was one among 
the exiles with the Hyde family. The 
hardships and privations were many and in 
Council Bluffs she was permaturely confined 
October 15, 1846. Her babe, a little girl, 
died on the 27th. This was her only
child.
Apostle Orson Hyde remained at the 
Bluffs called Kanesville until 1850, and 
was the editor of the first paper published 
in Iowa, called the Frontier Guardian. He 
remained there until 1850, when he crossed 
the plains to this valley with his family. 
In 1855 he was called to settle Carson,
then in Utah but now in Nevada, and Sister 
Mary Ann Hyde went and shared in his 
labors in colonizing the new country, and 
remained with him until he was called 
home. Afterwards, in the early hardships 
of frontier life in Sanpete Co., where 
Apostle Orson Hyde was called to preside 
over the Stake, Sister Mary Ann Hyde 
was his faithful amanuensis and companion 
in all his varied labors. 
In Spring City, where they resided, when 
the Relief Society was first organized in 
1868, Sister Hyde was chosen president and 
accepted the position until 1879, eleven 
years when she was appointed president of 
the Relief Society of Sanpete Stake, which

[Column 3]
position she occupied at the time of her 
death. She journeyed from settlement to
settlement visiting the several branch
societies, advising, counseling and stimulat-
ing to higher endeavor and nobler effort the
organizations. She had rare executive
ability, and was of a highly spiritual nature;
with the two combined she was eminently
qualified for the important labors of her
position.
Sister Hyde enjoyed her labors and mag-
nified her calling. The Saints in Sanpete
Stake will miss her presence among them
and will feel her loss deeply. She often
spoke in the gift of tongues and always
with good effect; scores of young people
have been strengthened in their faith thro’
her teachings in the exercise of this precious
gift, which she held sacred.
During her illness she was tenderly cared
for by the Hyde family, she resided with
one of the daughters generally when in the
city, to whom she was fondly attached, and
in that home her spirit took its flight to the
realms on high.
The funeral was held in the Second Ward
meeting house, Bishop Heber C. Iverson
conducting the services. The music was
excellent, hymns appropriate. The speakers
were Elder A. M. Musser, Apostle Anthon
Lund, Sister E. B. Wells, Dr. J. E.
Talmage and Elder Alonzo E. Hyde.
Brother Hyde paid a beautiful tribute to
her life and character in his remarks. Her
form reposed in a lovely white casket, and
her robes were white as snow, befitting the
purity of a life devoted to the work of
God upon the earth. There were many
beautiful floral offerings and a sheaf of
ripened grain. Two of Sister Hyde’s 
brothers attended the funeral, one from
Goshen and one from Nephi, also a nephew
and family from Mexico, and others of her
relatives.
The following day the remains were taken
to Spring City, where she had lived and
labored many years, and after suitable ser-
vices Sister Hyde’s body was laid to rest
beside her husband, there to await the morn
of the First Resurrection; though she is not
there, but has gone to join that innumer-
able company on the other shore, where
there is no more pain or sorrow forevermore.
E. B. W.


OBITUARY.
	It becomes our painful duty to record the death
of our dear sister and counselor to the president of
the Relief Society, of Newton, Cache Co., Utah,
Sister Mary Christena Ingberg Christensen, who
died April 30, 1900. She was born in Denmark,
February 14, 1841.
	Sister Christensen embraced the Gospel in her
native land and emigrated to Utah in 1865. She
crossed the plains with ox teams and walked all
the way and forded the rivers from Omaha to Salt
Lake City. She was married to Elder James
Christensen and lived in Brigham City and later
moved to Newton, Cache Co., where she died. She
will meet her husband and three sons who pre-
ceded her. She leaves four sons and three grand-
children and a host of friends to mourn her loss.
	She was a kind and loving wife and mother, and 
died in full faith of the Gospel.
RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT.
	Whereas, Death has come into our midst and
taken from us our honored sister whom we all
l[-]ved, an earnest supporter and counselor of the
society, be it
	Resolved, By the members of this society, that
we tender to the bereaved family our sincere sym-
pathy, and earnestly desire that God will comfort
them in their affliction.
	Resolved, That a copy of these resolutions be
sent to the family, a copy be placed on record and
one forwarded to the EXPONENT for publication.
						MARTHA BECK.

SALT LAKE CITY, UTAH, JUNE 15, & JULY 1, 1900.


[Column 1]
JUNE NINETEEN HUNDRED.
	The month of June, Nineteen Hundred,
has been unusually full of stirring events
and happy occasions. Many pretty wed-
dings and brilliant receptions, some given
to guests from a distance, and others to
emphasize some occasion of special im-
portance.
	The first day of June Brigham Young’s
birthday was celebrated, both in the city,
with appropriate ceremonies, and at Saltair
with eloquent and telling addresses from
men of learning and famed for eloquence.
No tribute we as a people can pay to this
great leader and pioneer can be too much
of an ovation to his great genius, his
ability to plan and execute with skill,
energy and wisdom for the people whom he
loved so devotedly, and to whom, under
God, he gave his time, his talents and his
best efforts devotedly and untiringly.

	The General Conference of the Y. M.
and Y. L. Associations, which convened in
this city on the 10th, 11th and 12th of
June, and which brought the officers of
these institutions from afar—Mexico,
Canada, the Eastern, Northwestern and
Southern States, was a never to be for-
gotten occasion, and will be far-reaching in
its results, not only on those who were
favored to attend, but upon the great
work they represent. The serious illness
of the General President and the President
of the Church, Lorenzo Snow, was the one
drawback to the perfect enjoyment of the
conference. In consequence of this the
reception that had been planned to be
given in the Bee Hive had to be changed
to the Gardo House.
	This reception was on a magnificent
scale in every particular. The house itself,
which is elegantly furnished, was artistic-
ally decorated by specialists, and outside
banners flags, bunting. Chinese lanterns,
arclights and all the pretty effects of ap-
plied electricity gave the beautiful grounds
the appearance of fairyland. A dancing
floor on the west side of the lawn accomo-
dated the dancers, and bands of music were
playing both indoors and out. Young
ladies in shimmering white, and a great
variety of becoming colors, flitted here and
there dancing or helping to entertain those
who were not dancing and guests from a
distance, and the young men were particu-

[Column 2]
larly attentive and desirous to do all in
their power to make everyone happy and
comfortable. It was one of the most bril-
liant affairs of its kind ever given in this
city, tho’ Salt Lake is famous for success-
ful entertainments.

	The Scandinavian Jubilee was held in
June, beginning the 14th, in commemor-
ation of the half century that had elapsed
since the Gospel was first introduced into
the Scandinavian countries, and it was an
event of great importance in Church history.
Their representatives came from long dis-
tances to take part in the proceedings, which
were of great interest. The Tabernacle
decorations were very fine and in good
taste, and the flags displayed of the several
countries were quite a study. The enter-
tainments were all in keeping with the
occasion and gave opportunity for social
intercourse and renewing of old acquain-
tannce that was very pleasant and satisfac-
tory.
	One session was entirely given to the
women, Sister Minnie J. Snow presiding,
which was a sort of departure for them and
shows they are falling in line with the
spirit of progress. Sister Stevens read a
poem of her own composition at this meet-
ing, which we publish on another page in
this issue. They have their poets and
orators, several of them vigorously gifted in
these respects, and are quite famous for
beautiful singers and musicians, that have
not only done honor to their own people,
but to Utah. Sister Snow was presented
with an exquisite bouquet of flowers, an
expression of affection which she fully appre-
ciated.

	On the 22nd of June the Sons and
Daughters of the Pioneers of Utah county
celebrated with a feast and meetings at
Pleasant Grove. A program had been pre-
pared and visitors expected from a distance.
The exercises were in charge of the presi-
dent, Apostle Reed Smoot, who in the
kindliest manner cared for all present. A
bowery under the trees was comfortably
seated and afforded ample room for a larger
gathering. Carriages had been sent to the
station for the guests coming by train, and
at 10 a. m. there was an informal recep-
tion. Then music by the Pleasant
Grove band and prayer by the chaplain,
President Edward Partridge. Hymn, “O,
ye mountains high” and address of welcome
by Professor J. L. Brown, responded to by
Apostle Reed Smoot. The address assigned
to Governor Heber M. Wells was given by
Apostle A. O. Woodruff. A song by Miss
Arville Clark, with organ accompaniment,
by Professor Lund, was greatly appreciated.
A part of the program, the Pioneer
Fathers, was given by Apostle Rudger
Clawson. A song by Miss Mary West was
very sweet and tender.
	At the banquet two hundred guests were
seated and a fine menu was partaken of,
which the guests appreciated by partaking
bountifully. Professor J. L. Brown acted
as toastmaster and was quite humorous in
some of the parts. Mrs. E. B. Wells gave
“A Morning in Camp.” A buffalo hunt,
Mr. Hancock, a stampede, wittily given by
J. Golden Kimball, son of Heber C. Kim
ball, who always reminds one of his father
in his manner of expressing himself. “The
First Glimpse of the Valley,” Moroni
Snow, son of Apostle Erastus Snow. Song
by Professor A. C. Lund, rendered in most
excellent voice, “The Blue and the Gray.”

[Column 3]
Song, Miss Arville Clark, “Annie Laurie,”
she has a fine voice and good expression.
	Afternoon exercises. Apostle Brigham
Young spoke in a most impressive manner
npon the subject “The Debt We Owe.”
“The Battalion Boys” was given by S. L.
White. Mayor W. Clark recited, in a graphic
manner, ‘‘The Settler’s Story.” The com-
pany sang “Babylon, O Babylon.” Apostle
Reed Smoot made some very well timed
remarks, urging the duty of keeping in
mind the privations and toil of the Pioneers
and paying tribute to their memory and
deeds of valor.
	The affair was a very happy one and it
seems appropriate that in every county in
Utah such a society should be organized
and kept up. All honor to the brave
pioneers who made it possible for thousands
to have happy homes in these vales of the
mountains.

	On the 13th of June the Primary Asso-
ciation held a General Conference in the
Tabernacle, the children assembled in great
numbers. The General Board were all
present in person, and also Presidents
George Q. Cannon and Joseph F. Smith, of
the First Presidency of the Church. A
very pathetic feature of the conference was
the prayer offered by the children for the
venerable President of the Church, Lorenzo
Snow, who had been expecting to address
them and was too ill to be present. The
president, Sister Louie B. Felt, presided at
this great gathering of the children of Zion.
The vast work accomplished by this organ-
ization during the twenty-one years since
the movement was started is a spiritual up-
lift such as can only be appreciated by those
connected with this magnificent work. Its
leaders and originators deserve the highest
tribute of praise from the parents in the
Church.
	At evening a banquet was given in the
Sixteenth Ward meeting house. Tables
were laid for three hundred guests. Presi-
dents George Q. Cannon and Joseph F.
Smith were present also several Apostles and
Joseph E. Taylor, of the Salt Lake Stake.
Elder Joseph H. Felt made a humorous
opening speech when all were seated, and
President Louie B. Felt expressed herself in
a few well-chosen words of welcome to her
guests. President George Q. Cannon asked
the blessing upon the feast. A program
had been prepared and Sister Cornelia H.
Clayton took the part of Toastmistress, to
which she seemed admirably adapted. Sis-
ter E. B. Wells responded to the toast, “Our
Grandfathers.” President George Q. Can-
non, “Our Grandmothers.” Maria Y.
Dougall, “Our Fathers.” President Joseph
F. Smith, “Our Mothers.” Aurelia S.
Rogers, “The Primaries.”
	The affair was most enjoyable, especially
meeting with workers from a distance in a
social way. President Felt and her co-
laborers are to be congratulated in having
made the conference and entertainment such
a success. Songs were well chosen and
finely rendered by some of the sweetest
singers of Israel.
	The Primary Association is an institution
that stands alone in the world. There is
no such training for children in any other
church or locality, and certainly it has
given excellent proof of its importance in
the training of the little people of Zion.

	June has been a notable month this year
in the matter of accidents, fires and nations
at war or declaring war, one can scarcely


contemplate the happenings of the month
without a feeling akin to horror, and the
outlook is, judging from present conditions, 
indeed very threatening to peace and safety.
	National Conventions held recently by
both great parties argue great political 
contests at election time, and the enthusiasm 
may turn ones attention from the sickening
depression caused by dwelling upon calami-
ties and causalities of recent occurrence.
	June has been beautiful in bloom this
year, roses and other fragrant blossoms in
great abundance, and with the numerous
parties, receptions and school entertain-
ments and weddings that have been given,
the flowers have added much in the way of
artistic decorations.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


A QUIET WEDDING IN JUNE.
	On Wednesday, June 27, 1900, a very
quiet wedding was solemnized in the Salt
Lake Temple, Bishop John R. Winder
officiating. The bride and bridegroom
were Miss Mabel Tamson McBride, formerly
of Fillmore, and Mr. John Phillip Tate,
of Tooele. The only friends present ex-
cept the official witnesses, were Mrs. E. B.
Wells and Mrs. E. J. Stevenson.
	Miss McBride had been with the writer
for some months and had rendered valuable
assistance in the office work of the
EXPONENT, making herself quite familiar
with its details. Previously she had taken
the training for the kindergarten and had
been quite successful in the work be-
fore coming to Salt Lake. Here she
taught for the L. D. S. College in the
Social Hall, but the school being discon-
tinued on account of unavoidable circum-
stances, she sought other employment.
	The young couple became acquainted
while attending school at the Brigham
Young Academy, in Provo. When the
country’s call was made for troops from
Utah, Mr. Tate was enrolled as a member
of Battery C. and remained with it until
the company was mustered out by orders
from headquarters. He is a young man of
excellent character and habits and good
business ability. Mr. and Mrs. Tate ex-
pect to reside permanently in Tooele.
	The EXPONENT offers its heartiest con-
gratulations to these young people just
entering upon the matrimonial voyage, may
the choicest of heaven’s blessings attend
them on their way and their bark glide past
the danger shoals and dark places in safety
and be guided always wisely in the direc-
tions to secure peace and the riches of
eternal life.
	I do believe who love hath known,
	Or sublime friendship’s purest, highest tone,
	Hath tasted of the cup of ripest bliss,
	And drank the choicest wine life hath to give.
	Hath known the truest joy it is to live,
	What blessing rich or great compared to this?


A RARE JUNE WEDDING.
	Undoubtedly the month of June is the
most delightful time of the year to have
pretty weddings, and there have been a
large number of very elaborate ones this
June season, the most significant one, per-
haps, being solemnized on Friday, June
29, in the Salt Lake Temple, the cere-
mony being performed by President George
Q. Cannon at 4 p. m. The bride and
bridegroom were Miss Maud M. Ford, of
Cleveland, Ohio, and Mr. Le Roi C. Snow,
of Salt Lake.
	A royal reception was given by the


parents of the bridegroom at the Bee Hive
House, in this city. Eight hundred in-
vitations had been sent out, though many
of them were to friends at a distance in
different states. However, some hundreds
of the relatives and friends assembled to
offer congratulations and good wishes on
this happy and auspicious occasion.
	The bride was attired in an exquisite
white gown of soft material, and Miss
Mabelle Snow in pink crepon, little Lucile
Snow in pale blue silk, all charming and
dressed in good taste.
	The decorations outside and the illum-
inations were profuse and in bright colors
that corresponded with the artistic decor-
ations and festivities within the grand old
residence, where so many brilliant parties
have been given in the days gone by.
	It is utterly useless in our little paper to
attempt an elaborate description of the
beauty and splendor of the scene without
and within. The colors of the decorating
were fastidiously blended, and the refresh-
ments carried out the same idea of color in
effect. Bands of music were playing
throughout the evening, and Professors
Lund and Anderson, also Miss Mary
Young rendered vocal selections adapted
to the occasion. Appropriate recitations
were given by Messrs B. S. and Morris
Young.
	Mr. and Mrs. Snow received their friends
in the east drawing room, which was
beautifully decorated and draped, making
it a bower of beauty in which to receive
the congratulations and good wishes of
their “dear five hundred friends.”
	The presents were displayed on tables in
the library and were for the most part in
the same style of elegance as the house, its
furnishings and surroundings, in fact every-
thing seemed in keeping with the auspici-
ous union of two young lovers just enter-
ing upon the smooth and shining sea of
matrimony without a ripple or a wavelet
to disturb the onward passage of Hymen’s
bark.
“There’s a bliss beyond all that the minstrel has
		told,
	When two, that are linked in one heavenly tie,
With hearts never changing, and brow never cold,
Love on through all ills and love on till they die.”


[Column 1]
MISS AMERICAN’S VISIT.
	Miss Sadie American, who recently paid
a visit to Utah, is a typical Jewess, well and
favorably known throughout this country.
She is the corresponding secretary of the
National Council of Jewish Women of the
U. S., and is a woman of strong character
and pronounced opinions. She is a con-
spicuous figure in Washington at the trien-
nials of the National Council and always
commands the attention of the audience
wherever she speaks because of her brilliant
originality and vigorous speech. She im-
presses one as feeling and believing in what
she endeavors to impart to others. She
made a good impression at the Congress in
London when she read a paper in the Large
Hall. Westminister Town Hall, on Vaca-
tion Schools. Much of her work has been
along this line and she has been very suc-
cessful. Miss American spoke a few words
in the large Tabernacle in this city on Sun-
day, June 17, although the Scandinavians
were holding their Jubilee and indeed it
seemed quite appropriate, considering she
represented a people whose origin was of
the orient. During her visit she was the
recipient of many favors from the Jewish

[Column 2]
people of Salt Lake, whose guest she was,
and from the two great societies N. W. R. S.
Y.L.M.I.A. represented in N. C. W. of U.
S., which we have every reason to believe
she fully appreciated. Miss American was
accompanied by her mother, Mrs. American,
who shared in the good wishes and hospi-
tality extended to the talented daughter.
	On Monday afternoon Mrs. Ida M.
Bamberger gave a reception in honor of
Miss American at her elegant home in this
city, which was a most enjoyable affair.
The house was simply a bower of roses and
palms, and the music of a fine band of
string instruments was enchanting. A
number of lovely young girls, who had
just been admitted to the Sacrament,
dressed in the white in which they had
received their initiation to the Jewish syna-
gogue waited upon the guests, who num-
bered hundreds coming and going during
the afternoon. The refreshments were
dainty and refreshing, and served in most
elegant style. Mrs. Bamberger’s mother,
Mrs. Maas, of Cincinnati, had arrived unex-
pectedly just in time to be one more guest
of honor and add to the enjoyment of the
party. Mrs. Bamberger may congratulate
herself on giving so much genuine pleasure
to those who availed themselves of this
rare opportunity of meeting Miss and Mrs.
American and Mrs. Maas and taking part
in the social conversation besides enjoying
the music and flowers and the friendly
atmosphere that prevaded the beautiful
home of the hostess.
	On Tuesday morning Miss American
spoke to the Salt Lake Jewish Council in
their own meeting, called especially for
the purpose of listening to one who had
had a large experience in matters of inter-
est to them. Tuesday afternoon a luncheon
was given at the Lagoon in her honor by
the Jewish ladies to which a number of
other guests were invited, and it was a
most enjoyable affair, notwithstanding the
heat. The banquet (for indeed it was) was
served in the most hospitable fashion and
the arrangement of the party altogether
was admirable, indeed there was nothing
lacking on the part of Mr. and Mrs.
Bamberger that would add to the pleasure
and happiness of the day.
	Wednesday, June 20, a Lake party of
about a hundred ladies was given at Saltair
in honor of Miss American. Mrs Minnie
J. Snow kindly tendered the car which con-
veyed the guests thither. Mrs. Snow was
not able to attend the gatherings in honor of
Miss American, on account of the illness of
President Snow at that time, but we were
favored to have her present to participate in
the festivities at the Lake. Miss American
thoroughly enjoyed the bath in the beauti-
ful Salt Sea of America and we believe her
trip to the Lake will be one of the bright
spots in her visit to Utah. Refreshments 
were served in the most informal way, but
seasoned with happy converse. Mrs. Susa
Young Gates acted as toastmistress, and is
certainly complete mistress of this difficult
role. The first toast called for was
“Women from the West,” and was re-
sponded to by Mrs. E. B. Wells, in a sort
of welcome to the distinguished guest of the
occasion. Miss American responded in a
delightful manner and with much feeling,
to the sentiment, “Women from the Far
East,” in which she spoke of her pleasant
acquaintance with the Utah Women whom
she had met and become attached to in the
National Council.


Mrs. Simon Bamberger, “Friendship’s
Ties,” was graphically portrayed in a gra-
cious manner. Miss Rose Goldberg (a
prospective bride) spoke tenderly and
beautifully to the toast “Old Hopes, Old
Friends, Old Times.” Her words were
aptly chosen and her manner simply de-
lightful. “The Link Between the
School and the World,” responded to by
Mrs. Emma J. McVicker, was characteristic
of this scholarly woman, who is conversant
with matters of both social and educational
nature in the community. “Speed the
Parting Guest,” by Mrs. Minnie J. Snow,
was fittingly appropriate, and both in spirit
and effect a beautiful close to the enjoyable
and friendly feeling that had pervaded the
intercourse of the few days visit of Miss
American. All good wishes attend her in
her summer vacation and rest in California,
the land of flowers.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
EDITORIAL NOTES.
	We had the pleasure of attending the
annual meeting of the Primary Association
in the Tenth ward, Thursday, May 24. It
was twenty-one years since the organization
was effected and was a sort of gala day for
the children. The meeting house was
prettily decorated with flowers of great
variety, and the president, Sister Howarth,
looked as happy as the little ones. She had
prepared a short program in which the chil-
dren did themselves and their teachers great
credit, both in singing and reciting. Of the
Salt Lake Stake Primary officers there were
present Superintendent Camilla Cobb and
Assistant Mary L. Morris, also President
Elizabeth Paul, of the Tenth ward Relief
Society. Brief remarks were made by the
sisters and by Brother Keddington, coun-
selor to the Bishop. The little folks spent
the afternoon at Liberty Park to celebrate
their anniversary. This is one of the Primary
Associations we had the privilege and honor
of organizing, in May 1879. Sister Jennie
McLean was its first president, and was
most faithful and energetic in this and in
all her duties. Sister Howarth seems ad-
mirably adapted to work with the little
ones, and we certainly wish her earnest
endeavors may prove successful in training
some of the children of Zion.
	Mrs. A. W. Carlson gave a Kensington at
her very pretty home, on Friday, June 29,
or one might with all propriety call it a
garden party, for the heat was so intense
that most of the guests preferred to sit out
on the lawn and in the shade of the beauti-
ful trees and the hedge of pretty soft
shrubbery. The view from Mrs. Carlson’s
home is particularly fine, magnificent at
sunset. It was an enjoyable afternoon
notwithstanding the heat. Mrs. Carlson
has a happy faculty of entertaining her
guests, and is a graceful and charming
hostess. There were perhaps forty ladies in
the party, many of them old friends who
had not met each other for years, and re-
newing old acquaintances in this pleasant
fashion makes one feel doubly indebted to
the hostess. Surely gatherings of this sort
without parade or ostentation are pleasant
places in one’s memory afterwards, a change
from the humdrum of daily toil; and really
one should occasionally take these breath-
ing spells on life’s highway, and value the
opportunity of a few hours interchange in
social converse. There were delicious
refreshments served in a rare hospitable
way, five o’clock tea with all the dainties

[Column 2]
one could imagine as well, quite unlike the
English pretty custom of serving tea and
wafers. Mrs. Carlson may certainly con-
gratulate herself upon the success of her
Kensington, tho’ as for sewing, etc. the
ladies were usually occupied in using fans
instead of needles.
	Saturday, July 16, at 3 p. m., the Society
D. R. met at the residence of the writer to
celebrate two important days, “Old Glory”
and the ’’Battle of Bunker Hill.” The
parlors were decorated in blue, and buff and
the stars and stripes were conspicuous here
and there. The portico was festooned with
bunting, while roses in great variety, car-
nations, California poppies, pansies and
larkspur gave the rooms a gala day appear-
ance. Vice-Regent Maria Y. Dougall
presided. Meeting opened with prayer by
Mrs. M. W. Wilcox. The ladies sang
America, Mrs Willard Cannon leading, Miss
Vera Cannon accompanist. Roll call was
responded to by patriotic sentiments.
Several letters were read from absent mem-
bers, and informally recollections of the
original occasion being observed were made
the theme of the speakers. Mrs. Willard
Cannon sang with spirit and thrilling
melody, “The Star Spangled Banner,” and
Mrs. Annie W. Cannon read most feelingly,
“The Flag Goes By.” After the
exercises ice cream, cake and lemonade
were partaken of, and as the guests were
saying their good byes, they carried away
handsfull of roses. May the fragrance of
that day be as fresh and sweet in their re-
membrance as the roses were on that bright,
sunny day in beautiful June.
	On Tuesday, June 25, Mr. and Mrs.
Morris B. Young gave a dinner party and
evening entertainment at their hospitable
home in Forest Dale. Notwithstanding
the heat was intense a sumptuous dinner
was served at 3 o’clock in the afternoon.
The dining room was fragrant with the
sweet flowers and the table decorations
were very pretty. The guests of honor
were Mrs. Hannah Snow Lewis and Mr.
and Mrs. Oliver Snow, of Ohio, cousins of
President Lorenzo Snow and of the hostess,
Mrs. Young, his daughter. Other guests
were: Mrs. Z. D. H. Young, Mrs. M. I.
Horne, Mrs. Margaret P. Young, Mrs.
Emma Woodruff, Mrs. Alice and Mrs.
Regina Pond and the writer. The conver-
sation was very highly flavored with rem-
iniscences of early days in the valley, in
which Sister Eliza R. Snow and the vener-
able President Lorenzo Snow had taken
part. Later on towards evening quite a
party arrived and there was music and
singing, and the porches and shade of the
trees was filled with the younger people.
President F. Y. Taylor and wife, of Granite
Stake, Bishop Millen C. Atwood and wife,
of Sugar Ward; Drs. Leslie W. and M. O.
Snow; Elder Le Roi C. Snow and Miss
Ford and many others of the Snow family.
Ice cream, cake and lemonade were served
to all the large number of guests.
	On Friday afternoon, June 15, quite a
large party of ladies assembled by invitation
of Mrs. C. R. Howe, at her residence in this
city, in honor of the 77th birthday anniver-
sary of her mother-in-law, Sister Julia C.
Howe, who is one of the best known
women in the city, and is universally be-
loved. She has been connected with chari-
table and philanthropic work ever since
coming among our people, and is a woman
of exceptional attainments in many respects,

[Column 3]
and has had large opportunities in early
life, and is particularly well acquainted with 
the Bible, which of itself is a rare acquisition.
Sister Howe has not only been associated
with the work of the Relief Society, but
also with the Y. L. M. I. A. and the
Primary Association, and in all the posi-
tions she has been called to fill she has been 
efficient and untiring in her efforts, and she
has the adaptability necessary to success in
managing young people: She has a fine
personality and is remarkably well preserved
for her age, looking much younger than
her years. There were present at the party
Sisters M. Isabella Horne, Bathsheba W.
Smith, Margaret Y. Taylor, Matilda M.
Barratt, Elizabeth Webb, Elizabeth J.
Stevenson, Susan Smith, Lydia Ann, Han-
nah and Susan Wells, and Maria W. Wil-
cox, whose birthday occurs on the same
day, though she is a few years younger.
There were many other sisters present, in
all thirty or more, and the afternoon was
very happily spent in congratulations and
general social conversation and birthday
greetings. The rooms were very prettily
decorated, there were roses, carnations and
sweet peas in abundance. Delicious refresh-
ments were served in dainty fashion, the
hostess, young Mrs. Howe, knowing ex-
actly how to entertain her guests, which is
a happy faculty. May Sister Howe have 
many more such delightful birthdays, tho’ 
we are aware that every year takes from us
one or more who have been accustomed to
meet with us at such reunions.


THE MOTHERS’ CONGRESS.
			(Concluded.)
	Educators for years have realized the im-
portance of this strength of union, but
its worth has never until the last few years
seemed to arouse the women to action.
Goethe tells us “A talent may be perfected
in solitude, a character only in the world.”
It is certainly characters we want and
character can only come to woman and man
alike, and that way is through service for
the children of God.
	God’s greatest gift to mankind was love;
man’s greatest gift to his fellow is love; we
know thoughts are things, and as we think
of people we are helping them to grow into
ideals, for our thoughts generally rule our
acts.
	Poor though we may be, without influ-
ence, without ability, yet can we help
humanity to attain to the best, the truest,
the highest. So I would say organize, if
the spirit of progression pervades you will
have crowded meetings, for there are
women and mothers who hunger for knowl-
edge, as they feel their inability more and
more each day, to battle with the responsi-
bilities which surround them.

	The evening session of the Mothers’
Congress was the “fathers’ evening,” and
was presided over by Professor Byron
Cummings. Rev. Clarence T. Brown
opened with prayer and Professor Cum-
mings followed with remarks that expressed
the recognition of the noble and worthy
aims of the women’s congress.
	Hon. George M. Cannon, in an address
entitled “The Relation of the Parent to the
School,” spoke upon the importance of
educating our children and also that morals
should be inculcated as a part of his school
training. The world is progressing con-
stantly and the young should be trained


[Column 1]
and educated to meet its growing knowledge.
	Prof. William M. Stewart, of the
University of Utah, spoke upon the subject
of “School and Domestic Life.” An un-
fortunate condition existed, Prof. Stewart
said, in these relations. The father usually
leaves his home early in the morning and
returns at night, and usually devotes him-
self to his newspaper or goes out to partici-
pate in separate public interests which occu-
pied his attention, the wife had her separ-
ate interests and the children theirs. There
in reality should be accordance and union
of usefulness in the home. All should
“pull together” to accomplish the highest
results. Prof. Stewart also spoke on the
evils of the saloon, and declared it to be a
blot upon the civilization of the world.

	The Mothers’ Congress reconvened in
the morning at 10 o’clock. The meeting
opened with prayer by Mrs. Zina D. H.
Young after which Mrs. Lucretia Kimball
spoke upon the “Educational Preparation
of Parenthood.” Mrs. Kimball said: “I
come before you here as a mother who feels
how far short she has fallen below her
ideals of motherhood and desiring to gain
from the ideas and association here aid in 
bettering them. I desire to speak upon
Parenthood rather than Motherhood. I be-
lieve that the spiritual should be considered
first, and that upon the development of
spiritual the physical and moral will follow.
What would it mean if men and women set
themselves to study for the holy profession
of parenthood? When a girl evinces a
talent for music we decide that everything
should be done in preparation for the devel-
opment of her gift. But how much atten-
tion or care is devoted to the development
of the holy gift of motherhood?”
	Mrs. Lucy Hoving, of Logan, expressed
it as her sentiment and hope that the time
might come when no girl would be per-
mitted to marry without first having
mastered the Kindergarten course.
	Miss May said this question of Kinder-
ten training was important enough for
thorough discussion. One girl had been
allowed to take Kindergarten course, who 
wanted to go on the stage. She did so,
aad it seemed to crush out all desire for the
stage together with all the frivolity and
lightness that had hitherto ruled her life.
	Mrs. E. E. Shepherd followed with an
address upon the subject of “Honor Be-
tween Men and Women.”
	There has been an idea for ages that the
single life is the only pure life, and we have
celibacy enforced in the priests in many re-
ligions. Considered in its true and holy
light, lived in the way that God intends,
marriage is holy. The progress, morality
and purity of the race depend upon the
right understanding and practice of right
laws.
	The greater freedom of women and their
wider mingling with the world has obliter-
ated, to a great extent, the thought of sex.
	How often we hear parents say, “Oh my
boy is sowing his wild oats, he will come
out all right.” How would you like to
have your daughters sow wild oats? A
boy who sows his wild oats, is never the 
equal of the the pure young girl joined with
him in marriage.
	What can we as Mothers’ Congress do to
destroy this false standard? We must set
ourselves to consider these subjects and
help to solve these questions which affect
the progress and morality of the world.

[Column 2]
	The meeting adjourned at 12 o’clock to
meet at 2 p. m.
	“The Rights of Children” was the subject
under discussion in the afternoon at the
Mothers’ Congress. Mrs. R. T. Hilliard,
of Ogden, reviewing the physical side of the
question and Miss Whalen, of the same
city, the ethical.
	It is becoming recognized that the child
has his rights equally or even more im-
portantly than the adult. Every child
primarily has the right to be well born, to
have a perfect and healthful body, and the
right to have this body properly cared for.
Every child should also have the benefit of
normal training, that his hands may be
skilled to create beautiful things. If a child
is properly trained the aptitude for re-
sponsibility will come with years. There
is a growing interest in the question of the
right training and culture of children. This
unrest has evidenced in the many mothers’
organizations throughout the land, and
there is no doubt a better condition soon to
develop from this unrest. We must not
decide ourselves selfishly to our own chil-
dren; we must better the entire world or
our efforts to regenerate the race will be in
vain. Mrs. Hilliard gave some clever
illustrations of the importance of the con-
sideration of economics in the solving of
social problems.
	The Chaminade chorus under Mrs.
Lewis directorship sang two beautiful
selections from Rene the King.
	Mrs. Stewart in response to President
Little’s call for discussion of Mrs. Hilliard’s
paper, said that she thought the most im-
portant right of the child was to be desired.
No one has the moral right to bring an un-
welcome child into the world. They should
also have a place. Usually they are made
to feel in the way, there is no place pre-
pared for their play in the home and it is
not strange that they are found on the
street and in undesirable spots.
	Mrs. Alice E. Harford gave the group of
children’s songs postponed from the pre-
vious day’s program, illustrating the kind
of songs desirable and appropriate for chil-
dren. “The Wind,” “The Tulips,” “The
Owl and Hare,” “You Seen my Kitty,”
were the titles of the songs sung and were
received with applause.
	President Little appointed a nominating
committee to prepare a list of officers to pre-
sent for election, after which Miss Whalen,
of Ogden, made an interesting address deal-
ing with the ethical rights of children.
Her talk was enjoyed throughout and
elicited much applause.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
R. S. REPORTS.
WASATCH STAKE.
	The National Relief Society Conference
of Wasatch Stake convened in the Stake
house at Heber on Monday, May 28, at
10:30 a. m.. Meeting opened by singing
“Sweet is the work.” Prayer was offered
by Elder Joseph Moulton. Singing,
“With all my powers of heart and tongue.”
The officers’ roll was called, showing
twenty seven present.
	There were present on the stand Stake
President Annie R. Duke and her counsel-
ors, also officers representing the different
wards, which were all reported as being
generally in a flourishing condition.
	Instructive remarks were made by sister
Ann Murdock, Patriarch John Duke and
others.

[Column 2]
	At the afternoon session the annual re
port was read and accepted, after which the
time was well occupied by various speakers
on topics of interest, among the number
being Brother J. W. Rigdon, a son of Sid-
ney Rigdon, of Church history fame, who
spoke in a very interesting manner con-
cerning the testimony of his father in re-
lation to the Book of Mormon and the
Prophet Joseph Smith. Spoke of the in-
dustry of the Latter-day Saints which had
caused “the desert to blossom as the rose,”
and said they need have no fear of being
driven from their homes in the mountains as
long as they were faithful in keeping the
commandments of the Lord. President
Hatch and counselors and T. H. Giles each
addressed the conference and felt to give
praise for the great good being accomplished
by this organization. The Stake officers
were presented and all unanimously sus-
tained, after which conference adjourned
for six months.
		HANNAH HARBOUR, Stake Sec. 
SEVIER STAKE.
	Relief Society conference convened at 10
a. m. at the meeting house, President
Christina Sorensen presiding. Singing,
“Earth with her ten thousand flowers.”
Prayer by Brother Robert F. Goold. Sing-
ing, “Prayer is the soul’s sincere desire.”
	Officers present on the stand were, Presi-
dent Christina Sorensen, Counselors Mary
E. Magleby and Ellen Thueson, Treasurer
Caroline Bohman, also Sister Elizabeth
Stevenson, of Salt Lake Gity, and Sister
Marilla Daniels, of Provo, who had come
to attend the conference and visit some of
the branches with the Stake Board.
	Roll call, fourteen wards represented out
of sixteen. Minutes of forenoon meeting
of last conference read.
	Several of the presidents reported their
societies in pretty good working order,
wheat in good condition, stored in tithing
granaries; they hold testimony and intel-
lectual meetings; the sisters are united,
have good meetings, are willing to help in
all good works, some do not attend meet-
ings regularly; work meetings held when
necessary; teachers visit and bring in good
reports; sisters willing to donate; small
wards are more diligent in their labors;
the same sisters belong to the Relief Soci-
ety, the Young Ladies and the Primary 
Associations; all sisters expressed them-
selves as being pleased to have the sisters
from headquarters visiting with us.
	President Sorensen introduced Sister
Stevenson and Sister Daniels.
	Sister M. Daniels was pleased to hear
reports, spoke of wheat being represented
this morning, only one ward had their own
granary, encouraged the building of gran-
aries. President Woodruff’s instructions to
the sisters were to keep on gathering grain.
Read from the EXPONENT of April 15,
1898, how to build a granary out of noth-
ing, by sisters of St. George. Be prepared,
for great changes are taking place. Spoke
of the first organization of the Relief Society
by the Prophet Joseph Smith, also of going
back to Jackson County to build up Zion.
Every mother should encourage her
daughters to join the Relief Society. Gave
her experience in performing duties [-]nd
labors in the Gospel.
	Sister E. J. Stevenson said: “It is no small
matter to speak to sisters of the Latter-day
Saints. We will receive the Holy Spirit if
we live for it.” Spoke of building gra[---]


[Column 1]
ies, of the sayings of President Brigham
Young that the sisters should build their
own Relief Society halls and granaries.
Spoke of the building of the Fourteenth
Ward Relief Society Hall by the help of
the brethren and their own energy. It is
the duty of the young people to attend and
take hold of responsibilities. Our mission
is to encourage all in good works, attend
to family prayers, be punctual in opening
our meetings.
	President Sorensen was pleased with the
remarks made, urged the sisters to come on
time.
	Minutes read by the secretary. Singing,
“Redeemer of Israel.” Benediction by
Bishop Orson Magleby. 
	Afternoon session convened at 2 p. m.,
President Sorenson presiding. Singing,
“Guide us, O Thou Great Jehovah.”
Prayer by Brother C. P. Christensen. Sing-
ing, “O ye mountains high.” Minutes of
afternoon session of last conference read.
	Other presidents present reported their
wards, gave favorable reports of their
labors. In intellectual meetings they
read from the history of Joseph Smith,
Doctrine and Covenants and other good
books. All the sisters feel to do their duty
and improve by instructions given.
	Sister Stevenson encouraged the sisters
to go on in good works. Said teachers are
the backbone of the society. We should be
careful in financial matters, be judicious
and happy with what little is ours. Spoke
of the noble spirits being born in this gener-
ation. We should know the mind
and will of God on marriage, and there
would not be so many divorces. Spoke of
the work for the dead. Teach our children
to pay tithing, cultivate and improve, there
is no stopping place.
	Stake President Seegmiller was pleased to
hear reports; was considering the great
results of the organization of the Relief
Society in these days, and of the great
work that will yet be done in the future.
Asked God to comfort all with His Holy
Spirit and that our daughters may be de-
sirous of accomplishing what they were
sent here for.
	Sister Daniels encouraged the sisters
to be charitable, our mission is to save souls
put in practice all the good we can.
	Minutes of meeting read, closing remarks
by President Sorensen, thanks to President
Seegmiller. Joseph S. Horne and all the
brethren and sisters who had attended the
meetings. We have had a feast of good
things.
	Singing, “How firm a foundation.” Con-
ference adjourned for three months. Bene-
diction by President Joseph S. Horne.
	The sisters also visited with us according
to program arranged. Many valuable in-
structions were given and many blessings
enjoyed, and all the sisters who had the
privilege of listening have felt the great out-
pouring of the Spirit of God in our meet-
ings. All are willing to come up more
fully to the duties required of them. The
sisters will observe the 17th of March more

[Column 2]
fully in the future; in all paying member-
ship fee. Encouraged the sisters to take
the EXPONENT, it is our paper.
			MARY SWINDLE, Sec.
	Monroe, June 8, 1900.

A WOMAN MISSIONARY.
	[Mrs. Addison Pratt on the Society Islands,
1850.]
			(Continued.)
	Our three eldest daughters have children
under their tuition. They almost feel the
responsibilities of mothers. It is a task
equal to taming wild animals. Our third
daughter, Lois, has a little girl eight years
of age, as near as we can judge, her parents
do not know. Her father is a half-breed
Frenchman. A sweeter tempered child I
never knew, always submissive to the re-
quirements of her teachers, and easily per-
suaded to leave off all her unpleasant habits.
I should consider it a great loss of labor and
mental exertion to leave her behind when
we go from here, and allow her to fall
back to her native state and forget all we
have taught her. Ellen has wrought a
visible change in the two she has under her
care.
	Oh, what wonders might be wrought in
the training of these children! Who
would not be content to be buried from the
world to effect it?
	Occasionally I have a leisure day, and
not having much variety in my reading, I
read even my old letters, which my mother
and sisters sent me years ago. The sad
remembrance of days of sorrow came vivid-
ly before me, and I wept—a rare occurence
with me. Sister C. said she did not like to
read old letters, it brought back memories
which gave her pain. But with myself it
is an established habit to review the past,
and live my life over. It seems sublime
and sacred, though it should cause sighs
and tears.
	Brother Crosby has at length succeeded
in delivering a discourse in the Tahitian,
much to the understanding of his hearers.
In the evening service several native breth-
ren came to me for comments on the vision
of Daniel. In my broken manner I talked
of the “Ancient of Days,” told them how
it was explained by Elders in the Church.
They understood my words, and were satis-
fied.
	The “House of Prayer,” as the natives
call it, is the most comfortable place on the
Island, built as it is over water, six large
windows on each side, with slats instead of
glass and sash, to be thrown open or closed
to suit high or low breezes.
		(To be continued.)
