CUPID INTERVIEWED.
Stay, Cupid, tell me—What is love?
	“’Tis something like a tree—
‘Known by its fruits.’ I fancy, sir,
	And think you will agree.”

What are its fruits—sweet words and smiles?
	“Nay, these its blossoms are,
The promises of fruit to come,
	It may be near or far.”

And what are broken promises?
	“Frost-bitten buds, of course;
Then sweet words change to bitter ones,
	And smiles to frowns, or worse.”

And letters—notes—love’s messages?
	“Oh, letters are but leaves,
Whereof the swain disconsolate
	Hope’s chaplet fondly weaves.”

If letters looked for never come,
	What must I then suppose?
“Your tree is barren—dead—or in
	Another’s garden grows.”

Hold! What of kisses soft and warm?
	“I really couldn’t say—
I never deal with metaphors
	When kisses come my way.”

“But still I answer—Love, true love
	Is very like a tree;
The longer grown the stronger grown
	Where’er that growth may be.

“Such love is not ephemeral,
	It dies not with the day;
Its flowers are heavenly immortelles,
	It teems with fruit alway.

“But soul with soul must sympat[-]ize,
	As sun and soil agree,
Or there shall come nor fruit nor flower,
	For love is like a tree.
					O. F. WHITNEY.


[Column 1]
PARIS LETTER.
			July, 6, 1900.
Dear Sister Wells:
	A few days since I had the pleasure of
sending you an invitation to participate in
the ceremony of unveiling the monument to
Gen. Lafayette, which occurred on the 4th
inst., in the place du Carrousel, (hereafter to
be called Square Lafayette) and believing
that you would be interested in knowing
that the Daughters of the Revolution were
well represented at the function, I give the
names of the committee as follows: Mrs.
John K. Gowdy, Mrs. Ernest H. Tripp,
Mrs. A. E. Cullop, Miss Sarah E. Hunt,
Mrs. George Geran, Mrs. T. G. Webber,
and Miss Tarquinia L. Voss, chairman.

[Column 2]
	Miss Voss effectively read a dedication
poem by Frank A. Putnam, which was
loudly applauded, and the Daughters placed
a large floral piece (about four feet in
diameter) of yellow roses and blue flowers,
the colors of the association, at the foot of
the monument. Among the list of honor-
ary vice-presidents I was more than grati-
fied to find the name of our able and
esteemed governor, Heber M. Wells.
	Among the most notable persons on the
platform were the American Ambassador to
France, Gen. Horace Porter, our Ambassa-
dors to Germany, Russia, Italy and Switzer-
land; His Excellency, the President of the
French Republic (Loubet) and his Cabinet;
Archbishop of Ireland, of St. Paul, and all
the commissioners, including Mrs. Daniel
Manning and Mrs. Potter Palmer. Sousa
and his famous band played our national
airs and the flags of America and France
floated side by side from public and private
buildings alike. In short, America cap-
tured Paris for that day.
	It was a novelty, I assure you, for a
daughter of Utah to march down an avenue
covered with crimson carpet and lined on
each side with soldiers of the guard in their
gorgeous scarlet and gold uniforms at pre-
sent arms, with bands playing and flags
flying.
	Paris at all times is a busy place for the
sight-seer, but this week it has beeil un-
usually so for me. On Sunday I attended
church and met Dr. and Mrs. Thurber,
cousins of Mrs. Byron Groo. The Dr. is
pastor of the American Church here and I
thought his sermon broad and very excel-
lent. On Monday we attended Mrs. Daniel
Manning’s reception given at the Elysee
Palace Hotel, in honor of Mr. Daniel
French, the sculptor of the Washington
monument, which was unveiled on the 3rd
inst. After the unveiling of the Lafayette
monument on Tuesday we attended Gen.
and Mrs. Porter’s reception, said to have
been the most brilliant ever given by an
American Ambassador in Paris, and in the
evening we attended a banquet at the Con-
tinental Hotel, given by the American
Chamber of Commerce. There were over
four hundred guests seated and we had
speeches from Gen. Porter, the French
ministers of War and Commerce, Archbishop
of Ireland, our other foreign Ambassadors
and others of note. The banquet lasted
until after midnight and was very enjoyable.
	I have had a very pleasant hour with
Madame de Mercy, the president of the
Ladies Club, the only woman’s club in
Paris; and Madame Bogelot, who since her
visit to America in 1893 is so pronounced in
praise of our women that she is now called
the “American-French woman.”
	Mrs. May Wright Sewall has asked me to
call on her this evening, so with these social
matters and the Exhibition my hands are
full.
			MARY E. R. WEBBER.
	P. S. Mrs. J. B. Farlow sails on the
Palatia from Bologne on the 9th for home.
Miss Teasdel is at Etaples with an art
class.


A MORNING IN CAMP.
	This was a subject given me to speak
upon at a celebration given by the sons and
daughters of the pioneers of 1847 at
Pleasant Grove, Utah, on the 22nd of June,
1900. I made a few notes which I did not
use on the occasion, and as the object of
perpetuating the storey of the pioneers,
who sought out these vales in the desert, is
one that ought to receive some attention, I
felt to put in print what I had written, as
some thought might thus be awakened and
lead to recollections with others who had
similar experiences.
	A morning in camp is not very amusing,
and if at all pleasant must depend upon
circumstances, one morning will scarcely
suffice as an illustration.
	I must go back to my first morning in
camp in February, 1846, when we had
slept on the ground for the first time in our
lives, a number, thirty-one of us in
one tent. We had crossed the Mississippi
river the day before. When we woke on
the following morning the ground was
covered with snow, a beautiful picture to
look at—tents, wagons all beautiful with
the white covering, and some of us were
romantic enough to admire the scene not-
withstanding the cold and the many dis-
advantage of being out of doors in a snow 
storm, and could not help contrasting the
scene with pictures of Ancient Israel as they
journeyed hither and thither in the olden
times in far off Eastern lands with their
flocks and herds, pitching their tents here
and there, as we were doing.
	Sister Eliza R. Snow described one of
these mornings thus:
 
	“Lo a mighty host of Jacob,
		Tented on the Western shore,
	Of the noble Mississippi,
		Which they had been passing o’er,
	At the last day dawn of Winter,
		Bound with frost and wrapped in snow,
	Hark, the cry is Onward! Onward! 
		Camp of Israel rise and go, 

	All at once is life and motion,
		Trunks and beds and baggage fly,
	Oxen yoked and horses harnessed,
		Tents rolled up and passing by.
	Soon the carriage wheels are moving,
		Onward to a woodland dell
	Where at sunset all are quartered,
		Camp of Israel, all is well.”

	Some mornings our horses were lost or
cattle had strayed and we would be hindered
for hours, and lucky if not for days. Oc-
casionally we found pleasant spots to camp
in, though some were miserable—damp,
rough and always uncomfortable to make
beds down on the ground.
	Mornings we usually had time to get
some kind of a breakfast and put things in
order, but we had to fly around pretty
lively to do it, and quite frequently we had
heavy biscuits and many such annoyances—
kettles upsetting and putting out the fire
in the middle of the cooking. We didn’t
cook scientifically, but we did some things
by weight and measure, for fear the food
would not hold out on the journey. We
always had “starters” that gave the word,
“Take down the tents, put out the fires
and pack up the cooking utensils, don’t


[Column 1]
break the dishes, and get ready quick, jump
in the wagon and take care you are not left
behind.”
	Some people seemed to be ready without
an effort. Altogether we were a happy
crowd; boys and men whistling while they
harnessed the horses or yoked the cattle;
women with babies were particularly well
cared for. President Young and Heber C.
Kimball, the life of the camp, always up
stirring about early while some of the boys
were snoozing. I remember Brother Brigham
Young round the camp at Sugar Creek with
a bucket full of composition tea giving us a
cup of it because we were chilly and cold, we
had just come from our warm fires out into
the cold prairie, facing storms, wind, snow
and sleet; it will hardly bear talking about
even at this late date.
	Sometimes we found in the morning that
we had camped in a spot where there were
wild flowers and clear water, and birds
singing, but this was a rare exception,
generally we were very hungry and before
we could finish our breakfast we were
ordered to pack up and be off. Sunday
mornings were better because we rested,
and about eleven o’clock the camp would
be called together for instructions.
	I assure you I looked over my diary written
when coming out of Nauvoo to see what I did
say about the mornings. Sometimes the
horses were lost and then we were in
great trouble, generally it was muddy,
the water was poor, and once old Bill was
dead and we all mourned because he had
been faithful and was good and strong.
	All of us who have had such wonderful
experiences make our stories too long, there’s
so much to tell; suffice it to say we came,
we conquered every obstacle. Some died
on the way and some were born on the
journey, in fact quite often the exciting
topic of the morning was a new baby. The 
tents blew over in the night quite frequent-
ly and then we had to crowd in wherever
we could, which didn’t help to make us
cheerful in the morning. Early in the
journey before we knew how to arrange and
dispose our kitchen apparatus, the sisters
would complain in the morning that the tin
cans had been poking them in the ribs all
night.
	There was not much fun in the morning,
all was hurry and bustle and the camp cry
was always be off, and we were generally
on our way before we knew it, for there
was no waiting, you jumped on or you got
left, and we girls were afraid to be left, for
there were Indians in those days as well as
buffaloes and wild cats, and we had to be
carried over the rivers in some man’s arms,
you know, because we were not experts at
swimming, and we couldn’t fly, tho’ we 
often wanted to.
	We had prayers in the mornings, in fact
am sure it was faith in God we depended
upon to bring us safely along. It was a
wonderful journey and now after more than
fifty years, looking back upon it, the wonder
is how we had the courage, the persever-
ance, the endurance to accomplish it; the
mornings were not the most agreeable, there
were too many things to do and to think of
though one sister I remember used to say
almost invariably, “I am glad we have a
clean camping place every day, that is a
luxury,” and one that has camped three or
four days or a week in one place would
appreciate that saying most certainly.
	Looking backward I can recall a few
bright mornings when something unusual
broke the monotony, or the scenery was

[Column 2]
more picturesque, or we had seen a beauti-
ful deer, perhaps two or three or half a
dozen such beauties, so graceful in their
movements. I recall a morning when the
boys, who had been out for the cattle found
wild honey and came into camp for buckets
to put it in; this was a great treat. Some-
how we always felt safe; there was order in
the camp, men stood guard at night and we
slept soundly in the open air. Those are
wonderful days to look back upon; how
marvelously we were guided to this land.
	One of the best things to have on such a
journey is cheerfulness under all circum-
stances. In our family we had a Welsh
sister whom everybody called Grandma,
and I have often said since, she kept us
alive with her jokes and witticisms and her
Welsh songs and prayers, for she always
prayed in Welsh and out loud. Whenever
she was offended she would break out in
her Welsh tongue, and invariably put every,
one in a good humor. She drove a little
light wagon aud one horse and took care of
the baby and her own things, and made tea
in her rare old china teapot every morning,
and if we could coax her to give us a cup
of her tea we considered it a favor. Funny
as she was we were all sort of afraid of her,
she rather kept the young folks in order
when she was around, even if it was in the
morning.
	If I should ever have a pioneer subject
again, I trust it will be the camp at eve-
ning, for of that there is something to tell.
					AUNT EM.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
LADIES’ SEMI MONTHLY MEETING.
	The Ladies’ Semi-Monthly Meeting, held
in the Fourteenth Ward Assembly Hall,
July 1, 1900. Meeting opened by singing
“Once more we come before our God.”
Prayer by M. W. Wilcox. Singing,
“Glorious things of thee are spoken.”
President Horne read a letter from Sister
Alder, which was very interesting. She
then told the sisters that she would like to
hear testimonies from them and that when
they testified to the truths ot this work it
strengthened others. Spoke of the old
folks’ excursion, had a very nice time. She
was glad to see President Joseph F. Smith
and other Apostles with the party. Said
Heber Goddard sang “Hard Times.”
Brother Joseph F. Smith said that we would
not have any more hard times. She urged
the sisters to do right and to keep the com-
mandments of God.
	Sister Seabeck was grateful to be with us
and always tried to come to the meetings of
the sisters, for there was a good spirit here
and she always acknowledged the hand of
God in all things and was glad to be num-
bered with the Saints.
	Sister Joanna Peterson felt thankful for
health and strength every day and testified
to the goodness of her Heavenly Father.
Hoped that she would prove faithful to the
end.
	Sister Martha Morris sang “Jesus the
Giver of all we enjoy,” and said she de-
sired to do what was right. We know now
that the day is drawing near when we will
see perilous times, when we hear of wars
and famine and so many accidents.
	Sister Smellie said we must love the
spirit of the Gospel, wanted to say that we
must continue to keep the commandments
of God and He will bless us. She spoke of
Sister Alder and said that it would be nice if
we all could donate a little money so that
she would be able to do more good on her

[Column 2]
mission. “I am sorry we did not have the
minutes here today, I want to speak of the
power of women.”
	Sister Hardy: “I desire to bear my testi-
mony to the truth of this work. May we
strive to set a good example to those around
us.”
	Another sister said that she rejoiced in
the work of the Lord.
	Sister Jane James felt thankful that she
had the Holy Spirit. She thanked Sister
Horne for her blessing, and rejoiced in a
knowledge of the Gospel. She had lived
with the prophet’s family, and had once
converted a young girl to the Gospel.
	Sister Zina D. H. Young: “It is by the
grace of God that we will do His will and
be humble, blessing our brethren and
having the love of the sisters. Always try
and do good, and try to look after the poor
and needy. We must love one another and
try to do all the good we can, each one of
us should invite others to our meetings.”
	Sister E. J. Stevenson was feeling very
weak, she said. “When shall we all meet
again. I feel grateful that I am well
enough to come to this meeting. Sister
Howe came and comforted me. May the
blessing of God rest upon us that we may
always do what is right. I am as thankful
as when the stars shouted for joy that we
have had our ears opened.” Said she had
done all she could helping to redeem the
dead.
	A young lady here feels the Spirit of our
Father, she feels that she would like to go
into the united order. “I rejoice that we
have had a feast of fat things. We must
stand up and speak of the goodness of God.”
	Another sister spoke of the goodness of
our Father, said that she was so happy.
	A sister prayed for all the sisters and
blessed them, and testified to the goodness
of her Heavenly Father.
	Sister Mair: “I am thankful that I be-
long to the Relief Society. Let us move
forward and do all the good we can. I
hope I will prove faithful unto the end.”
	Another sister said, “I rejoice with the
Latter-day Saints, where can you find
better women than here? Since I was
here I have had many testimonies. May
we all be faithful.”
				Sec. pro tem.


NOTES.
	MRS. DANIEL LOTHROP (“Margaret
Sydney”) has gone abroad. She has been
appointed by the National Society Children
of the American Revolution of which she is
the founder and national president, to repre-
sent it at the Paris Exposition, and has also
been appointed a delegate to the Inter-
national Peace Congress to convene in Paris,
September 25 to October 5.
	MRS. VIRGINIA G. SHARP, of Philadel-
phia according to the notes of the New
York Camera Club, is the first artist in
photography to whom has ever been
awarded the honor of having one of her
photographs purchased by the Art Institute
of Chicago, to be displayed among their
permanent collections. This is also the
first instance in the history of the art
institutes of the country where such pic-
tures have been accepted purely as works
of art. Mrs. Sharp has also had the
honor of being invited to contribute a num-
ber of her photographs for exhibition at the
Paris Exposition.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


For the EXPONENT.
	LOVE’S BEST.
Love is most sweet at its best.
	Ever tender, thoughtful, true,
Constant and fully expressed,
	With “What can I do for you?”
Under storm or the cloud,
	In any peril or pain,
Its sweetness all ills enshroud,
	As sunshine follows the rain.
Love comes with soothing and rest,
	With healing ready at call,
Earth’s charming, most health-giving guest,
	Turning to sweetness life’s gall.
Give me this love at its best,
	Love that endureth thro’ all;
Love that can bear any test.
	Or ne’er give any at all.
							L. M. H.


PRAY NOW.
When by thy busy tasks oppressed,
	There riseth in thine hour of care,
A whisper “from thy labors rest,
	And lift thy soul to God in prayer,”
Delay not till thy work is done;
	To cross the line that separates,
Earth’s climes from heaven; for they are one;
	In faith pray now, thine angel waits,

Earth’s greatest works, by sea or land,
	Are set to time, from place to place,
And in obedience strictly planned,
	The mighty forces run their race.
But no mischance or accident
	E’er wrecks the course, or e’er belates,
His work or messenger He sent,
	To thee, pray now, thine-angel waits. 

For wasted chance, for faith too slow,
	Life’s harvest yields, but what we earned;
And desolate at last will know
	Where we from waiting blessings turned.
	In weakness, need, or triumph’s hour, 
Though trials crush on joys estate;
	Near us His watchful, loving power;
Fear not, pray now, thine angel waits.
	AUGUST JOYCE CROCHERTON.
a, July 6, 1900.


[Column 1]
WESTEL WILLOUGHBY GREENE.
	The gentleman above named was indeed
one of Nature’s noblemen. He was born
in Watertown, Jefferson County, New
York, August 28, 1819. There in his
native town he founded the “Jefferson
County Nursery,” the first industry of its
kind in that county.
	In his chosen occupation he excelled,
being a lover and student of nature, its
floral kingdom especially. He was noted
also for his strict integrity and prompt at-
tention to business.
	His father, Jacob Greene, was second
cousin to Nathaniel Greene, of Revolution-
ary fame, and brother to John P. Greene,
a prominent character in the early history
of the Latter-day Saints.
	The death of Westel W. Greene occurred
very suddenly at his home in Watertown,
N. Y., on the 8th of last March. The be-
reaved widow, Elizabeth Ingham Greene,
writes to relatives whom she visited here
in Salt Lake City some years ago in com-
pany with her husband. Mrs. Greene tells
of the beautiful life of her husband, so full
of cheery kindness that all who knew him,
children, animals and his floral pets, as
well as men and women, involuntarily
gave out to him the sweetness of love
which his influence ever evoked. Of their
visit to this city, which was in the winter
time, she writes:
	Many times have we talked over our visit with
you, and the kindness we received from all the
good people. He often wished that he could see
all his friends and the beautiful valley in summer.
He would say he wanted to see them all again
more than he ever did before he went there, the
People all seemed so good.

[Column 2]
	Besides the widow Mr. Greene left two
sons and two daughters to mourn his sud-
den departure.
	In concluding her letter, Mrs. Greene
writes:
	Dear cousin, these lines we found in a book in
which he kept some of his most choice writings.
I thought you would like to see them, they are dear
to me it is so like him. 
	Accompanying are the following beauti-
ful lines:
			“When I am dead
I would not have the rude and gaping crowd
Around me gather, and ’mid lamentations loud,
Tell of my virtues and with vain regret,
Bemoan my loss, and leaving me, so soon forget;
But I would have the few, the kindly heart, 
Who when misfortune came so nobly did their
	part,
And oft by thoughtful deed their love express—
These would I have, no more, no less—
			When I am dead!

			“When I am dead
I would not have the high and storied stone
Placed o’er my grave, and then be left alone: 
But I would have some living thing I once did
	love,
Ere I did leave the joyous world above,
Placed o’er me, and in each succeeding year
I’d have my friends renew them and oft linger near,
With loving thoughts upon the dear one laid be-
	low,
And talk of times departed long ago—
			When I am dead! 

			“When I am dead,
Forgive—O, this I pray for more than all—
The anguish I have caused—the deed beyond re-
	call!
Think kindly on me as I lay so cold, so still,
So poor a subject for thine angered ill.
Think of some generous deed, some good word
	spoken,
Of hearts bound up I found so sad and broken:
Think gently, when this last, long rest is mine,
And gaze upon my form with looks benign—
			When I am dead.
			L. L. GREENE RICHARDS.


U. W. P. C.
	The U. W. P. C. held its regular meet
ing in the EXPONENT office, June 30, 1900,
the president in the chair. Opening prayer
by Phebe C. Young. The secretary then
called the roll, members responding with
quotations from Tennyson.
	The 6th of July had been set apart by
the clubs of the city for an outing to the
Lake for the benefit of the Free Kinder-
garten. The president of the club council
being out of town the council had not been
called. The club discussed the best way of
carrying it through successfully. Luella
Rhodes moved and Emma J. McVicker
seconded that the club have an excursion to
the Lake Friday, July 6, and all the mem-
bers do what they could to make it a suc-
cess—carried.
	Luella Rhodes then reported the work
done in the Domestic Science School. The
school opened on the 25th of June, with a
membership of twenty-six.
	The president reported from the Feder-
ation meeting which was held at Mrs.
Plummer’s. Each club member was taxed
ten cents for the Federation to be held next
October.
	Mrs. Hyde, the delegate from the Press
Club to arrange for Field day at the Lake,
asked for instructions from the clnb. It
was decided to hold a special meeting be-
fore then to decide permanently what we
would do.
	The program was then carried out as
follows:
	Emma J. McVicker gave a very com-
prehensive review of the book entitled,

[Column 2]
“Red Pottage,” written by Miss Mary
Cholmondeley. She considered it a finished
work, which is not true of most books.
When the author pictured up, in some
cases vividly, the faults of the different
characters she portrays, she shows the
reader something to admire in them. Mrs.
McVicker considered the time well spent in
reading the book,
	Dr. Pratt read a poem entitled “A
Legend,’’ by Mrs. Augusta J. Crocheron,
and Grace Anderson read a poem entitled
“Is Jesus Immortal,” by Mrs. Mary Jane
Tanner.
	On account of Field day being our next,
meeting day the club decided to hold the
next meeting on July 30. The minutes
were read and with a few remarks approved.
Closing prayer by Mrs. E. B. Wells.
			GRACE ANDERSON, Sec.

	At the meeting of the U. W. P. C.,
Monday evening, July 30, the president,
Mrs. Aimiee C. Schiller, being absent,
Honorary President E. B. Wells was called
to the chair by unanimous request. Prayer
was offered by Dr. Romania B. Pratt.
Miscellaneous business was considered,
Field Day being one of the items and Mrs.
Wells by permission made a few remarks
regarding women having suitable repre-
sentation in political matters and on the
State tickets, especially in educational
affairs. She also thought the club should
have current events each month, consider-
ing the present condition of the world.
	Mrs. Rhodes stated that the Press Club
was the only one of the clubs that took any
part in the Domestic Science class recently.
Mrs. Ruth M. Fox read a beautiful poem of
Eliza R. Snow’s. Red Pottage, by Mary
Cholmondley was discussed by the club,
Mrs. Emma J. McVicker opening the dis-
cussion.
	Mrs. Ella W. Hyde was appointed to
give current events at the meeting in
August. Papers will be given by mem-
bers upon the Chinese situation and
Woman’s International Congresses at the
Paris Exposition.
	Adjourned until August 30. Dismissed
with prayer by Mrs. McVicker.
			RUTH M. FOX, Asst. Sec.


NOTABLE WOMEN,
	LADY HENRY SOMERSET has been re-
elected president of the World’s Woman’s
Temperance Union.
	AMELIA E. BARR is a woman who has
been the mother of fourteen children, has
written thirty-two books, prepared a pro-
fessor for Princeton College, and at three 
score years of age is a superb picture of
vitality—as fresh and sweet of heart as a
young girl.
	MRS. HUGHES—more widely known by
her name used in literary work, Gwyneth
Vaughn—is a talented and energetic
woman whose home is among the northern
hills of Wales. She is a member of the
Woman’s Liberal Federation, a lecturer
for the Suffrage Association and an active
worker in the British Woman’s Temperance
Association. A fluent speaker in both
English and Welsh, she is in the field or at
her desk, by pen and voice helping forward
the human cause. It is said that she has
spoken in every county in Wales. Her
two sons and charming daughter, to whom
she is a devoted mother and comrade, are
allied with her in the good work.

SALT LAKE CITY, UTAH, JULY 15 & AUGUST 1, 1900.


[Column 1]
“THE WORLD IS UP IN ARMS.”
	The commotion of the world at the pres-
ent time is almost incredible. Civilization
and enlightenment have reached such high
ground that it is hard to believe that such a
tempest of nations should have set in.
Using a very old fashioned phrase, “The.
whole world is up in arms.”
	Notwithstanding the earnest and strenu-
ous efforts that have been made during the
last few years to bring about settlements of
difficulties by arbitration, there seems to be
a tremendous power brought to bear to
overthrow and break down all barriers that
serve as safeguards to human life and lay
waste the countries and nations in a uni-
versal war, that shall involve all govern-
ments, however exclusive they may have
been heretofore.
	Beginning with Cuba and the war with
Spain, a spirit of war has gathered as it
were, all its forces, to kill and destroy, with-
out mercy, until it is horrible to contem-
plate. And there seems no alternative un-
less a power more potent than the puny
hand of man be brought into requisition to
stay the deadly weapons of warfare and the
terrible grasp of the adversary of peace and
good will in its devastating and downward
course. The torch of liberty, of which
America as a nation has boasted, is not
lighting the world as it should do, and men
are not paving the way for that PEACE chat
surpasseth understanding while their hands
are literally or indirectly imbued in the
blood of their fellowmen.
	No wonder the disciples of Jesus said “In
the last days perilous times shall come and
men’s hearts shall fail them,” and so on, if
nations will persist in making war upon one
another, as they are doing today. How
can those ignorant of God and His laws be
taught the better way, the precepts of the
Savior, “Love thy neighbor,” if educated,
cultured people, who profess to lead the
world in its higher advancement, only fol-
low the same methods of subduing the
powers that oppose as those who have
never known Christ and Him crucified, or
heard of the true plan of salvation preached
and taught by the Savior and His Apostles?
	The Chinese problem may be a very dif-
ficult one to wrestle with, but it cannot
better conditions to kill until every sort of
diplomatic intervention has been tried.
China has great resources of which we at a

[Column 2]
distance know very little; and what will it
avail to send a few thousand brave men to
fall at the hands of her millions, and how
can it be expected to answer the desired end?
	Meantime what can women do who have
reared these brave men who now go out to
battle at the country’s call? It is noble to
defend one’s own country and its honor and
institutions, and yet even then the cause
must be justifiable where blood is to be
shed. Surely it is time now for deep and
earnest thought and every effort should be
put forth to bring about peaceful reconcili-
ation instead of boasting of skill and
strength to compete with other nations. It
is far better to offer heavy rewards for all
foreigners in China delivered alive than to
attempt to cope with their cunning in the
use and manipulation of implements of de-
struction. What avail is the learning of the
whole world if it will not help us to set
better examples of righteousness than those
maintained by force of arms, called into
question every time a difficulty arises?
	And again, meantime women must wait
and watch and pray, and the armies of
women who pray will be more than
the armies of men who slay; and the
Lord will hear and answer their
prayers in His own due time; but as faith
without works is dead they must work
diligently and lay the foundation of such
principles in their sons as shall lead them
to higher and holier purposes in the govern
ment of the world than going to war with
intent to kill. The time is coming, how-
ever far off it may seem at present, when
swords will be beaten into plongh-shares
and spears into pruning hooks. May God
speed the day!!!
	Looking at conditions now it seems use
less to allude to such a time; and yet it is
nevertheless the prophecy of the future by
the greatest men and women—poets, sages,
seers and philosophers; and why should we
not believe in the best for the human race?
Tennyson foresaw it; Isaiah foreshadowed
such a time, and when even the
beasts of the field, the lamb and the
lion, should lie down together. And,
therefore, women need not despair though
all seems dark today as far as peace among
the nations is concerned; yet the silent
forces that are working and the heartfelt
prayers of the tens of thousands
of mothers that are offered daily will reach
the ears of the Omnipotent, and truth and
righteousness will eventually triumph, the
earth will be redeemed and Satan will be
bound.
	But woman will have to do her part in
this work of redemption, and there will be
both wise and foolish virgins, undoubtedly.
It will be the duty of all to study the ques-
tions as presented by the events that tran-
spire from day to day and from time to time
and not be led into error, but walk in the Gos-
pel light and listen to the still small voice
within. More especially should the women
of the Latter-day Saints, who have had
great opportunities of knowing concerning
the future and God’s dealings with the
children of men, study those grave and
serious questions, for where much is given
much is required. Let your light so shine
that others seeing your good works may be
stimulated to follow in your footsteps.


CONTRARY to all preconceived ideas, a
greater number of men than of women,
says the Chicago census enumerators, re-
fused to tell their ages.


AUNT ZINA’S PARTY.
	On Tuesday afternoon, July 26, a num-
ber of sisters met at the residence of Sister
Zina D. H. Young, to show their love and
appreciation of this Mother in Israel, whose
whole life has been such a striking example
of self-sacrifice and devotion to the Gospel
and its work here upon the earth in this
dispensation.
	Sister Zina has truly earned the appella-
tion “Mother in Israel.” The sisters who
were present on the occasion of the gather-
ing mentioned had a most enjoyable time,
although the affair was an informal one,
yet it was a sort of meeting. Sister E. B.
Wells made a few appropriate remarks and
referred with love and admiration to the
many sisters who had been wont to meet
with us on similar occasions who were now
on the other side of the veil, and whose
great nobility of character we cherished
still in memory with fond appreciation.
Prominent among these were Vilate
Kimball, Mary Ann Young, Emma Smith,
Eliza R. Snow, Elizabeth Ann Whitney,
and later whom all present would remem-
ber, Phebe Woodruff, Sarah M. Kimball,
Elizabeth Howard and many more whose
names were familiar to the Latter-day
Saints and that we should not allow their
names to be forgotten, for they had helped
lay the foundation for others.
	Sister Wells then suggested that Sister
Bathsheba W. Smith preside. The sisters
sang Sister Eliza’s hymn, “O, my Father,”
Sister Camilla C. Cobb leading and Sister
Minnie J. Snow playing the accompaniment
on the organ. An eloquent prayer which
touched every heart was offered by Sister
Elizabeth Webb, and was followed by the
hymn “God moves in a mysterious way.”
	Sister Zina, in her own sweet, motherly
way, welcomed the sisters to her home and
heart and praised the Lord, as she always
does, for all His blessings to us as a people.
	Sister B. W. Smith spoke briefly and
alluded to the sisters whose names had
been mentioned, particularly “Sister
Emma,” with whom she was intimately
acquainted. Sister Zina then paid a loving
tribute to “Sister Emma.”
	Sister Sarah Jane Cannon followed in
a similar strain, referring tenderly to the
experiences of women in this Church and
their wonderful integrity.
	Sisters Margaret Y. Taylor, Hannah C.
Wells, Margaret P. Young, Maria W. Wil-
cox, Mary Alice Lambert, Sarah P. Snow,
Julia C. Howe, Naamah Twiss Young,
Elizabeth J. Stevenson, Emma S. Wood-
ruff and Minnie J. Snow each made excel-
lent and timely remarks, beautiful tributes
to those among whom they had lived in the
family relation, and the great experience
gained by being thus associated. Those
who spoke referred to other sisters, who had
been faithful in all things: Marinda Hyde,
Leonora Taylor, Presendia Kimball and
Sarepta Heywood. The sisters sang
“Praise to the man who communed with
Jehovah.”
	Aunt Zina was then blest by all the
sisters uniting their faith, Sister L. L.
Greene Richards being mouth in offering
the prayer.
	Sister Zina then spoke in the gift of
tongues and at the earnest solicitation of
all present gave the interpretation herself.
Afterwards all joined in singing “Praise
God from whom all blessings flow,” and
the little meeting was dismissed by Sister
E. B. Wells.


After the exercises the guests repaired to
the dining room and partook of the good
things provided by one and all of the sisters
assembled. Sister Lambert pronounced
the blessing upon the food. A beautiful 
vase of roses in the centre of the table were
the gift of Sister Woodruff to our beloved
“Aunt Zina.”
	Besides the sisters mentioned as having
taken part in the meeting there were pres-
ent Sisters Lydia Ann Wells, Maria Y.
Dougall, Phebe Y. Beatie, Julia A. Brixen,
Lizzie S. Wilcox Amelia Maeser, Marjorie
McCarty and Elizabeth Langton.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


DR. TALMAGE’S NEW BOOK.
	Great Salt Lake Present and Past, is the
title of a book recently issued by the
Deseret News Publishing House.
	The author is Dr. James E. Talmage,
whose scientific knowledge is good author-
ity on many special subjects. His book is
certainly a very complete and comprehen-
sive work of its kind, and the people of
Utah have great reason to be proud of this
description of the lake, which they have
always felt was such a fine resort and
added so much to this intermountain region
because of its differentiation to all the
other lakes in America. To the student of
geology more especially this little work
will apply with greater interest than to
those who only consider it from a general
point of view.
	To one intensely fond of natural beauty
in scenery Salt Lake and its surroundings
is a picture of marvelous attractions morn-
ing, noon or night; in sunshine or in
storm. Its continuous mountain range of
islands and great picturesqueness to the
view, and the variety of birds that live upon
these deserted and seemingly unin-
habited islands play a conspicuous part in
these scenes of solitude: their graceful
flights, their brilliant plumage and the
sound varieties whether of the whirring
of wings or screeching, screaming or
melancholy croaking or cooing—all these
signs of sight or sound accentuate the wild
charm of this “Dead Sea of America” over
and beyond the Jordan.
	Dr. Talmage has conferred a favor on
the State by writing such a book and by
having it illustrated, giving as it does facts
and figures that will be invaluable to
the student of natural or geographical
history, as well as the geologist.
	Dr. Talmage tells the reader not only of
the lake, its beauty and health-giving
properties, but of the process of making
salt which we use daily with our food. The
book contains so much that is food for the
mind that one need not hesitate to recom-
mend it as well worth not only perusal, but
study, and as a book of reference, and in
many respects the book would be an aid in
research valuable to the historian.
	The people of Utah are very proud of
Great Salt Lake, and whatever fields of dis-
covery are open our young men and women
should avail themselves of the knowledge,
and not remain in ignorance of the vast re-
sources of this wonderful, and as yet in
many places unexplored country. We
venture to say there are many things in the
book referred to that will be a surprise to
the student of science and natural history.

[Column 1]
UNVEILING OF PIONEER MONUMENT.
	The final ceremonies of unveiling the
pioneer monument, as completed, with the
three added figures, the Indian the trapper
and pioneer group, took place July 24, at
10:30 a. m. Seats had been arranged in-
side the iron railing for speakers and dis-
tinguished guests, and the public filled the
street roundabout and occupied all the
available space in the windows and porches
of buildings near by. The Brigham Young
Memorial Association had charge of the
exercises, (Hon. James Sharp president)
which were mostly of a reminiscent char-
acter. At 9:30 Held’s Military band
stationed near the monument opened
the exercises with some of the old familiar
airs appropriate to the occasion, in com-
memorating the founding of the State.
President George Q. Cannon made the ad-
dress of the day.
	He said “that being one of the pioneers
who came here in 1847, many reminiscen-
ces crowded his mind and he could not
help drawing contrasts between conditions
now and those existing here fifty-three
years ago. While some people cannot or
will not acknowledge that Brigham Young
was an inspired man and a prophet of God,
all will concede the fact that he was a
great man and a strong leader of men.
When the Pioneers first looked into this
valley all was desolation: the courses of
the streams running into the valley being
marked by willows, the only green to be
seen. When Brigham Young saw the
valley he declared it to be the stopping
place of the pilgrims, and this in the face
of assertions of trappers and hunters that
it would be impossible for a community to
live here. He sent exploring parties to
the south and to the north to investigate
the country and all returned declaring that
the decision of Brigham Young was a wise
one. The people suffered almost untold
hardships during the first years of life in
this valley when the elements were more
inclement than now. They suffered the
pangs of hunger in their most extreme dis-
tress. Once a week in my uncle’s house,
where I lived, said the speaker, we would
weigh out our provisions in the family and
then eke them out for the week. Our
rations were very short, we did not have
enough to eat; we were hungry, very
hungry—hungry to the ends of our fingers;
hungry to the ends of our toes; always
hungry—one good meal would not suffice
to satisfy us, our hunger was so great.
We cut thistle tops to eat and I never
tasted such “greens” in my life. I
actually began to get fat on thistle tops.
Even boiled hides were eaten by the peo-
ple, and even after the grain had been
planted and began to grow, giving promise
of a bread supply, the crickets came and
swept it all away and added more hard-
ships to those already endured by the set-
tlers.
	“When gold was first discovered in
California, Brigham Young advised the
people to remain here and cultivate the
land, promising them that they should
be prospered if they did so. And while it
has been charged against Brigham Young
that he discouraged the development of the
mineral industries of Utah, the sequel
proved that his advice was wise, for by re-
maining at home and producing food the
people placed themselves beyond the dan-
ger of a shortage of food supplies and put
them in a position to pursue the search for

[Column 2]
gold and silver without jeopardizing the
lives of their families by lack of something
to sustain life.
	“I am not much of a believer in monu-
ments, for I think that men and their good
deeds should live in memory; but this
present monument is a very proper one.
The story of the pilgrimage of the Pioneers
and their settlement here should be told
and retold to our children in order that
they may know of the heroic deeds done by
their forefathers.”
	Hon. James T. Hammond said that he
did not believe in the worship of a monu-
ment, but thought the accomplishment
which it represented was the thing to be
revered. Mr. Hammond reviewed the his-
tory of the erection of the monument and
the life of the sculptor, Mr. C. E. Dallin,
and introduced that gentleman, who said:
	“Friends, it is some twenty years since
I turned my face eastward toward the un-
known land of my hopes and aspirations,
and as one who has visited strange lands
and seen many sights, I return to the font
where I first drank the life-giving waters 
of pure inspiration. To those who are
born and reared amid these circling hills
have little need to tell what a potent
spell they have, and as whatever little I
have done has been directly tracable to
their influence, you can well understand
how they are intimately connected with
the most sacred part of my being. To the
mighty forms of sculptural beauty and the 
ever changing lights and shades of the
peaks my mind was ever atuned and as the
history of sculpture plainly shows, sculp-
tors have ever been reared amid the
mountains. These silent formative  in-
fluences are even deeper and more lasting
than we realize, and to you who have the
privilege of this silent communion of the
hills your spirits must ever be awake to
the beauty and poetry that daily speak to
you from the mighty heights.
	“Art of every kind is simply the realiza-
tion that beauty lies around us on every
hand, and the artist is only he who pos-
sibly realizes more fully this truth, and he
only becomes an artist in trying to reveal
this truth to his fellow man. Art to the
many is considered a luxury or an accom-
plishment, whereas in truth it is one of the
most necessary functions of a well ordered.
life, and to deprive man of this means of
expression would plunge him into a bar- 
barism from which there would be no hope
of ever rescuing him.
	“The monument as it now stands is an
attempt to depict the early history and
condition of Utah, and with the exception
of Brigham Young, the statues are repre-
sentative. Thus the Indian represents the
early condition that prevailed in this valley
before the advent of the whites, and the
trapper represents the first white man that
ever penetrated the wilderness. The
Pioneer group represents the hardy, in-
domitable family that first made their home
in the wilderness, and from them sprang
the first civilization of the great West.”
THE UNVEILING. 
	It was just 10:58 a. m. when a trumpet
call was sounded and the veil of banners
was loosened and fell from around the
monument disclosing the new bronze
figures, while the band played “America,”
Then there were cheers and the crowd
surged about the monument to view the
figures that finished the monument to the
Pioneers and their brave deeds.


Miss Margaret Young, a daughter of
Major R. W. Young, and a great grand-
daughter of Brigham Young, drew the
cord that released the veiling.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
AUTOBIOGRAPAY OF SARAH B. LAYTON.
	William Bates, my father, was of English 
descent. He married one Elizabeth Jeffries
in 1816, and settled in a small town called
Sandy, in Bedfordshire, England.
	The house in which I was born was a
small concrete building which stood near
the St. Ives river, facing the west. There
was one door and a window in the front, a
door and a window in the back. On the
first floor were three rooms, one large room
and two small pantries while on the second
floor were but two, one large room and a
large closet. The thatched root, the picket
fence painted blue and the little flower gar-
den in which I used to spend a few spare
moments, hold a cherished spot in my mem-
ory. A few years ago the rosemary trees I
planted in my youth were still growing.
	In the meadows, outside the fence, was a
lovely place, where I and my sister and my
brothers whiled away our childhood hours
running in the grass, and in the shade of
the old willows.
	I was born July 6, 1826, being the third
child of my parents, the others names were
Ann, William Jeffries, and John Richard.
	My father and mother belonged to the
laboring class. We, as children, were
brought up in a country village. My
mother was of a religious turn of mind, and
from my earliest recollection she taught us
to read the Bible; and as I advanced in
years I took great pleasure in reading that
book. I used to go miles to hear different
denominations preach what they called the
Gospel. I would listen with the utmost
interest to them, but used to take great
pleasure in listening to a sect called in
England Calvinists. Many times I
have walked over seven miles with my
mother on Sunday to hear them preach,
before I was twelve years old. One day I
was listening to one of their speakers. In
his discourse he said “that little children
who were not born of parents who were
elected were crawling over hell like frogs
and toads.” Yonng as I was it struck ter-
ror to my soul; and I began to think if it
were possible that God was so unjust that
He would consign little children to such
endless misery, I could not find any such
doctrine in the Bible, and from that time to
this I have never listened to any more of
that doctrine.
	About that time, I remember, there were
to be some races in a place about three
miles from where we lived. I had a great
desire to go, but my mother told me I could
not go, she said she would take me to the hill
so I could see the people pass, there I could
help her gather fir apples. I went with
her, but took no pleasure in looking at the
passing people nor in gathering the fir
apples. I went off some distance from my
mother and sat down and cried bitterly. I
closed my eyes to everything around me,
but all at once spoke these words as plainly
as ever words were spoken: “When dan-
gers thick around you stand, and you are
beset on every hand, the Lord will be
your friend, in Him put all your trust.”
From that moment I dried my tears and
forgot all my troubles; from that time I
tried to pray for myself. I had been
taught a prayer, but that no longer satisfied
me; for I had been taught that God was a

[Column 2]
being without body, parts or passions, and I
did not know what to do. For that reason
went from one sect to another. I could
not accept the Calvinistic doctrine of elec-
tion, and I knew that my parents did not
believe it, At last I went to a Methodist
Sunday School. I then thought their re-
ligion was more liberal than any other.
When I was thirteen years old I dreamed
one night that I was standing on a hill
looking toward the west, and I saw the
world on fire, and as I stood looking I saw 
a personage coming toward me. I stood
still until it came to me and then it said:

	No ill shall enter where you dwell,
		And if the plague come nigh,
	’Twill sweep the wicked down to hell,
		And bear the Saints on high.

	This seemed strange to me, for at this
time I had not heard anything of the Church
of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints.
	I never had the privilege of going to
school to learn to read or write, but I did
go to sewing school until I was fourteen
years old then I started to do fine needle work
for a lady by the name of Pym. She sup-
ported a school and gave me a chance to go,
but mother could not spare me, so for the
common schooling I had practically none
in my childhood.
	I became very much attached to the
Methodist belief, and wished to become a
member of their church, but my mother
objected. One night I thought I would go
and join a class but that night I took very
sick and could not go. After that I did not
go to their church.
	The lady for whom I had been sewing
asked me to come ard join the choir in
what is called the Church of England. I
still continued going there for
some time to please my mother,
for she did not like the Methodists, and did
not want us to go to their church, but that
seemed hard for me.
	I continued to go to the Church of England
until the fall of 1841. At that time I
chanced to hear Apostle P. P. Pratt and
Elder John Snider. They had come to
England preaching in the interest of the
Nauvoo Temple.
	On the thirteenth of October of that year,
while on my way home from my work in
the Hazel Hall, as that was the name of
the mansion where I was working, I was
taken with a severe pain in my side, and it
was with much difficulty that I reached my
home. I had a mile to walk through a
park covered with trees and shrubs. After
left the park gate it was a lonely road the
rest of the way home. The pain in my
side was so severe I felt that I could never
reach home. From that day I did not set
foot outside the door for about two months,
for I was taken down, with the typhus fever
and was very low for some time.
	One night, as I lay very sick, I dreamed
that my spirit left my body, I saw my body
very plainly. Then I went and watched
the sexton dig my grave and then returned
to the house where my body lay in the cof-
fin, and watched the men take it on their
shoulders. I thought I walked backward
all the way to the cemetery, for I was afraid
they would not carry my body straight. I
thought I walked above the ground, and
knew everybody just as I had known them
in life. From that time I thought that my
time had about come to leave this earth; but
had no fear of death, as I realized that all
must die sooner or later. I told my poor
mother and she felt very bad for some time.

[Column 3]
After several weeks the doctor said the
danger point had passed, and things seemed
a little brighter. No one but my mother
was allowed to see me for weeks. The
lady I had sewed for used to send every
evening to inquire about my condition, and
one time she sent me a great treat, that
was a quart of pure new milk. In England
pure new milk was hard to buy.
	In September, 1841, my sister, Ann
Smith, and her husband joined the Mor-
mon Church, and on Christmas day had
decided to give a party to the Latter-day
Saints and Elders together. They were
in good circumstances and things were
arranged. On Christmas day my brother-
in-law came over to our house with a
horse and cart, and plenty of wraps
to take me to their home. Mother was
afraid for me to go, for it was about five
miles, but the weather was unusually fine,
and finally mother consented, or I should
not have gone. They wrapped me up in
quilts and carried me to the cart. I was
very thankful to once more have the privi-
lege of getting out in the fresh air, and to
see something new, and I almost forgot my
sickness.
		(To be continued.)


OBITUARY.

To the memory of Sister Violet Ivie, who de- 
	parted this life. May 25, 1900.

	Whereas, in the dispensation of Providence God
has seen fit to call home one of his daughters who
has labored long in the Relief Society cause, not
alone of Richfield, but having been for fifteen
years secretary and afterwards president of the
Vermillion ward, until her removal to Richfield,
she having been a faithful member of our Society
and also leader of our singing.
	Whereas, she proved herself a hard worker and
a faithful member, ever ready to do good, and she
was one whose kindness and generosity of spirit
brought sunshine into many hearts, helped the
needy in time of want and was in herself a fitting
embodiment of her constant prayers that she might
be humble and united with all that is good; there-
fore be it
	Resolved, That we her sisters of the Relief
Society of Richfield do deeply feel her loss, never-
theless in this our loss we feel to bow to the man-
dates of an Alwise Creator who doeth all things
well.
ANOTHER PIONEER GONE.
	Sister Thirza Melvina Thurber departed from
this life May 9, 1900, after a severe illness of about
one month’s duration. Sister Thirza was born
December 23, 1836, at Dresden, Wheaton Co.,
Tennessee. She was the daughter of Jesse and
Amelia Berry. She came to Utah in 1849 and
married Albert K. Thurber February 26, 1851.
After their marriage they resided at Cottonwood
about one year when they moved to Spanish Fork
and lived there until the fall of 1874, when they
were called to Richfield, where they have since
resided. She was the mother of eleven children,
eight of whom survive her. All were present at
the funeral except A. D. Thurber, who resides in
Mexico.
	At the organization of the Stake presidency of
the Relief Society of Sevier. May 25, 1879, she
was chosen as first counselor to Sister E B. Bean,
which position she filled with untiring zeal, ever
ready to do what was required of her in that posi-
tion for nearly twenty years. She was also a faith-
ful worker in the Richfield ward society; therefore
be it
	Resolved, That in her death we, the members
of the Richfield Relief Society, recognize the loss
of a faithful coworker.
	Resolved, That we extend our sympathy to the
bereaved children who have been deprived of a
devoted, faithful and tender mother.
	Resolved, That a copy of these resolutions be
published in the Woman’s Exponent, a copy be
placed on the record of our Society and a copy be
presented to the family.
						HENRIETTA WRIGHT,
						HANNAH M. OGDEN.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.

R. S. REPORTS.
MARICOPA STAKE.
	Report of the Relief Society conference of
the Maricopa Stake, held June 14, 1900,
opened with the usual exercises. Present
of the brethren were Stake President Collins
R. Hakes and Counselor H. C. Rogers, also
Ward Sunday School Superintendent George
T. Wilson. Four Stake officers of the Relief
Society were present and six ward officers.
Four out of the six wards were reported
favorably, though there had been much
sickness and some deaths in the Lamanite
ward since last conference. The sisters of
the Relief Society of that ward have had
work to perform in taking care of the sick
and laying out and dressing the dead, but
by being humble they are blessed.
	President Mabel A. Hakes had been to
Salt Lake to General Conference to repre-
sent our Stake since our last conference,
and was glad she could do so favorably.
The subjects spoken upon were storing
grain, or as we have to do, canning it, on
account of so many insects in this country,
following after the fashions of the world,
unity, charity and example, etc., all tend-
ing to strengthen and encourage the sisters
in well-doing, making this one of the best
conferences we have ever had.
			ANNA E. FULLER, Sec.
CASSIA STAKE.
	Minutes of the conference of the Relief
Society of Cassia Stake, convened at Elba,
June 22, 1900, President Louisa Haight
presiding. Present Counselor Ann Wilson,
various ward presidents, also Stake presi-
dent William T. Jack and counselors.
	Conference opened by the choir singing
“O Lord, our Father, let Thy grace.”
Prayer by Brother Wells Hess.
	President Louisa Haight felt pleased to
meet with the brethren and sisters of Elba,
had a love for the people and was glad to
visit here.
	Minutes of previous conference read and
accepted. Reports were given by Relief
Society presidents of various wards. Re-
ports showing many of the sisters were
energetic and faithful, while others were
dilatory; also that the sisters of Oakley had
a Relief Society house nearly completed.
She loved to labor with her sisters in this
work of love and grandeur.
	Counselor Ann Wilson advised the
mothers to teach their children the com-
mandments of God
	President William T. Jack spoke on the
influence of mothers in the home and with
her family, and the necessity of her attend-
ing to spiritual duties, so she will be fully
prepared for duties and responsibilities of
the home. Sisters should use their in-
fluence for good, both at home and abroad.
	Conference adjourned until 2 p. m. The
choir sang “Prayer is the soul’s sincere
desire.” Prayer by Bishop Thomas Taylor.
Afternoon session. Meeting opened by
the choir singing “Softly beams the sacred
dawning.” Prayer by Brother William F.
Brim. Singing “Our God we raise to
Thee.”
	President John L. Smith spoke on the
duty of teachers, we should go with the
Spirit of God and teach the principles of the
Gospel.
	Sister Rosabell A. Brim exhorted all to
improve every opportunity to learn the
principles of the Gospel.
	President William T. Harper said if we

[Column 2]
learn to do the will of the Lord it will lead
us to do good and be brave in the work.
We should set a good example before our
children and those around us. We can not
do too much good to lead mankind aright
and teach them aright and teach them the
Gospel.
	Sister King said it is for mothers to lay a
good foundation in their children, and in-
stil into their minds that which is right.
	President Louisa Haight spoke regarding
each society celebrating the 17th of March,
and each member giving ten cents and the
same be forwarded to the Central Board,
then we may have some of the presiding
sisters visit us. Spoke of having aids to
the Stake presidency. As aids Sister King,
of Almo, and Sister Cynthia Ward, of Elba,
were chosen and unanimously sustained.
	General and Stake authorities were pre-
sented and sustained by the conference.
	Conference adjourned for three months.
Congregation sang “The Spirit of God.”
Benediction was pronounced by Bishop
Thomas King.
		LUCYNTHIA ROBBINS, Sec.
BEAR LAKE STAKE.
	Minutes of the Relief Society conference
of Bear Lake Stake, held in St. Charles,
July 1, 1900.
	Our officers meeting was held in the
school house, commencing at 10 a. m.
President Julia P. Lindsay called the meet-
ing to order. Of the Stake officers present
there were Counselor L. M. Hart, Mary
Bagley, and Amelia Hoge, Stake aid.
	Hymn. Prayer by Bishop Kutch. Hymn.
Roll called, eleven Officers of the several
branches present. Minutes of the previous
conference lead and approved.
	The following branches of the society
were reported: St. Charles, president First
ward; Fish Haven, president Second, ward;
Garden City, Ovid, North Liberty, Bloom-
ington, Liberty, Bennington, Montpelier
and Wardboro.
	President Lindsay congratulated the
presidents on the success each branch was
making. Desired to impress upon the sisters
the necessity of taking proper care of their
grain. This mission is no less important
now than when given to us by President
Young many years ago.
	Remarks were made by President James
H. Hart, Counselor L. M. Hart and Sister
Amelia Hoge. Subjects of importance were
touched upon by the speakers. Members
of the Relief Society were encouraged to
continue in the noble work. The teachers
should be regular in their visits that the
sisters may be strengthened and encouraged
to labor in the accomplishment of the noble
purpose for which the Relief Society was
organized.
	Several questions were asked which were
answered by Stake officers to the satisfac-
tion of the inquirers.
	After singing, benediction was offered by
Elder Quinton.
	Afternoon session of conference, was held
in the meeting house, commencing at 2 p.
m., meeting called to order by President
Lindsay. Singing by the choir. Prayer
offered by Bishop Philemon Lindsay. Sing-
ing, “O, my Father.” Sacrament admin-
istered. While the bread and water was
being partaken of, the choir sang “Ye chil-
dren of our God.”
	President Lindsay said she had much
pleasure in meeting in conference, had
never yet performed a duty without receiv-

[Column 3]
ing blessings. Spoke at some length on
the great blessings we enjoy in this goodly
land, and of the promises made he sisters
by the Prophet Joseph, if they would bs
faithful. It is no light matter to neglect
our meetings after having our names en-
rolled, for we then covenant to uphold that
organization. We should economize our
time, that we may devote a portion of it to
spiritual culture..
	Counselor Mary Bagley expressed her
gratitude for being permitted to live in this
important day. Testified of the goodness
of God unto her, of being healed by His
power and of the many blessings received
through being a member of the Relief
Society.
	Elder Alfred Osmond esteemed it a great
honor to address the sisters on this occasion.
It requires constant effort for us to perform
the duties required of us. Much has
been accomplished through the Gospel; had
it not been for this, probably many of those
intelligent men of olden times, such as Paul
and others, would never have been known.
Spoke at some length on the advancement
woman is making, she has been and is to-
day by many looked upon as inferior to
man. Woman can accomplish a great
amount of good in spheres where man can
do but little, she has a mission here on earth
which no one else can fill.
	President James H. Hart said those who
commenced this great work in which we
are engaged had a hard road to travel.
Spoke of the necessity of our striving to
overcome our evil passions and subjecting
ourselves to the will of God.
	President N. E. Pregmire, of the Y. L.
M. I. A., spoke a short time. Felt proud
of the Gospel, more so than of anything
else. There is a great responsibility resting
upon the mothers, greater than many
realize.
	President Lindsay made a few closing re-
marks, the choir sang “Thy God
Reigneth” and benediction was pronounced
by Elder John Bagley.
		NANCY H ALLRED, Stake Sec. 
ST. JOSEPH STAKE.
	The semi-annual conference of the Relief
Society of the St. Joseph Stake was held at
Pima, Arizona, June 22, 1900, commencing
at 10 a. m., President Elizabeth Layton
presiding. After singing by the congre-
gation and prayer by Bishop James Cluff,
of Central, the officers’ roll was called,
showing an attendance of seventeen.
Minutes of the conference held December
17, 1899, read and approved.
	President Layton made opening remarks
in which she expressed her appreciation of
the attendance of brethren and sisters; urged
the presidents to see to it that their secre-
taries were alive to their duty, and por-
trayed the importance of each report being
correct.
	Verbal reports were heard from the Lay-
ton and Thatcher branches, showing that
the members were alive to their duties,
were storing grain and sending missionaries
monthly to the Y. L. and Primary Associa- 
tions.
	Stake Aid Elizabeth Moody felt that we
were greatly blessed by having our grain
and fruit farms matured. Spoke to the sis-
ters about drying and canning fruit and
making it secure from moth that it might
be kept for a time of scarcity. The prophets
have declared that famine would come and
their words will not fail. If we live pure,


[Column 1]
holy lives we will be preserved when the
judgments of the Lord are poured out upon 
the wicked.
	The Central ward was reported as having
missed some meetings during the last
quarter on account of the president and
several of the members attending Dr. 
Shipp’s class in obstetrics, but aside from
that they were progressing nicely, although
but few in number,
	The Graham society had had some little
misfortune with their wheat, but had tried
to make the best of it; had adopted the plan
of lecturing in their testimony meetings, 
and find it very good.
	Bishop John Taylor, of Pima, testified 
that water was scarcer than it had ever
been before in this valley, that some of the
grain had not been irrigated for three
months before harvest, yet notwithstanding
this fact we had an average good crop, in
many instances better than when water was
plentiful; he felt to give God the praise.
Urged the sisters to be diligent in paying
their tithing, and asked the blessing of the
Lord upon them.
	The Bryce and Pima branches were re-
ported by their respective presidents as
being in a thriving condition.
	Stake Aid Mary Taylor realized that
through the Relief Society a grest deal of
suffering was averted. We should prepare
ourselves for the events that await us. Said
it is not our mission to tear each other
down, but assist in building up.
	President William Johnson thought the
sisters have as great a labor to perform as
do the brethren, therefore it is just as neces-
sary that the sisters be diligent and faithful
in the performance of their duties as [--]ose
who hold the priesthood. Said the Relief
Society is the greatest aid that a Bishop can
have It is no time to slacken our efforts.
The signs of the times indicate the coming
of the Savior, therefore let us listen to and
obey the commandments of the Lord.
	Conference adjourned until 2 p. m.
Singing, “Know this, that every soul is
free.” Prayer by James McBride, senior.
	At 2 p. m. conference met according to
adjournment. Singing “Our God we raise
to Thee.” Prayer by Bishop John Taylor.
Singing, “Now let us rejoice.”
	President Olive Larson, of Mathewsville,
reported their society as flourishing.
	Stake Counselor Adelia Curtis advised
the sisters to study the articles of organiz-
ation. Be generous and charitable towards
each other and perform our every duty.
	The Eden society was reported as doing
well considering their scattered condition
and few members.
	Statistical and financial reports were read
by the secretary, Salina Phillips, who also
reported her visit to the last annual confer- 
ence in Salt Lake.
	Treasurer Maggie Allred read the
treasurer’s report for the six mouths past
and bore testimony to the divinity of the
work.
	Bishop Cluff said the Relief Society is
doing a great work. Asked the spirit of

[Column 2]
God to rest on all, that we might realize
the responsibility resting upon us.
	Rhoda Foster bore her testimony to the
goodness of God to her, said if we are liv-
ing in the line of our duty we can judge
between right and wrong.
	Stake President of the Y. L. M. I. A.
Laura Nuttall wished to be obedient to
every call. The duties of Relief Society
members are many and varied; the success
of the society depends on the members.
	President Layton was gratified with the
reports of the different branches, also the
remarks of the previous speakers. Urged
the sisters to subscribe for the EXPONENT,
that they might keep pace with similar
organizations. Spoke of the annual dona-
tion, requested the secretaries to record their
reports and not trust alone to preserving
the blanks.
	Congregation sang “Lord dismiss us”
and benediction was pronounced by
William Johnson.
			SARAH WEBB, Sec.

PREAMBLE AND RESOLUTION.
	The grand educational work which
Alexis E. Frye is doing in Cuba calls for
the hearty endorsement of all patriotic
Americans, and in view of the fact that
Mr. Frye brings to this country 1,450 Cuban
teachers to receive instruction at Harvard
University during the summer of 1900, and
is planning to visit some of our principal
cities as an object lesson to demonstrate
what has been accomplished in a free re-
public, and for the purpose of revealing to
these teachers our own people in their
homes and in their daily life, it is there-
fore,
	Resolved, That the General Society 
Daughters of the Revolution, in conven-
tion assembled, recognizing the great value
of the work which Mr. Alexis S. Frye has
undertaken in such a patriotic spirit, de-
sires to place on record its deep appreci-
ation of such service and the true and
noble aims it represents; and it is further
	Resolved, that a copy of this resolution
be engrossed and transmitted to Mr. Frye,
after being duly signed by the officers of
the General Society.
	In presenting the resolution Miss Hunt
said:
	This movement of Mr. Frye’s has a
deeper and broader significance than the
educational so far as what Harvard can
and will do. It means that our own peo-
ple will have a deeper appreciation of the
patriotism of the Fathers of the Republic;
that the sacredness of liberty will receive
renewed recognition; that the blessings of
a free government “by the people” will
become apparent in greater degree, and as
the sacred pledge of our country is to be
kept, let us endeavor, as descendants of
patriots, to inspire our Cuban friends with
the same spirit which animated our
ancestors.
		GENERAL SOCIETY D. R.
