THE WARRIOR’S DEATH,
“What is his bridge to heaven?” they cried,
	And the warriors held their breath,
As the grizzled king of a hundred fights.
	Went down to the river of death.

“What is his bridge to heaven?” they cried,
	Is it bastioned with buckles and spears,
And girded strong with the iron blades
	Of the battles of bygone years?

“And what are the voices he hears in his dreams?
	Are they the clamors of fight,
Or the echoes of splendid victories that come,
	As he stands by the river at night?”

“Nay, nay,” and they stand by in wonder and
		awe
	For all that he builds on there
Are a withered blossom, a baby’s shoe,
	And a lock of a woman’s hair.

And the only voices he hears in his dreams,
	As the world dies out in his ears,
Are an old love-ballad, a baby’s laugh,
	And the sob of a dead wife’s tears.
					Pearson’s Magazine.


NEWS OF WOMEN.
	MME. PEGARD has jest been named by
the French government chevalier in the
Legion of Honor. To this able and ener-
getic woman is due the woman’s palace at
the Exposition and on the occasion of the
inauguration of this building the decoration
has been conferred. Among thousands of
men, this makes but the forty-seventh
feminine member raised to this grade since
the order was instituted a hundred years
ago. Nearly all the appointments are
recent, and have been made under the re-
public.
	HOUSEHOLD ECONOMICS was so well re-
ceived at the International Congress of 
Women at the Paris Exposition, that Mrs.
L. H. Larned, of Syracuse, president of the
National Household Economic Association,
who presented the subject, has been in-
vited to visit Germany, Holland, England
and Scotland, to tell women in these
countries more about it. Mrs. Larned has
accepted the invitation and is now travel-
ing through these countries sowing, as she
journeys, household economic seed. Her
report at the autumn annual meeting of the
association will be of special interest.
Plans for this meeting, which will be held
in Toronto, October 2, 3 and 4; are being
completed. The invitation to convene at
that place was extended by the mayor and
council of the city, and the Household
Economic Association of Canada.


[Column 1]
DISCERNMENT.
	John said in one of his epistles, “Beloved
believe not every spirit, but try the spirits
whether they are of God.” In this same
epistle of the twelve disciples we read much
of love and is not the world to be redeemed
by love? How could our missionaries en-
dure? How could they be successful if it
were not for the love of the souls they go to
redeem?
	When I belonged to a certain faction of
Mormons, I heard many sermons on author-
ity. I used to get tired, it seemed like a
sing-song, almost, to me sometimes, and
though I could not have gone back to
Methodism any more than I could have
been contented after using the gas light, to
read by the light of a candle, yet some-
times I used to think it would be a change
to hear a sermon on the love and example
of Christ. The spirit within me did not
seem to testify as to the truth of that part
of the doctrine. I received that faction
just as I would a higher class of sectarians;
a class who possessed a greater light be-
stowed upon them by our beloved
prophet, Joseph Smith. I have asked the
question sometimes, “Was it not because
they themselves had usurped the authority?”
	Sometimes when I would think of it, it
would remind me of what a sister who be-
longed to this faction told me. Her father
had been a coal miner. They were good
people, noble souls, but had been taught
wrong principles. She told me that her
father was a very high tempered man and
had always insisted on convincing his fam-
ly that he was the head of his house, and
the method he used was by knocking them
down with his fist whenever they differed
with him. Many of the people of this work
possess in a measure the discernment of
spirits. They judge us by our works they
judge the tree by its fruits, and did not
Jesus say, so we read in John 13th chapter
35th verse: “By this shall all men know
that ye are my disciples, if ye love one to
another.”
	I have heard some people say of certain
persons, “such a one is such a gentle spirit,
it seems impossible for him to do wrong.”
And it will be impossible for him while he
keeps the love of God within him. We
know by experience that certain spirits
follow certain spirits to try to overcome
them. A man or woman who speaks out
all he or she feels, regardless of how he
or she hurts or wounds, is far happier, I
think, than the one who treasures hard
feelings for want of courage or the desire
to make things right. Before we sought
after a reconciliation between us and our
heavenly Father; before we sought a change
of heart, if any one wounded us, though
we did not treasure malice, yet we pre-
ferred those who did would take a different
course, we preferred to have nothing more
to do with them, but after enjoying the
love of God we feel that we should en-
courage a forgiving spirit, for “to err is
human, to forgive divine.” If a gentle spirit
is converted and has not experienced a
change of heart, it is possible if hardness of
heart possesses them that if they do not get

[Column 2]
the spirit of forgiveness, it is possible that
hatred and revenge may overcome them.
Hatred if nursed may bring out imagina-
tions that look something worse than vipers.
it is not heaven to live with a person who
is not willing to make things right by
improving their faults or getting rid of their
wrong feelings.
	How necessary it is that we possess the
love of God in our souls. “Do not let the
sun go down upon your wrath,” we read
in Eph., 4th chapter and 26th verse. Let
love reign in your hearts. Mothers, do
not let the spirit of revenge nestle in your
bosoms, if you do your child may bear the
marks of that weakness. Let the words of
our Savior ring in our ears and live in our
bosoms, “Little children love one another.”
				LITTLE SISTER.


AUTOBIOGRAPHY OF SARAH B. LAYTON.
			[Continued.]
	It was about noon when we arrived at
their home. Some of the guests had arrived
when we got there. Soon a gentleman
came into the room where I was sitting. He
looked at me and then turned to my sister
who was just coming in and said, “Is this
your sister?” to which she answered “Yes.”
“Then,” he said, “We shall very soon
have her in our ranks. I can see the blood
of Joseph rushing through her veins.”
	I was filled with anger, and in a moment
I said, “I know you never will.” He was
a stranger to me; in fact they all were. I
set my mind firmly against them and all
they could say. They were very kind to
me; some of them tried to tell me about the
Gospel; some bore a faithful testimony;
they sang beautiful hymns, some I had
learned from the Wesleyan hymn book. But
the more they tried to attract my attention
the harder I felt towards them. After the
close of the party they asked me how I
had enjoyed myself, and I told them it had
been the most unhappy day of my life.
Some of them said they would pray for me,
but I told them I did not want any of their
prayers in my behalf, and that they had
better ask the Lord to open their eyes to
see their delusion. I was a mere child,
but I knew what they said was Bible doc-
trine, for I had read that time and time
again. I sincerely believed they were the
false prophets that the Bible said would
come in the last days. I hardened my heart
against all they said and wept to think I
had fallen into such a snare.
	The next morning I asked my sister for
some writing material as I wished to write
to my mother and have her come and bring
me home. The letter was written but my
mother never received it, I do not know
what became of it. I could not walk around
for I was so weak, and I was so sorrowful
that I did not even pray as I used to. I
kept in that mood until the Wednesday fol-
lowing Christmas. My sister and her hus-
band appeared very happy and were very
kind to me.
	After they had retired I thought I would
pray and ask the Lord to show me through
His spirit if I were doing right in en-
couraging such a bad feeling. I had prayed


[Column 1]
many times and asked the Lord to tell
me what I should do, and I had faith He
would do so again. I wanted to serve Him
but did not know how. I loved the
Wesleyans but my mother would not
allow me to join their class, and I always
thought they were the most liberal in their
views of any of the religious sects I knew
about. I said nothing to my sister nor any
one else, but poured out my soul to my
Heavenly Father, with full assurance that
He would answer me. I shall never forget
how very different everything those people
had said looked to me. I was in a room
alone most of the time and I prayed earnest-
ly unto the Lord to show me if I had erred
in my thoughts or actions.
	The next night I shall never forget. I
went to bed and the struggle I had with
the destroyer I shall never forget, neither
can I ever describe; but the angel of the
Lord stood by me all that night, and when
morning came I was very much distressed.
I realized there were two powers: the power
of God and the power of Satan.
	The Elders all left my sister’s place im-
mediately after Christmas. On Friday, the
last day of the year. I prayed sincerely to
the Lord that if the Latter-day Saints were
right He would inspire some one of the
Elders to come and I would be baptized.
From that time I began to think that I
would give everything to be a member of
that Church. I never told even my sister 
about what I had done, nor through what I
had passed. It was an ordeal that I can
never forget, but the Lord heard my prayer.
	New Year’s morning came, and shortly
after I arose. I sat alone in the parlor and 
heard a rap at the door. I listened very
attentively to see who it was. My sister
went to the door, and was very much sur-
prised, for there stood the Elder who one 
week before had said that I would soon be-
long to their ranks. My sister had called
him by name and asked what had brought
him back, but he answered that he did not
know. He then asked if her sister were
still there? She told him that I was, and
asked him to come in and see me. He
came in and sat down and commenced to
talk to me. I was ashamed to talk to him
for the manner I had talked to him one
week before. He looked at me a moment
and then said: “Are you ready to be bap-
tized?” That word spoke volumes to my
soul. I answered “Yes.” He then said,
“I know now why the spirit of the Lord
inspired me to come here.”
	He sat down and taught me many things
concerning the Gospel. That was the first
time I had ever listened to the first principles
of the Gospel. The more I heard, the more
I felt thankful to my Heavenly Father for
sparing my life to hear and obey the truth.
	That night was very cold and foggy, but
I did not fear the cold nor did I think of my
weakness. Two of my friends led me to
the water and the Elder baptized me in the
name of the Lord Jesus Christ. I walked
out of the water a well person, and the joy
I felt that night I shall never forget. My
soul was full of peace and love. I asked
my folks to forgive me for being so mean as
I was but one week before. My sister was
very much pleased when she found out my
intention, for I had not spoken a word to
her about it until after I had told the Elder
I was ready to be baptized. This was
January 1, 1842.
	The next day was Sunday, and we all
fasted until after sundown. I did not have

[Column 2]
anything but my new religion, that seemed
all I needed. I had read the Bible from
beginning to end, and now the Lord opened
my understanding. I saw things so differ-
ent to what they seemed before, I was not
afraid, nor did I care who knew I was a
Mormon.
	I remained with my sister until the 8th,
then I walked home, two weeks and one
day from the time when I was taken away
in bed. I expected to find my mother and
father and friends surprised, and thankful
to see me well once more, but I was the
one to be surprised. When I entered the
house I found some one had conveyed the
news to my mother, that I had joined the
Mormon Church. She treated me very un-
kindly and told me she did not wish to see
me again. She said if I had but died when I
was sick how thankful she would have
been; she cried bitterly I told her that I
would have joined with the Methodists
but she would not allow me to. She said
the Elders were wicked to baptize me with-
out her consent. My answer to this was
that the Lord had consented, and with His
help I would stand firm to the covenants I
had made with Him. From that day to
this I have never had the shadow of a doubt
cross my mind with regard to the principles
of the Gospel.
	I soon found that my friends were few
and far between, but I knew that my
Father in heaven was my friend, I had
proved Him. I had volunteered to stand
by the Church I had joined and defend it,
but my mother’s unkindness caused me to
shed bitter tears. But in spite of all this
my religion grew dearer to me every day.
	When the next Sunday came there was
considerable snow on the ground, but I
told mother I must go to meeting. She
said that if I did go she hoped that I would
not come home alive. I was blessed with a
great deal of will power, so I went. Oh,
how strange it seemed to me! To think
that after the love and kindness that was
shown to me a short time ago, now no one
seemed to care for me. There was no one
to speak a kind word to me, no love, every-
thing looked dark and cold.
	I did not know much about the religion I
had embraced, and there was no one within
five or six miles who could talk to me about
it. But there was consolation with the
Elders and Saints, so to meeting I went.
While there I forgot all my troubles, and
thought only of the exceedingly good time
we were having.
	After meeting I told my sister that I
must go home that night, mother was very
unkind to me, but I should go so as to give
her as little chance to bring anything against
me as possible. So I started and in time
arrived in safety. The first words my
mother said when I came in were’ “I wish
you had died before you came back!”
	I was weary of my journey, for it was
very hard walking, so I went up stairs and
asked the Lord to soften my mother’s heart.
In a short time my mother came and told
me to come down and get something to eat.
Had I done as I felt, I would not have gone
down for I was not used to such treatment.
It did seem hard to think that I had not a
friend now where I once had scores. Not
even one of my old associates would recog-
nize me when we would meet, or if they
did it was to insult or abuse me.
	The next Sunday came, the snow had
melted and left the roads very muddy.
Again I went to meeting, but with the

[Column 3]
same unkind treatment before going and on
returning. But I felt as strong as a lion.
I did not fear anything that might come
along. My health was good and I felt that
I must bear my daily cross. I knew that
God had promised that He would not put
more upon us than He would give us
strength to bear. My only comfort seemed
to be in reading my Bible and pouring out
my soul to my Creator. It seemed that
every time I read new proofs were given me
of the genuienness of the work in which I was
engaged. Though father and mother
and brothers, kindred and friends were
against me, yet in the Bible and humble
prayer I thought I had weapons with which
I could fight the world. I had something
dearer to me than all earthly things com-
bined.
		(To be continued.)


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


A BRAVE WOMAN.
	There is no braver woman whose home is
in the Capital City than Mrs. Peary, the
wife of the arctic explorer. Mrs. Peary left
her Washington home and sailed on the re-
lief ship Windward in the middle of July to
Etah, Greenland, where, according to pre-
vious arrangements, if all went well, she is
to meet her husband some time in August.
	Should she not find him there awaiting
her she may come to the conclusion that he
is not alive, but has perished in his latest
attempt to reach the North Pole, for no
tidings have come back from Greenland of
any recent signs of Lieutenant Peary, and
it is recorded that last winter was one of the
severest ever known in the Arctic circle. 
Mrs. Peary is a woman of interpid bravery
and undying devotion, and she will un-
doubtedly, if the explorer does not meet her
at Etah, spend the winter there in the hope
that some unforeseen circumstance has de-
layed him.
	She is accompanied on the trip by her
daughter, who, it will be remembered, was
born in the Arctic regions seven years ago.
The little girl inherits the fine constitution
of both her father and mother and is strong
and vigorous, and her friends have little
fear for the effects of the climate upon her.
	Even if Lieutenant Peary meets his wife
according to arrangement there will be a
note of sadness in their reunion, for the
husband is yet ignorant of the death of his
little child who was born after his departure
for the North, and who died last summer
after all communication with him had 
closed.


NOTES.
	MISS PERMEAL FRENCH, State Superin-
tendent of Public instruction of Idaho, has
been nominated for a second term on the
Democratic and the Populist tickets. Miss
Jessie Reilly has been nominated for the
office by the Republicans.
	One is apt to fancy that the British Queen
at home would be handsomely dressed, but
such is not the case, for when she is at
home her Majesty is simply attired in a
black dress, not always of silk, and wears a
widow’s cap with small lappets at the back,
her silver hair plainly brushed on either
side of her temples. Upon her fingers are
plain memorial rings, and she invariably
wears a bracelet having the portrait of her
latest grandchild or great grandchild placed
in it as a medallion. The Queen always
has a handkerchief, bordered with lace,
resting in her hands as they lie folded in her
lap, the survial of an ancient fashion.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


A THREAD OF THOUGHT.
Written for the Relief Society Jubilee, March
			17, 1892.

My thoughts flew back like a shuttle,
	To our first known record of time;
And looping that time with the present, 
	I have woven them into rhyme. 

When the Morning Stars together 
	Sang out their joyous praise,
And the new, bright Sun, in splendor,
	Sent forth its cheering rays;
Dispelling the night’s cool vapors, 
	And chasing the clouds away;
That the evening and the morning,  
	Might complete the grand First Day 
 
We were there, with God, our Father,
	And voted “Thy will be done,”
And our Mother, Queen in Heaven, 
	Smiled on us every one,
Smiled on each Eve, each Sarah,
	Rachel, Rebecca and Ruth,
Elizabeth, Mary and Martha,
	Each daughter that stood for truth.

We were all ALL there, my sisters;
	And we loved each other well;
And doubtless selected classmates,
	With whom upon earth to dwell.
And our Father marked the course
	For each and we all accepted, 
Nothing was done by force.

We can think how some of us, gazing
	Down the long, long line of years,
Had sufficient faith and courage
	To enter as volunteers.
While others, afraid of their weakness,
	And by earth’s strange shadows appalled,
Longed to follow, and yet concluded
	To wait till their names were called

And some even then with a shudder,
	Held fast to the Gracious Hand, 
And asked “Shall I have power,
	On those slippery heights to stand?
Can you really trust me, Father,
	And shall I be strong enough, 
To carry those heavy burdens
	O'er places so dark and rough?

And our Father, in loving pity,
	Answered “Your guides shall bring 
Your message to me always,
	And I’ll help you in everything.
Never forget, my daughter,
	That sacred pass word, prayer;
Keep it always within your bosom,
	And whisper it everywhere.”

Well! here we are, my sisters,
	In the classes we came to fill;
Learning our daily lessons,
	Doing our Father’s will.
He will clasp our hands with welcomes,
	When the mystic veil is drawn,
And as conquerers we enter,
	Where we hailed the First Great Dawn.
				L. L. GREENE RICHARDS.


[Column 1]
		A WOMAN MISSIONARY.
	[Mrs. Addison Pratt on the Society Islands, 
1850.]
			(Continued.)
	November 26. The weather is intoler-
ably dry and the fleas are like the plague in
Egypt. Sister Crosby suffers terribly from
their bites poisoning her blood. Yesterday
we made a party at the residence of Brother
C. F. Grouard, for the purpose of showing
his native wife our method or habit of
waiting on company. We told them what
our design was, that we would help them
do the cooking. They were pleased and
prepared a good supper. We furnished
dishes to set the table and instructed them
how to deport themselves. They en-
deavored to imitate us as much as possible,
and we had a merry time. In the evening
the house being fitted up to look attractive,
we had two instruments of music, besides 
the sweet voices of the native women. Mr.
Grouard seemed quite proud that his wife 
had acquitted herself so much to the satis-
faction of her visitors. We feasted on

[Column 2]
melons at the close of the party, the finest
ever raised on the Island.
	November 29. This morning we arose
early and to our joyful surprise heard the
Ravavai was in sight, which we had not
expected for several days. Brother James
Brown was on board, a prisoner by order of
the French government. When the schooner
landed in Tahiti he was in prison living on
bread and water. He was discharged with-
out a trial on condition that he would re-
main a prisoner on the vessel till conveyed
from out the bounds of the French protec-
rate. The Queen of Tubuai sent on board
and brought him on shore. He appeared
in good spirits, and intends going to Rava-
vai, or to some Islands not under the
French flag. The ship brought glorious
news. I could not sleep the first night,
so great was my excitement. Employment
now for several days answering the wel-
come letters.
	The queen is a thinking woman. She
mourns over the ignorance of her people,
and her own also. They are good readers
and could they have books printed in their
own language and adapted to their capa-
bilities, what inspiration it would give
themto acquire knowledge.
	I have been reading the history of Eng-
land in the reign of Queen Elizabeth. I
would admire her character but for the one
foul blot, the execution by her consent of
Mary Queen of Scotts. Oh, how mysteri-
ous the transactions of men appear in their
political and religious superstitious.
	A prominent young woman is sick whom
I have visited. She needs treatment of
some kind, and all the medicine I have is a
bundle of hops and a little sage. I use the
herbs and encourage the sisters to have
faith in their efficacy. I find their faith
effective in many instances. An elderly
woman came to me in the night and wished
me to go and see a sister of hers who was
very sick. I arose from my bed and went
with her. She led me through a dark,
woody place, over an intricate path, but I
feared nothing. I prayed and laid my
hands on the sick woman. I told her she
should be better in the morning, and so it
proved. “According to thy faith be it unto
you.” Consecrated oil, which we brought
with us from home, has been blessed to
their use often, all on account of the faith
they have in it.
	Ellen succeeds admirably in expounding
the scriptures to their understanding, show-
ing her knowledge of the Bible and the
Book of Mormon, far exceeding my highest
anticipations. Frances sometimes appears
discontented, uttering ejuculations like
this: “Oh, that I were in Salt Lake City
today!” I ask: “Would you like to be
there with no father to provide wood aud
other comforts we have here?” By recall-
ing reminiscences of the past she answers
in the negative. I feel sensibly the sacri- 
fice it is to our daughters to be without
good society.
	December 1. The rain is at last begin- 
ning to descend. We hail it as a welcome
messenger. How the streams will rise and
everything be refreshed. The vessel
was all ready yesterday morning for another
sail. Suddenly the wind sprang up from
another quarter, which will detain it an in-
definite time. Mr. Grouard thinks he has
more perplexities than any man on the Is-
land, having charge of the vessel besides
other duties, involves him in seeming diffi-
culties, and some real ones. The native

[Column 3]
sisters trouble him, having little judgment
about their business. He is tired and home
sick, and has reason to be so.
	For the first time the House of Prayer
has been cleaned, the seats scoured with
sand, a new business to the natives. When
completed they seemed delighted at its ap-
pearance. I told them it must be cleaned
every month, that the Lord would be the
better pleased with us for a strict observance
of cleanliness. The appointed time is the
Saturday preceding the sacrament of the
Lord’s supper.
	The 13th day. Profound stillness ex-
cept when I hear the mallets of the old
women pounding bark to make cloth. The
vibration of the beam on which they pound
is like a drum; it breaks the monotony and
is preferable to no noise at all. In Brother
Crosby’s dwelling, where we often resort at
evening, we can hear music: Ellen with the
flutina, Alma Crosby with the violin, and
his father playing the flute. It is far from
being inferior, though not perfect.
	The 18th day. This morning the Ravai
left the harbor with a fair wind for Ravai-
vai. Brother James Brown bade us fare-
well.

	We are anxiously expecting the
Ravai, and with her the children hope to
see their father. Joyful anticipation!
Captain Grouard has had a son born since
he sailed. There was great excitement
among the friends, all interested were in-
vited to be on the spot to welcome the new
comer as soon as it might be presentable.
At such times the woman, as soon as the
birth is over, is taken to the river and im-
mersed; she is never known to take cold.
Some females after giving birth to offspring
go into decline aud never recover. It does
not. however, appear to result from their
common usages, as they generally do well-
Nature does all for them. Happy would it
be for our women if they were allowed to do
more.
	The 24th. Today I cleaned house and
made preparations for Christmas. The
native sisters are preparing food. They
praise my house when they see it clean,
but do not incline to clean their own. I
encourage them by telling them that the
Lord is better pleased when we keep our-
selves neat and clean. That if we do not,
we must not expect angels will visit us,
for they do not take delight in those who
are filthy.
	The 25th. We commenced Christmas
by trimming up the house with green
boughs and flowers. In the centre of the
room a large pillar supports the roof. By
the side of it I placed two small ito trees of
the most vivid green, extending almost to
the roof, the native houses have no lofts.
To these I attached the long yellow tea
leaves of which the women make wreathes
on account of their bright color and pleas-
ant flower. Added to these, the bright,
fresh flowers of the bouraw scattered about
among the branches, forming a pleasing
contrast of tints, green and yellow.
	In the same manner over head and around
the entire room I hung boughs, flowers
and branches of the limetree with the fruit
on. Our most intimate friends prepared
for us a splendid dinner, to which they
were invited, bringing the food to the
mission house. I told them the object was
to celebrate the birthday of our Savior, and
they seemed pleased.
			(To be continued.)

SALT LAKE CITY, UTAH, AUG. 15 & SEP. 1, 1900.


[Column 1]
WAR, FAMINE, DESTRUCTION AND
			DANGER.
	The glad news that came by dispatches
on the 18th of August concerning the lega-
tions at Peking in China must have seemed
almost too good to be true after the pro-
longed strain upon the people caused by the
terrible news which came day after day of
the inhuman atrocities perpetrated.
	To those whose friends or relatives were
thus hemmed in, as it were, from all com-
munication, it must, have been something
akin to a message from the “other world,”
and the relief from anxiety and the strain
upon one’s mind and heart, can never be
described in fitting words. The whole
civilized world felt deeply this terrible out-
rage. What must have been the feelings
of those near and dear? It is impossible to
imagine. If these people some of them
live to tell the tale, vivid pen pictures of
their sufferings will be part of the historical
data of the future.
	That the great gates of the Chinese em-
pire were opened by the allied forces, prin-
cipally, it would seem Japanese and Russian,
is really true; and the whole enlightened
world must assuredly rejoice in such an
event. The Chinese wall has been thought
impregnable for thousands of years, but at
last the inventions of men in instruments of
destruction have been strong and effective
enough to open the gates of Peking to the
world outside. Surely the world does
move, and progress is being made along all
lines, material and scientific. 
	Now that the gates of China are opened
what will be the next move on the part of
those Chinese Boxers, who fancied they
were holding the world at bay? no doubt
those heroic soldiers, with their mighty
Generals and leaders who have done such
extraordinary feats already, are prepared to
do more; those who live, but how many
dead and wounded are to be accounted for,
cannot as yet be determined, and though
news travels quickly now across the sea and
land, yet the soldiers’ wives and families
will suffer unspeakable agonies of suspense
before facts in relation to the dead and
wounded can be made public.
	The Boxers have been made world-wide
notorious, and yet a few mouths ago
thousands of people never had heard the
name, who are now familiar with the re-
cital of their deeds of cruelty, Miss Anna

[Column 2]
M. Benjamin has a fine article in the
Outlook for July 1900, written in Peking
the latter part of May of this year, in which
she speaks of that city as on the “eve of
anarchy.” Miss Benjamin tells us the
Boxers, though only recently heard from,
is in reality a secret society which existed
through the whole Mauchee dynasty. It
was undoubtedly formed by the Manchee
couquerors. It used to be not very long
ago the White Lotus Society, and it has of
late years been termed “The Great Knife or
Sword Society.” Miss Benjamin states in
her article that “because of the Boxers’
continuous murder of innocent Chinese,
they may yet feel the vengeance of the
Chinese law before they carry their
righteousness to any great lengths. She
thinks that the Dowager Empress could at
that time have nipped the whole thing in
the bud with a few vigorous measures.”
	Before this paper goes to press we shall
undoubtedly be informed of many soul-
thrilling facts in relation to the breaking
down of the gates of Peking, and the relief
of those immuned within the city’s
walls

	The terrible state of anarchy which seems
to exist in the civilized world at the pres-
ent time is indeed terrifying, though at
present only directed against those in high
places, yet such a spirit of rebellion against
law and order is not to be easily quelled or
confined within given bounds. The old
saying, “Uneasy lies the head that wears a
crown,” is assuredly applicable in this age
of unrest and unholy ambition. Well may
we refer to the prophecies of ancient and
modern prophets, for there seems no safety,
and cry out as we may and do for peace,
“Peace seems to be taken from the earth,
and the avenging sword has gone forth to
slay.” In the footsteps of war comes famine
and the cry has reached the farthermost
corners of the Western world from far-off
India of the terrible suffering there. Ap-
peals for help for the starving children are
almost continuous and would move to pity
a heart of stone.

	In all countries throughout Christendom
it would seem more strict measures are
being taken and more precautions used to
safeguard rulers and men in high places.
Even in the free Republic of the United
States this has been necessary. Surely we
in this age have “fallen upon evil times,”
notwithstanding the enlightenment of the
nineteenth century and the efforts made to
educate the masses and all the various
methods adopted, plans undertaken and
money expended to reach all classes of
citizens and especially the younger gener-
ation. It appears almost incredible in the
face of such far-reaching educational ad-
vantages as now exist that there should be
such misunderstandings, such lack of re-
straint of evil passions. Yet one is obliged
to acknowledge these facts and accept the
situation as there is no other alternative.
	However, it is not well to look on the
dark side or suffer discouragement or failure
to prevent one from attempting to better con-
ditions.
	There are, no doubt, greivous mistakes
in educational systems as there are also in
government; some of these are easily seen,
others, perhaps, are not so clearly under-
stood.
	The importance of training for citizen-
ship is one which demands attention by

[Column 3]
educators as well as parents. It is a fact
that classical education, or scientific or
business course, all these are sought for and
obtained by young men and young women,
all these special lines of usefulness are
taught without the individual getting into
the broader fields and wider range which
prepares one to become a good citizen of a
commonwealth where every man and every
woman is or ought to be a part of the body
politic. There must be a development of
certain qualities and habits of character,
such as courage, discipline and loftiness of
purpose; these are some of the things essen-
tial to maintain free institutions and good
government. One must have the courage
of his convictions, whether moral, political
or religious, in order to exercise his rights
in the face of possible intimidation, other-
wise no amount of learning will save the
man or woman from degenerating or his
faith political or religious from becoming
mere mockery. Responsibility is one of the
factors in bringing out qualities that dis-
tinguish character.
	We are aware that as regards political
education there are various new methods
introduced in the schools and universities of
the present day.
	Of course we are not ignorant of the fact
that Political Economy, so called is taught
in the schools and also sociology and poli-
tics in the class rooms, but there must be
exactitude and ideals; and yet one must not
lose sight of the overwhelming importance
of character.
	Public opinion with many regulates the
public conscience, and the newspapers of
the day have much to do with educating
public sentiment, and as many of them are
sensational it is hardly safe to trust the
average newspaper to regulate the public or
form disinterested public opinion. The
grave responsibility then is thrown back
upon institutions of learning and they must
be such as teach self-government, and a high
moral influence, independent of money
making power, a fixed standard and quality
of permanence. A liberal education in the
best sense, a public spirit, the development
of loyalty, a breadth of character, a per-
manent interest in the profound things of
life.
	If every young man—or nearly so, as it
seems nowadays—who goes into the school
or college is to be a Dr. of Philosophy or
Professor, or possible D. D. or L. L. D., or
graduate with some high sounding title,
making the profession their sole aim and
ambition, where are we to get the men who
are to be the great leaders of men, to serve
the public in this great commonwealth.

	The Executive meeting of the Inter-
national Council in Paris was held in the
American Pavilion. This council repre-
sents eleven nationalities. Mrs. May Wright
Sewall, president, and the Countess of
Aberdeen, vice-president at large, were
present. Lady Aberdeen, for reasons partly
political and partly personal resigned her
position as chairman of the comm ittee on
Peace and Arbitration. This was g[-]eatly
regreted by the ladies of the Council. In-
formal conferences are held every Tuesday
from 4 to 6 o’clock. Mrs. Sewall leads
these conference, speaking in French and
in English alternately.
	In order that the ladies who atttend may
get acquainted tea is served before the con-
ference begins, and small tables are set
around the room and the ladies are intro-
duced to each other by the hostess, Mrs.


Sewall. Many French societies are repre-
sented. Each society seems working for the
Congress that will be held in September.
	Miss Vidart from Switzerland, spoke at
one of these conferences with great power,
taking her own country as an example,
twenty-two cantons, every one of them
independent, had formed a unity through
federation. This is the idea of the National
Council all over the world women agree on
many vital questions. The International
Council can help and strengthen each of
these National Councils and societies in
many ways.
	Mr. W. T. Stead, in a speech at one of
these meetings blamed his own country
very severely. He told the ladies what the
Boer war had cost the country in money
and men, but added he could not make out
what it had cost the country in sorrow for
the dead and anxiety for the living.  He
closed by encouraging the International
Council to work for universal peace and
arbitration.

	Better news from China has been coming
of late and America’s share in the relief
work seems to have been done in a way to
do credit to the country. Secretary Hay is
commended for his timely and wise advice
in matters pertaining to the United States
government, and this republic is the one
least under suspicion of having ulterior
motives. Altogether affairs seem to look
more favorable than could have been ex-
pected so soon after the terrible outbreak.
	However there must still be great un-
certainty where so little can really be
known and so many countries involved.
One must not expect too much, even though
some formidable barriers have been removed
that hindered communication between
China and other great powers. It is de-
voutly to be hoped that a better condition
of things may be developed ere long, and
that the “Treaties with foreign powers in-
stead of war, and every avenue be opened
for the betterment of mankind the world
over.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


A WORD TO WOMEN.
	As the time of political Primaries and
Conventions has come, considerable interest
is awakened in regard to the questions of
selecting candidates to fill the several
offices of those whose term expires soon
after the November election, and also to
name members of the next Legislature to
be voted for. In all these matters it is
most desirable that everything that is done
should be with a view to the best welfare
and prosperity of this new State. Women
here have the same responsibility resting
upon them as men, although they may not
perhaps take as prominent a part, yet they
should not forget the obligations they are
under to the commonwealth and should
therefore prove their loyalty to the public
weal by prudent measures and careful
thought in all their political efforts. To
begin with they should go to the local
Primaries and urge good men to go and pre-
vent if possible these meetings being run by
partisan office-seekers. Now that women
have equal political rights they should ap-
preciate the privileges that pertain thereto,
and try by paying attention to the details of 
these public affairs to comprehend what may
be done to better conditions, and tho’ there
are few women who would take any public
position, yet they should see to it that good
men are nominated and if some women


have the ability and courage to secure a
nomination to stand by their own sex and
not shirk the responsibility and advantages
granted by the men of Utah in the memo-
rable convention in 1895.
	Very few women so far have sought any
place of trust but there ought to be more
than one or two women in the Legislature in
both houses; and there are women perfectly
well qualified for educational positions.
There are women, too, who are abundantly
able to assist in making the laws that con-
trol the lives and property of their families
and the public at large, and who are in all
respects equals in education of the men who
are elected to do this public duty.
	The matter of registration is one of the
most important things and there should be
no carelessness on the part of any citizen in
this regard; having rights dare to maintain
them, though the duty may sometimes
seem an unpleasant one. Remember how
much more highly favored we are in this
intermountain country than the women of
other parts of the United States. There
will be no house to house canvass but it is a
duty every voter owes to himself or herself,
and to the public weal, to see his name
properly registered. Every individual who
has the good of society and of the com-
munity at heart should do his utmost to
help in this matter, use every effort possible,
as many may not feel the importance at-
tached to registration as those do who have
had better opportunities of knowing what
public duties and obligations mean. The
need of being careful about registration can-
not be too strongly emphasized, especially
as there are only certain days provided by
law in which the work must be done.
These are October 9, 10, 16 and 30.


[Column 1]
IN MEMORIAM.
	We are called to chronicle the death of
two of our faithful sisters in the Relief
Society of Monroe, who have borne the
burden of the day and proved faithfnl to the
end.
	Sister Lizzie Calkin Smith was born in
Gloucestershire, England, July 1, 1821.
She heard the Gospel while a young woman
and was baptized in 1848. She had much to
battle against; she left father, mother,
brothers and sisters and emigrated in 1852,
walking across the plains the entire distance,
except ninety miles, and then was induced
by one of the company to ride, as her health
was very poor; but she felt happy in doing
so, as she had promised the Lord if He
would open the way for her to come to Zion
she would willingly walk all the way.
	She reached Salt Lake City in September,
1852. On the 6th of November, the same
year she was married to Brother Asa
Calkin. They resided in Salt Lake until
1861, when they were called to help in set-
tling Dixie, where they remained during
the trials and hardships incident to settling
a new country. In February 1873 while
visiting friends in Salt Lake City, she re-
ceived the sad news of her husband’s death
but was unable to reach home before he
was buried. She worked faithfully for
three years in the St. George Temple.
	In 1880 she was married to Bishop E. N.
Smith, of Rockville, who had been left a
widower with seven children. She soon
won the love and respect of the children
and strove to fill the place of mother as near
as it could be filled.
	When the Primary Association was or-

[Column 2]
ganized she was selected president, by
Sister E. R. Snow, and also president of
the Relief Society of the same ward, which
position she faithfully filled until the family
moved to Monroe, November, 1891. In
this place she has been connected with the
Relief Society since her arrival. She was
treasurer of the same for four years and
filled that position with honor.
	In 1898 her husband died and since that
time old age has made its demand. Aunt
Lizzie has lived a useful and goodly life,
and will receive the reward of the faithful.
She died August 6, 1900.
	Her motherly counsel and words of en-
couragement will be remembered by all who
were acquainted with her.

	Just one week later, August 13, our dear
sister Flora Clarinda Gleason Washburn,
died at the ripe old age of eighty-one years
and twelve days, being tenderly nursed and
cared for by her children. She leaves three
sons and four daughters to mourn her loss,
and a great many grandchildren and great-
grandchildren.
	Flora Clarinda Gleason Washburn was
born August 2, 1819, at Tolland, Berkshire
Co., Mass. Her parents were Joel and
Lorena Gleason. They removed to
Lenox, Ashtabula Co., Ohio, in the year
1824. Her mother died sixteen days after
reaching Ohio. Clarinda joined the Church
in April, 1834, the only one of her father’s
family that ever embraced the Gospel. She
received her endowments in Nauvoo and
was called at a meeting in Nauvoo to go to
Macedonia, Ill., and organize a Relief
Society, which she presided over until she
was called to go and live at the Mansion
house, the home of the Prophet Joseph
Smith. After the death of the Prophet with
the family of B. F. Johnson, she started
with the first company of Saints in search of
a new home, after having lost everything
she owned of an earthly nature, except the
clothes she had on her body, through the
mobbings and persecutions of the wicked.
	She remained at Winter Quarters a little
over a year and a half, and started for the
valley on the 3rd of July. She harnessed,
fed and drove her own team part of the
way, and arrived in Salt Lake City October
18, 1848. Afterwards she married Abra-
ham Washburn in February, 1849. She
removed from Salt Lake City to Sanpete
Co. the following November, arrived there
November 21, and on the 22nd gave birth to
the first white child born in Sanpete Co.
	She shared in all the hardships incident
to settling and building up a new country.
She was appointed a teacher in the first
Relief Society of Manti, afterwards was
made president, which office she held until
she moved with her family to Monroe,
Sevier Co., Utah, in March, 1872. In
November, 1872 she was appointed presi-
dent of the Relief Society of Monroe, which
position she held until Feburary 6, 1897,
when she was honorably released on account
of her age and poor health, having won
many a laurel to adorn her brow in the
eternal world.
	Not only as president has she been faith-
ful, but whatever her willing hands have
found to do she has done with a will.
	She was buried on the 15th of August,
the brethren and sisters bearing testimony
to the many and faithful labors performed
by our dear sister.
		RELIEF SOCIETY MONROE.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
LADIES’ SEMI-MONTHLY MEETING. 
	The Ladies’ Semi-Monthly Meeting
opened at 2 o’clock p. m., President M. I. 
Horne presiding. Singing, “Come, come, ye
Saints. Prayer by Sister Mary Silver, Sing-
ing, “Welcome best of all good meetings.”
	President Horne said Sister Woolley was 
away visiting in the country and we had
no minutes to read. Asked Sister L. L. 
Greene Richards to act as secretary for the 
meeting. Told of a kindly offering for the 
poor made by the Davis Shoe Co. They
had given her twenty-four pairs of shoes to 
be distributed and she would hand them 
over to the sisters for the poor of their wards
at the close of the meeting. She then asked
the sisters to occupy the time. 
	Counselor Clara C. Cannon bore testi-
mony to the truth of the Gospel, and said
she felt that it would be a terrible thing to 
lose that testimony. Related some of her
experiences and prayed that we might so
live as never to have our testimonies dimin-
ished, but that they might grow brighter. 
	Sister Julia C. Howe followed in the
same line the previous speaker had taken
up. Gave as a key by which to know if 
we are really enjoying the testimony of the 
spirit of God, “If we love our brethren and
sisters and all our Father’s children.” By
this we can know that the love of Christ is
abiding with us. 
	A sister in the congregation bore a faith-
ful testimony to the truth of the Gospel. 
	Sister M. Silver spoke of the blessings
which certainly follow the paying of tithing
as well as obedience to all the command-
ments of God. 
	Counselor E. J. Stevenson exhorted the
sisters to look after the suffering ones; be 
missionaries at home and always on the 
alert to do good. We must see to it that
no hard feelings exist in our hearts toward
any one. Speak no evil; set proper ex-
amples before our children always. “The
rain falls on the just and the unjust,” but
the spirit of the Lord will not dwell in un-
holy temples. We must be pure. 
	Sister Barnes spoke of the importance of 
our Temple work, also of looking after the
newly arrived Saints, not leaving them to 
feel lonely, but showing our love for our
Father’s children by taking an interest in 
their welfare and being kind to them.
	Sister Perry said truly she had
wondered why she should be the only one
of her father’s family to accept the Gospel.
But she had found it was that she might
labor in the Temple for her dead kindred.
	Sister Reed told of her having been an 
invalid for years, yet how the Lord was 
blessing her now, renewing her strength to 
the surprise of her friends.
	Counselor Sarah J. Cannon spoke of the
progress the sisters were making and of the 
necessity of the younger sisters being initi-
ated into the Relief Society work, that they
may be prepared when the burden of the 
business, which is now borne principally by
the older ones, shall rest upon them. Ex-
horted all to diligence and faithfulness in 
dispensing love and cheerfulness among all
with whom we come in contact, bestowing
wisely, not wastefully, to those whom we 
should comfort and succor. 
	Sister Elizabeth Y. Yates, president of 
the Millard Stake Relief Society, was intro-
duced by President Horne and addressed the
meeting. Spoke of the joy she felt in 
hearing the voices of her sisters She al-
ways looked for the reports of these meet-
ings in the EXPONENT and enjoyed read-

[Column 2]
ing them. Told of a visit of a sister [---]
Stake, who had been backward in [---]
testimony. She dreamed she died an[---]as
conducted to a most beautiful man[---]on. 
There a testimony meeting was being held
by some of the Saints. The sister’s heart
was so filled with joy and gratitude that she 
felt she must get up and bear her testimony.
But she was not permitted to speak there
because she had not been valiant in the
testimony of Jesus while upon the earth.
That sister was so impressed by her dream
that she commenced from that time to be 
valiant, and is now a good faithful worker
and a bright speaker in the Relief Society.
	Sister Margaret Gardner and another 
sister, whose name was not assertained by
the secretary, spoke encouragingly and 
prayed for the prosperity of Zion, that the 
mothers would teach their children to pray
as a safeguard against many evils. 
	Sister Martha Morris sang, very sweetly,
“The trials of the present day.” Others
bore testimony of the truth of the Gospel, 
the efficacy of prayer. 
	Sister Smellie, by permission of President
Horne, read the poem, “A Thread
of Thought.”
	Sister Decker told of some of her exper-
iences which had given her a testimony of 
the truth of the Gospel.
	Several others, whose names were not 
known to the sisters on the stand, spoke of 
their love for the work of the Lord, and the 
comfort they found in striving to live their
religion.
	Sister Bassett said she had visited other 
churches, but there was nothing satisfactory
to her in any religion but that believed in 
by the Latter-day Saints. 
	Other sisters made remarks, all harmon-
izing with those of the previous speakers.
	Sister Bateman related an instance of re-
markable healing in her family, through 
faith and the administration of the priest-
hood.
	President M. Isabella Horne in-
structed the sisters regarding the impor-
tance of seeking first the kingdom of God 
and His righteousness, trusting him to ful-
fill His promise that all other things should 
be added. 
		L. L. GREENE RICHARDS.


[Column 1]
R. S. REPORTS.
BOX ELDER STAKE.
	The Relief Society conference of Box
Elder Stake was held in the Tabernacle at
Brigham City, June 12, 1900, President S.
P. Booth presiding. Singing “Awake ye
Saints of God, awake.” Prayer by Counselor
Louisa Peters. Singing, “How great the
joy that promised day.”
	The Stake officers were present on the
stand and of the General Board Jane S.
Richards and Emmeline B. Wells, also
Sarah A. Herrick, of Ogden.
	Minutes of the previous conference,
morning session, were read by the secretary
and accepted.
	President S. P. Booth was pleased to
be able to meet under such favorable cir-
cumstances and wished to give as much
time as possible to our visitors.
	President Jensen, of Mantua, President
Summerville, of Honeyville, President
Annie S. Johnson, of the First Ward,
Brigham City, gave favorable reports of
their respective wards.
	President Jane S. Richards spoke of our
own imperfections, if we were perfect our-

[Column 2]
selves we would have no mercy or charity
for others. The sisters of the Relief Society
are doing their part to help along. Coun-
seled the sisters to be united.
	Sister Sarah A. Herrick, of Ogden, took
great pleasure in listening to the minutes,
good always comes from doing our duty.
	President Hubbard, of Willard, spoke of
the blessings of the Lord that will be given
to all who keep His commandments.
	Meeting adjourned until 2 p. m. Sing-
ing, “Come follow me.” Benediction by
Counselor Amelia Madsen.
	Afternoon session. Singing “Come
dearest Lord descend and dwell.” Prayer
by Counselor David P. Burt. Singing “Ye
simple souls who stray.” Roll called, show-
ing thirteen wards present out of sixteen.
Minutes of the previous conference, after-
noon session, were read by the correspond-
ing secretary and accepted. The Stake re-
ports were also read.
	Counselor David P. Burt then presented
the following names of sisters who had been
selected as officers in the Third ward Re-
lief Society, Brigham City: President,
Lizzie Burt; counselors, Emma Lee and
Frederica Peterson; secretary, Maria
Phillips; assistant secretary, Rose Keeley;
treasurer, Sophia Thompson. Fourth
Ward: President, Sarah Wilde; counselors,
Elizabeth Wright and Martena Pulsipher;
secretary, Lulu B. Call; assistant secretary,
Emily Brown; treasurer, Maria Hansen.
	The officers were all unimously sustained.
Sister E. B. Wells addressed the conference.
Spoke of the growth of the Relief Society,
its great magnitude and made a specialty of
silk culture, encouraging all to do what-
ever they could towards advancing the im-
portant industry; also spoke impressively
on the high calling and privileges of the
women of Zion, the great blessing the Re-
lief Society had been to all who had been
members, and what a superior education
might be acquired through laboring in this
organization for practical charity and the
uplifting of humanity. Urged the young
sisters to take part in the work. Spoke
feelingly of obedience and faithfulness in
attending to our duties.
	Sister Jane S. Richards related her
past experience; we have to be reconciled
to things that take place. Advised the
younger sisters to join the Relief Society.
	President S. P. Boothe made a few
closing remarks, and after singing Doxology
benediction was pronounced by President
Ella Watkins. Conference adjourned until
September.
		OLIVE BYWATER, Cor. Sec.
				Box Elder Stake R. S.
	P. S. After the close of the conference
an officers meeting was held in which many
questions were asked and much good in-
struction was given to those desiring in-
formation.
BEAVER STAKE.
	Conference of the Beaver Stake Relief
Society was held in the Society hall, in
Beaver City, Utah, May 11, 1900, com-
mencing at ten o’clock a. m.
	Present on the stand of the sisters were
Stake President C. C. Shepherd and
Counselors M. A. White; Ward Presidents
J. W. Bickley, Winnie Miller, and Susan
Roberts; Vice-Presidents M. A. Tolton and
Sarah Walker; of the brethren, Bishops
George Mumford, George Eyres and others.
	Meeting opened by singing “I saw a
mighty angel fly.” Prayer by Elder John


[Column 1]
Lang. Singing, “May we who know the
joyful sound.” Roll called, nine officers
present. Minutes of morning session of
previous conference were read and accepted.
	President Shepherd said it does us good
to meet and hear the reports of the differ 
ent branches; also of the growth of these
organizations in the past fifty-eight yeats,
and the many duties devolving upon us—
building, looking after the sick, etc.
	Bishop Mumford spoke of the good spirit
here, also of the necessity of attending our
different conferences. We should not
neglect spiritual for temporal duties. Re-
ferred to the judgments which are abroad
on the face of the earth, and felt that more
of our young sisters should identify them-
selves with the Relief Society.
	Vice-President M. A. Tolton bore testi-
mony to the truth of the Gospel and the
divine mission of Joseph Smith. Spoke of
the necessity of living up to our
many duties.
	Sister Harriet Ashworth sweetly sang
“He Knows.”
	Sister S. M. Dell felt well in this work.
Prayed the Lord to bless us.
	Sister Sarah Walker said the Miners-
ville sisters had built a hall which they de-
sired to finish and dedicate another year.
They had a good set of sisters, alive to
their duties.
	Sister Edward Stevenson, of Salt Lake
City, who had arrived since meeting opened,
was pleased to be with us and feel the good
spirit here. This a grand work organized
by the Prophet Joseph. The first organiz-
ation consisted of comparatively young
women, now our young sisters do not unite
with us as they should. The prophet said
the Church was not fully organized until
the Relief Society was organized, which
was not only to look after the needy, com-
fort the afflicted, etc., but to provoke our
husbands to their duties; never allow our
children to speak lightly against the holy
priesthood and those in authority, brethren
or sisters. Spoke of the sad disaster at
Schofield, also the judgments befalling the
nations. Referred to the sacred duties of
teachers, our annual meeting and ten
cent donations and other obligations. Re-
ferred to the instructions of President
Young to the sisters in regard to building,
storing grain, etc. The young sisters
should come and learn how to train the
precious jewels entrusted to their care.
	Bishop George Eyres made encouraging
and eulogistic remarks.
	Meeting adjourned until 2 p. m. to L. D.
S. meeting house. Singing “Lord dismiss
us.” Benediction by President M. L.
Shepherd.
	Afternoon session opened at 2 o’clock p.
m. by singing “We thank Thee, O God,
for a prophet.” Prayer by Professor W.
G. Bickley. Singing, “How firm a
foundation.” Minutes of afternoon ses-
sion of previous conference read and ap-
proved.
	President J. W. Bickley reported the
Beaver Relief Society in good condition.
Have work meetings every week. Grain
has been in good condition until the present
time, but now mice are working through
the ceiling, but we intend to take im-
mediate steps to stop their depredations.
	President Susan Roberts reported the
Minersville Society in a prosperous con-
dition. The sisters are united and turn out
well both to religious and work meetings.
	President Winnie Miller reported the

[Column 2]
Greenville society prosperous; fair attend-
ance; rock on hand to build a hall.
	Statistical and financial reports were read
and the General and Stake officers of the
Relief Society were presented and sustained.
	A solo was sweetly rendered by Sister
Janet Shipp.
	Stake Counselor M. A. White spoke on
the many duties of mothers and the care
necessary in training our children. The
Relief Society is a great help to mothers.
	Sister Stevenson felt very humble in
standing before us. “Motherhood is a
grand mission. These noble spirits which
we have to bear were held in reserve for
this dispensation.” She spoke of the neces-
sity of a watch care over our sons and
daughters, gaining their confidence, teach-
ing them virtue and the necessity of purity
in matrimonial life, that wives may be
healthy and raise pure offspring; also spoke
on faith, household duties, economy,
cleanliness the word of wisdom and every
good virtue. The sisters should belong to
the Relief Society. It is suggested that
each pay a little towards building a house
for the General Society. Encouraged the
sisters to sustain the Exponent. We
should read good literature and not trashy
novels. Bore a faithful testimony to the
Gospel.
	President C. D. White spoke of the neces-
sity of teaching our children to labor and
lighten the burdens of mothers; also the law
of tithing. Sabbath breaking was spoken
strongly against.
	President Shepherd made closing re-
marks. Primary conference was announced
for the next day at 2 o’clock p. m., and
conference adjourned till the second Friday
in November. Singing, “The spirit of
God.” Benediction by Patriarch D. Tyler.
	ALICE M. T. TANNER, Stake Sec.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


OBITUARY.
	Sister Mary Ann Standring Roberts was born in
Sheffield, England, March 2, 1851. She em-
braced the Gospel in her native land and emi-
grated to Utah with her parents, John and
Elizabeth Cutler, in the year 1864. She was mar-
ried to Edwin Standring, Februrary 28, 1878. She
was the mother of two children, a daughter and a
son. Her son, John Edwin, survives her. She
was left a widow in the year 1888, and in March
1896, became the wife of John Roberts, Jun. She
was appointed assistant secretary of the Relief
Society in 1879, and in 1882 was sustained as
secretary, which position she held until the time of
her death. From the year 1888 she, in connection
with others, had charge of the Primary depart-
ment of the Sunday School. Her death occurred
on July 13, 1900, at Lehi, Utah Co.
RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT.
	Whereas, Our Heavenly Father has called from
our midst our dear sister, Mary Ann Standring
Roberts; and
	Whereas, We, the members of the Relief Society,
have been called to mourn the loss of a faithful
officer, a firm friend and a true Latter-day Saint,
therefore, be it
	Resolved, That We cherish her memory and
endeavor to emulate her noble example.
	Resolved, That we should extend our heartfelt sym-
pathy to the bereaved family, who mourn the loss
of a devoted wife, mother and sister.
	Resolved, That a copy of these resolutions be
presented to the family, published in the WOMAN’S
EXPONENT and also placed on the records of the
Relief Society.
						SARAH T. EVANS,
						HANNAH JONES,
						SABINA CANDLAND,
					(In behalf of the Relief Society.)
	Lehi, Utah.
	It becomes our painful duty to record the death
of our beloved sister, Lavina Haigh Glenn, who
departed this life, July 12, 1900. She was born


August 21, 1853. Sister Glenn embraced the
Gospel in her native land, England, and emi-
grated to Utah in 1869. She was married to
Alexander Glenn in 1870 and was the mother of
fourteen children, three of whom preceded her to
the “better land.” Five boys and six girls are left
to mourn her loss, and a host of friends. She was
a kind and loving wife and mother and died in
full faith of the Gospel. Sister Glenn was ill for
nine months and the sisters of the Relief Society
fasted and prayed for her, and all was done for
her that was possible to relieve her sufferings, The
sisters of the Relief Society of four wards marched
in front of the casket to the meeting house. The
funeral was largely attended and the floral offer-
ings were beautiful. The Relief Society gave a
beautiful broken circle to show their love for her.
	Whereas, Death has come into our midst and
taken from us our honored sister, whom we all
loved, an earnest supporter and teacher of the Re-
lief Society, be it,
	Resolved, By the members of this society, that
we tended the bereaved family our sincere sym-
pathy and earnestly desire that God will comfort
them in their affliction.
	Resolved, That a copy of resolution be sent
to the family, a copy be placed on record and one
forwarded to the WOMAN’S EXPONENT for publica-
tion.
						CECELIA C. KIRK,
						MAHALA JENKINS,
									Committee.

WAYNE STAKE.
	The Relief Society Conference of Wayne
Stake was held at the Fremont Relief
Society house, June 7, 1900.
	Opened by singing “Come listen to a
prophets voice.” Prayer was offered by
Patriarch E. H. Black. Singing, “O, say
what is truth” Minutes of previous
conference read and accepted.
	President Jane S Coleman was pleased
to have so good an attendance of the sisters
and brethren at conference. Prayed that
we ever may be united by the bonds of
love and good feeling. Spoke upon the
duties of the officers and the necessity of-
having our records in proper condition,
that the sisters of the Relief Society should
be exemplary in all things and workers in
the Church and kingdom of God. Spoke
upon the necessities of the poor, that they
should be looked after and not neglected,
for the Prophet Joseph said the poor would
be made to rejoice through this organization,
it would be the beginning of better days for
them.
	Counselor M. E. Hanks said she was
gratified to see so many young sisters and
brethren present. Spoke upon giving
respect to the aged and to parents, and to
teach reverence for the priesthood. Spoke
on raising families and read from Apostle
Woodruff’s discourse on that subject. Spoke
on the care mothers should give their chil-
dren and the good impression they should
make upon their minds in their early youth.
	Counselor Sarah S. Forsythe said we
should daily improve. It is the little
covert acts that creep in and make us weak.
Mothers should pray constantly for wisdom
to raise their children in the proper way.
A wide field of usefulness is open for our
sons and daughters. Mothers need to be
watchful and incline their aspirations in an
elevating channel. Said the mission of
mothers was great and responsible, to train
and raise the children aright.
	Sister Lucinda Brown was thankful she
was a member of the Relief Society, had
never heard better counsel given than had
been taught this morning. Spoke of past
times and of the present. Gave excellent
advice to young mothers pertaining to the
family. Mother is the one to instil into the
mind of the child the qualities that will
make them good and great in life.


Patriarch E. H. Blackburn said he
esteemed it an honor to be called on to
speak at the sisters’ conference. He enjoyed
the excellent spirit that was present with
us. Spoke upon the increase of the testi-
mony of the Gospel. We should honor
our callings, increase in faith, cultivate and
enjoy the gifts of the Gospel. Angels
were present taking record of all the good
that was done and said. Bore a strong
testimony to the truth of the Gospel.
	Singing, “Do what is right.” Benedic-
tion by Elder Allan Taylor.
	Afternoon session. Singing, “An angel
from on high.” Prayer by Elder Ole Oke-
land. Singing. Stake Secretary Anna
Coleman read financial report.
	President J. S. Coleman spoke on the
subject of sericulture. Several sisters in
the Stake had donated liberally to buy
mulberry trees, the result was that five
thousand yearling mulberry trees had been
planted this season. They were reported
growing well with very little loss. Said
she felt encouraged at our success in this
direction.
	The Relief Society of Fremont, Loa,
Thurber, Teasdale and Grover were re-
ported by their respective presidents in
good condition; Lyman and Caineville (by
counselors) doing well, working in unity.
At Giles and Caineville they were feeding
a few silk worms to gain some experience
for a future time.
	The Stake secretary presented the
General Board and Stake officers for accep-
tance; all were sustained.
	Counselor M. E. Hanks made remarks
on tithing, the word of wisdom and to
seek for the spirit of the Lord and keep it.
	Sister Nancy Blackburn was pleased to
hear the subject of raising families spoken
upon, the ways of the world were making
inroads among the people of God. Said she
was the mother of thirteen children. Was
pleased with the good instructions, they
were timely and good. We should live up
to the golden rule. Bore testimony to the
work of God.
	Sisters Anna Coleman and Elizabeth
Jeffrey spoke on living up to the principles
of the Gospel and being chaste in speech,
and not indulge in too much jesting to mar
the spirit of God.
	Sister Leah Blackburn said when we do
our duty the Lord will not withhold His
blessings from us. We should examine our
record daily and know if we are accomplish-
ing some good. Spoke of our privileges as
mothers having sons carrying the glad
tidings of the Gospel to those who know it
not.
	President Coleman said we had been
taught the word of the Lord, take it to our
homes and carry it out in our lives and be
benefited thereby. Live so that the Holy
Spirit will be our constant companion to
direct us at all times, improve every
spare moment by reading good books and
advance in knowledge and intelligence.
	The choir sang “Guide us, O Thou
Great Jehovah.” Benediction by Elder
John Adshead.

MRS. ROOSEVELT.
	Mrs. Roosevelt is a fine horsewoman, and
may be seen almost any pleasant day in a
riding habit of blue cloth and a black
Fedora hat, cantering over roads at Oyster
Bay beside the Governor. She is so slender
and girlish looking no one would suppose
her to be the mother of all those rollicking
boys who rush to the window to throw
kisses to their father and mother as they
start. The New York Tribune says:

	The Roosevelt house is on the crest of a hill
and is surrounded by trees. It has an attractive
square hall, and its walls are covered with souven-
irs of the Colonel's travels.
	A large deer’s head, with spreading antlers,
does duty as a hat rack, upon which hangs the
Colonel’s campaign hat. Above are his regimental
colors. In the dining room are the heads of a
buffalo, a bear and several deer, all brought down
by Colonel Roosevelt’s rifle.

	Mrs. Roosevelt is devoted to husband and
children. Her first care and thought are
for them, and social life is a secondary con-
sideration. She does not go into society
in general, but has her own especial friends,
whom she entertains charmingly.


Another heir to the British throne has
been born and duly baptized with the
Queen as godmother. This is the fourth
child of Princess Louise of Batenburg and
the thirty-seventh great-grandchild of Her
Majesty.

	MRS. ROGER WOLCOTT has been ap-
pointed by Mayor Hart to represent Boston
at the International Congress of Charities in
Paris during the Exposition. She has ac-
cepted an invitation to speak at the
Congress.

	The Colonial Dames of Connecticut will
give $100 to the Committee on Historical
Landmarks towards the erection of a monu-
ment on the site of the former home of the
Rev. Samuel Russell, in Branford, where
the first meeting in the interest of Yale
College was held in 1700.

	Recently several visits have been made to
the Relief Society conferences of the several
Stakes. President Jane S. Richards and
Josephine R. West attended Relief Society
and Primary conference in Malad Stake.
President M. Isabella Horne and Sister
Harriet Ellis, the Relief Society conference
of Millard Stake; Sister E. J. Stevenson and
M. A. Caine the R. S. and Y. L. of Paro-
wan Stake, and Sister M. A. Caine, the
R.S. and P. A. of Panguitch Stake. Presi-
dent M. I. Horne and Sister H. Ellis also
attended a special meeting of the Relief
Society at Park City.
