For the Exponent.
O, BE JOYFUL!

	O, be joyful, O, be joyful,
		Sing ye Saints of Latter-days;
	Giving unto God the Father,
		Adoration, love and praise.

	For the blessings of the Gospel,
		In our day restored to man;
	With its powers, gifts and gladness,
		Revealing clear salvation’s plan. 

	On the mountains ring the tidings,
		In the East the glory breaks,
	Ush'ring in the day of promise,
		When the world from slumber wakes.

	See, the clouds of doubt and error,
		Rolling with the mists away,
	A pathway opened up to heaven,
		Christ descends in white array. 

O, trembling earth, thy King is come,
		Refulgent rays o’er thee shine;
	In thy bosom swells emotion,
		Fires of love, serene, divine.

See the living Saints appearing,
		In their robes of glistening white,
	Crowns are given to the victors,
		who triumphant win the fight. 
						LYDIA D. ALDER.


THE DOMESTIC SCIENCE CLASS.
	One of the necessities of all cities, and
especially Salt Lake, is a School of Domes-
tic Science. An institution where all can
receive proper training to fit them for future
life. This school is not only for girls who
seek employment as domestics, nor for the
nurses, who, I am sorry to say, know
nothing about cooking, but for these young
girls who are growing up and know not
“when the hour cometh” that they may be
called upon to preside in the kitchen.
	While a person may go through life and
never be compelled to cook a meal, if she is
skilled in the art she is better able to know
when it is properly prepared.
	This school of ours that was held in the
Normal school building of the University
was a success and exceeded our most san-
guine expectations. It was started without
a cent of money but with the proverbial
generosity of the Salt Lake people a stove,
dishes, cooking utensils, etc., were donated,
and when our school closed our expenses
were all paid and a surplus to commence
another school.
	Every club in the city was supposed to
have a delegate who would interest herself
in the work, but it fell entirely on the Press


Club, Mrs. Georgianna Young, Mrs. Emma
J. McVicker and Mrs. Luella M. Rhodes.
	We expect to make arrangements to in-
clude it in a special course at the Univer-
sity, to be an elective study, as it were,
until the Legislature meets, when we hope
to secure an appropriation to carry on the
work and make a permanent study. This
work has progressed in London with marked
success, so that now a School of Domestic
Science is made a part of the training of
every girl. Every day between nine and
twelve there were over thirty-three pupils
at their work. The cooking department
was presided over by Miss Emily Cannon, a
graduate of the Boston School of Cooking.
The art of housewifery was taught by
Miss Edna May Davis, a thoroughly com-
petent instructress. In the sewing room
Miss Mary S. Smith, a graduate of the Pratt
Institute, N. Y., taught the girls the differ-
ent stitches, hemming, felling, patching,
darning, etc., and impressed upon them that
“a stitch in time saves nine.” Many of
the girls made articles of wearing apparel
for themselves. Some made handkerchiefs,
and some of the smaller girls made doll
clothes. This only shows what can be done
in just a five weeks’ course.
	At the closing exercises of the school
much encouragement was given the com-
mittee. Many of the parents and friends
expressed themselves as greatly pleased
with the work of the children and pledged
themselves to help support a Domestic
Science School. “As ye sow, so shall ye 
reap.’’
						L. M. R.


[Column 1]
CURRENT EVENTS.
	There has been rather a dearth of news
here of any great importance during the
month of August. What seemed to be
uppermost in the minds of the people was
the extreme heat that prevailed and the best
methods of keeping cool. The summer has
been the hottest experienced for many
years, especially in the East where large
numbers of deaths and prostration have
occurred daily, principally in New York and
Chicago.
	In a paper-given recently on current
events, the hope was expressed that the
“white flag of truce would soon float over
the nations of the world.” It is to be re-
gretted that the hope has not been realized,
for wars and rumors of wars still exist in
almost every part of the world, with the
prospect of still greater wars occurring
than we have yet had. The conditions
existing in China are the leading topic of
the day. It was first thought the trouble
originated with a lawless element called the
Boxers, but Minister Conger declares the
blame rests with the Imperial government.
Other opinions are that the responsibility
rests with the European governments, on
account of the jealousies existing between
them, and their lack of foresight and prud-
ence in meeting the crisis. But wherever
the blame lay, it was evident that the
foreigners at the legations were in great

[Column 2]
danger of being massacred, and that it was
of the utmost importance that the allied
forces should hasten to their rescue. After
a long and difficult march, and with numer-
ous victorious engagements with the enemy,
they entered the city of Pekin. They found
the foreigners all safe, but almost starved,
as they had been living on small rations for
some time and had only enough provisions
to last three days. After a siege of fifty-six
days of danger and privation we can imagine
their rejoicing over their deliverance. What
the future policy of the powers will be in
dealing with China is yet to be determined.
	In South Africa the Boer situation con-
tinues to be a vexed question. Kruger is
still defiant and declares that the war will
last a long time yet. The war proper is at
an end, but indiscriminate fighting is still
going on, the Boers keeping up a sort of
guerilla warfare, of which the English are
becoming very tired. There can be no
doubt but that England will be victorious,
in the end, but it will be after a long siege
with much harassment and loss of life.
	The situation in the Philippine Islands is
very similar to that in South Africa. The
Filippinos are still fighting for their in-
dependence. It is said their hatred for the
Americans is more bitter than it was for the
Spaniards. Their army is still well organ-
zed and the prospects are that fighting will
be continued in the Islands for some time to
come.
	The assassination of King Humbert of
Italy by an anarchist, is one of the sad
events of the month. His son, Victor
Emanuel III., in order to avoid riots and
disorder, immediately ascended the throne.
He is said to be small in stature, but of
strong character and it is thought will be-
come popular with his people. Attempts
to assassinate other rulers and heads of
governments have been made, among whom
was President McKinley.
	The Paris Exposition still continues to 
draw large crowds, as many as half a mil-
lion were reported to have been in attend-
ance in one day. There seems to be a
difference of opinion in regard to the success
of the Exposition. Some say it far sur-
passes the World’s Columbian Exposition
in Chicago in 1893, others say it is not so
good. It certainly should be better, as it
has seven years the advantage in the way of
advancement. Financially the Paris Expo-
sition is not a success. It has been officially
announced that it will close on November 5.
	The first trial in the celebrated Goebel
murder case has at last come to an end, and
Caleb Powers, who has been tried for com-
plicity in the murder, has been convicted
and sentenced to imprisonment for life. It
is to be hoped that all who took part in the
assassination of Governor Goebel will meet
the same punishment.
	Several notable deaths occurred during
the month, among which are the Duke of
Saxe-Colberg and Gotha, Admiral in the
British navy, and son of Queen Victoria;
Baron Charles Russell, Lord Chief Justice of
England; Collin B. Huntington, President
of the Southern Pacific R. R., and Senator
Ingals.


The campaign for the election of a presi-
dent of the United States, and also local
officers, has just commenced, and promises
to be a lively and interesting one. One
pleasing feature of the campaign in Utah
will be that a number of women will be
candidates for important offices. It is to be
hoped they will receive the support of every
voter in the State, especially of the women,
that our lady candidates may come off
victorious in the coming political struggle.
	Salt Lake City entertained a large num-
ber of strangers during the month of
August. The six biennial sessions of the
head camp Woodmen of the World, which
was held here, brought several hundred.
The great Eastern newspaper league repre-
sentatives also visited us, and many othei
tourists. The visitors were unanimous in
sounding the praises of our beautiful city
and its hospitality.
	The clubs of the city are resuming their
sessions and are taking up their winter
work, the work being along the lines of the
advancement of woman, and the improve-
ment of mankind, which is the motto of
our own Press Club.
				ELLA W. HYDE.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


SOUTH AFRICAN SITUATION.
(Read at the U. W. P. C., August 31, 1900.)
Madame Chairman and Ladies:
	In commencing my paper upon the
situation in South Africa, I shall have to
give you a long synopsis of the Transvaal
and Orange Free State. Since two years
ago, when Lord Kitchener crushed the
Dervishes at Khartoum the all absorbing
topic has been the war between the Boers
and the British in South Africa. 
	The early history of the Boers of South
Africa has its parallel in that of the Pil-
grims who landed on Plymouth Rock. When
the boat load of pilgrims left England to
come to America and found the freedom
they sought, a number of Dutch and
Huguenots, refugees from France, departed
from Holland about the same time for
similar reasons, and decided to seek their
fortune and religious freedom at the Cape
of Good Hope.
	The experiences of the two colonies were
somewhat similar. Our Pilgrim Fathers
had the Indians, and the Dutch had the
Hottentots and Bushmen to contend with.
	Notwithstanding all their trials and
tribulations everything was very favorable
with the Boers until France was obliged to
cede to England the South African settle-
ment which had been acquired with the
annexation of Holland. Even then the
settlers believd their deliverance from
tyranny had arrived, but their hopes were
only short lived. The British ruled the
colony as they had been taught in London,
and allowed no local impediment to swerve
them from their course.
	The Boer settlers had opinions of their
own and bitterly opposed the British rule.
if England had been wise in her maneuvre
and understood the country and people bet-
ter, and had been careful to send out only
sensible and sympathetic men there would
probably never have been any trouble.
	She insisted on the use of the English
language; she altered the system of the
court and local government, reducing the
rights which the people enjoyed.
	In 1853 the Boers, (the Boer means
farmer or peasant) sacrificed their farms at
whatever price they could secure and emi-

[Column 2]
grated to the Orange Free State. The
English told them if they left they could
expect nothing from them nor help. In
1854 the Boers had such a desperate battle
with the Matabele that they implored Eng-
land for assistance, but in vain. The Boers
were then compelled to establish a govern-
ment of their own, which is today em-
bodied in that of the Orange Free State.
	Since that memorable day when the
British flag was hauled down from the flag
staff at Bloemfontein fort, the British and
Boers have not been very friendly, and the
British regret that their flag is absent from
the fort. The Boers will yield their lives
before they will allow it to be raised again.
Nowhere in the world is the American re-
public admired so much outside of its
own country as in South Africa. Both the
Transvaal and Orange Free State constitu-
tions are patterned after the United States.
South Africa is a modified counterpart of the
United States. It differs in some features
but has the same general foundation.
	The president is elected for five years
and oversees the Executive Council which
constitutes the real government. The Ex-
ecutive Council consists of three heads of
departments and six unofficial members of
the First Raad. These nine officials are the
authors of all laws, treaties and policies that
are proposed to the Volkraad. There are
two Volkraads, the first like our Senate, the
second like our House of Representatives,
but with far less power.
	The First Raad consists of 27 members,
elected for and by the people, and they must
be born in the country. A naturalized
Boer is ineligible to the Upper House. The
popular opinion is that a naturalized citizen
takes less interest in the affairs of the coun-
try. The First Raad was established in
1835, the Second Raad in 1890. This was
in order that the Uitlanders might have
representation in the affairs of the govern-
ment. All members are obliged to wear
black clothes and white ties.
	All court proceedings are conducted in
the Dutch language and none but Dutch
speaking lawyers are admitted to practice at
the bar.
	The Boer of today is a man who loves
solitude above all things. He and his
ancestors have enjoyed the chief spirit of
the early settlers of South Africa for so long
that it is his greatest delight to be let alone.
	Physically the Boers are a superior race—
the roving existence, the life in the open
air and the freedom from cares, all combine
to make the Boers almost physically perfect.
They can stand any amount of pain and
discomforture and can live for a remarkably
long time on the smallest quantity of food.
A Boer can subsist on a five pound piece of
“Biltong” (beef that has been dried in the
sun until it is as hard as a stone) for ten or
fifteen days without suffering any pangs of
hunger. In time of war “Biltong” is the
principal diet in army rations.
	The religion of the Boers is one of their
chief characteristics; it comes first and is
uppermost in their thoughts. The Old
Testament is the pattern he strives to fol-
low. He reads from its pages every day, and
when he wishes to exchange cattle with his
neighbor or give his daughter in marriage
lie consults the Old Testament and finds
therein the advice that is applicable to the
situation. The State controls the church
and again the church controls the State. It
is necessary for a man to become a factor in
religious affairs before he can become of any
political importance. The Dutch reformed

[Column 3]
church has been the State church since
1835. The Heroonnde Dopper branch of
the church is a result of a disagreement in
1883 over the singing of hymns during re-
ligious service. The Doppers led by Oom
Paul peacefully withdrew, and started a
congregation of their own, and the more
progressive faction proceeded to sing hymns,
which the Doppers declared was extremely
worldly.
	The great festival in the Boer’s calendar
is that of Nachtmaal or Communion, which
is generally held in Pretoria the latter part
of the year. It behooves every Boer to
journey to the capital once a year to partake
of Communion. Pretoria then becomes the
Mecca of all Boers, the pretty little village
is filled to overflowing with pilgrims their
wagons and cattle. Those living in the re-
mote part of the country are obliged to start
several weeks before the “Nachtmaal” in
order to be there at the appointed time.
In many instances the journey to and from
requires six weeks. They camp in the
open square in the center of town surrouud-
ing the old brick church, which to them is
the most sacred edifice in the world.
	Transvaal and South Africa without a
description of Stephanus Johannes Paules
Kruger, would be like the play of Hamlet
with Hamlet left out. Kruger is a man of
absolutely no education, although he has
applied his inherent abilities so as to he able
to acquit himself of his high office in a
manner to be envied by many who have
enjoyed a hundred times as many ad-
vantages. Since his youth he has been a
leader among the countrymen. His person-
ality is most unique: he impresses one as
being a king in the garb of a farmer; a
genius in a dunce cap; an awkward country-
man in store clothes, with a silk hat in-
tended for some one else; his coat is far too
small to meet around his corpulent body and
his trousers seem to have a natural antipathy
for his shoes. He is uncouth in his manner,
is suspicious of every stranger and every-
body. He is slow to make friends, but once
he trusts a person, volumes of proof are
neceesary before he alters his opinion. He
never forgets a good deed and never par-
dons a man who does a bad one.
	Although the majority of his countrymen
are of Dutch or Hueguenots ancestory, he is
a German. His paternal ancestors emigrated
to South Africa in 1713. Paul was born
October 10, 1825. His remarkable vitality
and capacity for hard mental labor are the
results of the great care which he has be-
stowed upon himself and the regular habits
he has followed for twenty years. He rises
at half past five every morning and follows
a daily program from which he never de-
viates unless he is away from home. He is a
speechmaker of ordinary ability; he speaks
without preparation, indulges in no flights
of oratory, uses plain language that is
understood by all. His speech is in the
Taal dialect, which bears the same relation
to the Dutch language as the low German
does to the high German. This war has
been a terrible strain on his nervous
vitality and he has been obliged to forego
the habits of a lifetime. Instead of retiring
at seven o’clock it has often been one or two
in the morning. So exhausted has he been
that it is with difficulty that he has climbed
to his bed. I say climbed, for he has an
ancient Dutch bed which came from Hol-
land with the first Jacob Kruger. This bed
is five feet from the floor and is reached by
a stairway.
			(To be continued.)


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


ACROSS THE YEARS.
Perhaps the breath of flowers in the room
	The faint reminder of their presence here,
Has wakened thoughts as sweet as their perfume.
	And like the sunbeams full of warmth and cheer.
The pansies smile at me with eyes so blue,
	I press my lips to them and think of you.

So long, alas! we two have dwelt apart.
	Life has bestowed her meed of toil and tears
[-]nd shadowed days upon my eager heart;
	But now, at peace, I gaze across the years,
And thankful am for all the good and true
Which has been mine, and most of all for you.

Fate separates, but distance cannot harm
	The sweet companionship which once was ours,
Nor take from me the memory of you charm.
	Which wakens with the fragrance of these
		flowers;
No years of absence ever can undo  
The bonds of tenderness which cling to you.

Across the years I hear your loving voice, 
	Urging me always to some higher aim,
When I have won I know that you rejoice;
	When I have failed you grieve but do not blame.
If in my humble way some good I do,
I owe it to those other years and you.
				JENNIE NOONAN WHELESS.


AUGUST TWILIGHT.
With downward pointing horns, the ghostly
		moon,
	Omen of draught, hangs midway of the west,
The hidden locust thrills his ancient tune
	Of dying summer, and the cricket’s din
Sounds louder, as if frosts had touched his breast
	And strain’d to sharps his little violin.

There is a purple dusk about the world.
	Like asters all diffused in airs, and deep
Within the grayish-golden west impearled, 
	The evening star seems like a flow’r’s white
		soul, 
Flown there to bide above its kin and weep
	While winter’s icy sickle works them dole. 

From tree to tree the katydid disputes, 
	And the shrill-sounding locusts rasp their wings, 
And tree-toads with their hesitant soft flutes
	Piping low queries, list and pipe again. 
The frost-fear trembles in all creature things
	And every voice seems prophecy of pain.
						JAMES BUCKHAM.


[Column 1]
AUTOBIOGRAPHY OF SARAH B. LAYTON
			(Continued.)
	The next Sunday came and I tried hard to
get my oldest brother to go with me to
meeting, but his wife and my mother over-
powered me and prevented him, so I went
alone.
	That day when I arrived there one of the
Elders told me that a new meeting house
had been opened about two miles from
where I lived. This was very good news
to me.
	After the forenoon meeting I went home,
thinking to go to this new place. I asked
my brother if he would go with me and he
said that he would like to go, for he had
heard nothing of Mormonism but what I
had told him. His wife was always un-
willing for him to go, but he went. When
we had gone about half of the way, for the
first time my strength failed me and I
fainted dead away. My brother caught me
and after some time I revived and we pro-
ceeded on our way. It was very dark and
cold and we knew nothing about where the
place of meeting was. My brother called at
a house and asked if they would tell him
where Mr. Coleman lived. They answered
that he was a Mormon and asked if we
were, to which my brother answered
no. Then they told him and after awhile
we succeeded in finding the place.
	Weak and tired as I was when we arrived
we were welcomed by all. They had heard
how I had embraced the Gospel and the
opposition and unkindness I had received.

[Column 2]
Many kind and encouraging words were
spoken to me, advising me to hold fast to
the truth.
	There were several there whom I had 
met in the Methodist chapel, and we were
glad to see each other, and to know that we
had embraced the truth together. Some
bore their testimony, and one lady spoke in
an unknown tougue. This was all new to
me and to my brother. We felt well paid
for the effort we had made. Brother Chris-
topher Layton was one of those who had
joined the Church and left the Methodist
chapel.
	When I reached home that night my
father had just gone up stairs to go to bed.
I started to speak in an unknown tongue.
My mother cried out for father, saying that
those Mormons had bewitched her child;
she seemed much distressed. I could not
stop for some time, but when I could talk I
told that it was common in the Church and
that we had heard it that night in meeting.
I did not know what I had said, but my
mother was never so unkind to me after. I
often thought it was one way of softening
the hearts of my parents.
	I worked hard with my brother all that
week, and on the next Sunday he was bap-
tized; This was in February. After that
I had some one to go with me to meetings.
	Our next task was to get his wife to go
with us. There were people being added
from time to time. In April there were a
great number and among the number was
my brother’s wife. After that we did not
rest until we got our mother to go to the
meetings. She soon began to think that
there was something in Mormonism more
than she had thought. She was well ac-
quainted with the Bible and knew that what
I had told her of the Gospel, and what the
Elders preached was Bible doctrine, and
she became more troubled in her mind, for
she had much sectarian folly to overcome.
She had been a strict Baptist from her
childhood, and never did anything on Sun-
day but go to meeting and read the Bible.
	My father was not at all religious, and did
not care to go anywhere on Sunday, and
that made it hard for mother. But she
tried to bring us up as she had been brought
up, to honor the Sabbath. We labored
hard with her, and finally the prejudice
gave way. The Lord seemed to enlighten
her mind, she overcame her feelings and
about the middle of May she applied for
baptism. That was a day for rejoicing in
our family. I freely forgave all of her un-
kindness to me, for I was so thankful to my
Heavenly Father. From the day of her
baptism she was true to her covenants.
She never went into a sectarian church
again. My father never troubled himself
about where we went and he would not go
with us.
	When the weather was warm enough the
Elders were told to go and hold services in
the streets. My brother and I used to be
one with them, and stand by them while
they preached. I have often been to two
meetings and then walked six miles to at-
tend an open air meeting, then returned;
but I always thought it was the kingdom of
God first. I never feared what any one
thought about me, I tried to live above re-
proach.
	The lady I had done needlework for sent
word that she desired me to do more if I
were well enough, although my mother
had told me that Lady Jane Pym would
not employ me any more if she found that I
was a Mormon. But she did not trouble 

[Column 3]
me. When she sent for me I went to the
mansion and asked to see the lady. In a
few minutes the butler came and took me to
the lady’s room. When I appeared before
her she looked at me and said:
	“Sarah, I am pleased to see you. Are
you able to sew again?”
	I told her that I was. She then spoke of
my not being at church any since I took
sick. I told her no, I had not been there
any, nor I did not intend to go any more,
for I had joined the Mormon Church.
	She looked at me and said, “What, as
young as you are? What do you know
about religion?”
	I said that I did know that the Church of
Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints was right.
	She asked if my mother allowed it, but I
told her that the Lord had shown me that
it was right and I joined for myself. I
was then asked whether I could tell her
what I believed, and then I explained to
her that we believed in faith in God, re-
pentance, baptism for the remission of sins
and the laying on of hands for the gift of
Holy Ghost; also that we believed in the
Bible and the Book of Mormon. There
were no more questions this time, but told
me to go the work room and tell the maid
to get me what I wanted.
	When I went again she sent for me to
come to her room. On entering she began
questioning me about our Church. These
I answered as well as I could. I then
asked her if she would not like to read
the Book of Mormon. She expressed a de-
sire to read it, so I brought it for her. I
used to ask the Lord before I went to give
me wisdom to talk to her, for she was a
bright, educated lady, while I was an illi-
terate person, and untrained. I took her
the book and she kept it about six weeks,
and when she gave it to me she said that it
was a wonderful book. Her husband,
Squire Pym, was greatly interested in it, so
she said. I offered to lend them some
tracts to read, which she accepted, and I
took them to her. She had known me from
my childhood and had known my father
and mother many years. They knew I
had never been taught these things by
man, and whenever I would take my
work to her she would not only sit and
listen to me explain the Gospel in my weak
way but would have her oldest daughter
come in and listen. They were always
very kind to me and I felt very anxious
that everybody should understand the Gos-
pel, for I was as sure that it was the truth
restored to earth as I was that the sun rose.
	When I was in my seventeenth year I
had another sick spell, but was often ad-
ministered to by the Elders. There was a
law in England that compelled every one
to have a doctor in attendance when sick-
ness came, for that reason I was obliged to
have the doctor come. I have never had
very good health. And I sometimes think
that it is for my good; had I always been
well I might have wandered from the path
of truth and right, for I had such a strong
will-power. I always had a desire to lead
out in some way that I thought would be
profitable to me. I used to work very hard 
with my needle, and I was taught never to
spend my time idly; for before I was six
years old I had made enough of what was
called pillow lace to buy me a new dress.
That dress I shall always remember, for
young as I was my mother let me select it
for myself. It was a green and white, and
was never so proud of any other dress.

WOMEN’S MEETINGS.
	The regular annual business meeting of
the National Woman’s Relief Society will
be held in the Salt Lake Assembly Hall on
Monday, October 1, at two o’clock p. m.
All officers and members of the society are
cordially invited to attend and participate in
the business of the meeting.
			E. B. WELLS, Secretary.
	The General Conference of the Relief
Society will be held in the Salt Lake
Assembly Hall, in this city, on Thursday,
October 4, meetings commencing at 10 a.
m. and 2 p. m. It is desired that there
should be a large attendance of the mem-
bers, and that each Stake organization
should be represented by its president, or
some one aifthorized to act for her. A
cordial invitation is extended to the Bishops
and all brethren interested in the welfare of
the society, also the Y. L. M. I. A. and
Primary Association.
		ZINA D. H. YOUNG,
						President.
		JANE S. RICHARDS
		BATHSHEBA W. SMITH,
		SARAH JENNE CANNON,
						Counselors.
	B. WELLS, Secretary.


A MOST EVENTFUL YEAR.
	The many remarkable events transpir
ing in the world at the present time cannot
fail to impress any one of a thoughtful
habit of mind with a deep feeling of awe
and solemnity, to say the least, and to a
religious nature the tendency must be to-
wards the cultivation of a higher spiritual
life; and to the Latter-day Saint it is like
the opening of a series of prophecies the
fulfilling of which we have been told from
year to year and from time to time.
	Only during the present year, the close
of the nineteenth century, pause and con-
sider how many telling events have taken
place, and in view of the dire consequences
of these occurrences think what disaster,
grief and fatal ravages have followed fam-
ine, desolation and death, carrying off
millions of human beings, while the careless
and indifferent still pursue the pleasures
and pastimes of the hour, heeding not the
judgments that are sweeping the earth
with devastating floods and fires and
plagues, and they see not neither do they


understand. Assuredly it would seem the
Lord has come out of His hiding place to
take vengeance on those who have been
disobedient unto His commandments and
have held Him in dishonor and scorn.
	These are the signs of the times that are
spoken of as ushering in the coming of the
Son of Man. Therefore, it behooves the
Latter day Saints who have been warned
and forewarned of these disturbances to
draw near to the Lord in humility and
faith, that they may claim the promise that
the destroying angel shall pass them by.
	The terrible catastrophe at Galveston
cannot fail to make men’s hearts sicken for
fear—a great city full of life and of wealth
swept away by the tempest in a few brief
hours, like a visitation of the wrath of the
Almighty. The bravest men and women 
naturally shrink from witnessing such
fearful suffering, and yet truly noble are
those, who, though tender and sympathetic,
ordinarily unable to endure the sight
of extreme distress, go to the rescue
of their fellow beings in times of their
direst need.
	The Red Cross Society, with the great,
magnanimous Clara Barton at their head,
lost no time in going to the scene of the
Galveston calamity. And though Miss
Barton succumbed to physical weakness
and had to be taken to her home, yet the
work she had planned will be executed by
her able assistants; and though it may be
utterly impossible to reach all cases of
sickness and destitution, yet it is good to
know the milk of human kindness flows
still in the breasts of many thousands of
men and women, who esteem it a sacred
duty to minister and succor those whom
misfortune has overtaken unawares,
and so fulfill the law of Christ, “Bear ye
one another’s burdens.”
	It is quite impossible to go into the de-
tails of the outrages, atrocities and crimes
perpetrated by fiends in human form dur-
ing the last few months in foreign lands.
One cannot help echoing the words of the
poet, Cowper—
“My soul is sick with every day’s report  
Of wrong and outrage with which earth is filled.”


[Column 1]
EDITORIAL NOTES.
	It was with sincere regret that we learned
of the illness of our beloved friend and
woman’s benefactor, Miss Susan B.
Anthony, and we hope and pray and trust
that ere this she has entirely recovered.
Miss Anthony’s name is a household word
in the United States and across the sea she
is held in reverence and honored every-
where, in the mansions of the great and
noble of the lands, and in the cottages of
the people. America has reason to be
justly proud of a woman who alone and
single-handed has carried out such a re-
markable destiny and carved along with
her thousands of other women, who, but for
the paths she has broken over rough and
untrodden ways, might never have attained
to the higher views of life's perspective
where they can look out into the broader
fields of human liberty and higher pro-
gression.
	Tuesday, September 5, President Lorenzo
Snow gave the brothers and sisters en-
gaged in the Temple work, an excursion to 
Saltair, in which by invitation Presidents
George Q. Cannon and Joseph F. Smith and
several of the Apostles and others joined
the party. Everything passed off pleasantly

[Column 2]
and the affair was a most agreeable recre
ation to those whose time is constantly em-
ployed in special service to benefit and bless
the living and the dead. We acknowledge
with sincere thanks an invitation to be one
of the numerous guests ot the occasion, and
regret that circumstances prevented our
making one of such a delightful party.
	Mrs. Mary Urquhart Lee, the teacher of
“Parliamentary Law and the Cultivation of
the Speaking Voice and other Mediums of
Expression” is in the city at the Kenyon
and has classes daily in both these depart-
ments of her work. Mrs. Lee is a delight-
ful person to be associated with even for a
time. Her methods are entirely new and
seemingly very original. Her talks are in-
tensely interesting. She has awakened
quite an interest among club women and
others, and her drills in organized classes
are quite wonderful. She spares no pains to
make the classes attractive and to demon-
strate clearly her ideas of physical perfection
in grace and poise. Even a few lessons are
helpful, and to those who take a full course
must be decidedly beneficial. Mrs. Lee is
a noted authority on Parliamentary Law in
America, so much so that several news-
paper men and lawyers have at times joined
her classes. She gives the most essential
points that she has acquired by years of
study and experience in teaching. The
women of Salt Lake may congratulate
themselves upon having this fine opportun-
ty of being taught by one so accomplished
in style and manner, so brilliant in conver-
sation and so bright and sparkling in all her
ways as to give one the impression that she
is in love with life and brave enough to
face all its vicissitudes. Mrs. Lee is vigor-
ous and self-possessed, broad-minded in her
views of the world of mankind and a
thorough cosmopolitan. She is pleased
with Salt Lake and it is to be regretted she
cannot stay longer in Utah.
	On Friday, September 21, an At Home
was given by Sister Priscilla Jennings which
was a most enjoyable gathering of choice
sisters. Her elegant rooms were brightened
with autumn flowers and leaves, the Utah
sunflower being, perhaps, the most con-
spicuous. Music on the pianola by pro-
fessional musicians added to the pleasure
of the afternoon. Of the sixty-seven invi-
tations issued to the sisters nearly all were
present to partake of the cordial hospitality
of the hostess, whose tact and geniality
make her “Teas and Parties” interesting
and thoroughly attractive. The meeting
of old friends under such favorable arrange-
ments for enjoyment is a positive luxury
to the sisters who devote themselves, their
time and ability to Church work, chari-
table and philanthropic, in addition to
home duties and obligations. Among the
venerable Mothers in Israel present were
Sisters Zina D. H. Young, M. Isabella
Horne, Bathsheba W. Smith, Elizabeth
Webb, Mary Alice Lambert and many
other women whose good works entitle
them to a high place in the history of the
Latter-day Saints. It is needless to say
that the tables were elegant in appearance
and the refreshments delicious and appe-
tizing. President Zina D. H. Young, by
request of Mrs. Jennings, pronounced the
blessing when the guests were all seated at
the numerous tables, large and small, in
the handsome dining room and library.
Mrs. Jennings is an ideal hostess, and this
last party was a delightful success, as al[-]
who participated in it will certainly agree


[Column 1]
and say long may Sister Jennings live to be
a benefit and blessing, a light and an ex-
ample to her numerous posterity and her
many friends.
	The Y. L. M. I. A. of the Fourteenth
Ward gave a banquet on the evening of
their regular meeting, Tuesday, September
11, in the ward hall. Mrs. Ruth M. Fox,
the superintendent, assisted by her coun-
selors and members, making such prepar-
ations as to assure the comfort and enjoy-
ment of all invited guests. The distin-
guished guest of the occasion, in whose
honor the party was given, was the highly
esteemed General President of this great
organization of young ladies, Mrs. Elmina
S. Taylor, who for more than twenty years
has labored unremittingly for the benefit of
the young women of Zion, and whose
seventieth birthday occurred on the 12th of
September. About two hundred people
were present and joined in celebrating the
birthday party, even though it was the
evening previous to the eventful day. Miss
Campbell made the address of welcome after
all had been seated at the tables, which were
profusely decorated with a variety of beauti-
ful flowers; the ivy twined about the plates
at each place was a dainty device and 
artistic in effect. Bishop George H. Taylor
pronounced the blessing after all were 
seated. Superintendent Ruth M. Fox was
busy here and there attending to all her
guests and genial as the autumn sunshine.
After everything had been arranged Sister
Fox announced that the young people
would give some humorous anecdotes,
jokes and witticisms including conundrums.
Their departure from the usual method of
procedure was a source of merriment and
prevented any tendency to eulogize the
honored lady, who is deeply sensitive and
dislikes public demonstrations on her own 
account, preferring appreciation in simpler
ways and in deeds rather than words.
After the young folks had given some not-
able exhibitions of good humor, Sister Fox
called upon Sister E. B. Wells to make a 
few remarks, in which she referred feelingly
to the labors of Sister Taylor and the future 
destiny of the young people of Zion. Elder
C. W. Penrose followed, beginning with
some quaint stories and merging into more
serious matters. The party was an admir-
able success in the way of solid enjoyment
and social equality, and cannot fail to have 
good results. Patriarch John Smith pro-
nounced the benediction previous to the
company dispersing.
	Miss Helen Margaret Ring, of Denver,
Colorado, recently visited our city and spent
a few days, during which time we had
several talks with her and found her a most
fascinating personality and brilliant conver-
sationalist. Miss Ring has traveled ex-
tensively and very recently visited Mexico
and Arizona also Mancos in New Mexico,
where she explored the ancient cliff dwellers
habitations and other places of special
interest to anthropologists. Miss Ring gave
her lecture on “Famous Women of the
French Salon” in the Congregational
Church under the auspices of the W. C. T.
U. of this city, Friday evening, September
7. She was introduced to the audience by
Mrs. E. E. Shepherd in a graceful manner,
and her own appearance was most attractive.
Her delineations of character were toned to
perfection, surpassing any lecture it has
been our privilege to hear. In fact she
brought the persons before one in pictures
so vivid that imaginative people might fancy

[Column 2]
the scenes transpiring upon the platform.
Miss Ring gave herself for the time being to 
her subject and that is what always wins
an audience of intelligent people. Miss 
Ring is original and that, too, is a strong
point in one’s career in this day of
mechanical technique. We very much re-
gret that she could not remain longer in our
city and hope we may hear her again at
some future time. The ladies of the W. C.
T. U. secured excellent music for the eve-
uning. The singing, too, was sweet and
uplifting. The entire affair was a rare treat.
	Friday, September 7, the Relief Society
of the Eighth Ward of this city, held its
annual meeting at which were present of the
General Board, Presidents Zina D. H.
Young, Jane S. Richards and Bathsheba
W. Smith, also the secretary, E. B. Wells,
and President M. Isabella Horne, of the
Salt Lake Stake, and her counselors, Annie
T. Hyde and Clara C. Cannon, and General
President Elmina S. Taylor, of the Y. L.
N. M. I. A., and some other guests—Presi-
dent Chamberlain presiding, her counselors,
Cartwright and Shelmardine, were in at-
tendance and the faithful secretary, Sister
Piggot. The hall was beautiful decorated
with a variety of flowers, that were a perfect
delight and added greatly to the enjoyment
of the afternoon. Counselor Cartwright
was present and Bishop E. J. Sheets came 
in during the meeting. The remarks by
the veteran Mothers in Israel, Sisters Zina,
Jane S. Richards, B. W. Smith and M. I.
Horne were deeply appreciated, as they
were mostly reminiscent of the early days
of the Relief Society and of the Church.
Sisters E. B. Wells, E. S. Taylor, Annie
T. Hyde, Clara C. Cannon and Bishop
Sheets also each spoke a short time. After
the meeting had adjourned all were invited
to partake of a delicious supper (really a
banquet) in the large and commodious
vestry, where everything was delightfully
arranged for the enjoyment of the partici-
pants. On such occasions the Relief Society
is active and fully competent to minister in
the best and most happifying manner.
Bishop Sheets occupied the seat of honor
and pronounced the blessing. The tables
were artistically decorated with roses, car-
nations, oleanders and other sweet and at-
tractive blossoms, and the good cheer was
an added flavor to the sumptuous feast.
Sister E. B. Wells was called upon by
President Chamberlain to give a toast, and
responded with “The Mothers in Israel,”
those who had passed behind the veil, but who
still live in memory and whose names and
noble deeds should be held in humble remem-
brance throughout all time. Bishop Sheets
also gave a toast, “The Veteran Fathers of
the Church, Prophets and Apostles.” This
reunion of the Relief Society was both a
spiritual and temporal feast, and the social
enjoyment helps to unite in closer relations
those who meet together for active work
and service in the society.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
MISS ANTHONYS ADVICE TO MOTHERS
	Only the opening paragraphs of Miss
Anthony’s greetings were read before the
Congress, and although, local leaders pre-
sented some suffrage resolutions, they were
suppressed. Leaders in the Congress
deemed it unwise to take up the snbject lest
inharmony arise. Miss Anthony’s letter
was as follows:
	MY DEAR FRIEND;—I am in receipt of your

[Column 2]
letter asking for a letter to the National Congress
of Mothers. It is true, as you say, that I am al
ways a very busy woman; nevertheless I always
have time to give my word of sympathy to every
organization for the betterment of the world.
	I have watched the progress of the Congress of
Mothers with a great deal of interest. Women can
find no more important work than a study of
the best ways and means for performing their
duties to their children.
	I trust those who are assembled in Des Moines
will not fail to recognize that the duty of the
mother does not lie entirely within the four walls of
the home, but that it extends also to the making
and controlling of the conditions which surround
these children when they go beyond the home.
	For this purpose she must have a voice in the
government of the municipality of the State and
nation. Not only the ordinances of the Common
Council, but the laws made by the Legislature and
by the Congress itself, vitally effect the conditions
which influence the home.
	Another strong reason why the mother should
have a voice in political matters—should possess
the power to make and unmake both laws and
lawmakers—is in order that the children shall con-
sider her opinions of as much value as those of the
f[---]er
	It seems to me, therefore, that the very first thing
that should be done by the National Congress of
Mothers is to demand for all women perfect
equality of political rights.
	Enclosed is a petition to Congress for a sixteenth
amendment to the Federal Constitution w[-]ich shall
enfranchise women. In the name of motherhood I
urge that this body, by its president and secretary
shall sign this petition. To procure the powerful
influence of the ballot is in direct line with the ob-
jects of this association, for whatever you may de-
cide to be the paramount duties of the mothers, its
possession will the better enable her to discharge
them.
	Hoping that you will have an interesting and
instructive Congress, and with kind regards to all
its members, I am very sincerely yours,
				SUSAN B. ANTHONY.

R. S. REPORTS.
	The Stake presidency of the Relief Soci-
ety of Bannock Stake, President Mary A
Call, Counselors Sarah M. Call and Rosa
B. Knowles, and Secretary Hannah C.
Hatch, have made their annual visit through
the Stake, their starting point being the
Lund ward. President Letitia Banks re-
ported the society as being in a flourishing
condition, the teachers making their regu-
lar monthly visits without fail and keeping
an accurate account of the articles received,
so that in any case their books could be re-
ferred to as being reliable.
	The visitors each bore a faithful testi-
mony and gave some excellent advice, the
main subjects being the storing of wheat,
fasting and prayer and also a few words to
mothers on the rearing of their children.
	We were entertained at Sister Bevins.
	The two next wards visited were
Thatcher and Cleveland. The attendance
in both wards was very slim owing to the
prevalence of a contagious disease
among the people, although the disease is
almost abated, there being no new cases.
We held meeting in both wards and gave
some good instructions to the attending
sisters. The next meeting was held at
Mound Valley. The spirit of God was
manifest in the meeting. The visitors
were kindly treated and spent the night at
Sister Barbara Williams.
	The next place visited was Trout Creek,
where we lunched with President Mary
Jensen, then held meeting in the meeting
house, where the good instructions that
were given were kindly received.
	The next place was Grace. We traveled
late to get there, in wind and dust. We
were kindly entertained by the Relief
Society president, Sister Rich. We had a
better attendance there than in the previous
wards and a good spirit prevailed. Then


[Column 1]
we were kindly entertained for dinner at
Sister Sullivans.
	Sods Springs was the next place visited,
where we enjoyed the delightful soda water.
President Lau received us and we had a
good meeting. From there we went to the
branch of the Lund ward and organized a
branch of the Relief Society.
	We had a good attendance of both
brethren and sisters, a good spirit was
manifest and some good instructions given.
	Sister Cramer was sustained as president,
and Sister Peck and Sister Hedda Anderson
as her counselors, and Sister Jergeson as
secretary and treasurer.
	The next place visited was Ten Mile, a
branch of Hatch ward, where we had a
good attendance. The many subjects
handled were unity, charity and the storing
of grain. We were entertained by Presi-
dent Esther Banks in her usual genial sty[---]
	The next meeting was held at Hatch
ward, where there was the largest attend-
ance, according to their number, all being
present who had their names enrolled except
two. A good spirit prevailed and many
good instructions were given.
	The last meeting was held at Chesterfield.
President Columbia Loveland, of Chester-
field, gave a good report of the society. Felt
weak in her position, yet willing to go
ahead and do her best. The Stake presi-
dency gave some good instructions and en-
couraged the sisters and said that they had
enjoyed their visit through the Stake very
much, and thought the sisters were doing
a good work in this part of the Lord’s vine-
yard.
		HANNAH C. HATCH, Sec.
SALT LAKE STAKE.
	The quarterly conference of the Relief
Society of Salt Lake Stake was held in the
Assembly Hall, Saturday, September 8,
1900, President Horne presiding. Meeting
opened at ten a. m. by singing, “Once more
we come before our God.” Prayer by Cor-
responding Secretary Maria W. Wilcox.
Officers present on the stand. President M.
Isabella Horne; Counselors A. T. Hyde and
C. C. Cannon; General Presidents Z. D. H.
Young and B. W. Smith; Sisters M. W.
Wilcox, J. C. Howe, E. J. Stevenson and
Emma A. Empey.
	Conference continued by singing, “O
Jesus, the Giver.” Minutes of morning
session of previous conference, held March
5, 1900, read and accepted.
	President Horne made opening remarks.
Regretted that no more were present today.
The last conference had to be omitted and
we had really not held a conference for six
months. “Where there is a will, there is a
way,” and if the sisters had a party or a
parade to attend they could find time to go.
The things of the world should not take the
place of the things of the kingdom. I wish
every one would read the 3rd chapter of
Isaiah. The young people of this day in the
kingdom do not have the trials and the
hardships to endure that their parents and
grandparents had. If we do not listen to
the promptings of the Spirit we will regret
it greatly in the time to come. We do many
things that it would be better to leave un-
done. Presidents talk of these things in
your societies, urge the young women to
come and assist in the Relief Society.”
	The financial and statistical reports were
read by the secretary, also the treasurer’s
report and all accepted.
	Superintendent Empey gave a report of

[Column 2]
the nurses. There are twenty-five nurses
who are ready to answer any call for charity.
“I am pleased to say that there have been
only four cases of extreme destitution that
we have had to provide for. There is a
good spirit among the nurses and they are
willing to work wherever they are needed.
Those who have done the most charity
work are those who are willing to do more.
I have enjoyed my work, though it has been
quite hard at times, but we understand the
work now, so that in future it will not be so
difficult. I do not think there is any need
of any one who is suffering to lack attention.
There are many who do not feel like accept-
ing charity, and this is commendable.
Such cases should not suffer, but can pay a
small amount. There are two of the nurses
who do only charity work, they will not
accept pay, but give their time freely when
required by the poor.”
	Reports were then given from the First
Ward, President Steel The reports gener-
ally showed that the different associations
were in good condition.
	Second Ward, President Bassett. She
said they had adopted a plan of working in
the Temple one day a month for the poor.
	Third Ward, President Smith; Fifth
Ward, Sister Pettit; Sixth Ward, President
West; Eighth Ward, Sister Chamberlain;
Ninth Ward, reported by teacher; Tenth,
Ward, President Paul; Eleventh Ward, re-
ported by the counselor; Twelfth Ward by
President Druce.
	President Horne made closing remarks,
approving what had been reported. Hoped
the house would be filled this afternoon.
	Meeting adjourned till two p. m. Sing-
ing, “Our God we raise to Thee.” Bene-
diction by Sister B. W. Smith.
	Afternoon session. In addition to those
present in the morning, there were Apostles
Young and Grant and President Joseph E.
Taylor and President Elmina S. Taylor.
	Meeting commenced by singing “Come
come, ye Saints” by members of the Tem-
ple choir. Opening prayer by Sister Sarah
J. Cannon. Singing, “Lord we come be-
fore Thee now.” Minutes of afternoon ses-
sion of previous conference held March 5
read and accepted.
	President Horne was pleased to announce
that Apostles Young and Grant were with
us and we will be pleased to hear from
them during the afternoon. First we will
hear from our “mother,” Sister Zina D. H.
Young.
	Sister Young spoke in a most feeling
and interesting way. Gave many incidents
of great interest connected with her event-
ful life. “When I hear the sisters pray for
me I think ‘O, am I worthy?’ Let us try
to be cheerful, be upright, be honest. Let
us be an example to our children. How
thankful we should be for good parents.
I pray that we may all be obedient and
strive to keep the Spirit of the Lord.
	President B. W. Smith spoke of the sis-
ters. Urged all to be faithful, honest,
truthful and virtuous, that we may merit
the blessings of God.
	Sister S. J. Cannon followed, expressing
her gratitude for the privilege of attending
these meetings. It is a greater work than
we realize.
	President Joseph E. Taylor said we should
try to create circumstances rather than to be
controlled by them. “We are crowded so
much by the affairs of the world that it
would seem that we hardly have time to
serve the Lord. I have enjoyed hearing

[Column 3]
he testimonies of the sisters from their rich
experiences, and we should treasure them
up. I have been urging the young sisters
to come to the front and relieve and assist
the older sisters, take advice from them, and
profit by their experience.
	“President Brigham Young said: ‘God
will hold you responsible for the faith of
your children.’ The mothers have too many
household cares, they should be relieved in
a measure, that they may have more time
to cultivate their children. There is an in-
fluence at work in the world that tends to
limit the bearing of children and this in-
fluence is creeping in among us. I wish to
urge upon you sisters that wherever you
hear a whisper of this, use your influence
against it. What was the greatest blessing
and promise to Abraham? Offspring. There
are some that through physical causes are
deprived of this great blessing and who
mourn over this condition. To such I
would say be faithful and prayerful and the
time will come that you shall have this
great blessing and you shall rejoice.”
	Counselor Annie T. Hyde would like to
have these meetings once a week, that we
might meet together and receive instruction.
Teachers should visit the sisters, not simply
to gather donations, but to give spiritual
advice and aid and to relieve the suffering.
The work of the nurses is very praise-
worthy. The two years for which this first
class was engaged is nearly over and we
wish to have another class started. Presi-
dents should find out how many would like
to join. If there are any among the young
ladies, would be pleased to have them join.
If there are any in adjoining Stakes who
can come, they will be admitted.
	President Horne spoke to presidents and
secretaries: “Be sure to have your reports
in on time.” Was surprised to hear that in
some of the wards children were sent around
to gather donations. This is not right, but
the sisters should go to give counsel and
comfort to the Saints.
	Apostle Brigham Young said all that he
had heard this afternoon was in harmony
with his thoughts. “The work of the Re-
lief Society is a labor of love without hope
of reward in this life. The duties are
onerous, many of them disagreeable. In
the pioneer days my mother’s home was a
hospital. Strangers, sometimes a half
dozen at a time, were cared for when they
were sick unto death. The Relief Society
is a power for good in Zion. Ever since I
can remember I have heard the voices of
these good women raised in defense of the
right, for good, for purity of life in public
and in private. Their voices by and by
will be stilled. Their experience is like re-
fined gold, and more precious than gold are
their words. Those who are not here to
listen to them will be the losers. In relation
to what the Prophet said concerning the
duties of mothers, I do know that the Lord
never requires anything of His son or His
daughter that is impossible.
	Apostle Heber J. Grant followed. “Ac-
cording to the number present the sisters
have done better today than we did at our
conference. There are many who have the
letter of the Gospel, but not the spirit.
There is nothing truer than has been said,
that the right spirit is obtained by attend-
ing our meetings. The adversary has no
power over a faithful man or woman. When
we follow the advice and counsel of those
in authority all will come out right. Gave
several instances in proof of this. When


we do not partake of the right spirit and 
have the letter only, then we go wrong. 
"If each sister here will make up her mind to
bring some one else here with her for your
next conference she can do it. I echo all 
the sentiments of respect and love for the 
aged sisters and for all the sisters who are 
faithful. After all is said and done it is the 
faithful mothers in Zion that make the faith-
ful children."
	President Horne felt very thankful for
the good words from our brethren. "Re-
member what has been said and profit by it."
Also felt grateful to the singers. Adjourned
conference until December 1. Singing, 
"O God, our help in ages past." Benedic-
tion by Counselor Clara C. Cannon. 
	AUGUSTA W. GRANT, Sec.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


OBITUARY.

	SISTER MARY ALMINA WATKINS.

	Sister Mary Almina Watkins passed from this
life April 29, 1900, after an illness of over six
months. She was the daughter of John and
Louisa Parker Hammond, and was borne at Ma-
lone, Franklin Co., New York, December 4, 1827.
She was baptized when a child, her parents having
embraced the Gospel in the early days of the
Church. They left their home and joined the
Kirtland company. Circumstances did not permit 
them to reach Nauvoo until 1842.
	There she was married to William S. Watkins
December 4, 1844. Together they enjoyed the
association of the Prophet Joseph Smith and his
brother, Hyrum, and passed through the sorrow
and persecution endured by the Saints at the time
of their martyrdom.
	In 1846 they left Nauvoo and in 1848 located near
Kanesville, Council Bluffs. Here they remained
until 1852 when they crossed the plains, arriving
in Salt Lake City, Utah, September 12, of that
year. Just a few days prior to entering the Salt
Lake valley, Sister Watkins gave birth to a daugh-
ter.
	Afterwards they located in Big Cottonwood in
what is known now as the Holliday settlement.
They moved from there to Brigham City in 1861,
at which place she resided until her death. She
was associated with the order of celestial marriage
and proved a faithful, loving helpmate to her hus-
band and family. Twelve children were born to
her, nine of whom were living at the time of her
death, and all of them are members of the Church.
	Sister Watkins served as a teacher in the Relief
Society of Brigham City from its first organization
until the city was divided into wards in 1868, she
was then chosen president of the 4th ward. This
position she held up to the time of her death, She
was always found faithful to her trust and was a
comfort and a help to those in sorrow or need. It
was always a delight to her to bear her testimony
to the divinity of the mission of Joseph Smith and
to tell of her life and good works to the young
people.
	The following resolutions were unanimously
adopted at a recent meeting of the Relief Society of
the 4th ward of Brigham City:

		RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT.

	Whereas, Our Heavenly Father has seen fit in
His infinite love and mercy to call home our be-
loved president, Mary Almina Watkins, therefore.
	Resolved, That in her death the Relief Society
has lost a staunch and valiant worker, one who
was ever ready to give a helping hand and cheer-
ing word to those in need.
	That we, her associates, have lost a faithful,
loving sister, and that we will miss her wise
counsel and advice. but we feel that our loss will
be her gain.
	That we present a copy of these resolutions to
her bereaved husband and family to express our
love and appreciation of her good works, also that
a copy be forwarded to the WOMAN'S EXPONENT
for publication and a copy be placed on file in the
ward record book.
					SARAH J. WILDE.
					EMMA F. CRAWFORD,
								Counselors. 
					LIZZIE WRIGHT,
								Secretary.
					ADELINE PARKER,
								Asst. Sec.
					MARIA HANSEN,
								Treasurer.


[Column 1]
GRANITE STAKE.
The first conference of the Relief Society
of the Granite Stake of Zion was held at
the Mill Creek meeting house, August I,
1900, Counselor Mary P. Young presiding.
Meeting opened at ten o’clock a. m.
Singing, “How firm a foundation.” Prayer
by Counselor James R. Miller. Singing,
“The Spirit of God.”
On the stand were Counselors Mary P.
Young and Katharine H. Brockbank, Maria
B. Winder and Armeda S. Young, of the
Granite Stake Relief Society; also Counselor
James R. Miller and Stake Clerk Thomas
Alston and Assistant Superintendent Rich-
ard Horne, of the Sunday School.
All the presidents of wards were present
excepting Mountain Dell.
The secretary called the roll, showing
thirty-one ward officers present and four
Stake officers. The secretary read a brief
outline of the conference at Mill Creek,
where the new Stake of Granite was organ-
ized with the Relief Society and the other
organizations, January 28, 1900, also noted
the visiting tour of the president, Frank Y.
Taylor, to every ward in the new Stake, as
the Relief Society president and officers were
honored with the privilege of accompanying,
him with all the other officers in the Stake;
read the account of the number of Stake
officers’ meetings since the Stake was organ-
zed, five in number; the visits President
Emma Woodruff with her officers had made
in the different wards were at Taylorsville,
May 8, 1900; South Cottonwood, May 17;
Big Cottonwood, May 23; Mill Creek, June
12; East Mill Creek, June 13; Granger
June 15; Mountain Dell, July 15. The re-
port was accepted.
The statistical and financial report of the
last six months was read and approved.
Counselor Mary P. Young called on the
presidents to report their respective wards.
President Sarah B. Gibson reported
Sugar Ward; President Stevenson, Forest
Dale; President Sarah A. Turnbow,
Farmers Ward; President Elizabeth Hill,
Mill Creek; President Ann Eliza B. Neff,
East Mill Creek; President Emily W.
Stevenson, Big Cottonwood; President
Mary Rawlins, South Cottonwood; Presi-
dent Margaret Carruth, Murray Branch;
President Elizabeth Pixton, Taylorsville;
President Mary E. Porter, Granger; Presi-
dent Charlotte Nielson, Hunter.
Each of these sisters gave excellent re-
ports.
Counselor Mary P. Young: “We visited
Mountain Dell about two weeks ago. The
people are in a very scattered condition.
The president has sold her property and in-
tends to move away.”
Brother Hill, of Mill Creek, favored us
with the beautiful song, “The Holy City.”
Counselor James R. Miller said: “It gives
me pleasure to meet with the sisters of the
Relief Soeiety in their first conference. You
will improve from time to time. Next year
you will be better than this year, and so on.
I like the reports from the sisters, think
they have all been well reported. God
bless the sisters of Granite Stake and all
other Stakes.
Elder Richard Horne said: “I am pleased
and glad I came. You have given in good
reports. I have taken note and have, seen
many sick in my mind today and these sis-
ters going among them, I feel to say God
bless these good sisters, for they have laid
their hands on many a fevered brow to com-
fort and bless.” He spoke of the great

[Column 2]
blessings that were in store for the faithful
sisters, and said the will of the Lord will
teach us patience. Spoke of Noah’s day
when he was building the Ark, his patience,
perseverance and obedience. Then came
his reward and the perishing of those who
disobeyed and sinned. Related a dream
which a sister had who had been very in-
different in taking any part in the Relief
Society, but would help with money and
kind words. The Lord saw the good in
this woman and permitted her to have this
dream. She thought she was in heaven in
a large, pleasant room and felt quite satis-
fied and happy with the comfortable and
pleasant surroundings. Throngs of people
were passing to and fro. A very few passed
her and went through a doorway which
was opened slightly a short distance ahead
of her. She began to be anxious and de-
sirous-of seeing what was in the other room.
As the door opened she caught a glimpse
inside. It was so beautiful and glorious
s[-]e thought she would enter. As she
[---]ose and started that way a hand was laid
[--]on her shoulder and a voice said, “You
[--]nnot enter there. It requires a greater
work than you have done before you can
e[-]ter there. This left a great impression
w[-]th her and she took hold in the Relief
Society and all other good works with much
interest and earnestness.
Brother Holm: “I am happy, my broth-
ers and sisters, to live upon the earth and
belong to the Relief Society. We have
many in this conference who have sacrificed
pleasure and sat by the side of the sick. We
[-]ust realize that great responsibilities are
[-]eighing upon us, and we must do them if
we get in that room Brother Horne spoke
of. I rejoice to visit the sick and the poor.
The Lord blesses us because we take care of
the poor. The poor are given us to look
alter. To have the Spirit of the Lord in
our souls is better than all else. God loves
the pure in heart, the meek and the lowly.”
Siister Mary B. Hamilton was pleased to
welcome all present in behalf of the Mill
Creek Ward and extend an invitation for
them to partake of the lunch prepared in an
adjoining room.
Counselor Mary P. Young made a few
closing remarks and referred to our good
meeting and the many good instructions
that had been given. She moved that a
vote of thanks be extended to the singers
and the young brother who spent his time
in leading the singing, which was carried
unanimously.
Meeting adjourned until 2 o’clock p. m.
Benediction by Elder Thomas Alston.
(Continued.)

Press Club held at the EXPONENT office’
August 31, 1900, President Aimee C.
Schiller in the chair. Prayer by Phebe C.
Young. Roll called. A letter was then
read pertaining to year books, from the
secretary of the Federation, corresponding
secretary instructed to answer it. Mem-
bers present paid ten cents tax for Feder-
ation reception. A paper on the Transvaal
war, by Luella M. Rhodes, proved to be an
excellent one. Current events were given
in an interesting manner by Ella W. Hyde.
Mrs. Rhodes’ paper was discussed by Mrs.
Phebe C. Young and others.
	Miss Helen Margaret Ring, the guest of
the evening, a newspaper woman of Denver,


being invited to address the club, also took
up the paper and said she had never heard a
paper read that covered so much ground in
so short a time. She related some very
nteresting incidents in connection with
Mrs. John Hayes Hammond, while traveling
in Mexico. She threw out some bright
ideas in regard to the subject.
	Meeting adjourned. Dismissed with
prayer by Ella W. Hyde.

40	WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


A WONDERFUL INVENTION
	“Man’s work is from sun to sun
But a woman’s work is never done.”
	Woman has been called the weaker vessel
and yet comparing her labors with those
her companion man, most people
agree that she has the greater burden to
carry. When he starts for work in [-]he
morning, hers has already begun; when he
returns at night, hers has not ended. And,
therefore, we think that any one who can
make life less arduous to her is bene-
factor to the world at large. One of our
young sisters, Mrs. Dora B. Rancher, has
invented a wonderful system of dress cut-
ting which entirely obviates the nec[---]ity of
measurements. This simple little instr[--]
ment is adjusted on the form in less ti[---]
than measures can be taken in the ordinal
way. It is then removed and placed on the
goods to be cut out. No drafting of figur-
ing is required whatever. Some of the
leading dressmakers in Salt Lake City have
used it and are delighted with it.
	This talented little lady is the daughter of
Brother Hyrum Bigelow and Sister Martha
Meecham Bigelow, formerly of Provo,
Utah. For nearly two years she has labored
indefatigably to perfect her invention,
which will soon be upon the market. The
Adjustable Dressfitter cannot fail to make
a mark in this age of progress.
	Our sisters will do well to give it their
careful consideration when they are visited
by her or her agents. Orders can be left at
the Woman’s Coop, 44 Maine St.
				MARY F. KELLY


[Column 1]
		NOTES AND NEWS.
	MRS. MARTHA A. B. CONINE has been
selected as the Denver delegate to the meet-
ing of the National Municipal League which
meets in Milwaukee on September 19.

	MISS ROSE ELIZABETH CLEVELAND
sister of ex-President Cleveland, and Miss
Ames, daughter of ex-Governor Ames, of
Massachusetts, have bought an old home-
stead in Seven Hundred Acre Island, Me.,

[Column 2]
which they are to have remodelled into a
fine summer residence.
	A NEW monthly magazine will appear in
Boston this month called The Patriotic
Review. It will be published from Septem-
ber to May, inclusive, each year, and will
be devoted to the interest of patriotic and
historical societies throughout the country.
Miss Marion Howard Brazier will be editor
and Miss Ethel Brigham, of Brookline,
manager.
	CLARA BARTON, president of the Red
Cross Society, who was in Galveston, Texas,
to look after the distribution of relief sup-
plies, was suddenly stricken down and is
seriously ill. The physicians were impera-
tive in their orders that she leave for her
home in Washington immediately. She
was taken ill at her rooms in Tremont
Hotel with her staff of nine gathered to-
gether. Miss Barton had just completed an
outline of her work, assigning each mem-
ber of her staff a particular part of the work.
	MISS CLARA BARTON was honored in a
special manner at the reunion of the Grand
Army of the Republic in Chicago. Her
work at the head of the National American
Red Cross has made her name a loved one
among the old soldiers, and they delight in
giving her extraordinary marks of their
appreciation of her services. Miss Barton
was invited to address the Convention which
she did in a stirring speech, which was
loudly applauded. Almost every sentence
evoked a demonstration of some sort.
	The struggles of the Cuban teachers at
the Harvard summer school to learn Eng-
lish furnishes good stories for the Boston
papers. The “Listener” of the Transcript
tells the following:
	One day the teachers were invited to some sort
of an evening function at the Longfellow house on
Battle Street. It happened that shortly before the
hour for the assembly some ladies who were in
front of that house were politely approached by a
group of male Cuban teachers, who, with their
hats in their hands, stood bowing.
	“If you please, dear madams,” said their spokes-
man, “we are invited at this house tonight. We
wish to attend. We were been on an excursion to
the distance, and have not the time to go to our
houses, so that we wear, as you see, our day
dress. Perhaps you can tell us if it would be per-
mitted to us to go to the reception in our day dress?
If it would not, then certainly shall we take the
time to go to our houses and put on our night
dress!”
	The ladies assured them that they would do
much better to go as they were than to put on their
night dress, and they bowed gravely and gratefully
and withdrew.
