HOUSE OF LIFE.

My Lord built a beautiful house for me,
	With arch and pillar and wall,
With many a chamber fair to see,
And corridor, closet and hall.
The carpets and curtains were rich and fine,
	And every convenience was there;
With workmanship cunning, this dwelling of mine
	Was fashioned and furnished with care.

And I was mistress and owner of all,
	This opulence, beauty and worth,
Like Eve in her garden whom God did install, 
To dress it, the gem of the earth.
I looked from my window and lo, I beheld
	More palaces lovely and grand,
Eclipsing in splendor my own, and dispelled
	Was my joy in both palace and land.

No more did I polish each casement and stair,
	And brush every dust-flake away,
Nor open its windows to woo the sweet air,
	Nor to deck it with garlands so gay,
Its fair rooms grew tarnished and laden with dust,
	And cobwebs hung heavy and gray,
Unloved and abandoned to moth and to rust,
	Its beauty began to decay.

A footstep draws near! my Lord—He is here!
	He gazes in pain and amaze—
"This wreck is the Temple for you I did rear,"
	For shame I could not raise my eyes.
"O, foolish one, what didst thou lack, but the will?
	If these were too narrow for thee,
More stately apartments to plan and to build?
	Who loves not my gift, loves not me."

He passed, and I dared not beseech Him to stay—
	My opened eyes saw clearly now,
How foolish and blind was my envy; straightway
	I made with repentance a vow—
To honor my gift, to fill it with light,
	Embellish and keep it with care,
That henceforth it be in my Lord's kingly sight
	A dwelling place lovely and fair.

When next His dear footsteps draw near to my
		door,
	With gladness I'll usher Him in;
No mildew or rust shall He see as before;—
To restore it at once I’ll begin.
I'll love my dear home for the Giver's sake,
	Nor sight if more stately ones be,
For God, the dear Giver, can make no mistake;
	He knows what is best for me.
							LU DALTON.


[Column 1]
CURRENT EVENTS.
	To crowd the events of a month’s hap-
penings into brief space seems almost an
impossibility. Every day finds the news-
papers filled with accounts of storms, rail-
road accidents, strikes, wars, crimes—in
fact, calamities of every kind, too numerous
to mention.
	Heading the list comes the appalling

[Column 2]
and most heartrending misfortune to the
people of Texas. On September 8 the
city of Galveston was visited by one of the
most terrific storms ever known in this
country. This dreaded hurricane, so ter-
rible in velocity, so destructive in its path,
carrying into the Gulf many thousands of
lives, destroying millions of dollars of
property, was one never to be forgotten.
Every state, every hamlet, sorrows with
Galveston. The shock of the news is still
with us, but hundreds of thousands of
hands are reaching out to help them.
There seems so little to say and so much to
do. Miss Clara Barton and her little band
of faithful workers were, as usual, among
the first to reach the afflicted city. Too
much cannot be said for this noble cause,
the Red Cross, and its untiring workers, al-
ways ready and willing to alleviate the suf-
ferings of their fellow creatures.
	While there are men who think that a
sand island, exposed to storms from the
gulf, will never prove a suitable site for a
great city, the prevailing opinion seems to
be that solidity in construction and proper
sea walls will make the new Galveston
safe, and that its present site is so pecularily
adapted to commerce, that there should be
no thought of abandoning it.
	The presidential campaign is now in full
sway. The headquarters of both papers
are sending out men true to their respective
parties, who tell us the beautiful doctrine
of their party, all of which we are to con-
sume, digest, and last, but not least, vote
upon.
	The distinguished Governor of New
York, and Republican nominee for vice-
president, Mr. Theo. Roosevelt, honored
our beautiful city with a visit during the
month. He was royally entertained and
welcomed, and departed with words of
praise and gratitude for the people of the
liberal-hearted West.
	Several unfortunate holdups took place in
our city. One life was lost, but fortunately
the desperadoes are safely lodged behind the
bars.
	The Goebel murder case still heads the
docket of the Kentucky courts. One man
was sentenced to life imprisonment, one to
death and the trials of all others implicated
in this cowardly crime will fellow in quick
succession.
	The great Anthracite coal strike, by
which 140,000 men were out of employment
has fair prospects of being agreeably settled.
	In California the second week in September
was signalized by the celebration of the
50th anniversary of the state’s admission to
the Union. There were impressive military
parades, naval parades and other festivities,
continuing four days.
	So much noble work has been under-
taken of late along philanthropic lines. I
read with pleasure recently of ladies in
Brooklyn and other Eastern cities starting
free libraries during the summer in parks,
whereby our less fortunate sisters and
brothers had access to books and current
literature while seated under the shade of
trees sheltered from the hot rays of the sun.
Such work is truly noble and commendable.

[Column 3]
	Direct communication between the
United States and Germany was opened
September 1, the route being from New
York to Fayal, thence to Emdew, Ger- 
many. The occasion was signalized by the
exchange of cordial greetings between the
Emperor of Germany and President
McKinley.
	The war in South Africa is now virtually
at an end. Kruger has emigrated and is
now enroute to some foreign port.
	In the Phillipines the situation remains
about the same, continuous gorilla warfare
characterizes the existing state at the pres-
ent time.
	The Chinese situation is more complex
than ever. War clouds are gathering thick
and fast over the far East.
	In concluding this brief review of the
month’s events, I cannot refrain from re-
iterating the sentiments expressed by our
writer of last month’s events, that the day
is not far distant ere the white flag of truce
may float over the land; that men and
nations may live in peace and harmony,
united with the world, united with each
other.
		AIMEE C. SCHILLER.


SOUTH AFRICAN SITUATION.
		(Concluded.)
	The president has been married twice.
His first wife only lived a short time.
Several years after he married a niece of his
first wife, by whom he has had sixteen chil-
dren, seven are still living.
	Allen Sangru in the Ainslee says:
	If Paul Kruger died today the Boer nation
would pass into history tomorrow; no man nor
combination of men could replace him. He alone
welds together the chain of humanity that is strug-
gling for liberty in South Africa. It is as though one
mighty individual were fighting the British Em-
pire. He is Atlas, supporting the world. He has
no counterpart on earth Enemies may ridicule his
religion, slur his principles and accuse him of hy-
procrisy, but he stands a figure as adamantine as
it is animated which no one believes will ever be
cast down. He is a Sphinx surcharged with wis- 
dom and energy to whom 40,600 Burghers look up
as their savior.
	Twenty-seven years ago Cecil John
Rhodes, then a tall, thin lad, was advised
by his physician to go to South Africa if he
wished to live more than three years. He
and his brother sailed and reached Natal
while the diamond mines of Kimberly were
in full blast. Cecil remained there for
several years and finally went back to Ox-
ford to complete his education. Returning
to South Africa with the Rothschild money
he began to be a factor in politics, mining
and government and in 1875 he was in the
zenith of his glory. He was many times a
millionaire, the head of one of the greatest
enterprises, director of the affairs of the
dominion that occupies one tenth of a conti-
nent, almost the sole owner of Rhodesia.
His power was nearly absolute over a ter-
ritory that extended from the Cape of Good
Hope into Central Africa, then eastward to
within a few miles of Indian Ocean. But
with all this wealth, all this power, Cecil J.
was not satisfied, he looked at the map of


[Column 1] 
Africa and saw the Transvaal and Orange
Free State flag occupying the position he
had marked for the British emblem. In
order that he might strike at the head he
determined t[-] commence operation by first
attacking and exterminating the Transvaal
as the fall of that would carry with it the
Orange Free State. This was the plot [-]f
the Jameson raid formed, but the dream
so near realization one dav was dissolved
the next, and he was obliged to assign the
premiership of Cape Colony. The failure of
this raid was especially galling to Rhodes,
as it was the first time his great political
schemes had failed. He then planned upon
a commercial death by opening Rhodesia
for settlement. Several thousand persons
were lured from their happy homes, but
only remained about a year, and then were
glad to go back to the Transvaal and Oom
Paul. In the last ten or twelve years the
Transvaal country has proved to be of such
enormous value that it has attracted and
will continue to attract investors from all
parts of the world. The gold products
alone yield over one hundred and twenty-
five million dollars a year, more than a
third of the world’s production. Coal in
good quantities has been found, but the
process of development is so slow that al-
most all the coal comes from Wales. Lately
American coal has secured a firm foothold.
Everything in the eatable line is grown in
the Transvaal except sugar and coffee.
They have vast timber lands, but almost all
timber used in the mining district is trans-
ported from Puget Sound, the native timber
being unsuited for the underground sup-
ports.
	Thus it is easy to see the reason the
British want this country. The yield is so
enormous and the Transvaal being an
inland state it promises a feeding ground for
those states between it and the sea. Every
ton of freight that enters the Transvaal
from the sea, i[-] subject to a high custom
duty. Freight shipped from Europe is taxed
by the steamship line, the Cape Colony R.
R., the Orange Free State tax it before
allowing it to pass through the territory
and then the Transvaal custom duties.
	Mr. Kruger says all the blame of this
war is on Rhodes, with Miller and Cham-
berlain as assistants, and he says they will
not be content until the Brirish flag floats
over the whole of Africa. All the land
that England has in South Africa she has
taken at the muzzles of repeating rifles and
machine guns. This seems to be the civil-
ized method of extending the bounds of the
empire they talk about so much. Mr.
Rhodes wants all of the country south of
the Zambizi river united, and must have
possession of the Transvaal and Orange
Free State before he can be president of the
United States of South Africa. The Old
Man says we will fight until not one Boer
remains to defend our country. The Boers
are born sharpshooters and from infancy
are taught to put a bullet in a buzzard’s
skull at one hundred yards away, so that
the Boer army is filled with lads from 17
to 21.
	It is said that if the worst came the Boers
have sufficient food stuff stored away in the
Z outpanberg mountains to last 18 months.
	Mr. Kruger says, “We are a pecular
people and if God wills that we lose our
independence, then it is better we should
all pass away. A Boer would brood over
his thoughts, the women and children
would hate the conqueror, and when there

[Column 2]
is so much bitterness there would be no
happiness. 	It would be better to die, then
each one could say 'I have fought the good
fight, I have finished my course, I have
kept the faith. Henceforth there is laid up
for me a crown of righteousness.' " 
	LUELLA M. RHODES.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
	AN INTERESTING JOURNEY.
Mrs. Emmeline B. Wells:
	DEAR FRIEND:- Since returning home
from my very delightful trip, among my
papers I found the WOMAN’S EXPONENT
you gave me, and thinking of you was re-
minded of my promise to write to you.
	We had a very delightful trip from start
to finish, and would have been delighted,
if the limitation of our travels had permitted,
to have remained a week longer in the
beautiful and impressive Salt Lake City, a
city which is a marvel to me in many ways.
Its rise and rapid growth from a barren and
an alkali plain; its broad beautiful streets,
bordered with immense trees of luxurious
foliage, and the beautiful Temple and
stately buildings, also commercial buildings
and fine houses; those beautiful mountains
with their different soft pinky shades, en-
shrouded in the hazy atmosphere, is ever a
delight which I never would tire of looking
upon. The greatest marvel is the Mormon
Church, which I in my ignorance had sup-
posed was almost a relic of the past, to find
such large audiences and following, and the
greatest mystery of all to me is the belief.
*	*	*	*	*	*	*	*	*
	We spent three weeks in Spokane, a city
among the pines and mountains, and found
many things to interest us in its natural
beauty of surroundings and industrial and
commercial activities. From there we
stopped off a day at Butte, and met a friend
who was living there, who made our stay
very pleasant, taking us all about the tree-
less and verdureless city and the wonderful
mining town. The sulphur from the
smelters has destroyed every green tree,
shrub and grass, leaving the city destitute
and naked, as it were, only bare walls of
stone and brush, the multi-millionaire’s
homes have the same naked appearance,
save on Clark Street was a small, weak, wild
cucumber vine, scarcely three feet in height.
	We rode up and up on the mountains,
viewing the mines in various places on the
rounds and also the city and country at 
large, sneezing and wheezing from the sul-
phur fumes. It was novel and interesting.
The ores the earth contains and the man-
made machinery invented to abstract them
from their hiding places.
	We resumed our journey through the
mountainous and picturesque Montana, and
made imaginary pictures of what that
country, so prolific in gold, copper and
other minerals, would be in one hundred
years from now, as its agricultural and
mineral resources become more fully de-
veloped.
	Well, the wonders of the Yellowstone
Park were finally reached, and wonderful
beyond description were the boiling, bub-
bling, spouting springs, the colors and
activity are indeed wonderful and so many
scattered along, sometimes clustered many
together, others solitary in different places.
Throughout the Park the various
mountain, river, lake and canyon scenery is
ever changing and beautiful.
	I will not attempt a description, for you

[Column 2]
may have seen it. We spent sixty days
very pleasantly there, then on to Fargo,
Dakota, where we stopped one week with
strangers. Then to Brainard, Minn., where
we stopped a week with a sister-in-law,
then to Clearwater, Minn., visited a cousin.
Next stop at Minneapolis and St. Paul,
where we visited all places of interest and
arrived in Chicago, September 14, 11:30 p.
m., more impressed than ever that there is
not a yard of this earth but what is beautiful
and wonderful beyond description. I see
beauty everywhere, in the sunshine, in the
shadows, in man, in woman. Where can
you look and not feast your eyes on beauty.
	We had a glorious trip and no occurrence
to mar our pleasure. For that reason we
hold to the truth that all is good, God made
everything that was made and called it
good, therefore we call everything good;
consequently good is coming our way all
the time.
	Should you see Judge and Mrs. Kinney
please give them my best regards, also
accept the same.
				Yours truly,
					J. H. ALLEN.


LETTER FROM BROOKLYN.
Dear Sister:
	A few months ago you wrote a letter to
President Garrett, of the Brooklyn Confer-
ence, in reference to the new branch of the
Relief Society just organized. I ask your
pardon for not acknowledging the same,
	The society was organized May 12. In
July the president of the society was forced
to resign, owing to her leaving Brooklyn.
The society was reorganized and at present
we are in good and regular standing, having
a roll call of fifteen members.
	We meet twice a month; one meeting is
devoted to business, taking in new members
and devising plans for the good of
the society; the other meeting is de-
voted to testimonies, readings and anything
that will help the spiritual growth of the
society. We feel we have a great work to
do here in Brooklyn to help forward the
cause of truth and to be an example to the
world of what our Elders teach, and our
worthy president, Miss Agnes Rose Lane, is
anxious for any suggestions or methods we
may not have that will help us in our work.
	We have given an entertainment which
proved very successful, both as a social and
financial affair, enabling us to raise the
means to help those requiring assistance;
several are in need of relief. I am sure you
will be pleased to hear from us and we ask
an interest in your prayers that we may be
in love and unity with each other, serving
our Master as opportunities are given us.
	Hoping to hear from you at an early date,
and with God bless you in your noble work,
I am your sister in the Gospel,
		BELLE SHAFFER,
			Secretary Brooklyn R. S.


MADAME JULES SIMON, the widow of
the noted French statesman died on Sep-
tember 21. Mme. Simon was one of the
first International Congress of Women’s
Work in Paris, the first body of women to
meet under the auspices of a national
government. At the second congress held
in Paris, in June of this year, Mme. Simon
was elected an honorary vice-president. A
memento of commemorative appreciation of
the services of her illustrious husband.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


THE WAYSIDE WELL.
He stopped at the wayside well,
	Where the water was cool and deep;
There were feathery ferns ’twixt the mossy stones.
	And gray was the old well-sweep.

He left his carriage alone,
	Nor could coachman nor footman tell
Why the master stopped in the dusty road
	To drink at the wayside well.

He swayed with his ungloved hands
	The well-sweep, creaking and slow,
While from seam and scar in the bucket’s side,
	The water plashed back below.

He lifted it to the curb,
	And bent to the bucket’s brim
No furrows of time or care had marked
	The face that looked back at him.

He saw but a farmer’s boy,
	As he stooped o’er the brim to drink, 
And ruddy and tanned was the laughing face
	That met him over the brink.

The eyes were sunny and clear,
	And the brow undimmed by care,
While from under the rim of the old straw hat
	Strayed curls of chestnut hair.

He turned away with a sigh;
	Nor could footman or coachman tell
Why the master stopped in his ride that day
	To drink at the wayside well
						WALTER LEARNED.


[Column 1]
AUTOBIOGRAPHY OF SARAH B. LAYTON
			(Continued.)
	In my eighteenth year I again took sick.
I lost the use of my right hand, and that
was a great trial to me, not to be able to
use my hand, for with that I had earned
much. I then tried braiding straw, but
my hand continued getting weaker, and at
last I could do nothing The doctor came
and examined it and said it was my spine
that was affected and that I would have to
go to the hospital at once. I thought I
could never stand to go there, as I had
never been from home one night in my life,
but now to leave my home and friends, it
seemed as if it were more than I could
bear. Our presiding Elder advised me to
go. It was a Saturday when I went. It
was about ten miles from home. Mother
and the folks said that I could go an out
patient, that of course would allow me to
live at home, but go to the doctor for treat-
ment. When I arrived there the doctor
said he would have to examine my back,
so he drew his thumb down my back so
hard that I shall never forget it. He said
that I would have to come an in patient.
That meant that I should stay in the hos-
pital for treatment all the time. I cried
and said that I would rather go home and
die than to stay there, but mother told me
that if I would stay there I might get the
use of my hand. That was a great trial to
me. I was taken up to the fourth story
and was shown into what is called a two-
bedded ward. I walked in and there found
a strange woman occupying the one, I was
to occupy the other. Everything was as
clean and neat as it could be.
	My supper was some milk porridge, but
my appetite had not come. Then came
the nurse with some medicine and some
opium to put me to sleep; but it did not put
me to sleep that night.
	It was springtime and everything was
beautiful outside, the shade trees and the
gravel walks, but there was no pleasure
for me, for I was a stranger to everybody.
I went to a large window and dropped a
towel out so that I could go out for it. I
went, glad to get out of my prison, to the
place where I had dropped it. It happened

[Column 2]
to drop by the matron’s window. I did not
know, neither did I care, but when I
started up stairs a lady met me. She
looked very angry and said, “What have
you been down for? Do you not know
the rules?” She then asked my name and
told me to go back and read the rules, that
they were in my room, and said, “Do not
let me see you down again until after ten.”
	I did not say anything, for I did not
know what to say; and as for the rules,
I never did read them.
	At eleven o’clock the bell rang for church
but I said to the sick woman in the same
room that I would not go to their church.
She said I must, but I still insisted that I
would not go. I was then told that I would
have to take the blame. I wanted to get
out and did not care for the blame that
would be given me.
	After the service the minister was shown
in by the nurse. He looked at my name
on the card at the head of the bed, and said
in a stern voice, “Sarah Barnes, I did not
see you at church today.”
	He then wanted to know my reason, and
I told him it was just because I did not
want to go. I was asked whether I had
read those rules, or if I could not read?
But I informed them that I could, but did
not care to read their rules. The minister
wanted to know if I did not believe in re-
ligion, but I declined to tell him at that
time, I had never seen him before. He
left me then. He was a very pleasant ap-
pearing man and gentlemanly in his man-
ner. His hair was very white and he
seemed very nice.
	When he left the room the nurse asked
me why I objected to go to church? “For,”
said she, “you must go as long as you stay
here.”
	But I told her I would not, for the
church I believed in taught me better. I
was then asked what that church was, and I
told her that it was the Church of Jesus
Christ of Latter-day Saints. She said she
was very sorry for me, for she said they
were a very wicked people; their leader
was a false prophet.
	From that time I began to tell about the
Gospel. I had some books with me and I
told them to read them. It was soon
known all through the building that I was
a Mormon. Some came in the room to see
me, to see what I looked like. Some said
they were afraid to pass the room, but I
told them there was no danger, and some
said that I ought not to be allowed to be in
the hospital. I tried to show them that it
was the true Gospel that was spoken of in
the Bible, that should be restored in the last
days by an angel who was to fly through
the midst of heaven, having the everlasting
Gospel to preach to those who dwelt upon
the earth. There were none there who had
ever heard an Elder preach, and they knew
nothing of it but from rumors.
	Well, Wednesday came, the day for all to
go to church again; but again I was absent.
At the close of their service the minister
again come to me and spoke of my being
absent, and asked what church I belonged
to, was it the Methodist, Calvinist or
Presbyterian, or what was it?
	“I do hope,” said he, “that you do not
belong to that deluded sect called Mormons,
led by that imposter, Joe Smith.”
	I quickly said, “I do belong to that very
church that you speak of.”
	He felt sorry that so young a girl as I
should be led into such a delusion.

[Column 3]
“I came into the church of my own ac-
cord,” I said, “after being fully convinced
that it was true.” This ended our talk for
that day.
	I had let some of the patients take the
books that I had with me to read, and many
of the patients began to listen to me in
telling about the Gospel. But Sunday
came again and this time when the minister
came in he had with him all my books and
told me that it was not allowed for me to
do as I had done, and that if they wanted to
hear from me they would have to come into
my room but that I might have the privi-
lege of talking to them if they wanted to
come into my room and listen. He then
began to ask questions about our religion,
which I answered very readily. He said
he understood that we had a new Bible, but
I told him that was not true. After an
hour’s conversation he left.
	On his next visit, the Wednesday follow-
ing, I was very low. I had been taken
very sick and they had put me in a warm
bath and it came near ending my life. I
fainted before I had been in there two
minutes, and they took me out and rolled
me up in a blanket and carried me to my
room and laid me on the bed, they thought
I was dead. Several of the inmates gathered
around me and felt my pulse, but no signs
of life could they find. I could hear every-
thing that they said and feel every touch,
but I could not move nor breathe. The
next thing I heard was that I must be
moved to the dead house. What a feeling!
How I prayed to the Lord to give me
strength to make some sign. At that
moment a young woman by the name of
Mary Harris suggested that they try putting
a looking glass to my lips and press it hard,
and that if there were any life it would be
shown by the breath on the glass. This
was tried and to the astonishment of them
they found the sign. The word “dead-
house” struck terror to me. In a short
time I could open my eyes, but I found that
I was very helpless, for I had not strength
to speak.
	When the minister came in the next
Sunday he seemed troubled when he saw
me so weak. I told him that I would be
better when he came next time. As he left
the room he called the nurse out and told
her that if there was anything that I wanted
to let him know what it was and he would
see that it was made right. He said that
my life was too precious to be neglected.
She came in and told me, but I said that
there was nothing I needed.
	Before he came again I had been almost
used up. My back was cupped; that is,
they had laid me on my face and cut twelve
gashes on each side of my backbone, and
then rubbed me with something that sali-
vated the whole of my back. The marks
still are there and will be as long as I live.
	He, the minister came and seemed to feel
so very sorry for me in my suffering, and
would spend considerable time with me and
ask if there was anything that I could fancy.
	The next week Lady Jane Pym drove up
to the hospital and inquired for me and
offered any assistance that I needed in the
way of comforts. The matron showed me
all the respect that it was possible for her to.
She would come in and sit and talk with me.
		(To be continued.)


If you would have, your songs endure,
build on the human heart.

SALT LAKE CITY, UTAH, OCT. 15, & SEP. 1, 1900


[Column 1]
A HOPEFUL OUTLOOK.
	It is rather a hopeful outlook for the
future that men of exceptional ability have
been appointed by the President of the
United States to represent this country in
the International Court of Arbitration.
The Hague agreement stipulated that each
of the signatory powers were to appoint
men of recognized standing on great
national questions, to arbitrate differences
between interested governments that were
at variance.
	Very much has been said and written in
regard to the Hague Conference being a
failure, but the leaven generated there will
work its way among all civilized people,
and the little seed sown, though it may
have been small, will certainly become a
strong and powerful tree, under whose
wide-spread branches generations yet un-
born may sit at ease and rejoice that war
and all its attendant evils and terrible con-
sequences are known no more.
	The sentiment in favor of peace created
through the agitation of the meetings held
simultaneously, just previous to the sitting
of the Hague Conference, will never die
out while women mourn their dead slain in
battle, since that gathering of the repre-
sentatives of sixteen nations. Sixteen
powers signed the Articles of the Conven-
tion. The Pope sent a letter assuring the
assembly of his cooperation, and stating
that he had at times previous performed
the functions of an arbitrator, and promised
to continue his efforts in the advancement
of civilization; and we know the highest
civilization makes for peace.
	The fact of an International Court of
Arbitration on neutral ground once fully
established will have its influence upon
national differences, and create a feeling of
greater permanency in securing justice to
all concerned in the causes to be adjusted.
Notwithstanding it may take a long time
to bring about the much desired results,
yet we should not lose heart in pursuing
the work that is sure to triumph ultimately.
	Though men may agree that it is all in
vain, because wars have been waged since,
(and it is the way of the world) yet, never-
theless, we know ignorance is hard to
overcome, and it is the few who are sueing
for peace against the many whose methods
are crude, and whom it is difficult to con-
vince that there is a, better way to settle

[Column 2]
even national disputes, than to array armed
forces against each other.
	Not only does the furtherance of the
measures to establish this International
Court of Arbitration look hopeful, but the
theories and philanthropic sentiments of
women now being developed along practical
lines that will tend to helpfulness in ways
heretofore directed wholly along charitable
lines; instead investing money in enter-
prises that will supply work for those
needing help and actually encouraging
habits of industry in those more or less
dependent, who through force of circum-
stances are not in a position to create work
that is remunerative. When women’s
clubs and societies can launch ou tand do
such things as are now proposed in some
of the large cities of the East, young men
will not enlist in the army so frequently for
the sake of a living, and brought up in the
country air and taught healthfulness and
familiarity with nature, will give new life
and increased vitality to the boys and girls
who have the material benefit of such
training.
	One lady in dealing with this subject says
charity deals with symptoms, philanthropy
with the disease; but she went on to tell
the need of law which is a vital truth, for
when any effort is made the workers are con-
fronted by the law, city, state or national,
and the question goes back to the honesty,
fearlessness and disinterestedness of the
law makers and law enforcers. All giving
of material aid should be with a view, if
possible, to make the recipient self-helpful.


[Column 1]
		EDITORIAL NOTES.
	The sisters of the General Board and
other sisters have been busy of late visiting
conferences. President M. Isabella Horne
and Sister Harriet Ellis attended the Utah
Stake Relief Society; President Jane S.
Richards attended Morgan Stake; Sisters
Elizabeth J. Stevenson and L. L. Greene
Richards, Emery Stake; President Susan
Grant and Sister Mary E. Irvine, Uintah
Stake.
	Miss Susan B Anthony is reported to be
slowly but surely recovering from the col-
lapse which prostrated her early in Septem-
ber last. She is now able to ride or walk
out a little every day. Women everywhere
will rejoice that she has been spared to
direct and advise those who love her so in-
tensely and who look up to her for wisdom
to guide the women’s movement for higher
advancement.
	The women of the L. D. S. Church every
where will, we are sure, be delighted to
know that they are to have a Woman’s
Temple or building in the not far distant
future. The presidency of the Church
have very generously given a very valuable
piece of land on the block east of the mag-
nificent Temple in this city, facing west at
the south corner of Temple avenue. The
structure when completed will, the sisters
may rest assured, be a handsome and com-
modious edifice.
	The women of Utah are honored in the
appointment of Mrs. Emma J. McVicker
for Superintendent of Public Instruction to
fill the unexpired term left vacant by the
demise of Dr. John R. Park. Mrs. Mc-
Vicker is an honor to the women of the
State, thoroughly qualified for the position,
<a>nd an all around woman of affairs. She has

[Column 2]
the hearty congratulations of the EXPONEN[-]
and a sincere wish from the editor for th[-]
future success of her educational ideals fo[-]
the State of Utah.
	The Annual Executive Meeting of the
National Council of Women Will be held at
Minneapolis, Minnesota, on November 13,
14, and 15, 1900. By invitation of the
Women’s Council of Minneapolis the pub-
lic meetings will be held in the First
Unitarian Church at 8th and Mary Streets.
Other meetings in church parlors or in
Hotel Headquarters, as usual. The
National organizations will be represented by
the president or proxy and one delegate or
alternate. Patrons and members of com-
mittees are entitled to be present and par-
ticipate in proceedings.
	The At Home given by Mrs. F. S., Mrs.
F. Dewey and Mrs. Joseph T. Richards at
the Richards residence on A. Street,
Saturday afternoon, October 20, was a bril-
liant affair. Three hundred guests were
invited and a large number attended during
the hours from 2 to 6 o’clock. The dec-
orations were on a very magnificent scale,
the front parlor in green and white chrysan-
themums, with vines and ferns, and very
artistic, the rear parlor in red and the dining
room in yellow and white. The ladies of
the house were assisted in receiving by Mrs.
J. T. Houx, of San Francisco, Mrs. C. S.
Varian, Mrs. A. G. Ewing, Mrs. Ed. Ferry
Mrs. Flank Jennings and Mrs. W. W.
Riter. The ladies who assisted in serving
refreshments were Mrs. Fred W. Scarff,
Misses Mary Young, Edyth Ellerbeck and
Ida Noble. The gowns of the young ladies
corresponded with the colors used in dec-
orating the dining hall.
	The General Conference of the Church of
Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints convened
October 5, 6, and 7, in the Tabernacle in Salt
Lake City, and was opened by President
Lorenzo Snow, who delivered an eloquent
and inspiritional sermon, touching upon
many important matters of historical inter-
est and also such as relate to the present day
and the lives of the Latter-day Saints. His
remarks concerning the building up of
Jackson County were in line with the spirit
of the times and his words altogether were
pregnant with significance of the work of
the Lord in the “last days,” or the fulness
of times, and of the position he occupies as
the head of the Church. President Snow,
although in his 87th year, speaks as forcibly
as ever and can be heard distinctly in that
large building. The President is a very
remarkable man and a grand representative
of his Puritan ancestry.
	The Utah State Fair, held at the Expo-
sition building and grounds, in this city,
was opened October 2, 1900, by Secretary
James A. Hammond, in the absence of the
Governor with an immense audience. The
Fair lasted through the week and was
thronged most of the time. The weather
for one or two days was rather unfavorable,
yet the showing of attendance was good.
The cattle, horses and poultry surpassed
other years, and the mineral exhibits were
fine, and also in some other departments
the excellence of workmanship and of
articles excelled previous exhibits; but in
art, education and textile industries the
exhibit was not what it should have been
to show the material progress that has been
made in these special lines. No pains was
spared, however, on the part of the presi


[Column 1]
dent, Bishop J. R. Winder, and the Board
of Directors, to urge the people to bring
forward exhibits, and the able and efficient
secretary, S. W. Sears, worked during the
year with an indomitable will and persever-
ance to make the State Fair an unparalleled
success for Utah.
GENERAL CONFERENCE RELIEF SOCIETY.
	The General Conference of the Relief
Society was held in the Aseembly Hall, in
this city, Thursday, October 4, commenc-
ing at 10 a. m., president Zina D. H.
Young presiding. Meeting opened by
singing “Now let us rejoice,” etc., led by
Prof. C. J. Thomas, Mrs. Ida Daynes
Cannon accompaniest. Prayer by President
M. Isabella Horne. Singing, “Let us pray
gladly pray,” etc. Roll called, responses
from seventeen presidents, eleven counselors
and seven by other representatives.
Minutes of the April conference read and
approved.
	Miss Lutie Grant sang a solo very sweet-
ly, title, “One Sweetly Solemn Thought.”
	President Young made an address of wel-
come and of greeting to the sisters in her
usual motherly way. She rejoiced to see
their faces and appreciated their presence.
Spoke upon the sacrament and urged those
present not to neglect the privilege of par-
taking of the sacrament, and to put aside
all unkindness and they would receive the
blessing promised. Sister Zina spoke
beautifully upon humility and faith and
reverence for those who bore the holy
priesthood. She remarked that we none of
us know what the Lord has in store for us,
therefore we should be united and humble,
that we may be prepared for all the events
of Providence, for we must be tried in all
things.
	President Mary L. Woolf, Alberta Stake,
Canada, was glad to see the faces of the
aged sisters, those whom she had known
and had heard of all her life. She realized
they were growing old and feeble and
needed the support and help of younger
women. Sister Woolf felt we as sisters en-
joyed the greatest blessings of any women
on the earth, in connection with Mother
Eve, in bearing the souls of men, though
we feel very weak sometimes. Spoke of
her twelve children and thought tho’ we
might have trials and afflictions, we should
bear them heroically and ask for strength to
overcome. Referred to herself and counsel-
ors visiting the Stake, accompanied by
Sister Card, who was now an Aid in the
society. They felt very happy in visiting
from place to place, and encouraging and
stimulating the sisters to good works and
renewed efforts in the path of duty.
Thought we should teach our children the
benefit of prayer and the use of the conse-
crated oil in administering to the sick; re-
lated some instances concerning this in her
own family. When the Savior was on the
earth He said to Peter “Knowest thou me ?”
And when Peter replied, Jesus said to him,
“Then feed my sheep.” The speaker felt
we should strive to carry out this saying of
the Savior by giving spiritual food to the
people and to the children.
	Counselor Sarah Jane Hostetter, of San
Luis Stake, Colorado, said their Stake was
more prosperous than it had ever been
heretofore. The sisters were erecting two
new buildings. In their Stake Dr. Ellis R.
Shipp had been teaching obstetrics and
nursing in Sanford, and she had done a
good work there. They enjoyed the

[Column 2]
EXPONENT and read from it in their meet-
ings. She felt they were improving. She
rejoiced to be in Utah and in visiting this
city, it was her first visit here. She had
only been twelve years in the Church, and
she wanted to learn concerning the work
and to retain all she could.
	Mary N. James, the representative from
San Juan Stake, said the sisters in Bluff
were going to build in the spring. They
had a good share of material on hand, were
going to build of stone, they appreciated the
EXPONENT, used it in their meetings. She
referred to the Navajo blanket she had
brought with her to send by Sister Emily
S. Richards to the National Suffrage Baazar
in New York City. It was a very elegant
one made entirely by a Navajo woman in
their settlement.
	President Ann C. Woodbury, St George
Stake, Utah, said she had visited all the
branches in the Stake except the White
River settlement, “which we intend visiting
as soon as I return home. We have some
wheat on hand and are helping in many
directions to build up the country. We
have been very successful this year in the
silk industry and we know that the words
of President Young are true in regard to
sericulture. It is an employment for our
children as well as the older people, and we
must teach habits of industry and economy
if we expect to be prospered in our efforts.”
	Sister Zina spoke a few words upon the
silk industry, urging the sisters to carry out
the counsel that had been given by Presi-
dent Brigham Young, and the Lord would
add His blessing to their efforts.
	Sister Caroline Stringam represented
Uintah Stake. In her remarks she referred
to a revelation of Joseph Smith, the prophet,
in the Doctrine and Covenants in regard to
those who have talents and hide them, and
realized that the Lord would call us to ac-
count for not making use of our opportunities
to do good. She spoke earnestly to the
young mothers upon the training of children
and gave some instances of how her own
methods had succeeded with young boys in
her own neighborhood. Felt that the souls
of these young men were precious in the
sight of the Lord. There were really no
poor to be cared for in that locality, but the
sick were properly attended to by the Relief
Society sisters.
	Congregation sang Doxology. Benedic-
tion by Professor C. J. Thomas.
	Afternoon session. Singing by the
Twentieth Ward Y. L. M. I. A. choir led,
by Mrs. Mary F. Kelly, Mrs. Ida Daynes
Cannon, accompaniest, “There Shall be
Showers of Blessings.” Prayer by Presi-
dent Thomas Ricks. Singing, “Some One
Will Meet at the Pearly Gates, Shall You ?
Shall I?”
	President M. I. Horne was the first
speaker. Her remarks were upon union
and faithfulness, the strict observing of the
Sabbath day, the partaking of the sacrament
in the right spirit, with clean hands and a
pure heart; and “we are laying the found-
ation for a great future hereafter. We
must pursue a course that will elevate
humanity. While we are doing this chari-
table work we must seek to benefit all that
are in need, but be wise and careful in our
judgment of all matters.”
	Sister Horne then explained about the
class in nursing, referred to what had been
done in the past by Dr. Roberts and the
good work she had accomplished. Prayed
the Lord to bless the sisters in their labors
and said many other good things.

[Column 3]
	President Sarah J. Cannon was delighted
to see so many of the Mothers in Israel.
Felt we had a great deal to do and we needed
wisdom and the Spirit of the Lord to aid us
at all times. She had felt very much blest in
her labors in the Relief Society. She had
been much interested in the minutes of our
last conference that were read in the morn-
ing session. We must teach our children
the Gospel, know where they are, do this
duty and not leave the others undone. Let us
do all our duties, pay our tithing and be
charitable. Sister Cannon remarked that
she had been acquainted with all the
prophets since the days of Joseph Smith and
knew of their divine mission and teachings.
	President B. W. Smith felt happy in
meeting with so many of our good sisters
and hoped the time would come when we
would have more, realized we were on the
increase. “We want the young women
and young girls. I was young myself, not
twenty, when I joined the Relief Society at
the first organization. We want to teach
our children to be honest, truthful and in-
dustrious, pay our tithing, keep the Sab-
bath day holy and have our children attend
meetings and partake of the sacrament.
And, sisters, let us keep out of debt, not be
using other people’s means. We want to
build a house for the Relief Society in this
city, bring our mites together and see what
we can do towards it. The Lord will bless
us in our undertakings if we perform every
duty.”
	President Emma S. Woodruff reported
the Granite Stake. They had fourteen or
fifteen branches, one new branch recently
organized. They had visited all around.
She was glad to have the opportunity to
testify to the work in which we are engaged.
“Let us be consistent Latter-day Saints,
keep the word of wisdom and do our Tem-
ple work. Liked to see the Temple full doing
the work for those behind the veil. Hoped
to be faithful and true in all things.
	Counselor Mary Bagley, Bear Lake Stake:
“As Sister Lindsay, our president, was not
well enough to attend this conference she
wished me to represent the Stake. Al-
though our president is seventy-seven years
old she made the trip around the county
this summer. The Lord has blest her in
many ways. She has presided now twenty-
one years and I feel blest that I am able to
travel around with her. There is no
greater blessing than to be Mothers in
Israel in the Relief Society.”
	Sister Lindsay knew Joseph Smith and
frequently bore testimony to his divine mis-
sion. “I pray the Lord to bless these aged
sisters who have been so valiant for the
truth.”
	Sister Emily S. Richards showed the
ladies the Navajo blanket which had been
sent from San Juan County, to be on ex-
hibition at the Woman Suffrage Bazaar in
New York City in December. She invited
the sisters to assist in the effort being made
by the Utah Woman’s Council, by giving
five cent donations, which would be grate-
fully received or any article suitable for
such a purpose that might be on sale in the
Bazaar to increase the fund. Also spoke of
the circular letter published in the Deseret
News October 3, and thought the young
ladies should encourage this undertaking.
	Sister Julina L. Smith spoke of our chil-
dren and their home training and habits.
Felt it important they should go to Sunday
School and attend meetings. Educate our
boys for missionaries and our girls to make
good wives and mothers. She had recently


[Column 1]
returned from a visit to Canada, and had
met there an English Lord and his wife
and party. Told us how punctual they
were in keeping an appointment they had
made with our people to meet some of our
folks and dine with them, even though they
had to ride and drive through a heavy rain
storm, both going and returning. His
Excellency remarked, “We made an ap-
pointment and we would not break it.”
Sister Smith felt the people were prospering
there and were serving the Lord. The
Relief Society, the Y. L. M. I. A. and the
Sunday School were all visited, and were
doing a good work. The vegetables raised
up there were very large and excellent, it
was easy to raise them. The people had
also started fruit trees. The Saints were
enjoying their religion and trying to make
an honest living; and meantime they are
making themselves comfortable homes as
circumstances will permit. They are not
without recreation, such as dancing parties
and simple amusements.
	President Mary A. Call, of Bannock
Stake, stated that the Stake had only been
organized a little over two years, and they
felt the need of the visiting sisters to give
counsel and encouragement. They had
saved some grain and had some granaries.
They had found it a good plan to line the
granaries with tin. Dr Ellis R. Shipp had
been there and taught a class in obstetrics and
nursing. She felt these classes were needed
in Zion. Spoke encouragingly upon the
progress women were making everwhere.
	President Louisa B. Benson, Oneida
Stake, said they had been called upon to
donate a hundred dollars towards fixing up
the Academy and they had complied. She
was sure they would do their share in any
undertaking to benefit the society. Spoke
very kindly of the retiring president, and
said that they valued her services and would
like to have the benefit of her counsel and
experience.
	Star Valley Stake was represented by
Annie K. Kennington, who said: “We have
a new president, Sister Alice J. Call, our
former president having moved away. We
have not a large Stake, we visit every six
months. We can’t boast much of our
grain, ours is a stock raising country. We
have one house of our own belonging to the
Relief Society. We have never yet had a
visit from the leading sisters, but anticipate
one sometime, we shall be very glad to
have them come to see us.”
	Pocatello: President Eliza M. Oram said:
“During the two years I have presided we
have visited the Stake throughout three
times. We have ten wards and three hun-
dred members. We are in a scattered con-
dition, though we travel by team and we
are united in the work. We had a visit
from Sister Grant and Sister Stevenson and
we enjoyed their visit very much, they have
the spirit of progress. We feel there are
not enough young women who appreciate
the work of this society. Brother Nuttall
came and went through the Stake with us.
Our sisters are not rich and money is scarce
with us, our sisters cannot give as those
who are working for a salary. I feel to
bless the sisters of the General Board. Sis-
ter Grant prophesied to us when she was
there and her words came to pass in less
than a month.”
	Bingham Stake, President Emma J. Ben-
nett: “We have visited all the places this
last spring except one. We have to travel
over seven hundred miles and do it all by

[Column 2]
team. I believe we are converting our
brethren up there. At first they seemed
afraid and did not come into our meetings,
but now we have a great many of them who
attend. They really did not know the
benefit of the Relief Society until they had
one in their own locality. We have had
Sister Stevenson’s counsel and instruction
we have had in our visits a wonderful por-
tion of the Spirit of God in our meetings in
the various places. I am pleased that we
are to have a building of our own, every
Stake will be interested in this, we are all
working for the same cause.”
	The Young Ladies’ choir sang a very
beautiful hymn, “Labor On.”
	The secretary gave out notices of meet-
ings and explained some important matters.
	President Thomas E. Ricks, of Fremont
Stake, gave a good report of the sisters’
labors in that place. He was thankful for
the Relief Society, and that we were to
have a place to meet in suitable to our
needs. Referred to the faithful labors of
the older sisters, some of them who had
been faithful since the first days of the
Church.
	President Zina D. H. Young: “If I had
the power to bless you, my sisters, more
than you are blest, I would do so; but the
blessings of heaven are with us. I always
feel like a babe in the hands of my Father
in heaven. Sisters, never speak a word
against the authorities of this Church. May
we each of us have wisdom to impart the
true principles to our children and to our
companions, and to the world, I rejoice in
the spirit that has filled this house,
the sweet peace, the loving forbearance of
our Heavenly Father, that takes us into
sacred nearness to Him. I pray that we
may all come together again in the name of
Jesus. Amen.”
	President Jane S. Richards: “I have had
several invitations to speak today, but wished
to hear from “Sister Zina” and others. I
never get tired in a Relief Society meeting.
We have had a glorious time today in this
conference, and I wished to wait until every
one else had spoken. I have enjoyed the
meeting very much, and have felt happy
and had enjoyment in hearing the reports
and in listening to the mothers, and
especially to Sister Zina. It is a great call-
ing to be a mother, and to train our children
whether we are mothers or grandmothers.
I feel as some of the sisters do who have
spoken about partaking of the sacrament.
Sister Horne spoke of this. I think we can
all improve in this matter. When we think
of the love of our Father while we are par-
taking, and we want our children with us.
I have admired very much all who have re-
ported, especially the one who spoke of the
rough boys. I must tell a little story: One
day there were four little boys quarreling,
calling each other bad names as I was going
along the street. I stopped a minute and
talked to them. I told them I was sorry to
hear such language and tried to persuade
them to make up, ‘but,’ I said, ‘I am going
on now and I’ll be back in ten minutes.
You little boys partake of the sacrament. I
shall be obliged to report you to the Sunday
School unless you make up before I come
back. When I returned they had made it
all up. We ought to teach all these little
things. What a favor and privilege it is to
live when Temples are built and the Lord
will accept of them and we have the oppor-
tunity of doing a work for our dead friends.
Sister Smith said she liked to see us in the

[Column 3]
Temple, she has the spirit of it. When I
went for my grandmother I left her name.
Brother Edlefson said, ‘No, do for her
yourself,’ I was gratified to have the feel-
ing that my grandmother was by me and
followed me. We have everything to en-
courage us. When Jesus was upon the
earth He worked among the poor, just as
we do when we are trying to do the work
He did. Weakly people, when they go to a
good meeting, come home feeling well and
lifted up in their hearts to go on in doing
good. May the Lord bless us and keep us
safe, is my prayer.
	Conference adjourned until April. 1901.
Singing, “God be with you till we meet
again.” Benediction by Sister Elizabeth J.
Stevenson.
		E. B. WELLS, Secretary.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


REAPERS’ CLUB.
	The Reapers’ Club have been for some
time studying American history and our
members have given interesting papers on
the subject, commencing with an account of
the very early times as recorded in the
Book of Mormon and containing the history
of our land up to the landing of the Pil-
grims and early settlement of Massachusetts.
	We have had one paper and talks on the
American Mound Builders and others on
noted American poets, writers, etc. Some
account has also been given of our eminent
women, among them being Martha
Washington, wife of the first president of
the United States; Eliza R. Snow, the gifted
poetess, author of the inspirational hymn,
“O my Father,” and Miss Clara Barton, the
noted philanthropist, with a description of
the Red Cross work.
	We have listened to some papers and
lectures on the lives and educational methods
of Pestalozzi and Froebel in regard to
Kindergarten work, etc., and talks by our
own club members. A few scientific sub-
jects have been briefly dwelt upon, namely,
electricity, and a sketch of T. A. Edison,
the inventor. One of our charter members,
Dr. R. B. Pratt, gave a very instructive
lecture on the construction of the eye.
			J. A. DRUCE, Cor. Sec.


BIOGRAPHICAL SKETCH.
	Mary D. Israelsen was born in Kasfjor-
den, Nordland, Norway, August 9, 1836.
Her parents being poor, she early learned
to labor. She was always noted for her
obedience and industry. Her parents were
religiously inclined and were members of
the Lutheran Church. She was, therefore,
from early childhood a member of that
church.
	On October 25, 1855, she was married to
Telleo J. Israelsen, of the same place. In
1861 she embraced the Gospel, her husband
having joined the Church some time before
She was the only member of her father’s
family that ever accepted the Gospel and
joined the Church. Remarkable enough,
her husband was, also, the only member
of his father’s famiiy that has ever joined
the Church.
	In 1864, together with her husband and
children, she emigrated to Utah and settled
in Hyrum, Cache County, where she ever
afterwards resided. In the early 80’s both
her husband and oldest son were on foreign
missions at the same time. This son has
since performed another mission in his
native country, Another son just returned


from Norway in July, where he had been
laboring as a missionary.
	Her husband died December 11, 1897.
She departed this life September 2, 1900.
She was the mother of twelve children, five
sons and seven daughters. Of these three
sons and five daughters survive her. She
had thirty-eight grandchildren, twenty-
nine of whom still live. One of these is
on a foreign mission at present. She also
had one great grandchild at the time of her
death
		RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT.
	Our beloved sister, Mary D. Israelsen, has been
a faithful worker in the Relief Society of Hyrum
for nineteen years.
	Whereas, God, in His infinite wisdom has seen
fit to call from this sphere of action  our beloved
sister and colaborer in the Relief Society,
	Resolved, That we mourn in her death a faith-
ful worker, a wise and loving mother and a true
Latter-day Saint.
	That we esteem her exemplary life and her
faithful labors among the needy, worthy of the
emulation of every member of our society.
	That we mourn and sympathize with the chil-
dren, grandchildren, relatives and friends, and
realize that our Father doeth all things for the
best and that we must be submissive to His will.
				LUCY WILSON,
							President.
			ELIZABETH CRITCHLOW,
			FINETTE WILLIAMS,
							Counselors.
	GEORGINE RALPH, Secretary.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


OBITUARY.
SKETCH AND RESOLUTIONS.
	Resolutions adopted by the officers and mem-
bers of the Emery Relief Society in honor of their
beloved sister; Beata Burton Hansen, who de-
parted this life after an illness of one month with
typhoid fever. Sister Hansen died July 26, 1900,
at her residence in Emery Co., Utah. She was
born in Helmstad, Sweden, December 14, 1838.
She embraced the Gospet of Jesus Christ of Latter-
day Saints in her native home and emigrated to 
Utah in 1863. She was married at Manti City,
Sanpete Co., in 1863, to Peter Hansen. She was
the mother of ten children, six sons and four
daughters, eight of whom survive her. They were
all present at her funeral except one son who is
filling a mission to the Northern States. She
along with her husband was called to leave their
home in Sanpete, which they did, and go and help
to build up what is known as Castle Valley, in the
year 1880. They helped to build up three settle-
ments, known as Castle Dale, Ferron and Emery.
	Sister Beata was a good, faithful sister. She
was a mother to the orphan and a friend to all
who knew her. She acted as counselor in the
Emery Relief Society for a long time and after-
wards as a teacher in the Relief Society. She
never tired in doing good. 
	Sister Hansen was conscious to the last and
called her children to her bedside and bade the
all good bye, then passed peacefully a[-]ay.
	Resolved That as our Heavenly Father has
taken our beloved sister and friend to her heavenly 
home, that we desire to emulate her noble example
and that we herewith express our heartfelt sym-
pathy for her husband, her children and grand-
children. Be it further
	Resolved, That these resolutions be placed on
the records of the society, a copy be sent to the
family and one to the WOMAN’S EXPONENT for
publication.
				ELIZA O. JORGENSEN, Pres.
				LUELLE CHRISTENSEN, Sec. 
HATRIET CAMPKIN YOUNG.
	It is with sincere regret we record the death of
one of our faithful sisters in Three Mile Creek,
Box Elder Co., who departed this life on August
30, 1900.
	Sister Harriet Campkin Young was born at
Biggleswade Bedfordshire, England, on the 23rd of
May 1852. She was the daughter of Isaac Campkin
and Martha Webb.
	In the spring of the year 1856 Brother Campkin
and family started for Zion and arrived at St.
Louis in safety. While there Brother Campkin
was taken down with typhoid fever and in a short 
time succumbed to that disease. The widowed
mother with her family of five small children con-


tinued their journey, crossing the plains with
Captain Willey’s handcart company, arriving in
Salt Lake sometime in October of the same year.
After a short stay they came to Box Elder Co.,
locating at Three Mile Creek, where they have
since resided.
	Sister Harriet accepted the principal of plural
marriage and became the wife of Thomas Young,
December 23, 1878. She was the mother of four
children, all of whom survive her. We deeply
sympathize with them in their bereavement, know-
ing they have lost a faithful wife and loving
mother.
	Sister Harriet has been identified with the Relief
Society from its organization, and has ever been
an earnest worker in the cause of truth. At the
reorganization of our society. August 15, 1891, she
was chosen a teacher, which position she held at
the time of her death. She magnified this calling
with credit to herself and honor to her sisters.
Her good counsel and instruction will long be re-
membered by them.
	Naturally of a quiet, retiring disposition, yet she
was always at her post of duty and on hand at
every call. She was a true and faithful Latter-
day Saint and as such we mourn her departure,
yet feel to acknowledge the hand of our Father,
knowing “He doeth all things well,” and we
feel to say “Sister, Rest in peace, all is well, your 
reward is sure.”
			NELLIE ROBERTS, Secretary

R. S. REPORTS.
GRANITE STAKE.
	Afternoon session commenced at 2 o’clock
p. m., Counselor Mary P. Young presiding.
Singing, “Now let us rejoice.” Prayer by
Counselor Jenson, Mill Creek Bishopric.
Singing, “Guide us, O Thou Great Jeho-
vah.”
	At this meeting Sister Wheeler, president
of the Stake Primary, her counselors and
Counselor Jenson, of Mill Creek, were also
on the stand.
	Counselor Mary P. Young introduced
Sister Alma Ash as having just returned
from England and wished her to make a
few remarks.
	Sister Ash: “I have been on a visit to my
friends. I was the only one of my family
that had joined the Church. My parents
thought it a disgrace to be a Mormon.
Since then all my mother’s family have
joined this Church.” She spoke of the in-
fluence so different out in the world to that
we have at home among the Saints. She
visited the Sunday School and many of the
Saints while there. Met Sister Wood-
ruff’s daughter and son-in-law and had a
pleasant visit with them.
	President Yates, of Millard Stake said:
“The few minutes I stand before you I
want your faith and prayers, as I wish to
be guided in the right direction. I feel
very thankful that the Lord inspired the
Prophet Joseph to organize the Relief
Society.” Encouraged the young mothers
to train their children in the faith of the
Gospel, it depended on the mothers to first
begin with themselves and then with their
children. Apostle Lyman gave us a mis-
sion, for the presidents of the Relief Society
to send and get all the young women and
young mothers to become members, and
labor with the non-tithing payers and teach
the children tithing. She spoke of the care-
lessness of mothers in not trying to set a
proper example, and the great influence it
had upon their children.
	Singing by the choir conducted by Brother
Hill, “Kind words are sweet tones of the
heart.”
	Sister Armeda S. Young spoke of the
necessity of our gaining the confidence of
our children while they are young and
teaching our girls the value of virtue, and
the influence they can wield over the young

[Column 2]
men for good by persuading them to shun
all evil habits.
	Counselor Katharine H. Brockbank
wished to learn and train her heart in the
truths of the Gospel. “We miss Sister
Woodruff very much.” Spoke of her 
mother, Sister Howard, and the desire she
had to emulate her example.
	Counselor Mary P. Young said some of
the remarks of Sister Armeda Young had
brought reflections to her mind that she	
wished to mention. “We have great re-
sponsibilities as mothers. A remark of
President Brigham Young’s was brought
forcibly to my mind, ‘Mothers, if your chil-
dren go astray the Lord will hold you re-
sponsible.’ This has weighed upon my
mind for a long time. Sisters, are we
doing our duty ? Do we do the little
things? Encourage our children? Get
them ready for their Sunday School and 
Primary meetings? This is our duty.”
She spoke of the good instructions given in
the General Conference of the Relief Society.
“One more item comes to my mind—that
of punctuality and obedience, as Brother
Horne spoke of in the days of Noah.” She
then excused President Emma Woodruff to
the conference
	Sister Maria B. Winder: “I feel it an
honor to belong to the Relief Society sisters.
I think they are the best people in the
world.” Spoke of the life of the Prophet
Joseph Smith and the great good he had
done.
	A solo was rendered, with much sweet-
ness and skill, by Miss Crismon.
	Sister Wheeler, President of the Primary
of the Stake, spoke of the responsibility she
felt in her position along with the Relief
Society and the interest parents should take
in their children. “It seems to me that all
the women’s organizations come under the
Relief Society, they are helps to that organ-
zation.” Felt as Sister Young had remarked-
that the responsibility of our children rested
upon the mothers. Announced the Primary
conference to be held in this place the fol-
lowing day. Wished the mothers to attend
and see what their children were being
taught.
	Sister Zina Cannon, president of the Y.
L. M. I. Association of the Stake, spoke of
the relation of the Relief Society M. I. and
Primary to each other. Had once been a
teacher in the Relief Society. Felt that the
young ladies left too much of the hard work
to the older sisters. Thought that the
mothers should know where their daughters
were at nights and not allow them to go
with bad, rough young men, but they
should be home at night; and the young
ladies should not countenance that which is
wrong in young men. Spoke of the inno-
cence of our young girls, and the danger
they were in by going with bad boys. Re-
ferred to her young girlhood days. Hoped
that we would all work unitedly together.
	The president of South Taylorsville re-
ported her ward.
	Brother Thomas Alston said: “One thing
about the meeting I have regretted all day
is that some of the secretaries have failed in
sending in reports. There is a maxim,
that says, ‘Whatever is worth doing is
worth doing well.’ If you have not made
out your reports you have not done your work
well. It is a fact that we are too careless
in these matters. Referred to Holy Writ.
Spoke of our being on hand to do all our
duties on time. It is not those who say
Lord, Lord, but those who will do the
works will gain the reward.


[Column 1]
	Bishop Hamilton, of Mill Creek: “I re-
member a few years ago on my way to Re-
lief Society I met a brother who had just
come out of the penitentiary. I invited
him to come with me. In his remarks he
said, ‘My dear sisters, I love every one of
you.’ I might say the same. I see my
dear mother and a great many good women
here. I have made quite an effort to get
here, but I have been working in the ser-
vice of the Lord. He said we should take
an interest in all the organizations. My
dear sisters, may God bless you and may
we become more acquainted with each
other and with the principles of our re-
ligion.”
	Counselor Mary P. Young: “It has been
moved and seconded that we return a vote of
thanks to the good sisters who have pre-
pared and furnished us with refreshments.
	Singing, “Hail to the Prophet.” Benedic-
tion by Counselor Mary B. Hamilton.
			ARMEDA S. YOUNG, Sec.
		MALAD STAKE.
	The Malad Stake Relief Society held its
quarterly conference at Fielding on Sunday,
August 12, with Stake President Lucinda
Hoskins presiding. On the stand, of the
General Board, were President Jane S.
Richards, Stake President Lucinda Hoskins
and counselors, Secretary Eliza A. Hall and
a good representation of the presidents of
the different branches.
	Meeting opened by the choir singing
“Come, thou glorious day of promise.”
Prayer was offered by President O. C.
Hoskins. Continued by singing “Come
ye that love the Lord.” The minutes of
the previous conference were read and ac-
cepted.
	President Lucinda Hoskins felt happy to
see so many of the sisters in conference;
called for reports from the different branches.
	President Annie Sawyer reported Malad
city; President Jane A. Harris, Portage;
President Eliza A. Ward, Washakie;
President Hannah Griffiths, Samaria;
Counselor Sarah Rudd, Plymouth. All
were in excellent condition, a good spirit
prevailing in each branch reported.
	President Jane S. Richards said, “We do
not desire to speak only as we are dictated
by the Spirit of the Lord. The Relief
Society was organized for the purpose of
taking care of the sick and of those that
cannot take care of themselves. We think
and worry too much over our work and do
not pay enough attention to our spiritual
affairs.” She had never missed anything
by attending her meetings. “We should
divide our means with those less fortunate
than ourselves. It would be well for the
presidents to hold meetings at the home of
some of the sisters that never attend meet-
ings. It has been done and with the very
best results.” She admonished the teachers
to visit all the sisters in each ward and try
to get them to attend their meetings in the
Relief Society, and would gain rich bless-
ings thereby.
	Bishop Henry Hess, of Fielding, esteemed
it a glorious privilege to meet in the Stake

[Column 2]
Conference. “There is a great glory in
store for those sisters who will live humble
and take the advice of those dear sisters that
are over us.
	Myron Richards, Bishop of Riverside, al-
ways enjoyed the instruction given in the
Relief Society conference by these dear sis-
ters who are laboring so diligently for the
upbuilding of the kingdom of our Father in
heaven.
	President Lucinda Hoskins adjourned
the conference until 2 p. m. Meeting
closed by singing “Should you feel inclined
to censure.” Benediction by Stake Counse-
lor Wm. H. Gibbs.
	Afternoon session opened by the choir
singing “High on the mountain tops.”
Prayer was offered by Elder E. Wilcox.
Continued by singing “Again we meet
around the board. ” The sacrament was
then adminisiered by the brethren. Minutes
of the last afternoon conference read and
approved.
	On motion of Christina McCrary, seconded
by Lucinda Hoskins, Jannette Parkinson
was sustained treasurer of the Malad Stake
Relief Society.
	President Alice A. Smith reported the
Fielding Ward; President Lucy Mason, St.
John; President Julia Richards, Riverside;
all in good running order, the sisters alive
to their duties.
	Stake Counselor Christina McCrary and
Secretary Eliza A. Hall then spoke a short
time.
	Sister West, of Ogden, felt thankful she
had a name and standing in the Church and
kingdom of God. Spoke of the healing
ordinances and many other subjects.
	President Jane S. Richards spoke at some
length on the benefit the young ladies
would derive by joining the Relief Society.
We should be willing to make many sacri-
fices in order to keep our children in the
Church. Asked the presidents of associa-
tions to visit the different branches and be-
come acquainted with each other. Asked
God to bless and unite us all together, that
we may be saved in the kingdom of God.
Stated that President Zina D. H. Young
sent her love to all the sisters in Malad
Stake of Zion and would have loved to have
been in our conference.
	President O. C. Hoskins made excellent
remarks, bearing testimony to the instruc-
tions that had been given in our meetings.
The choir sang “Lord, Thou wilt hear us
when we pray.” Benediction was pro-
nounced by Elder Frank Walker.
			ELIZA A. HALL, Sec.
