SOMETIMES.

Sometimes we find a pearl upon the shore,
	Of perfect tint, and form of untold worth, 
And there beside the ocean’s ceaseless roar,
	We marvel such a gem could be for earth.

Sometimes there floats on ether’s ozone wave,
	A fragrant breath, as from an unseen flower,
As if it were Immortal Hand that gave
	The luscious increase from Elysian bower. 

Sometimes we hear a sweet, entrancing strain, 
Breathed forth in twilight’s holy, soothing hour,
Which thrills the finest tendrils of the brain
	As by electric and enchanting power.

Sometimes there comes to us so fair a day, 
A sky so blue, so free from darkening cloud,
It seems a brilliant meteor gone astray,
	While all our senses sing and praise aloud.

Sometimes we find a perfect summer land, 
Where balmy breezes bear the fragrant breath 
Of ever blooming flowers, where nature's hand
	Doth ne’er o’er spread the earth with nature's
		death.

Sometimes we meet in life a noble friend,
	Who true and faithful proves e’en unto death,
Our inmost natures seem in one to blend
	As roses with the summer zephyrs breath.

Sometimes in life the human heart may know
	A love devoted, fervent, true and pure;
Affection that will beam with ne’er unceasing
	glow, 
Through all life’s fitful changes we endure.

Sometimes there comes to us so great a joy— 
A bliss so fraught with holy peace and light—
A happiness with none of earth’s alloy,
	Which sets all mortal cares and fears to flight.

Sometimes we have emotions which transcend
	All other blissful feelings here on earth,
’Tis when we know that God is our enduring.
	Friend,
	And through His love we’ll gain Celestial birth.

ELLIS R. SHIPP.


[Column 1]
A THANKSGIVING INCIDENT.
	Horace, I’m going home to Thanks-
giving tomorrow, it will be our last in
New England, if we go West in the spring,
and I must spend it in the old home with
mother and the folks,” said a timid young
bride to her husband, who could not think
of refusing her, tho’ he knew his mother
would object, which she did very strongly.
But young Mrs. H. was determined, tho'
saddened by the opposition; or did she
have premonitions of the future which she
could not comprehend. Be that as it may, 
we shall see what followed.
	Thanksgiving morning came after a
sleepless night, for so sensitive was the
young bride that an unkind word or even

[Column 2]
a look was enough to bring tears to her
eyes, so pitifully young and tender-hearted
was this bride of a few months.
	The breakfast table presented some ap-
pearance of approaching festivities, with
evergreen vines and bowls of winter
flowers, and the indispensable savory
meats and pumpkin pie. But tho’ the
family urged the young couple to remain
with them, Mrs. H could not consent, and
soon the merry sleigh bells were jingling
at the door, and off they flew over the hills
and down the accustomed road by the pond
where skating was already beginning, for
the weather was cold in those days and
snow and ice were plentiful.
	The sleigh ride was exhilarating and the
gloom wore off as they gaily sped along,
every house, every tree was familiar and
they talked of the village people as they
sped over the country roads, but ever and
anon came to the young wife the forebod-
ings of the future leave-taking of the old
home and friends, the forest, through
which they drove on their way reminded
her most of all of what it would be to leave
the dear old woods and giant trees under
which she had so often sat and dreamed
wide awake of the happy days to come.
“And now were these day dreams to be
realized?” was her thought as she sped
homeward.
	Looking at the house and trees beautiful
in the white snow, out came the dear
ones to welcome her, and her thoughts
were, "This is a day for giving thanks.”
	We are all here except one or two
whom we expect later because of the dis-
tance,” said the dear mother. “We’re
glad to see you here, we were fearful you
would be detained at home. Now we
shall all be together once more before you
take that long journey Westward.”
	Those were not the days of fast trains
and electric cars. People came from miles
away in old-fashioned coaches or one
horse chaise to celebrate festive occasions
and in the winter time sleighs were plenti-
ful, everybody used them.
	Through the great, wide rooms adorned
with hemlock boughs and evergreen vines,
with the scarlet berries, the young wife,
led by the little ones, wended her way,
pausing before the great fire of pine knots
to take off her wraps, for the staircase was
cold after the long ride in the frosty morn-
ing air. Already the younger children
were plying her with questions and telling
her how they missed her when gathering
the evergreens and ferns to decorate with.
	“We didn’t know where to find the pret-
tiest vines, such as you always brought
home other years, and we had such a
tramp around by the great rocks we’ll
never care to go again even in summer
time if you’re not here to show us.”
	“Come and look at the table, Sarah, you
will see what a  Thanksgiving dinner
smoking hot is ready, only waiting for
William and Eliza, with little Isadore,”
and before the party reached the North
room, never used except when tables were
set for many guests, because too large to
warm with only a fireplace, another sleigh

[Column 3]
came jingling its bells up the lane, and out
flew the occupants, and wild with delight
came little Katie and all the crowd to greet
with real thanksgiving the new arrivals.
	Soon all were seated, the mother having
arranged them all in order on each side
with the eldest married son at the head of
the table to carve the steaming roast turkey
while she served the vegetables, taking
sp cial pains to have the baked beans pip-
ing hot from the large brown earthen pan
in which they had come from the great
brick oven in the kitchen. The mother
herself pronounced the blessing and the
feasting was graced with the usual New
England Table Talk of bounteous harvests
and Thanksgiving events, entertainments,
sleigh rides and balls.
	The dinner over the family gathered into 
the long living room where the air was frag-
grant with the perfume of spruce and hem-
lock. Outside the snow was falling, but
little heed was paid to the storm, for around
the blazing fire the young people and chil-
dren kept up a constant stream of lively
conversation, laughing, jest and song,
nothing could bespeak the genuine enjoy-
ment and goodfellowship of the household
more than this scene presented oh that
Thanksgiving in the old home.
	But we must hasten on to tell our story
and not linger over details, however
agreeable that may be to us.
	There was a sort of feeling, a premonition
among the guests that this was a farewell 
party, and each one tried to shake off the
foreboding that seemingly darkened their
hearts and the house itself even tho’
lamps were lighted and the pine knots blazed
higher as the evening advanced. Around
the hearth in a half semicircle the family
group gathered, and stories of frolic and ad-
venture were told to dispel the gloom, and
old, old songs were sung and childhood’s
memories recalled, and as it grew late, the
little ones were put to bed with prayers
upon their lips, and the ring around the
fireside broken in upon, when on a sudden,
in the middle of singing “Lochinvar,” the
joyous music of a band burst upon the
evening air; the doors were flung open and
in came the jolly crowd of musicians and
their friends. It broke the spell and dis-
pelled the gloom and for a few hours sweet
strains of music filled the rooms and glad-
dened all hearts, and was indeed a chorus of
thanksgiving and praise. What we owe to
music is indeed beyond language to express.
The young people and the band had come
in unbidden to salute the bride, not having
had the opportunity to serenade the bridal
party at the time of their wedding as they
had gone away immediately to the adjacent
town, but hearing they were at the old
home for Thanksgiving, and also being
aware of their intention to go West in the
Spring, this was an opportune time, and
the music and revelry was kept up to a
late hour, so that there was no further
chance to indulge in the “blues,” or to bid
a melancholy adieu to those who had come
long distances, except in haste, as one after
another the sleighs drove away after it was
past the midnight hour.


But as coming events cast their shadows
before, so it was with those assembled there
at that time, and they parted disguising
their real feelings in pity for each other.
Byron says:
	“Lets not unman each other—part at once
	All farewells should be sudden when forever
	Else they make an eternity of moments.”
	Never again did those assembled on that
evening all meet together. Out from under
that roof tree, where so many happy days
had been spent, so many festive occasions
celebrated, they went, and no doubt the
picture lingered lovingly in their memory
long years after. It is now more than fifty
years, and the vision is in the mind of the
writer still vivid. The wintry scene with-
out; the winter decorations within; the blaz-
ing fires; the New England dinner; the
generous hospitality; the jokes, the songs,
the merriment—and over all the shadows of
the future; the broken home; the scattered
flock; the desolate hearthstone.
	Where are they now? Some sleep in
warrior’s graves, who were babies then; and
one beside the sea in the far-off Indies, the
mother in an unknown grave on the broad
Western prairie, and others died at home
and their graves are marked by monuments
of stone; and some of the older ones still
live with large families of their own, and of
these a few are aged and feeble, looking for-
ward to the brighter and more complete
Thanksgiving, when they shall meet the
blessed mother and the loved ones gone be-
fore.
	Many joys have crowned the lives of some
of those assembled on that day, although the
world is full of sorrows and of mourning,
yet there have been many melodious days
and happy hours, and looking backward
over life’s changing, shifting scenes, one
cannot regret, but must rejoice that such
family meetings occur to link more closely
together kindred ties, even if the shadows
do hang over them.
	A New England Thanksgiving lives in
the memory of the writer one of its most
beautiful, ideal pictures; and may the cus-
tom which originated with our Pilgrim
Fathers, and which has become general
throughout the United States, be perpetuated
forever.
AUNT EM.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT.

R. S. REPORT.
PANGUITCH STAKE.
	Panguitch Stake Relief Society conference
convened in the Tabernacle August 24,
1900, Stake President Elizabeth DeLong
presiding.
	Present on the stand besides the Stake
officers were Sister Margaret A. Caine, who
had come especially for the conference,
President Cameron, Bishop A. Hiller and
counselors, Bishop Culbert King, Patriarch
Joseph L. Heywood, beside in the congre-
gation many prominent men of Panguitch
and other wards.
	After singing, and prayer by David
Cameron, the roll was called. Minutes of
previous conference were read and accepted,
also the order of business in accepting the
resignation of Sister Barbara Myers and
sustaining the new officers, which was made
part of the records. A vote of thanks was
tendered Sister Myers for many years of
faithful service as Stake President.

[Column 2]
	Mary B. Heywood reported the Panguitch
ward. Said the March 17 anniversary was
well observed and had the effect of putting
new life into the society for a little while.
	President Sarah Howell reported Circle-
ville. Felt they were doing fairly well
under the circumstances.
	President Mary T. Wilcox reported
Escalante. They had seventy-five members
and an average attendance of thirty. The
teachers gave good reports.
	President Delilah Rowan reported
Marion. Their society was small but com-
pared favorably with those already reported.
	President Isabelle King reported Kings-
ton. Some young girls had lately joined.
The sisters were all good and willing to help
whenever called upon.
	President DeLong was pleased to see so
many present. Had visited all the societies
except Marion, would go there later. Had
no Mothers’ clubs but were all mothers or
would like to be, and ought to be qualified
to fill any position in the gift of the Gospel
or the gift of the government. Wanted to
give the time to Sister Caine, who had
come to talk to us, prayed the Lord to bless
her to this end.
	Sister Caine felt pleased with all she had
seen in regard to the society here. The
Lord had never given a law restraining the
Latter-day Saints in gaining knowledge.
The Prophet Joseph studied diligently all
the time and at his death spoke three dif-
ferent languages besides his own tongue.
Wished to extend the greeting of Aunt
Zina and counselors, also Sister Wells, who
are just as interested as they can be in all
the sisters. Wanted to talk on the real ob-
ject of society work this afternoon, as it had
been inferred there would be more present.
	Counselor David Cameron felt the society
was a great place for learning, looked upon
it as a society of [-]otherly queens. We are
equally interested in Father’s work, men
and women, and would hardly know how to
get along without the Relief Society in
time of sickness and need. Prayed the
Lord to bless the society everywhere.
	Congregation sang "Zion stands with
hills surrounded." Prayer by Bishop King.
	Afternoon session. Conference recon-
vened at 2 p. m. After singing and prayer,
Bishop Allen Hiller said he knew of no
organization that was of more importance in
the ward than the society, nor more alive
and active.
	Report of 1899 was read and approved.
	Sister Permelia Hatch then reported
Mammoth society. Their ward was nearly
broken up on account of the reservoir flood.
	Sister Caine wanted the sisters to under-
stand the purpose of the organization, no
other can take the place of this. It was to
qualify them for high and holy positions.
Mothers can better prepare for family
prayers than any one else, also to instil the
principle of tithe-paying upon the children.
Retrenchment as taught by President Young
did not mean altogether in dress, but in
cooking good and wholesome food and less
pastry, and in all our home work to find
time to improve the mind. If we sustain
those who are placed over us the Lord will
bless aud preserve us against the judg-
ments to come. President Woodruff
approved of the International Council of
Women and of the Relief Society affiliating
with both the National and International
Councils. Asked the sisters to sustain the
EXPONENT.
	Singing “How firm a foundation.”

[Column 3]
	General and Stake officers were sustained
unanimously.
	Counselor Alice J. Cameron felt weak,
but well paid in attending meeting.
	Sister M. Isabelle Henrie felt we had re-
ceived much valuable instruction and with
the help of the Lord intended to improve
her opportunities better.
	Conference adjourned for six months.
Singing “Praise to God, immortal praise.”
Prayer by James Houston.
H. A. CROSBY, Sec.


A WONDERFUL MANIFESTATION.
	The history of the terrible disaster that
overtook Galveston, Texas, a while ago,
would be incomplete without a brief note of
the experience of some “Mormon” Elders
who labored as missionaries in that city at
the time of the flood which brought death
and destruction to so many souls. Accord-
ing to a letter received at the News office,
there were four Elders in the city, viz:
Peter Norton, of Pima, Ariz.; Horace L.
Johnson, of Thatcher, Ariz.; Samuel Shaw,
of North Ogden, Utah, and Heber N.
Folkman, of Plain City, Utah, and they all
were preserved as by a miracle, while
thousands fell on the right hand and thou-
sands more on the left.
	These Elders had then been laboring in
Galveston for the past three mouths and a
half. It seems, however, that their testi-
mony had been rejected and they were about
to leave when the flood came.
	On the fatal night they were stopping
with a family named Daniels. As the
water rose and reached the house, these
good people fled panic stricken to higher
ground, leaving the Elders alone in the
house. These went upstairs and commended
themselves to the care of divine Providence.
When the morning broke and the desola-
tion wrought stood forth in all its awe-
inspiring details it was found that the house
in which the Elders were, was the only one
in the entire neighborhood that had escaped
damage.
	It seems these missionaries were during
the entire night of horror unconscious of the
extent of the visitation. Elder Norton in
his letter to the News says part of the night
they slept, though the water was surging
and the wind was raging all around them.
When in the morning they went out to see
what damage had been done, they were
astonished to learn the extent of the disaster.
They saw corpses everywhere. Men,
women and children had met death either
by drowning or under the falling houses.
There was no difference now between rich
and poor, white and black. Death had
placed them all on about the same level.
	The miraculous preservation of the house
in which the “Mormon” Elders had been
given shelter that night must have been a
powerful testimony to some of the people
who had rejected their message. And there
are many such testimonies both in the his-
tory of the Latter-day Saints’ mission, and
in the experience of Church members. If
they were written they would form a volume
of pecular interest. To the eye of faith
they would demonstrate that God is the
same today as he was in the time of Moses
and the first Apostles.
	The memory of their experience will
probably never entirely fade away from the
minds of those Elders; it should be to them
an ever abiding testimony, and a subject of
thanksgiving;—Deseret News.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
THE MOTHER.
It was April's blossoming spring,
They buried me when the birds did sing,

Earth, in clammy, wedging earth,
They banked my bed with black, damp girth.

Under the damp and under the mould,
I kenned my breast were clammy and cold.

Out from the red beams, slanting and bright,
I kenned my cheeks were sunken and white,

I was a dream and the world was a dream,
And yet I kenned all things that seem.

I was a dream, and the world was a dream,
But you cannot bury a red sunbeam.

For though in the under-graves doom-night
I lay all silent and stark and white,

Yet over my head I seemed to know
The murmurous moods of wind and snow,

The snows that wasted, the winds that blew,
The rays that slanted, the clouds that drew

The water-ghosts up from the lakes below,
And the little flower-souls in earth that grow.

Under earth in the grave’s stark night,
I felt the stars and the moon’s pale light,

I felt the winds of ocean and land
That whispered the blossoms soft and bland.

Though they had buried me dark and low,
My soul with the season’s seemed to grow.

From throes of pain they buried me low,
For death had finished a mother’s woe.

But under the sod in the grave's dread doom.
I dreamed of my baby in glimmer and gloom.

I dreamed of my babe, and I kenned that his
	rest
Was broken in wailings on my dead breast.

I dreamed that a rose-leaf hand did cling;
Oh, you cannot bury a mother in spring!

When the winds are soft and the blossoms are red[-]
She could not sleep in her cold earth-bed.

I dreamed of my babe for a day and a night,
And then I rose in my graveclothes white.

I rose like a flower from my damp earth-bed
To the world of sorrowing overhead.

Men would have called me a thing of harm,
But dreams of my babe made me rosy and warm.

I felt my breasts swell under my shroud;
No star shone white, no winds were loud;

But I stole me past the graveyard wall,
For the voice of my baby seemed to call;

And I kenned me a voice, though my lips were
	dumb:
Hush, baby, hush! for mother is come.

I passed the streets to my husband's home;
The chamber stairs in a dream I clomb,

I heard the sound of each sleeper's breath,
Like waves that break on the shores of death.

I listened a space at my chamber door,
Then stole like a moon-ray over its floor.

My babe was asleep on a stranger arm,
"O, baby, my baby, the grave is so warm,

"Though dark and so deep, for mother is there!
O, come with me, from the pain and care!

"O, come with me from the anguish of earth,
Where the bed is banked with a blossoming
	girth,

"Where the pillow is soft and the rest is long,
And mother will croon you a slumber song.

"A slumber song that will charm your eyes
To a sleep that never in earth-song lies!

"The loves of earth your being can spare,
But never the grave, for mother is there."

I nestled him soft to my throbbing breast,
And stole me back to my long, long rest.

And here I lie with him under the stars,
Dead to earth, its peace and its wars;

Dead to its hates, its hopes and its harms,
So long as he cradles up soft in my arms.

[Column 2]
And heaven may open its shimmering doors,
And saints make music on pearly floors,

And hell may yearn to its infinite sea,
But they never can take my baby from me.

For so much a part of my soul he hath grown
That God doth know of it high on His throne.

And here I lie with him under the flowers
That sun-winds rock through the billowy hours,

With the night-airs that steal from the murmur-
	ing sea,
Bringing sweet peace to my baby and me.

	This poem was suggested by the following passage in
Tyler's Animisni. The pathetic German superstition that
the dead mother coming back in the night to suckle the
baby she has left on earth, may be known by the hollow
pressed down in the bed where she lay.


PRIMARY OFFICERS REUNION.
    The Primary officers of the Jordan Stake
held their first annual reunion on Thursday,
October 25, at the ward house, Crescent, at
2 p. m. Brother Hyrum Goff, of the Stake
presidency, and many of the Stake officers
of the Relief Society and Y. L. M. I. A.
were present, and about a hundred of the
Stake and local Primary officers.
    The program was a very interesting one
and consisted of songs, recitations and
music, interspersed with brief addresses by
some of our visitors.
    President Hyrum Goff in the toast, “The
Ladies,” told of the many amiable qualities
of the fair sex and Primary officers, in par-
ticular. Much good counsel and en-
couragement was given by all the speakers.
    At the close of the program delicious re-
freshments were passed around by a com-
mittee of young ladies and a delightful time
was enjoyed by all present.
		    LUCY M. HILTON, Stake Sec.


U. W. P. C.
	The U. W. P. C. met in the EXPONENT
office, September 29, 1900. Opening
prayer was offered by Dr. Romania B.
Pratt. Roll call responded to by original
sentiments. Mrs. McVicker spoke on the
educational work. The president reported
from the Club Council. A new president is
to be elected for the council next month.
All the Kindergarten work was re-
ported. The Kindergarten has been opened
and all club members who can are asked to
contribute. Emma Jenson and Phebe C.
Young are appointed as members on that
committee.
	A paper was then read on the Phillipine
Situation, by Grace Anderson. Emma
Jensen read a paper on Women’s Congresses
in Paris. Aimee C. Schiller read a well
prepared paper on Current Events, and a
short discussion followed.
	It was decided to hold our business meet-
ing October 1, at 3:30 p. m., to prepare for
the annual meeting and the election of
officers.
	Meeting adjourned. Prayer by Phebe
C. Young.
				GRACE ANDERSON, Sec.


MISS ANTHONY continues in about the
same state of health, neither better nor
worse.

    THE Paris Exposition report states that
50,000,000 visitors were admitted during
the time it was open.

    IT is reported that Holland has expressed
a wish to purchase on behalf of Kurger, for
the exiled Boers, a portion of German
Southwest Africa.


SCHOOL OF SELF-CULTURE.

		MRS. A. L. JOHNSON.

    To understand the proper method of
breathing is essential to health, and when
the breathing organs are in order we are in
possession of that abundance of life which
Jesus brought to earth when He said, “I
come that those that have life may have it
more abundantly.” Abdominal breathing
is essential to life and happiness, and with
a knowledge of hygiene and rhythmic
movement of the body all disease can be ex-
pelled from the system, and we can gain,
to a great extent, the elasticity of youth.
    But before this much desired condition
can be brought about we must have con-
trol over the joints and muscular system,
giving the body its freedom. Knowledge
of self-culture, rightly applied, will enable
us to regain our lost strength and power,
giving strength and easy movement to the
body, developing that physical beauty
which nature has endowed her children
with. Most of our ailments, especially
throat and lung troubles, are brought on by
a lack of knowledge in breathing. When
we breathe only from the lungs we are soon
exhausted, the supply being limited.
    A knowledge of abdominal breathing
gives an increase of force and vitality to the
entire system undreamed of. With a little
practice every movement becomes natural,
giving easy grace. As health returns to
the body the eye becomes bright, the form
erect, the voice sweet and clear. Few
realize the actual benefits to be derived from
a knowledge of self-culture. Mrs. Johnson
has a book on the various subjects that gives
full instructions for hygienic treatment and
exercises. Those at a distance can write
for the book and she will be pleased to give
any instructions by letter or make arrange-
ments for classes in or out of the city. For
further particulars call or address Mrs. A.
L. Johnson, 503 and 504 Templeton 
Building.


NOTES AND NEWS.

	MARK TWAIN has come home again after
a journey around the world. He is in good
health and fine spirits at the age of sixty-
five years.
	THE death of Charles Dudley Warner,
which occurred October 1, has taken away
one of the foremost literary men of the age,
but besides his loss to the world of letters,
he was a man of highly developed social
quality, much more genial than is usual in
men of such rare gifts and scholastic attain-
ments.
	The Suffrage Bazaar in Madison Square,
New York City, will be one of the unique
exhibits of the closing year of the century.
A distinguishing feature will consist of
booths and tables in honor of the most emi-
nent women in the suffrage movement.
Mrs. F. S. Richards, president of the Utah
Woman’s Council made the collection in
Utah for the Bazaar display, and forwarded
the articles to New York. Mrs. Gilmer
will be in charge of the Utah Booth or
Table. Governor Wells, of Utah, sent a
beautiful doll handsomely dressed, and the
ladies of the Council furnished an elegant
Navajo blanket. Other articles of fancy
work and pictures by home artists were
donated, also some books by home authors.

SALT LAKE CITY, UTAH, NOV. 15 & DEC.1, 1900.


[Column 1]
		THE NEW CENTURY.

    Whatever may be the fate of the world a
century later one cannot help feeling and
realizing that the United States, the Repub-
lic of America is marching onward to a
high destiny. American women have
brought about great changes in the last fifty
years—the advance towards a higher civil-
zation, a purer and better government is
largely due to the women reformers and
educators, not only here, but in other
countries.
    The rapid strides that are being made in
the arts and sciences are not altogether
man’s achievements, woman; too,  has
striven courageously in these directions and 
against popular prejudice, making it so
much the more difficult to attain a degree o[-]
excellence equal with man.
    Development goes forward and ideas ac-
cumulate as the world grows older, and
men and women who seriously contemplate
life and action, and who engage in any pur-
suit or calling of a lofty nature, enter upon
it heart and soul and seem to be lifted by a
sort of mental and spiritual enthusiasm into
higher ranges of sublimity than were known
a hundred years ago.
    Woman’s future in business will be large-
ly co-operative, for assuredly the movement
is gaining ground steadily; but men and
women will unite generally in these lines,
and women can be trusted in many matters.
They consider business matters more
seriously. With them a steady occupation
is almost a religion, conscience enters
largely into their daily life. Already
women have covered a broad field in this
direction, in figures and mathematics many
of the sex are experts. As clerks, steno-
graphers and accountants they are more
generally reliable than men, for the simple
reason that they are less given to habits of
dissipation, smoking, drinking, and so forth,
and consequently less inclined to make errors.
    The conscientious delicacy and tact
natural to the feminine nature fits them par-
ticularly for some of the professions, especial-
ly the medical, and it cannot be gainsaid
that so far where opportunity has been given
women in the professions, they have shown
themselves, in almost every instance, on
record, equal to the occasion, however per-
plexing or difficult, or even embarassing
the situation might be.
    That we are now on the threshold of

[Column 2]
greater changes than have ever taken place
in the world’s history, one must believe
who watches the trend of events, and as-
suredly these wonderful changes are for the
uplifting and betterment of mankind and
all humanity. The spiritual vision is clear-
er, greater light and knowledge has come
through research and the development of
higher intelligence, a nd we are approaching
a sublime crisis in the dawning of the new
century.
    That the future business of the world
will be conducted by men and women in
co-operative movements and institutions,
and that thus greater progress will be made
industrially, there can be little doubt, be-
cause both are equally interested in the suc-
cess of enterprises and the strong elements of
man’s nature can be modified by intuition
and the painstaking and persistency of the
feminine in woman. There will be greater
improvement along all lines of business and
trades when women not only give advice
and lend their influence, but when they
actually manipulate side by side the modus
operandi of the great schemes now almost
entirely in the hands of men.
    Domestic service, hygiene and sanitation
will become so perfect in household matters
that the drudgery of cooking will no longer
be a terror to the young bride, her own
knowledge of the science of life and of living
will be her safeguard, and the young mother
will never know (by experience) the bitter
trials of the mothers of the psst centuries,
for knowledge is power and the night of
ignorance is fast passing, and the morning
of science is dawning and the next gener-
ation will bask in its light, and it may be 
that man will so order his life that he may
live to the age of a tree, as the scripture
foretells.


[Column 1]
   REPORT OF THE NATIONAL COUNCIL.
    The National Council of Women of the
United States held its annual business ses-
sion in Minneapolis Minnesota, this year,
by invitation of the Local Council of that
city. These meetings were set for Novem-
ber 13, 14, and 15, 1900, but from the
multiplicity of business to be transacted, it
was not possible to close the executive
meetings until Friday, November 16, at
one p. m., and even then some matters
were left over to be finished by the
General officers.
    These were indeed most interesting
meetings of this great Council, and the
Minneapolis ladies proved their generous
hospitality and welcome, as well as sym-
pathy with the cause of woman’s work in
many ways, that gave the Council good
and lasting impressions of their warm-
heartedness and nobility; also of the Mayor
and citizens of that enterprising and rapidly
growing city of the far-famed Minnehaha.
    We regret that the weather was so in-
clement when the meetings closed that we
could not venture out to see those beautiful
falls mentioned by one of our greatest
American poets in the favorite poem
“Hiawatha.”
    On Monday, November 12, a luncheon
was given by the ladies of the Relief Corps
of that Post at half past one, at the Nicolet
Hotel, in honor of their General-President,
Mrs. Mary L. Carr, of Denver, and the
officers of the Council. Only a few ladies
had yet arrived, but we who had come in
early were royally welcomed. The parlors
were gaily decorated and the banquet pre-
pared was on a magnificent scale. Hand-

[Column 2]
some menu cards and a beautiful rose were
provided for each guest, and the tables ar-
ranged in the form of the letter T were set
for fifty persons. Mrs. Morrison, chaplain
of the St Paul Lodge, pronounced the blessing
and the most social geniality prevailed
among the guests. The president of the
Minnesota Post gave a tribute of welcome
to her superior officer, the General-Presi-
dent, Mrs. Carr, to which the lady replied
in fitting words of goodfellowship and love
for the cause for which they unitedly stood.
One or two others of the local Relief Corps
spoke, and officers of the Council, including
the writer, complied briefly with the polite
request. Mrs. Higbee and the other Past
presidents of the Local Council of Minne-
apolis each added a few choice words of
greeting and welcome, making the affair “a
feast of reason and a flow of soul.”
    The same evening a reception was given
by these ladies in the parlors of the Nicolet
Hotel, quite a number of gentlemen also
being present, Grand Army men and other
citizens mingling in the brilliant affair.
Many handsome costumes were worn and
decorations of the order and flags and ban-
ners were quite conspicuous. Patriotic
musical selections by the band; and fruit
punch was served by young girls in soft,
dainty silk and organdy dresses of the most
approved styles, with ribbons and laces to
match. It was a festive occasion and one
full of pleasant memories of the grand
women who are interested in the home and
the state of Minnesota, and the welfare of
the world. Such women are growing in
numbers throughout this great and free
Republic. Mrs. McCune arrived in time
for the evening reception, she having to
travel from Omaha on another route from
the writer.
    On Tuesday from four to six, another
reception was tendered by Mrs. Walker, of
Minneapolis, and other local ladies, to the
Council, at the handsome residence of Mrs.
Walker, who is famous for her philanthropic
work and for her personal help in the way
of culture to the public. Every day, we
were informed, her gallery of choice paint-
ings is thrown open so that the students
and people desiring to avail themselves of
this privilege of cultivating the eye and
heart by gazing upon the workmanship of
great artists, have a wonderful advantage
in this private collection, many of them
gems of great value.
    Here, too, the ladies were entertained
uniquely by a band of girl musicians, who
discoursed sweet strains of music during
the entire time, and the music room and
dining room were beautifully decorated
with a variety of lovely flowers, daintily
scattered about here and there. Tea, coffee
and cocoa, with wafers, candies and cake,
were served to all guests who could leave
the picture gallery long enough to partake
of the dainty refreshments. At the table
we had the pleasure of meeting Mrs. Has-
kell, whose father and husband are news-
paper people of the Boston Herald and
Minneapolis Tribune; Mrs. Holman,
daughter of the hostess, and widow of the
Reverend Dr. Holman, who took a tour
through Utah for his health a few years
ago. Mrs. Holman speaks with much
feeling of the kindness received from our
people in the south, and we very much en-
joyed a few minutes conversation with one
who had sojourned for a time among our
people and knew something of their cor-
diality to strangers. She mentioned


[Column 1]
especially the Woolleys of Kanab, Can-
nons and Brooks of St. George.
	The Local Council of Minneapolis also
gave a reception to the officers, delegates
and members of the National Council on
Thursday afternoon at half past three, in
the parlors of the West Hotel, the Coun-
cil’s headquarters. Mrs. Higbee, the
president of the Council, was hostess. Mrs.
May Wright Sewall, the president
of the International Council, was
present at this reception, but was obliged
to leave early for home. She, as well as
Mrs. Fannie Humphreys Gaffney, always
shine in brilliant assemblies of this kind.
Both have great charms and dress with ex-
cellent taste. The writer imagines that one
would know at a glance that Mrs. Gaffney
lived in the City of New York, both by her
dress and manners. Dr. Kate Waller
Barrett, although always in the black garb
of her calling, is a woman of the South, one
must know it by her Southern polish and
attraction. She carries the unmistakable
stamp both in manner and speech. Bril-
liant, witty and emotional and a wonderful
speaker. Rev. Annie Garlin Spencer is
another type with all the collegiate graces of
manner and correctness of language. Her
gray costume might make one suspect her
a Quaker, and she comes from Providence,
Rhodelsland; but instead of Puritianism she
holds most broad and liberal views on
religious questions and all others. Then
we have Mrs.Solomon, a pronounced Jewish
type, but with the grand and lofty aspira-
tions of the tribe of Judah concerning her
people, interested in all that makes for their
advancement, but especially in religion and
the Bible, a woman of strong character.
	Though, as our readers know, we are not
given to describing costumes, yet while
mentioning others Mrs. McCune, of our
own city, might with all due propriety be
spoken of, because she wore an elegant Paris
gown of black lace which suited her style
remarkably well, and was sufficiently con-
spicuous for the writer to observe, even
among so many elegant dresses.
	Mrs. Peck, the Vice-President, was not
able to be present, but there were presidents
and delegates from many of the National
organizations belonging to the Council,
some of whom we knew well and others we
had not known heretofore. Mrs. Mary
Newbury Adams, of Iowa; Mrs. I. C.
Manchester, of Rhode Island; Mrs. [-]iske,
of Minneapolis; Mrs. Lillian E. Hollister,
of Michigan; Mrs. Emma R. Wallace, of
Chicago, and Mrs. Flo Jameson Miller, of
llinois. Miss Bower, of Detroit, and Mrs.
Terrell of Washington, D. C., were women
we had known previously,some of them for
many years. The new acquaintances we
met more familiarly were Mrs. Olds, of
Elyria, Ohio; Mrs. Woods, Quincy, Ill.;
Mrs. Glazier, of Ft. Madison, Iowa; Mrs.
Burns, of Michigan; Miss Mannering, of
Chicago; Mrs. Parry, who gave us a copy of
her poems, and Dr. Cora Smith Eaton, of
Minneapolis, a schoolfellow and fellow-
graduate of Miss Babcock’s, who was de-
lighted to see us because we knew her at
home in Salt Lake, and sent loving mes-
sages to her by us.
	There is not space to tell all the good
things about the women of the Council, but
we must mention one or two facts. First,
that Mrs. Gaffney is a fine presiding officer,
just and courteous to all, with unfailing
patience and always punctual and knows
how to economize time. Her opening ad-

[Column 2]
dress was a masterpiece and a great credit
to the Council, as was the welcome extended
by Mrs. Leach and Mrs. Tuttle, of Min-
neapolis, and also that of Mayor James
Gray of that city. The Mayor dwelt
much npon women influencing public senti-
ment and their work in education, and on
the school boards, etc. Certainly one of the
ablest speeches made during any of the open
meetings, was on Tuesday evening, by
Professor Maria Sanford, of the University
of Minnesota. She elaborated upon the
larger life with broader purposes, if men
and women would work together upon
practical matters in the home, the city, the
state, the country and the world. Gave
practical illustrations of what cnildren had
been induced to do in cleaning and beautify-
ing that city, etc, when sufficiently aroused
by means of prizes offered and such like in-
ducements.
	At the gathering extended by the Local
Council, as at all the others, we met many
beautiful women, one cannot even remem-
ber the names, but it was an exchange of
greetings between women of a great variety
of faiths and from all points of the compass.
Music of the choicest kind; and flags and
flowers in available places gladdened the eye
and enlivened the scene. In a gathering of
this sort one notes the many highly intel-
lectual faces that make the ocoasion more
attractive than aught else. Unexpectedly
one meets with kindred spirits, and here
we met one of the Wells family of Wethers-
field and Hartford, also one of the Whitneys
of New England.
	Very little time can be given to social
gatherings when the Council meets for
morning, afternoon and evening sessions.
There are meetings to be attended and work
to be accomplished as well as speeches and
reports to be listened to, if one keeps in
touch with the spirit of the Council. News-
paper women seemed to enter most heartily
into the real merits of the Council, and to
comprehend its highest ideals.
	The objects of the Council have been
furthered at this meeting and we may look
forward to hopeful suggestions from these
able women. A telegram received from
Miss Anthony said: “I send greetings to
my daughters of the most comprehensive
organization of women, and the one in
which I have the greatest hope.”


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
EDITORIAL NOTES.
Prof. Evan Stephens has begun the com-
pilation of a Hymn Book for the
Primary Associations suitable to the needs
and understanding of the little folks, and
that will stimulate them to excel in vocal
exercises. We understand the hymns and
songs are by local writers and are carefully
selected.
We acknowledge with sincere thanks the
gift of the little pamphlet of the late Con-
ference and banquet, which is a brief sum-
mary of Primary work, though not given in
statistics, the papers and remarks show
plainly the progress of this beautiful work
among the children of Zion There cannot
be too much said of this noble undertaking
of these good sisters who freely, for the
love of the children, have taken hold of
this primary work which lies at the found-
ation of the lives of those who are to be
the very bulwarks of the Church in days to
come.
We are very much pleased to hear that on

[Column 2]
President C. O. Card’s birthday, Novem-
ber 5, of this year, the day was made a
social event at the Card home in Cardston,
Canada, and the commodious new brick
residence of the family was dedicated with
appropriate ceremony. May health, pros-
perity and all that tends to make life
valuable be and abide with President Card
and his entire family, and especially do we
rejoice that Sister Young Card has a home
where she can dispense the generous hos-
pitality in a social way which she has al-
ways delighted in doing.
Since our last issue of the paper visits
have been made by members of the General
Board and others. Cache Stake conference
at Logan, President Jane S. Richards and
Sister Ellen J. Stevens; Oneida Stake con-
ference, at Preston, President Susan Grant,
who also accompanied President L. B. Ben-
son, of the Relief Society of that Stake in her
visits to each of the settlements where she
has jurisdiction. Sister L. Lula Greene
Richards attended Tooele Stake conference,
at Grantsville. We have a letter from
President Ann C: Woodbury, who has
recently been visiting through the Southern 
part of St. George Stake visiting and hold-
ing meetings with every Relief Society in
the locality, and from others who have
visited at a distance, which will be published
later.
On Tuesday, November 20, President
M. Isabella Horne reached the great age of
82 years A party was given in her honor
by her daughters at the residence of her
daughter, Mrs. Minnie H. James. About
twenty of the sisters and intimate personal
friends were present, among them the
venerable sisters, Presidents Zina D. H.
Young and Bathsheba W. Smith, Sister
Julia C. Howe and other dear friends and
relatives. Many loving congratulations
were extended to the veteran Mother in
Israel and tokens of remembrance given.
Dainty refreshments were served and after
sweet interchange of greetings, and so forth,
Sister Horne blessed the sisters present, in
a song or chant in the gift of tongues,
which was very gratifying and inspirational.
Sister Zina gave the interpretation in her
sweet, motherly way and a general time of
rejoicing was enjoyed by all. The EX-
PONENT offers its best congratulations to this
aged mother and sister, and desires that she
may live many years yet, or so long as
she can retain her faculties of mind and
activity of body, or while life is desirable.


UINTAH STAKE.
Minutes of the 24th conference of the ‘
Uintah Stake, held September 6, 1900,
President Sarah Pope presiding. Singing,
‘‘Redeemer of Israel.” Prayer by Counselor
A. K. Bartlett. Singing, ‘‘We thank
Thee, O God, for a prophet.” Roll called,
four Stake and seven ward officers present.
Minutes of the previous conference read and
approved.
President Sarah Pope was pleased to
again have the privilege of meeting with
the sisters in conference, and said we had
with us two sisters from headquarters and 
no doubt all would be pleased to hear from
them.
The different branches of the society were
reported as being in very good condition. 
Sister Mary E. Irvine said she was not
here for pleasure, but because she was
called. President Joseph Smith turned the


key in behalf of women, and how they had
advanced since that time. We should look
after ourselves and not spend so much
time bothering our neighbors. When we
go before our Father in heaven He will not
ask us what everyone else has been doing,
but what we ourselves have done. 
Sister Susan Grant said one of the im-
portant missions of the Relief Society was
to store up grain and then take care that it
is not destroyed. We should always re-
member our prayers, for if we forget the
Lord in the hour of prosperity, He will be
slow to hear us in the hour of trouble.
Counselor Mary Hall thought the sisters
ought to turn out to meetings and hear the
instructions the sisters had to give them
after coming over such a road as they had
to travel.
Sister AbigailOaks said we often perform
duties because it is a duty, and not for the
love we have for the work. Children are
precious gifts from God, and we should
strive to train them so they will be able to
enter into the presence of our Father in
heaven.
Conference adjourned until two o’clock.
Singing, ‘‘Praise to the man who com-
muned with Jehovah.” Benediction by
Brother E. J. Longhurst.
Afternoon session. Singing, “Prayer is
the soul’s sincere desire.” Prayer by
Brother James H. Glines. Singing, “The
spirit of God like a fire is burning.” The
general and local officers of the Relief
Society were sustained.
Counselor A. K. Bartlett said we should
not neglect our duty and leave it for some
one else to perform. There is a great mis-
sionary work to be performed in the midst
of Zion. therefore how necessary it is for
mothers to understand the Gospel.
Sister Susan Grant said we should make
home the loveliest place on earth, so the
children would rather be there than any-
where else. It requires a great deal of
patience to raise a family of children, there-
fore we should pray for the help of the
Lord.
Sister Mary E. Irvine said some people
would spend all their time in training fine
animals and neglect their children. We
should train our children in love and not
anger. We should never find fault with
the Priesthood, and especially before the
children, it makes a bad impression upon
them.
President Sarah Pope felt to thank the
sisters for their visit and hoped they would
come again.
Conference adjourned for six months.
Singing, “Do what is right.” Benediction
by Brother Thomas Bingham.
ADDIE LONGHURST, Sec.


54 WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
THE PHILIPPINE SITUATION.
	The Filipino revolutionary government
and army no longer exist. In fact, there is
not a single regimental organization now to
be found. It is true armed bands of
Guerillas, more or less numerous, are har-
assing our troops and preventing the re-
turn of their own people to peaceful occu-
pations.
	Only a small proportion of the number of
natives have been in arms against us, the
rest are friendly and anxious for peace.
While they have suffered terribly it has not
been at our hands. In fact, the head men
of many of the towns captured by our
forces have voluntarily borne testimony

[Column 2]
to the good conduct of our soldiers. Even
such armed resistance as remains, although
it may be troublesome during the present
rainy season, will doubtless disappear on
the advent of the next dry season.
	Now the presidential election is over the
Filipinoes will no longer receive and dis-
tribute among the insurgents the anti-ad-
ministration speeches which are received by
the natives, who are strangers to the election
tactics in America. The distribution of
such material has led the natives to believe
that if Mr. Bryan should be elected the
American troops would be withdrawn, and
they would be left to govern themselves in
any way they might see fit. This, how-
ever, is a totally impossible condition. In
the first place, only a portion of Tagalos,
who number not over a million and a half,
have been fighting or working for indepen-
dence, the remainder of them, as well as a
majority of the other seven or eight millions
of the population, are favorable to us, and
the same is true of the foreign residents.
	The Filipinoes as a whole are certainly
not at present capable of establishing and
maintaining an independent government.
We have assumed obligations that must be
fulfilled. We have destroyed the governing
power of Spain which offered protection to
foreign property, foreign capital and foreign
inhabitants, and in some measure, at least,
had given the natives a semblance of civil
and municipal self-government. We are
bound in honor to substitute for it a govern-
ment which will give to foreigners a larger
measure of freedom and protection, and to
the Filipinoes not only more liberty, but a
more comprehensive and generous scheme
of self-government as well.
	General Otis was released from duty in
the Philippines at his own request. Ill
health was the cause of his leaving his post
where he had served so long and so efficient-
ly. General McArthur succeeded him on
May 1. General Otis approved a scheme of
municipal government before he left that
gives to the Philippines for the first time
the right of suffrage in the election of
officers and the establishing of town govern-
ment. Bishop Henry C. Potter has just re-
turned from Manila with the most un-
qualified praise of General Otis. He be-
lieves that we should stay there now, that
we could not get out honorably if we
would.
	The new Philippine commission has been
completed. Judge Taft, of Cincinnati, has
been elected chairman. Mr. Dean C.
Worchester, of the old commission, has
been retained and Judge Henry C. Ide, of
Vermont, General Luke E. Wright, of
Tennessee, and Professor Barnard Moses, of
California, have been appointed. The
commission is well constituted for organiz-
ing and establishing civil government in
the Philippines. They arrived in Manila
in June and announced that no attempt
would be made at present to supercede the
military executive. General McArthur is
competent to perform the duties of Governor
until the country is ready to receive a sys-
tem of civil administration. That Luzon,
at least, is not yet prepared for such a
change, is made clear by daily reports of
brigandage and armed resistance to
authority in very many parts of the
island. Organized insurrection is no longer
a fact, the archives of Aguinaldo's govern-
ment were seized by General Funston in
May. In June a far more important cap-
ture was made in the person of General

[Column 3]
Pis-del-Pilar, long regarded as the ablest
military leader in the Philippines.
	The work of our army in Luzon is de-
scribed by General Schwan in a letter
recently made public by the War Depart-
ment. The garrisons of the interior and
coast towns of Luzon are commanded by
young, energetic majors, who are leaving
nothing undone to perform with thorough-
ness the special task set them. This task
includes the suppression of guerilla bands,
and also open schools and establishing
municipal government. These things are
in course of accomplishment, the greatest
obstacle is the lack of confidence between the
soldiers and the inhabitants, which he be-
lieves will pass away when each class be-
comes better acquainted with the customs,
aims and standards of the other.
	The Philippine commission has chosen
Professor Fred N. Atkinson as superin-
tendent of instruction in the islands.  There
are 5,000 children in the city schools of
Manila, under the superintendency of
Professor George P. Anderson, a Yale
graduate. The teachers of these schools are
eighty-five natives, forty Panjards and
twenty-two Americans.
	On June 21 General McArthur promul-
gated an amnesty proclamation at Manila
under which many Filipino leaders have
accepted the authority of the United States.
General Pis del-Pilar, for example, is now
working harmoniously with General
McArthur, and has been traveling and per-
suading armed insurgents to accept the
amnesty terms.
			GRACE ANDERSON.


OBITUARY.

		       MARIA LOVE.

    It becomes our painful duty to record the death
of our dear sister and colaborer of the Second
Ward Relief Society of Nephi, Utah, Sister Maria
Love, who died October 7, 1900.
    Sister Love was born in Toronto, Canada, April
16, 1833. was a faithful Latter-day Saint, was
chosen a teacher in the Relief Society of Nephi at
its first organization in June, 1868, which office she
has filled true and faithfully up to the time of
her death.
   At a meeting one week before her death she
bore a strong testimony to the truth of the work
she was engaged in.

	One whom we love,
	One who had nothing but blessings for all,
	One who responded to every call,
	One who was ever so faithful and true,
	One who gave blessings to me and to you,
	One whom the angels will watch in her rest,
	One who is safe in the realms of the blest.

		RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT.

   Whereas, Death has come into our midst and
taken from us our honored sister whom we all
loved, an earnest worker and teacher of our Relief
Society, be it
    Resolved, By the members of the society that we
tender to the bereaved family our sincere sympathy
and earnestly desire that God will comfort them in
their affliction.
    That a copy of these resolutions be sent to the
family, a copy be placed on record and one be
forwarded to the EXPONENT for publication.
					    E. R. UDALL, Pres.
					    MAGGIE McCUNE, Sec.
Second Ward Relief Society of Nephi.
				
			SARAH G. B. SNOW.

    Again the grim reaper, death, has been in our
midst to summon one of the pioneer women of
Utah to her reward, Sister Sarah Gledhill Broad-
bent Snow, departed this life October 2, 1900. at
the residence of her son-in-law, Bishop John Das-
trup. The cause of death was heart failure, super-
induced by stomach trouble.
    Deceased was the daughter of Edward and Petty
Gledhill and was born March 31, 1833, in Oldham,
Lancashire, England, She embraced the Gospel


about 1850, and married Edward Broadbent in
1853. They emigrated to Utah in 1855, shared
with the Saints the hardships of the “grasshopper
war” and other times of scarcity incident to early
life in the valley. In 1863, while living at Gunni-
son, Sanpete Co., her husband was drowned in the
Sevier river, leaving her with two small children,
one of whom soon followed the father. In 1865
she married Bernard Snow, by whom she had two
children, one of them dying in infancy. Her
career has been a varied one, she living in many
towns of the State, where she has won friends for
herself by her integrity to the principles of the
Gospel, She came to this ward in 1879, and has
since been connected with the labors of the ward.
She was president of the Primary Association, she
also held the office of treasurer of the Relief Soci-
ety, and head teacher of the same. In 1896 she
was chosen counselor to President A. M. Dastrup,
of the Relief Society, which position she held at
the time of her demise.
    She leaves two children, seven grandchildren,
one sister and three brothers, besides numerous
other relatives and friends to mourn her deaʇh.
				  	      PAULINE DASTRUP,
					      DORA MALMQUIST,
								Committee.
Sigurd Co.. Utah,


WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


A GOOD WOMAN GONE.
She is not Dead but Sleepeth.
Caroline Hopkins Clark, wife of Bishop John
Clark, who departed this life October 30, 1900 at
the advanced age of 69 years, five months and 15
days, was born at Sutton, Warwickshire. England,
on the 16th day of May. 1831. She became a
member of the Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-
day Saints in the spring of 1849, and came to Utah
in the fall of 1866, settling in Coalville. She was
the mother of seventeen children, nine of whom
survive her—seven sons and two daughters—and
grandmother to fifty-one grandchildren and seven
great grandchildren. On the 22nd day of August,
1887, she was chosen president of the Upton Relief
Society, which position she held up to her death.
She was the daughter of Samuel and Ann Hopkins,
to whom were born three sons and seven daugh-
ters. At an early age she left the home of her
childhood for the Gospel’s sake—she being the
only one in the family to join the Church—and
came to Utah. Deceased was just and kind to all,
a loving wife, kind and indulgent mother and be-
loved by all who knew her, She died in full faith
of the Gospel with the hope of a glorious resurrec-
tion. The funeral services over the remains were
held from the meeting house on Saturday, Novem-
ber 3, and it was the largest attended funeral ever
held in Upton. The room was beautifully decor-
ated with flowers. President W. W. Cluff was the
speaker at the services. Twenty-three vehicles
loaded with sorrowing friends followed the remains
to their last resting place.
Just a few minutes before her death Mrs. Clark
repeated the following verse:
	Affliction sore, long time I bore, 
	Physicians were in vain,
	Till death did ease and God did please
	To ease me from my pain.
		CARD OF THANKS.
In behalf of my family I wish to heartily thank
all those who so kindly assisted in any way during
the illness and death of my wife. I also wish to
thank J. W. Simister and the members of the choir
for the excellent music rendered at the funeral.
May all be rewarded for the good they did.
							JOHN CLARK.


[Column 1]
AUTOBIOGRAPHY OF SARAH B. LAYTON
			(Continued.)
    By this time some of the inmates began
to be jealous of me, and talk about me and
do all they could. They said that I was an
impostor and that they dared not pass my
door; that I was a witch, and so on. But I
did not mind it for I had passed through all
that sort when I had first joined the Church.
I knew that friends had been raised up to
me in time of need by my Heavenly Father
and I learned to trust him.
    I asked the nurse if she would prop me
up when the minister came again, for I felt
as if I would like to talk to him. I prayed
that I might be given strength to talk

[Column 2]
and words to say; for of myself I knew
that I could say nothing, for I was entirely
unlearned. He came. The nurse propped
me up as I had asked and then I told him
that I wished to talk with him about my
religion. He seemed pleased and was as
kind as he could have been if he had been
my father. He had the windows raised,
for it was very warm, and then told me he
would like to listen if I was strong enough.
    I spent more than an hour in telling him
what there was in the Bible. A great many
things he said he had never seen in his
Bible, and thought it was from some other
book. But I told him that if he would
bring his Bible I would mark it for him so
he could read it. I had a synopsis of the
Bible with me and I knew that I could find
anything that I wanted.
    When he came again he brought his
Bible, but the first thing to be mentioned
was the Book of Mormon. He said that
the Bible did not say anything about such a
book. I asked him what Isaiah meant when
he spoke of the Stick of Judah and the
Stick of Ephraim that were to be made one
Stick in the hands of the Lord? He said
he had never seen such a statement in the
Bible. We had a long talk and he, when
he left, left his beautiful morocco bound
Bible with me. I had lost the use of my
lower limbs, but not of my hands, so I pro-
cured a pencil and set to work marking
passages and by the time he returned I had
about fifty marked for him. When he
came I handed it to him. As many as
thirty of the inmates at a time had come
and listened to our talks. I used to pray
that my memory might be strengthened
and that I might have wisdom that what-
ever I should say would be correct. On
his next visit, after inquiring about my
condition, he said: “My dear young girl, I
have lived to see the words of our blessed
Lord fulfilled in you, for he said, ‘These
things shall be hid from the wise and prudent
and be revealed even unto babes.’ I was
brought up and trained in college to be a
minister of the Gospel, but I never in all
my studies knew such words were in the
Bible; but I must confess that what you
have said is true.”
    One young lady made a remark thinking
to take me off what I was saying, but the
minister turned to her and told her I was
abundantly able to answer all questions,
and that if she could not be quiet to leave
the room. She did not bother me any more.
He was a constant visitor as long as I re-
mained there and his last words to me were
“God bless and spare your life. The doc-
tor has given me some hopes today.”
    A few nights before I left the hospital
two bright lights appeared at my window,
and I heard these words distinctly, ‘‘Arise
and depart from here; this place is polluted
and you will be destroyed, Joseph and
Hyrutn Smith.”
I told the night nurse and she said she
guessed it was an angel, for I had often
told her that angels did come to the earth
these days. How I prayed that the doctor
would send me home, for I feared to stay
there. When the doctor made his next
visit he told me that I would have to
undergo an operation, I told him that I would
never submit to that. To that he said,
“you may then go home, we can do no
more for you.” He looked at me and said,
“What do you want to live for? The fear
of death, or the love of the world ?”
My answer was the love of the world.

[Column 3]
He said that if I lived I would never be
any good to myself or to any one else.
Many of my friends came to see me and I
finally wrote a letter to my friends to come
and take me home. So after nine weary
weeks of torture and suffering I was given
a paper by the doctor stating that I was in-
curable. I was taken home and was given
a large supply of medicine. My mother
was told to report my condition occasionally.
    At the end of two weeks my mother went
to see the doctor, who asked if that poor
girl were still alive. He gave her some
more medicine for me. A few days after
that some of my old Methodist friends called
to see me, and while they were there I had
to take some of my medicine. One of them
asked me if I knew what it was I was taking.
I said that I did not, and then he told me
it was opium. I looked astonished at him,
for I had been told that he took a great deal
of that poison, and a more haggard and
sickly looking man it would be hard to find.
I thought that if I could not live without
taking that drug it was better for me to die.
I prayed earnestly that if my living de-
pended upon my taking that drug which I
saw made such ghastly looking sights, that
I might die.
    As soon as my strength permitted, I took
all of the medicine that I had and went out-
side and buried it and there covenanted with
the Lord that if He would spare my life that
I might be useful in the kingdom, I would
never take doctor’s medicine again.
    I was restored to health, and though-
fifty-five years have passed and I have had
much sickness to go through, I have never
taken any of their medicine to speak of.
    After I was restored, my companion in
the Church, Sister Sarah Martin, and I
ussd to distribute tracts for the mission-
aries through two different villages; we
made a round each Sunday evening. We
had three miles to the one place and two to
the other, but we never missed going sum-
mer or winter for years. Sometimes the
people would listen to what we had to say
and sometimes they would not; some would
tell us that they had burned those we left
last and they did not want any more: others
would shut the door in our faces, but
wherever the people would listen to us we
told them what the Lord wanted of them to
the best of our ability, and we had the
pleasure of seeing some of the fruits of our
labors.
    One night as I was walking with the
president of the Bedfordshire mission our
conversation was turned to the young man
with whom I had been keeping company.
I told the president that I would never
marry him in this world, for he was too
narrow-minded, I could never be happy
with him. The president told me that
Brother Underwood loved me as much as a
man could love a woman. “But,” said he,
“the half of a man you could love is better
than the whole of one you could not love.”
    This surprised me, and I asked him if he
meant by that that men had more than one
wife in these days.
    His reply was, “You must have caught
the spirit of it!”
    “Well,” I told him, “the Bible speaks of
men having more than one wife.”
    His only answer to this was that we have
the same Gospel that the ancients had.
    I could plainly see that if plural marriage
was a principle of the Gospel then, it must
of necessity be so now. That was the first
time I had heard it hinted at, that polygamy
was being practised in the Church, I learned


[Column 1]
little by little until I was fully convinced
that it was true. This was in 1846. My
sister was in Nauvoo at this time and when
I wrote to her I asked her if the men there
had more than one wife. She said they did
some of them, but it was not publicly
known to the world. My companion, Sarah
Martin, and I used to talk about it when
we were together, and read about it in the
Bible until we were fully satisfied about it.
We never spoke of it to any of our friends,
and we made a vow that if ever we did
marry we would marry the same man.
Time rolled on, but we never swerved from
our promise. We said that we would not
marry until we came to where the Saints
were. The young man with whom I had
kept company left his home and went to
London, but I had long since decided that
we two should never wed. At this time
that principle was not known among the
people there, but we knew of it, and had
set our minds on what should be our lot.
We had several chances to marry, but the
conditions were not what we wanted, so we
refused. 
	In the early spring of 1850, Brother
Christopher Layton returned from Cali-
fornia, where he had gone as a member of
the Mormon Batalion. We had known him
before he went to America. He had left
England in January, 1843, for Nauvoo, and
while there his wife had died leaving a little
girl one year old; this my sister kept for
him.
	One night there was an Elder at our
place who had just returned from America,
and we were very anxious that the people
should hear him, so we invited our neigh-
bors in. But instead of their coming with
a pure motive, they called in all of the
roughest element for miles around and
came to do mischief. As the Elder was
talking they began throwing stones at the
house. The house being made of concrete,
the plaster was soon knocked off and the
windows broken. They had formed two
lines along the path, thinking to catch the
Elder and some of the Saints and drag them
to the river and throw them into it. But
they were defeated, for we led them around
another way. They climbed over our live
fence and made their escape while the mob
waited.
	This was very hard on my father, for he
was not a Mormon and it worried him. The
mob continued throwing stones for some
time. Finally my father went out and
told them that the Elder was far away. This
he did for two reasons: one for the safety of
his property, and the other was to find out
who some of the men were. This he did.
The next day the landlord came and told
father that he would have to leave that
home. “For,” said he, “you see what
your wife and daughter have brought you
into.” I told father that the Lord would
help us to get another house. Before that
day was past he had secured another and
the following day we moved. The land-
lord saw what he had done, and sent word
that he was sorry for what he had done in
his anger. Within a year my father and

[Column 2]
mother moved back to the old home in
which I had lived all of my days so far.
	Soon after the return of Brother Layton
his sister sent for my mother and me to
come over, as he wanted to see us. We
were pleased to see him, as he had seen my
sister not long before. He told us that he
was going to return to Utah in the fall and
that he would be pleased to take me with
him. This suited me very well. The next
day he came over to our house and asked
my mother if she would give me to him for
his wife. This she refused, as she said she
did not believe in hasty marriages. He
asked me what I thought about it, and I
told him that my mother had always wished
me well, and that I would not go against
her wishes. He then told me that he should
go over to see Sarah Martin. I told him
that she would be glad to see him, as we
had been brought up close neighbors. He
asked her to be his wife and she consented.
She was about four years my senior. He
told us then that the Latter-day Saints be-
lieved in having more than one wife. We
told him that we knew that and had
understood it for a long time. He asked us
if we would be willing  to live together when
we should arrive in Utah. We told him
then that we had made that agreement long
since, and that we had no desire to break it.
	I did not tell my parents what I intended
to do, neither did Sarah Martin; but it was
understood between the three of us.
		(To be continued.)

A HOUSEHOLD OF WOMEN.

To describe a household of women I once
knew would be comical to the last degree,
if not so provokingly pitiful. There were
five rich widows (one of these women was
said to be worth ten millions) all belong-
ing to the same family, living in a hand-
some house in a fashionable part of New
York, paying heavy taxes without a word
to say in regard to municipal government;
with their maids and servants they num-
bered just one dozen women.
They had one colored man, with a head
the size of an apple, one eye and lame in one
leg. On the first Tuesday of every Novem-
ber, this gentleman, a kind of majordomo to
the establishment, represented these twelve
women annually at the polls, five of them
highly educated, the other seven able to
read and write, any one of them competent
to throw the little majordomo over the
wall; yet he was the only full-fledged citizen
of the Republic in this household. The
twelve women had not a word to say in re-
gard to the laws or lawmakers under which
they lived. They were practically slaves
according to what Benjamin Franklin once
said: “If a man’s property can be taken
from him without his consent he is a slave.
if he has no voice in the government under
which he lives, he is a slave.”
The “Fathers” said, “No just govern-
ment can be formed without the consent of
the governed.”
“Taxation without representation is
tyranny.”


Have these lofty sentiments any appli-
cation to one half of the people of this Re-
public, or are they mere glittering gener-
alities for orators in their fourth of July
orations or on the eve of their annual elec-
tions? We pause for a reply.
Women are constantly asked as to their
choice in the pending election. As there is
no mention of them on either the Republican
or Democratic platform as neither Mr.
McKinley nor Mr. Bryan has had the
courtesy to mention them in their prouncia-
mentoes, why should they feel the slightest
interest in either party? Their political
status will remain the same in the success of
either.
The questions of tariff, gold basis,im-
perialism, trusts, sink into utter insignifi-
cance compared with the emancipation of
forty millions of women, many of them
highly educated, the most virtuous and
law-abiding citizens of the Republic.
ELIZABETH CADY STANTON.

The Ballot in the Hands of the Women of Utah should be a Power to better the Home, the State and the Nation.
