CHRISTMAS REVERIES. 
1900.

The tremulous pulse of the universe
Throbs with "peace and good will to man," 
The millionaire opens his swollen purse,
Bestowing with bounteous hand,
While "Aids” and "Charities” swarm about,
In this noble work for the poor,
And we hear the merry childrens’ shout,
As they bound from the schoolhouse door, 
Off to their homes for a week of rest,
No studies nor books to annoy—
Entering sports with vigorous zest,
Think only of holiday joy.

The rich and the poor alike now plan
For their childrens greatest delight,
Their bank account or wages scan
While weary mothers work at night—
That none may be found that joyous morn
Without a stocking more than filled;
The table wel spread, the hearth made warm,
For the hungered, tired and chilled.
Many mysterious parcels hid
With warnings and shaking of head,
Of that trunk none must open the lid;
Each child must go early to bed.

How many dainty stitches enwrought
On trifles, the best one can give—
Interwoven with purest, most loving thought 
Which make the inanimate live,
Breathe to the heart more eloquent words
Than pen or tongue ere could express!
Music that’s sweeter than songs of birds,
Benedictions ever to bless—
Making us feel the greatest and best
Of all of God’s mercies to earth
Is Friendship and Love! frankly expressed,
How holy and grand are their worth!

While the tremulous pulse of the universe
Is throbbing with "peace and good will,”
Thus my soul is replete with reveries sweet,
Brightest hopes my being to thrill;
That the waning days of this century
May carry all doubt from the world;
That love among men forever may reign,
With the banner of peace unfurled,
The Millennial year be ushered in, 
That thousand years of love and peace,
When earth made clean, and free from mortal sin,
And souls from pain have sweet release. 
ELLIS R. SHIPP.


HER MOTHER'S CHRISTMAS GIFT. 
The room was bright with two electric
giobes, and at one end an open stove gave
out more light, warmth and cheer. The
furniture was not elegant, rather to the con-
trary, it was simple and inexpensive; but
the painted floor was covered with a large
rug evidently an old Smyrna with its bril-
liantly shaded colors; the chairs were rockers
and a low, easy couch rested between two
windows, piled with soft, downy cushions.
There were curtains, simple dotted lace
ones, and a low bookcase on two sides of
the room. Two pictures, art copies of
Turner, gave an air of exquisite refinement
to the simple room.
The lady who sat reading before the fire
was fitted to her surroundings; or rather,
her surroundings seemed a fitting frame for
the delicate, high-bred beauty which, now,
more than ever, surrounded her like a halo.
She was small and slender, with thin,
nervous hands on which sparkled several
rings; a face whose every line bespoke char-
acter and nobility, from the broad, low
brow, to the firm, yet delicate chin. The
gray hair waved away from the brow was
dressed carelessly yet artistically, low in the
neck. Her silken robe was neglige
in its cut, and fell away from the tiny
slippered feet.
The door of the room was opened quickly,
but quietly, and a sweet voice asked, “May
come in, auntie ?”
“Certainly you may, my dear. Close the
door on Jack Frost and draw up your stool
and come here by the fire.”
The young girl who entered was lovely
with youth, vivacity and all the details of
comeliness. Her bright blue eyes and pink
cheeks were not coarsely pretty, but the
coloring was as delicate as the petals of a
tea rose and the blue of the heavens be-
tween the stars.
She drew her ottoman close by the elder
lady’s chair, and after a moments silence she
said, wistfully:
“Auntie, I am very unhappy about my
mamma.”
“Why, Bessie, what’s the matter with the
lady mother ? Has she been taken suddenly
ill, or is she fretting over gifts for the
coming holidays, or what is the trouble?”
“There is nothing of that kind, auntie,
mamma is just the same as usual and
everything is all right at home. It is dnly
I—that is—well, I’ll, tell you, auntie,
all about it.”
“That’s a good girl; I knew there was
something on your mind lately, Pussy, and 
I have been waiting very patiently for you
to tell me what it was.”
“That’s just it, auntie, I don’t want to
tell you; that is, I have resolved--and re-
solved—not to tell you-but to tell
mamma—and, oh—I can’t!”
The last broken words brought a little
gush of tears and the girl leaned her head
on the friendly knee beside her and wept
silently, while the slender hands stroked the
pale gold hair and the soft voice comforted
and soothed the troubled girl.
“Dear me, Bessie, this looks almost seri-
ous. I am about to be alarmed. What
has happened between my pet and her dear
mother ? Come, dry thy bright eyes and
stop the little quiver in thy voice, and tell
me all about it from first to last.”
“Oh, auntie, its so easy to tell you things
and its so hard to tell my own mother, and
that’s the very whole of the trouble.”
The elder lady took the young face be-
tween her hands and looked a moment into
the deep blue eyes.
“Now, wait a moment, Bess, I hardly
understand. You must explain a little.”
“The girl put her own hands on the
slender ones pressed on her cheeks, and said:
“Dear auntie, I have been troubled for so
long because I feel that my own mother and
are not so near and dear as we ought to
be, as you and I are!”
“Oh!” softly said the elder lady, releas-
ing the girl from her clasp.
Bessie settled herself so that she could
look into the fire, and drawing the arm of
her companion about her neck, she went on,
sure of the loving interest of her listener.
“You see, auntie, I am not naturally very
talkative; I think I am very reserved. And
mamma is like that, too. Perhaps I should
never tell any one my secrets, if you had not
won my love and confidence years ago. I
can’t tell why it is, (but the patient listener
knew why) that I can come to you and open
my whole heart to you. I haven’t thought
much about these things till I have read
and heard so much lately in our meetings
about girls confiding in their mothers. I
can fancy what a lovely thing it would be
for mother and me to be like you and I are,
but I don’t know how to do it. Mamma
loves me, of course I know that, and we
can talk about our new clothes and going
to the theatre and our shopping, and all
that; but when I have tried once or twice
lately to speak to her about other things—
well—about my beaus, you know, or things
like that—I can’t get past the first word,
and mamma never helps me a bit; she just
acts more embarrassed and colder than I do.
Now its so different with you, and I can’t
tell why!”
The elder woman felt the tears filling her
eyes, and there was a suspicious ache in her
throat, but she said quietly:
“Yes, Bessie, I can understand all you
say. I have often told you to confide in
your mother."
“I know you have, auntie,” answered
the girl, eagerly, “and I am anxious to do
it; but cau one help one’s temperament?
And both mamma’s and mine are against it.
I am sure I don’t know what to do.” 
“Bessie dear, in my girlhood days I was
a quiet, reserved and silent child; I spent the
whole time at books and music. I had little
human companionship, for my own mother
was a reserved and quiet woman. I never
remember of sitting on her lap, or of any
caress, but a very occasional kiss, in my
life. I was full of romance and strong im-
pulse, and my circumstances and disposition
led my maiden feet to the brink of many
a precipice but I never lost my footing,
thank God for that!”
The girl reached up her hand and drew
the other hands down to her lips while she
kissed them over and over.
“I learned many lessons through my
own sufferings and trials. And chiefest of
all has been the fact that all humankind
longs and seeks for sympathy and com-
panionship. He who supplies that need to
the starving heart is the one who gains
uppermost influence and power over that
heart. And so I have studied how to win
the love and confidence of all creatures,
especially the young. And, Bessie, of all
my dear young friends, you come nearest to
being like my own child.”


[Column 1]
	Bessie stroked the slender hands about
her neck, and pressed them to her glowing
cheeks.
	“I have had no children, dear, you know
that; but you will never know what that
fact means to a woman’s desolate heart yet
the sweet love of Christ and the dear reli-
gion we both love so well has filled the ach-
ing void, and God has answered my prayer
in some measure and given me the love and
tender confidence of my young friends.
Now, dear, I am deeply touched by your
distress about your mother. I can see how
it has all come about; but for her sake, for
your own, and that you may not be yourself
such a mother to your children, you must
break the wall of reserve and find entrance
uto your mother’s heart of hearts, while
you open the deepest recesses of yours to
her.”
	“You always understand, auntie, and know
just what to say, don’t you?”
	The tender, aged lips trembled with
emotion, and the lady sighed as she said:
	“If so, God teaches me, dear.”
	The two sat silent a long while, and the
coals dropped down in the open grate, and
grew gray in their ashen covering.
	At last the lady said:
	“Bessie, what are you going to give
your mother for a Christmas gift?”
	“I don’t know, auntie; I had not thought
much about it. I dare say I shall buy her
something, for I am in school, you know,
and can’t do fancy work. Anyway, I hate
fancywork. I would rather make a whole
dress than to work a Batteuburg handker-
chief.”
	“Well, dear, there are so many good and
profitable things to do, tnat no one need
spend their time in distasteful employment.
I have a little suggestion which might be
made into a gift for the dear mother, and at
the same time accomplish the dearest wish
of your heart.”
	“What is it, auntie? I am all expectancy
You always have such delightful sugges-
tions.”
	The two sat talking and discussing for an
hour, and then Bess sprang up with shining
eyes and a happy smile on her lips as she
said:
	“All right, auntie, I think I can get it
done nicely by working in all my spare
hours. Goodby, you dear, sweet, precious
auntie, I shall never forget you for this.”
	And as the girl closed the door the lady
arose and watched the graceful form flit
down between the rows of lilacs and rose
trees, and out at the gate into the snowy
road beyond.
	“I cannot be selfish and keep her love all
to myself; it belongs to her mother, for God
so ordained it. Ah, thank Him, that
love is of Himself a part, in giving we are
not impoverished, and in taking away we
are not deprived!”
			*	*	*
	The Christmas morning was early filled
with merry shouts of laughter and gay ex-
changes of gift and greeting in Bessie’s
home.
	As Bessie’s mother looked at the many
and elaborate presents spread out on the
table for her, she gazed with pleased eyes at
a small box delicately tied with blue ribbon
and marked,
		From Bessie to Mother.
	The mother opened all the rest first, and
finally as she reached for Bessie’s parcel,
the girl herself could not bear it, and she

[Column 2]
flew out of the room on some trivial excuse.
	But the mother was so pleasantly engaged
in discovering all her gift treasures that she
did not notice the exit of her daughter.
	As she opened the pretty box, she saw
within a very simple, dainty daily calendar,
a sheet for each of the three hundred and
sixty-five days, fastened to a small, wooden
frame, each one pasted at the top, as on a
tab. On the top sheet was inscribed, in
bold letters, Jan. 1st, 1900, and beneath it
was written, in Bessie’s own beautiful pen-
manship:
Dearest Mother:
	I have written out the very innermost thoughts of
my heart on these leaves which you will tear off
day by day. And that they will reach to the depths,
of your own pure, loving soul is the hope of your
							BESSIE.
	The mother drew away the box from the
curious eyes of her little son, who stood by
her, and who asked curiously, “What are
oo tying about, mama?”
	But she gave him a kiss for reply, and
closed up her precious gift, as if it were too
sacred for the eyes of any one save herself.
	She turned aside in a window recess and
opening again her box,’ she lifted up the
second leaf, and--under the date she read:
	Mother dear, I have your love and you have
mine! but I want your confidence, and I want to
give you mine.
							BESSIE.
On another leaf she read:
	How I have longed to sit at your feet and have
you stroke my hair while I tell you all my secrets,
but I don’t know how to begin. Won’t you help
me?
	She had read enough to know that her
young daughter had given her the most
precious gift ever given by child to parent—
the gift of her heart’s confidence! And she
put the box up to her lips and kissed it
again and again. Oh, it was what she,
too, had been longing for these many years.
And she knew not how to gain it!
	As Bessie came into the room, the mother
held out her arms and the girl ran into that
loving embrace, while their silent tears
mingled as they kissed each other fondly.
	“Oo make mama ty, Bessie,” said the
baby-boy. “Tome away.”
	But the mother picked him up and kissed
him fervently, while she whispered to
Bessie, “You have made me so happy,
Bessie. So happy!”
	And the little brother asked wonderingly,
“Did she dive oo a Christmas dift, mama?
What was it, mama?”
	And the mother stroked the girl’s sunny
hair almost for the first time in her life, as
she replied:
	“Why, baby dear, Bessie has given me
her heart for my Christmas present.”
	In the twilight of that evening two ladies
came up the path bordered by lilacs and
rosetrees, and as they were ushered into the
dainty sitting room where sat the elderly
lady, a soft, mellow voice, now full of happy
tears, cried out,
	“Oh, auntie! mamma and I have both
come tonight to talk it all over with you,
you dear, sweet auntie. And mamma says
you are not to lose me, auntie, but you are
to take us both into your heart and let us
both be your dear confidents.”
	There were no more words for a little
season as the grey-haired lady arose and
clasped the hands offered to her and put up
her own trembling lips for a kiss from each.
	Then she drew up two low ottomans and

[Column 3]
the others sat down at her feet, and while
the delicate aged hands caressed the dark
hair and the golden, Bessie said softly:
	“Isn’t this a heavenly Christmas?”
	And the hostess replied reverently:
	“Aye, for it is sanctified by Love
Divine!”
					AMELIA.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


BAZAR NOTES.
	MRS. JULIA WARD HOWE recited “The
Battle Hymn of the Republic” one evening
at the Suffrage Bazar, and was received
with much enthusiasm.
	MISS JEANIE GORDON, of New Orleans,
had for sale at the National Suffrage Bazar 
which has just closed some of the old time
“open-kettle” molasses, now choice and
rare, as there are only a few places where it
is still made.
	MISS GAIL LAUGHLIN, who is investigat-
ing the domestic service question for the
U. S. government, acted as a sort of aid-de-
camp to Mrs. Catt during the suffrage Bazar,
and was flying about and doing as much
work herself as a full score of domestic
servants.
	MISS SUSAN B. ANTHONY, during the
Suffrage Bazar in New York, was the guest
of her cousin, Mrs. S. L. Lapham. The
change of air and scene did her good and
her health improved every day during her
stay. She made several visits to the Bazar
and her entrance was always hailed with
applause. “Is Miss Anthony here this
morning?” one woman asked. “Oh, yes,”
answered another, “I know she is here, for
I heard the people clapping a little whilet
ago.”
	MISTRESS MARGARET BRENT was one of
the handsomest of the many beautiful
dolls at the Suffrage Bazar, and was a
source of especial pride to the Southern
women present. She was the gift of Mrs.
J. William Funk, president of the Balti-
more Equal Suffrage Association, and was
the chief ornament of the Maryland booth.
She was dressed as a stately colonial dame
in purple satin and ermine, with a necklace
of pearls, and had her dark eyes fixed on
the spectator, and one hand stretched out as
if in appeal. She represented Mistress
Margaret Brent of Maryland, Lord Balti-
more’s kinswoman and executrix, who was
the first woman in America to ask for the
ballot. She appealed to the Colonial
Assembly for a vote in 1647, more than 200
years before the first woman’s rights con-
vention was held in the North. The
Maryland women at the Suffrage Bazar told
her story with great pride.

	MISS EUGENIE WASHINGTON, a great
grandniece of George Washington, whose
death has been announced, was born in
Jefferson County, now West Virginia, her
father being William Temple Washington,
grandson of Colonel Samuel Washington,
brother of President Washington. She was
a descendent also of Count de Flechie, a
relative of Lafayette, who was a captain in
the Continental Army. Miss Washington
was appointed to a clerkship in the Patent
Office in 1876, and was subsequently trans-
ferred to the Post Office Department whe e
she held the place until her death. She was
one of the founders of the Daughters of the
American Revolution, was for many years
registrar-general of the society and was one
of its honorary vice-presidents-general.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


A CHRISTMAS LETTER.
		JANE THORN.

“Now, what are you writing, my Anna?”
	“I’m writing a letter, you see,
To give Mr, Santa a message
	About gifts he’ll bring unto me.
I’m tired of writing, dear grandma,
	And want to go out now and play.
So please read and seal up my letter,
	And post it right off today.”

Away flew the darling, and left me
	To wonder what message she’d send—
What gifts of all others she wanted,
	Dolls, books or money to spend?
Her mother peeped over my shoulder, 
	And read the queer writing with me,
Sure, never was sent such a letter
	On land or from over the sea.

‘Dear Santa,” it read, “please send me
	Some white satin and velvet clothes, 
With a train, and a ring and breastpin,
	And glasses to pinch on my nose,
Some pretty white hair like my grandma’s,
	In a switch to take off at night,
And teeth to take out in a tumbler,
	And earrings that sparkle bright.

I don’t mind a bustle and corset,
	Though grandma says they are bad,
But just send the prettiest bracelet
	That cannot make any one sad.
That’s all for this Christmas, dear Santa.”
	The letter was ended here.
The mother and I looked at each other,
	And into her eyes crept a tear.

“What weeds have we sown in the garden,
	Of this innocent baby’s heart?
With our frills, our trains and our jewels,
	We each must shoulder our part.
Instead of our virtues she copies
	Our vices and follies, you see;
This letter has come like a warning
	From God unto you and to me.”

“The mind of a child is a garden,
	In which God planted good seed,
But the weeds of the world soon choke them
	’Tis the care of a mother they need,
But mother forgets in the worry
	Of providing the clothes and food,
That something must grow in that garden,
	And evil grows faster than good.

“If we think and talk altogether
	Of the world, its joys and its cares,
What wonder our gardens grow nothing,
	Save thistles and thorns, and the tares.
Good seed grows only by culture,
	The weeds spring up in a night!
We two have neglected our duty,
	And evil to us has seemed right.

“Come, let us accept of our lesson,
	Put away from us vanities’ snares,
And into our lives crowd more closely, 
	Good thoughts, good deeds and good prayers.
Remember that teaching means talking,
	And setting examples true,
We must make our own lives the better.
	Then her’s will be beautiful, too.

“This Christmas must bring to us, daughter,
	The gift of a purer heart,
The desire to serve the Master
	By choosing a meeker part.
And thus will our Anna be blessed,
	And thus will her odd letter prove,
A message from Heaven to us-ward,
	Filled full with forgiving Love.


[Column 1]
CENTURY’S GREETING TO AID RED
CROSS.
	The parting of the centuries on the night
of the 31st of the coming December will
have a celebration of world-wide import
and interest in the unique watch meetings
to be held in this country under the auspices
of the American National Red Cross.
	The specially organized Twentieth Cen-
tury Department of the Red Cross is now
preparing to hold watch meetings, as far as
practicable, in every city, town and village
of the United States. Mammoth meetings
are already arranged for in New York and

[Column 2]
other leading cities, in Madison Square
Garden and other great auditoriums, to see
the old century out and the new century in.
At Madison Square Garden Sousa’s famous
band will furnish the music and a grand
chorus of one thousand voices will be heard
in song until midnight, interspersed with
speeches and greetings.
	These “Greetings” will be an unique and
epochal feature. Not only the rulers of the
prominent nations of the world, but the
leading celebrities of every land—Tolstoi,
Joseph Chamberlain, Dreyfus, Zola, Kruger,
Sir Edwin Arnold, Hall Caine, Anthony
Hope, Lord Roberts and more than fifty
others, making a total of about one hundred,
have written special messages for the Red
Cross, on the progress of the 19th Century
and the promise of the 20th Century.
	Such a grouping of crystalized thought
from the master minds of all the earth on a
subject of such universal interest was never
before made. The contributions pledged to
the service of the American Red Cross
branch will form a most inspiring and im-
pressive memorial. So important and sig.
nificant are these “Greetings” that the
originals are to be preserved for the nation
in the Congressional Library at Washington.
	At every Red Cross watch meeting in
the city or village throughout the land there
will be opened simultaneously a sealed
packet containing these “Greetings” from
the Old World to the New on the close of a
century so unprecedented in achievements
and the coming of another so transcendent
in promise.
	In the noble memorial address by Miss
Barton to Congress some twelve years ago,
she recalled the fact that probably no sign
or figure in the secular world is sacred to so
many eyes as the Red Cross of Geneva. It
is the insignia of a humanity that knows no
foe. In the fiercest conflict of arms it passes
unchallenged on its mission of mercy. Its
international organization is founded on the
welcome and authorization of the united
governments of the civilized world. Its
chief directors abroad are men of the fore-
most standing, and the crowned heads of
Europe are its patrons. It is eminently
natural and fitting that it should be the
medium for bearing messages of cheer and
fraternity that will inspire and delight all
America in these great watch meetings.
	The object of these meetings is to raise
funds for the American National Red Cross,
and to signalize the dawning of another
century by giving world-wide prominence
to that fraternity between nations which
tends to ameliorate the horrors of war.
	The Red Cross in other countries has
been endowed by various means, but in the
United States it has worked from one
national emergency to another without an
endowment or sustaining fund, and it is
believed that, at the dawning of a new
century, the people of the United States
will gladly gather together in mass meetings
to watch the old century out and the new
century in, and at the same time to aid in
the national and international work which
has for its watchword “Humanity.”
	The American National Red Cross has
for the first time consented to give the pub-
lic an opportunity to contribute to a per-
manent sustaining fund for this broadest of
charities.
	It is not necessary to enlarge to American
readers on the history and work of Miss
Clara Barton and the American National
Red Cross, nor as to what she and the

[Column 3]
Society have done for the soldiers of the
Regular and Volunteer armies of this and
other countries. With the provision of such
a fund as is proposed, the occurrence of any
great calamity, such as recently visited the
city of Galveston, would find it in a position
to offer that most valuable of assistance—
first relief.
	It is expected that some adequate pro-
vision may be made for such a sustaining
fund through the agency of the watch
meetings and the subscriptions for the
sealed packet of “Greetings” which will be
delivered to every meeting in the country at
a very moderate charge.


THE NATIONAL BAZAR.
THE PIONEER’S RECEPTION.
	Magnificent weather greeted the first day
of the Bazar, and there was a great crowd
at the reception to the pioneers on the open-
ing night Of the pioneers invited, Mrs.
Stanton, Mrs. Howe and Mrs. Livermore
could not be present, but Miss Anthony,
Rev. Antoinette Brown Blackwell, Mrs.
Isabella Beecher Hooker, Mrs. Anna C.
Field and Mrs. Charlotte F. Wilbur were in
line. It was a pleasure to see that row of
white-haired ladies, most of them over
eighty, yet all of them fine looking, several
of them eminently handsome. It was a
work of time and difficulty to get near them
so great was the crush of the younger people
who pressed up to shake hands and congrat-
late them. The pretty girls who acted as
ushers finally linked hands and formed a
cordon, so as to keep off the crowd and make
the people pass in single file.
	Miss Anthony was, of course, the chief
attraction. Though still frail from the
illness brought on by her magnanimous and
successful effort to raise the sum needed to
secure the admission of girls to the Univer-
sity of Rochester, she looked very nice in
the point lace given her by the women of
the District of Columbia seventeen years
ago, and the historic ruby velvet gown that
has served as her “best dress” for the last
quarter of a century. Its good state of
preservation is her pride and a testi-
mony to her economic virtues. Except for
the remodelling of the sleeves, it has con-
ceded nothing to the changing fashions. The
enormous puffs in vogue when it was first
made have been taken off and cleverly
transformed into bags and needle cases,
which find a ready sale at the Susan B.
Anthony Booth.—Ex.


MRS. RUSSELL SAGE made several visits
to the Suffrage Bazar, and was a large buyer.
	ARISTOTLE is credited with being the first
European to discover the true origin of the
material from which was woven the precious
fabrics which for over a thousand years the
Orient alone had supplied to the West. In
his time the fabric known to use as chiffon
was first made by the Roman ladies, who
obtained their working material by unravel-
ing raw silk imported from Persia. The
actual introduction of silk culture into
Europe occurred much later, in the sixth
century, A. D., when two Nestorian Christ-
ians, risking death, which was the penalty
for such export, succeeded in bringing silk-
worm eggs from China at the instigation of
the Emperor Justinian.

SALT LAKE CITY, UTAH DECEMBER 15, 1900.


[Column 1]
MARVELOUS ADVANCEMENT.
	Assuredly the world is entering upon an
era of great importance in its history in the
opening of the new century, and if figures
are significant the closing of another
thousand years must cut some sort of
figure.
	Men talk of looking backward and re-
counting the advance made in all directions
during this century, but what of the nine
centuries since the year ten hundred?
What condition was the world in then? How
far had civilization progressed? What did
the Eastern hemisphere know of the West-
ern? Only tradition and very little of that.
	Since that time to consider the vastness
of the work of the world makes ones head
swim. The discovering of a continent and
of islands almost innumerable, large and
small. The discoveries not only of earth
but the heavenly bodies.
	To comprehend in part this subject one
must take in the universe, the sun, moon
and stars and some of the laws by which
they are controlled, and the order in which
they move, and their relative position to
each other and to the planet we inhabit.
The knowledge of elemental forces existing
that were unknown to men have been
brought to light by science and its applied
instrumentalities. Many things can now
be measured and weighed that were once
considered wholly exempt or outside of any
control.
	In fact, science seems to have transformed
the universe and instead of unknown space,
that to us was lost, empty or void, there is
ever the rush and bustle of activity, or an
eternal stream of life and power and action
which penetrates the realms of space and
the entire world. And we call all this pro-
gress and man’s invention and applied
science to the forces already existing, but of
which man had no practical knowledge
heretofore.
	In many, perhaps most respects, the
greatest strides in higher civilization have
been made in this Nineteenth century. In
the fine arts, literature, music and painting,
undoubtedly, unless one goes back to
ancient Greece, of which we have very little
except tradition.
	To be sure, we speak of the beginning of
the finer culture in the Elizabethan period,
but it is the boast of the age in which we
live, and an accepted fact, that the greatest
men and most profound thinkers and largest
culture and attainments have been of this

[Column 2]
latest century. The fact remains, however,
without any fear of contradiction, that
Shakespeare is without a peer in this or any
other age, and, therefore, he must be an
exception.
	No doubt there are those who would
differ with the writer, but nevertheless this
has been a century of learning, of refinement
and culture. Great things have been
undertaken, and many of them accomplished
and the world looks on and wonders at the
advancement in all the lines of thought and
phases of life.
	What may be expected in the twentieth
century from the indications of the present
lookout, who can or dare determine? Only
a prophet or seer dare venture a prediction.
	The changes that have taken place in
atmospheric conditions prove that not only
men’s environments are subject to the
changes that occur through discoveries and
inventions, but that there are many differ-
ences from year to year which man is
powerless to control.
	Those who are permitted to live far into
the twentieth century are likely to see and
learn still more marvelous things than
have been made known in the past.

	An attempt has been made to determine
the opinions of ten of the most eminent
scholars now living concerning the very
best books of the century or those that
have most influenced thought and activities.
	The men selected are James Bryce,
Harry Van Dyke, Arthur T. Hadley, T.
W. Higginson, Edward E. Hale, George
A. Gordon, A. M. Fairbairn, C. Stanley Hall.
	Of these men five are heads of colleges,
and all are men of broad views, and
superior knowledge of the world in its
widest sense.
	The books most mentioned are Tenny-
son’s incomparable In Memoriam, Words-
worth’s “Lyrical Ballads,” Goethe’s
“Faust,” Scotts “Waverley,” Victor Hugo’s
“Les Miserables,” Harriet Beecher Stowe’s
“Uncle Tom’s Cabin,” Ruskin’s “Modern
Painters,” Hegel’s “Logic,” Lyell’s
“Geology,” Comte’s “Positive Philosophy.”
	These seem more often mentioned except
one which is mentioned by nine out of the
Ten, Darwin’s “Origin of Species.”
	Other books mentioned are George
Elliot’s “Middlemarch,” Froebel’s “Edu-
cation of Man,” Napoleon’s “Civil Code,”
Spencer’s “Principles of Psychology,”
Renan’s “Life of Jesus,” De Tocqueville’s 
“Democracy of America,” Carlyle’s “Sartor
Resartus,” Grove’s “Correlation of the Phy-
sical Forces,” Hawthorne’s “Scarlet Leuter”
and others.
	The Latter-day Saints one and all, per-
haps, or if ten of the most eminent men in
the Church were selected to give an
opinion of this kind, would probably agree
that the book which had been most bene-
ficial in awakening thought and activities
in this century would be the “Book of
Mormon,” which was not exactly written
in this cenutry, but brought forth and
translated. Then the “Book of Doctrine and
Covenants” and “Pearl of Great Price.”
	Of others we should naturally think
“The Life of Joseph Smith,” “The Voice of
Warning,” “Key to Theology,” “Spencer’s
Letters” and the poems of Eliza R. Snow
and John Lyon.
	The writer also appreciates Tullidge’s
“Life of Brigham Young,” his “Life
of Joseph Smith” and his “Women of
Morrnondom,” “The Articles of Faith” and

[Column 3]
Orson Pratt’s “Key to the Universe,” the
last mentioned would probably be con-
sidered, by men outside the Church, the
greatest book by a Mormon author.
	The writer agrees with the judges ap-
pointed to give opinions upon the books of
the century, on Tennyson’s “In Memoriam”
as the greatest poetical production, and
would differ in reference to George Elliot’s
“Middlemarch” substituting as her best 
novel “Romola.” So far as the principle
of Evolution in Darwin’s work we would
not differ so widely, but in regard to his
origin of species, we are positive his theory
is incorreet. However, the world is moving
onward, and the Lord is teaching and
giving light and knowledge to mankind,
and Tennyson’s words are coming to pass-
‘Not in vain the distance beacons, forward, forward 
	let us range.
Let the great world spin forever down the ringing
	groves of change.”


HALF A CENTURY.
	Naturally one feels when fifty years of
life have passed, that he has reached an im-
portant period, and can look back with
pride if something creditible has been ac-
complished. Elder Andrew Jenson,
Assistant Church Historian, whose fiftieth
birthday anniversary was celebrated on the
eleventh of this month, has the satisfaction
of having spent thirty-four years in the ser-
vice of the Church.
	The party in his honor was arranged by
his family and two or three hundred in-
vitations sent out, some long distances.
	The entertainment was given in the
Fourteenth Ward Assembly rooms, which
were handsomely decorated with evergreens,
palms, etc. Four tables the entire length
of the hall were provided witn the bounties
of the earth for the delectation of the guests.
	The tables were well filled with the
happy crowd assembled, glad to do justice
to the variety of good things prepared for
the banquet.
	When the goodly company were seated
Apostle John Henry Smith pronounced the
blessing. Apostle Anthon H. Lund was
master of ceremonies and welcomed the
company in a few well chosen words. The
exercises were somewhat varied. Some
congratulatory telegrams and letters from
absent friends were read by Brother Lund.
Apostle J. H. Smith, President Angus M.
Cannon and Sister E. B. Wells were the
first impromptu speakers, each congratu-
lating him and wishing him every blessing
to which the faithful are entitled in this life
and hereafter. Afterwards Brother Jenson
made an address in which he spoke of his
labors as a historian and how much he en-
joyed the work, and of his love for the
Gospel and his desires for the future.
	President Joseph E. Taylor told a story
which related, in fact, to his own life and
that of Brother Jensen, and the coincidence
of birth on the same day of the year,
though he has attained to three score and
ten. His remarks were extremely felicitous
and carried one’s thoughts into the previous
existence.
	Apostle John Taylor was very amusing,
and specially complimentary to the ladies.
	There were songs by home celebrities and
recitations by little Miss Allen that were
very humorous and heartily applauded, as
also were the vocalists.
	A photographer came and took flash
pictures of the whole company, table, decor-
ations and all.


Counselor Arthur Barnes, of the City
Council, spoke a short time in a happy vein
and Mrs. Elizabeth D. Roundy read a care-
fully prepared and well written paper giving,
some idea of Brother Jensen’s early life, his
birthplace in Denmark, his travels to the
holy land and around the world. The
evening entertainment closed with singing
“Auld Lang Syne,” and benediction by
Elder Adolph Madsen.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


BIRTHDAY ANNIVERSAY.
	On the 2nd of December was the birthday
anniversary of Sister Armeda Snow Young,
and by invitation of her husband, Brother
Morris Young, and his mother, Sister
Margaret P. Young, a number of her friends
gathered at her home and held a meeting.
As it was Fast day many of the guests had
attended the morning Fast meeting in the
Temple, yet were not excused on that
account, therefore the Bishop of the ward,
Millen C. Atwood, and Frank Y. Taylor,
President of Granite Stake, in which she re-
sides, had arranged to hold the meeting at
the residence of Brother and Sister Young.
	President F. Y. Taylor took charge of
the meeting, Prof. C. J. Thomas of the
music. The first exercise was singing,
“Jesus mighty King in Zion.” Prayer by
Brother McGee. “Singing, O, my Father,”
	President Taylor made a few opening re-
maks, also President John R. Winder, one
of the presiding Bishopric of the Church,
and Bishop Atwood.
	Brother George D. Pyper sang in his
beautiful tenor, “Scatter Sunshine,” which
was a great pleasure to all.
	Other speakers were Sisters Zina D. H.
Young, Bathsheba W. Smith, Emma S.
Woodruff, Sarah B. Gibson, Margaret P.
Young’ Emmeline B. Wells, Brother and
Sister McGee and Sister Lillie T. Freeze,
who also sang very sweetly in the gift of
tongues which was interpreted by “Sister
Zina.” Brother Hafen also made some re-
marks.
	Brother and Sister Morris Young wel-
comed the guests in the beginning of the
meeting very cordially, and all the exercises
were rendered in a very heavenly spirit.
Prof. Thomas led in the hymn, “Who are
these arrayed in white?” and at the close
the hymn “Lord dismiss us with Thy bless-
ing” was sung by the assembly. Bishop
Winder pronounced the benediction.
	Tables were spread with a bountiful re-
past and decorations of white chrysanthe-
mums and beautiful ferns gave a festive ap-
pearance to the auspicious occasion.
	The dinner was a most delicious one and
thoroughly enjoyed by all present, as also
the conversation, which was mostly rem-
iniscent of “Auld Lang Syne.”
	Many heartfelt congratulations and good
wishes for future success and happiness
were showered upon Sister Young in re-
membrance of the day, and complimenting
her upon retaining her youthful appearance
and vivacity of spirits, and wishing herself,
husband and children all the blessings
of this life and eternal happiness hereafter.
	There were many others present besides
those who took part in the meeting, among
whom we noticed Prof. Evans (the artist)
and wife, Mrs. Minnie J. Snow, Brother
Le Roy Snow and wife, Mrs. George D.
Pyper, Mrs. Asahel Woodruff, Mrs. Mary
A. Freeze and many others.
	President Lorenzo Snow, Sister Young’s
father, was detained on account of a death
in the family, which was a source of regret


to all; but the affair was a very pleasant one
and the EXPONENT wishes long life, health
and prosperity to Sister Young, and that
she may always be able to entertain her
friends in as kindly and hospitable a fashion.


[Column 1]
	STORY OF ONE CHRISTMAS.
Who can tell what ships may be sailing towards
us over unknown seas?
	Christmas Eve, the memorable night
when the solemn stars shone out bright
and luminous on the pathway of Mary,
the Blessed Virgin, as she journeyed to-
wards Bethlehem of Judea; and she knew
not what awaited her, nor the tidings that
were to be given on that night of nights to
the country shepherds, abiding in the
fields, keeping watch over their flocks.
How the angel of the Lord came upon them
and His glory shone round about them,
and he declared unto them the glad tidings
of great joy, saying “Unto you is born this
day, in the city of David, a Savior, which
is Christ the Lord.” We all know the
sweet and touching story, so beautifully
portrayed by inspired men in days of old,
and handed down to us in Holy Writ.
	And thus many times since and even in
our own day have strange things come un-
expectedly in the course of human events,
and sometimes light and knowledge of
divine truth has been brought to the homes
and hearts of men and women, when they
knew not that the Lord was so mindful of
them, or that the angel spoken of in Reve-
lations had flown through the heavens bear-
ing the Everlasting Gospel to preach to
them that dwell on the earth, the message
of salvation; and sitting by their own hearth-
stones or toiling in the fields or workshops,
all unawares this message has come to
many thousands, not always upon Christ-
mas, or the Sabbath day, but sometimes
even so, and no greater Christmas gift can
one receive than the divine ray of light and
truth.
	Look back seventy years and picture to
yourself a home of peace and plenty in the
then far Western Reserve of Ohio, near the
great lakes. All is prosperous without and
within, and you may gaze into the living
room, where order and tranquility seeming-
ly abide, and hear the father, mother and
children singing an old Campbellite hymn,
as they sit around the fire of hickory logs.
Listen to the sweet voices! It is not a
Christmas carol, though Christmas is draw-
ing near, but a grand anthem of praise.

	“Burst ye emerald gates and bring
		To my enraptured vision, 
	All the ecstatic joys that spring,
		Round the bright Elysian.
	Lo! we lift our longing eyes,
		Break ye intervening skies!
	Son of righteousness arise,
		Ope the gates of Paradise!

	“Four and twenty Elders rise,
		From their princely station,
	Shout their glorious victories
		Sing the great salvation;
	Cast their crowns before His throne,
		Cry in reverential tone,
	Glory be to God alone!
		Holy, Holy, Holy One!”

	It is a fitting hymn for those whose hearts
are full of love and devotion. The evening
prayer is offered and the children in their
white night robes are taken away by the
nurse, after the usual goodnight kiss, and
the father and the mother sit talking over
the events of the day, refering to the chil-
dren and to those who are at school, and
proudly, too, of the eldest boy who is

[Column 2]
making rapid progress, while they are
somewhat puzzled what is best to do with
the girl who is almost as old as her brother
but prefers needle, thread and scissors to her
books.
	“We’ll send her to Painesville to the
girl’s Seminary,” says the father, anxious
to ease the mother’s mind.
	“I can’t help thinking,” says the fond
mother, “of my own home so far away, and
how little I realized until I had children of
my own what my mother must have felt
when I left her to come West with Aunt
Polly. Dear me, it is a dozen years ago or
more, how long it seems, and I’ve promised
them every year since I was married I
would visit dear old Bethany and New
Haven again.”
	“And so you shall this next spring. My
business has prospered beyond my wildest
dreams or ambition, the Lord has been more
merciful to me than I deserved, and there is
no reason now to delay your visit longer. I
shall go to New York to purchase goods and
will take you with me, and accompany you
to your old home and you can stay until I
transact my business and am ready to re-
turn,” said the husband in reply.
	“Aunt Polly will not consent to my leav-
ing the children, I’m sure, and I do not
know how I could trust them to hired help.
I fear my visit would be made uncomfortable
with thoughts of their being neglected in
my absence. I do not know what is best, I
wish they would come here and visit us.
How proud I should be to show them each
one of my little troop!”
	“Well, Mary, you have some months to
think it over and decide, and you can busy
yourself meantime with cutting and making
new apparel, for my wife must go home in
style and her girl friends will see tho’ she
went away out into the wild West, that she
is not behind them in dress or manner.”
	“O, as to that, I care very little, but
Aunt Polly’s feelings must be considered.
She has been father and mother to me, you
know, and she has no one except me and
mine to love or care for. But she is a
spinster and children annoy her, and she
cannot understand them. I must think the
matter over carefully.”
	“We’ve settled down for life now and we
must educate the children. Frank will
soon be ready for Oberlin, at the rate he is
progressing. His teacher told me today he
was far ahead of any boy in the school. We
need have no fears for him, and as for Lois,
she’ll be safe in the Seminary, and you must
make up your mind to go, my dear, and it
will be a benefit to your health and a great
satisfaction all around.”
	And so the matter was settled and sort of
dismissed from their minds, for it had often
been discussed before, but so many obstacles
always seemed in the way of accomplishing
the long journey, for such it was in those 
days.
	Silence reigned, such as comes from deep,
absorbing thought, when a loud knock at
the door caused Mrs. Emory to start and
exclaim, “Who can be coming so late?”
	Almost before she could finish the sentence
Mr. Emory was in the front entry unbolt-
ing the door, and the hearty “Good eve-
ning” of the strange voices rang through
the house, clear as a bell.
	“Walk in, gentlemen,” said the host,
and led the way to the fire. “My wife,
gentlemen. I have not the pleasure of
your acquaintance, unless—”
	The sentence remained unfinished, for the


[Column 1]
two strangers bowing to the lady said,
“We are looking for a night’s lodging. We
are traveling without purse or scrip, like
the disciples of old, with no place to sleep
for the night. We seemed to be directed
hither, and we hope to have shelter,” said
the one who had entered first.
	“Certainly you shall have food and
lodging, for a night at least. But be seated
and lay off your outside wraps, the room
will be warm after being out in the cold.
Have you come far?”
	“Only from Mendon today, but we have
been traveling some weeks,” replied the
eldest of the strangers, though both men
were in the prime of life.
	Mrs. Emory looked at the stranger guests
like one amazed. She thought they must
be Methodist Circuit Preachers, yet they
were evidently traveling on foot. As they
removed their overcoats Mr. Emory noticed
that they carried books, and observed, “I
see you must be preachers. May I inquire
of what denomination you are? Methodist,
I presume?”
	“And are you Methodist?” the younger
man asked, now speaking for the first time.
	“No,” replied the host, “we are Camp-
bellites only recently connected. But you
have not told me of what persuasion you
are. Evidently you are sincere to be
traveling through the country as you do in
this sort of weather.”
	With some hesitation the older man,
(whom we shall call Gray) replied, “We
are Elders of the true Church of Latter-day
Saints, and we preach the Everlasting
Gospel of life and salvation.”
	For a moment no one spoke. The words
seemed to have come like a prophecy, but
the woman was the first to break the spell.
	“You seem to come with power. What
are the books you carry? Will you tell us
if you preach from them?”
	“We have the Bible and we preach re-
pentance and baptism for the remission of
sins.”
	“So does Alexander Campbell and Sidney
Rigdon, and it is our own faith,’’ said Mrs.
Emory, modestly.
	“We do not know of your church, I
fear, and must inquire further of your doc-
trines,” said Mr. Emory.
	“We are willing and glad to explain our
doctrines and to prove them all from the
Bible. But have you not heard of Joseph
Smith and the Book of Mormon?”
	“What is the Book of Mormon? I have
heard that a man named Smith, digging for
gold in a hill in New York State found
some gold plates and made them into a
book called The Golden Bible, but that is the
extent of my knowledge about it. I paid
no attention at all to the rumor. And so
you are his followers, are you?” said the
host.
	“We are proud to be recognized as be-
lieving in the last dispensation of the ful-
ness of times, and to testify that angels
visited Joseph Smith and conferred upon
him the Holy Priesthood, and he has be-
stowed it upon others.”
	This is a very strange doctrine and hard
to believe without further proof than you
are likely to be able to give. But you shall
have entertainment for the night and in the
morning we will speak of this new prophet
and the Golden Bible again. Meantime
will you have supper? We were preparing
to retire when you came, but you must not
go hungry. Mary, (turning to Mrs. Emory)
ask Aunt Polly to set some supper for
these gentlemen.”

[Column 2]
	Aunt Polly was very ungracious about
doing as she was asked by her niece, and
muttered to herself as she spread the cloth,
for she was not religious and always de-
clared she had no patience with men who
got their living without work.
	While the strangers were in the kitchen
(which Aunt Polly liked best of all the
house, and kept spick-span clean) Mr. and
Mrs. Emory talked of the guests who had
come unbidden to partake of their hospitali-
ty, and both husband and wife felt there
was something peculiar about them, and
even awe-inspiring, but could not tell why
they were so impressed by their presence.
	“I shall ask them to pray,” said the hus-
band, “we shall see how that will affect us.”
	During supper Aunt Polly was very
crabbed with them and they felt she was
angry they had come, she let them see it
plainly.
	Mr. Emory asked them to have prayers
as soon as they came into the room again,
and they knelt down. The prayer was like
a benediction upon them and the whole
household, and especially Mrs. Emory, who
was of a spiritual temperament, was melted
to tears. After they had retired she said to
her husband, “I never heard a prayer like
that, never.”
	The husband was not so susceptible, tho’
he was sore perplexed in his mind as to the
message these preachers brought. Their
earnestness and the way in which they were
going about was new to him, and he could
not question their sincerity. 
	The following morning the Elders were
up betimes, and were waiting when their
host and hostess came down stairs. The
breakfast was ready and the family gathered.
Mr. Emory, partly to judge the merits of
the men asked the younger one, (Mr. Dillon
we’ll call him) to offer prayer. Nothing
was said when they rose from their knees,
but Mr. Emory began, Yankee-like, to ply
them with questions concerning the tenets
of their faith, and the ready responses with
quotations from Scripture quite astonished
him. Mrs. Emory paid the closest attention
to the conversation, and became fully con-
vinced of the truth of the doctrines they
set forth.
	Breakfast over Mr. Emory said, “gentle-
men I must repair to my place of business
and will bid you good morning, but if you
preach in this vicinity, as I judge you wish
to do, from what you have said, I shall
certainly go to hear you, and you shall not
go away empty-handed.”
	He then offered them some money, which
they gladly accepted, and thanked him
heartily for his hospitality and the generous
gift.
	Before the day was over Aunt Polly came
into the room where her niece was and said,
“Mary, those canting preachers who staid
here last night are ‘Mormons.’ They’ve
been to Isaac Hartmon, one of your good
Campbellite brethren, and about converted
him to ‘Joe Smith,’ and they are going to
preach there at his house tonight.”
	“Then I shall go and hear them,” Mrs.
Emory answered, quickly, though Aunt
Polly remonstrated. And she kept her
word. Both herself and her husband were
at the meeting and listened attentively.
	Mrs. Emory was baptized very soon after-
wards. This was in November, 1830. Mr.
Emory took a little more time to study over
the matter and in about a week after his
wife’s baptism he, too, obeyed the same
principle, as also did a number of their
neighbors and friends.

[Column 3]
	Mrs. Emory was very zealous, and she
soon gave up the idea of her visit to Con-
necticut, so anxious was she to become ac-
quainted with the Gospel as taught by the
Elders of the Church. Mr. Emory was a
man of very strong character and of sterling
quality, of mind and principle. He sought
the Lord diligently in prayer in regard to
what he heard from the preachers, and
especially in reference to the Book of
Mormon, its authenticity and the manner
of its coming forth. His one desire was to
see the Prophet Joseph for himself, and he
centred his mind upon this one subject. He
prayed earnestly for it, but he believed that
he must go to visit him, and intended to do
so as soon as he could leave home long
enough. The weather was growing very
cold and the snow was thick enough on the
ground to be very good sleighing.
	Mrs. Emory was not aware of her hus-
band’s intense feelings about seeing the
Prophet, though she knew he was pro-
foundly interested in the Book of Mormon
and wanted Scriptural proofs of the doctrine.
	Just before Christmas, when Mr. Emory
was busy in his store with holiday goods, a
tall, majestic looking stranger came in late
one afternoon and stepped up to the counter,
and reaching out his right hand said, “How
do you do, Brother Emory?”
	Looking him full in the face and failing
to recognize him, Mr. Emory replied, “You
have the advantage of me, sir. I do not
know who you are,” or words to that effect
	“I am Joseph the Prophet,” he replied.
“You have prayed me here, now what do
you want of me ? My wife and servant
girl are outside in a sleigh, we have driven
a long distance.”
	In a moment it all flashed upon the mind
of Mr. Emory—his prayers, his pleadings
with the Lord to be permitted to see the
Prophet and talk with him face to face of
the heavenly visions and visits of angels;
yet here he stood, his prayers were answered
and he felt almost condemned.
	He was as one in a trance for a brief
moment, then he said, “Come with me to
my home,” and he hastened with the glad
tidings of great joy to his wife who was more
overcome than even her husband with the
startling news and the unlooked for visit of
the stranger guests. She knew not how
to receive them royally enough, and
modestly hesitated, but they were at the
door directly and needed a welcome, for
they were cold, and tired with the journey
in the inclement weather.
	As much embarrassed as one would be in
a kingly presence, yet rejoicing at the
privilege of such an honor, they welcomed
them to their hearts and home.
	Can our readers imagine the picture?
Perhaps many of the Saints, who are so ac-
customed to entertaining the greatest men
of our Church in this day, may fancy they
know how these early Latter-day Saints felt
at that time; but if you call to mind the
newness of the whole situation, the rever-
ence in which they held even the Elders
who had taught them the first lessons in
the Gospel, you will comprehend better the
feelings of those who were thus summarily
brought face to face with the Prophet, who
had been the recipient of the wonderful
spiritual manifestations of which they had
only just been told.
	Late into the night they sat round the
fire and conversed. The house seemed to
have been transformed as if by magic into a
heavenly abode, and as though angels were
present rejoicing with them.


[Column 1]
	Emma Smith had come on this visit with
her husband with very little preparation,
and Sister Emory was proud to divide with
her the new goods she had expected to make
up for her trip to visit her parents.
	The days passed swiflty. There was so
much to tell and so much to hear. Sister
Emma—for so they called her—knew all
about the visions and manifestations of her
husband, and was never weary of repeating
these wonderful incidents to Sister Emory,
her neighbors and friends, for many came to
see and to hear of the new faith. Christ-
mas came very soon, in less than a week
after the arrival of Joseph and Emma, and
though the day was not celebrated then
with all the parade and extravagance that
prevails nowadays, yet there were gifts and
feasts and charities of a simple character,
and kindly feeling among peoples of all
classes and all religious faiths.
	Think if you can what it meant to these
people to have a prophet with them on
Christmas day after so many hundreds of
years that no direct revelation had been
given, and they had been told that there
would be no more, “the canon of scripture
was full.”
	The Lord had indeed been mindful of
them and they listened entranced day after
day to Joseph and Emma as they recounted
the thrilling events that had transpired since
the promulgation of the truths received from
on high, and the indignities, heaped upon
them and the persecutions they had been
made to endure.
	To those who are living now and are
acquainted with the leaders of the Church,
who are men of years, grown gray in the
service of the Lord, it is scarcely possible
to make them realize how very young the
Prophet Joseph was when he commenced
the great work of this dispensation. When
the Church was organized first he was not
yet twenty-five years old; and just before
the Christmas following that event he had
barely reached his quarter of a century. It
really seems incredible that a youth, a mere
boy, could have accomplished so much in so
short a time. Assuredly it is marvelous;
and the time will come when the world will
acknowledge his wisdom, and the influence
he has exerted in this century, now so
nearly drawing to a close.
	To be associated in a slight degree with
Joseph Smith was sufficient to inspire one
with his superiority to other men, even his
brethren in the priesthood, therefore it
is not to be wondered at that all those
liying now who remember him, should
testify to the influence and power he carried
wherever he was seen, no matter at what
time nor in what place.
	Nothing could be more beautiful than the
early life of Joseph Smith. In his manner he
was gentle and kindly, and he was always
affectionate to his friends, and at times
demonstrative. He was strong and ardent
in his nature and valued highly the quality
of sincerity in friendship. He was manly
to an unusual degree, yet tender-hearted as
a woman on occasions. In his tastes he
was literary as well as spiritual, fond of the
drama, of music and of poetry. A very
dear friend of mine who knew the Prophet
intimately when she was a child, assured me
she had repeated poetry to him by the hour,
and that she had known him to shed tears
when hearing some specially fine vocal
music, particularly old-fashioned songs and
ballads. With such noble characteristics it
is not strange that he was so intensely
beloved.

[Column 2]
	Blessed is the home where such men find
welcome: thrice blessed those who are
privileged to entertain prophets and men of
God.
	And therefore we repeat that to sit down
to a Christmas feast unexpectedly with a
man of destiny is far greater than any
material gift of this world’s goods. Such
privileges are only vouchsafed to the few;
yet Christmas is the opportune time to dis-
pense bounties and to share with others, our
friends and neighbors, the good gifts a kind
Providence bestows, and to show our apprecia-
tion in some genuine and substantial way
for those of our own household, if we can
do no more; but if possible extend a helping
hand to those less able to entertain, not as a
charity, but graciously, that their Christmas
may be one of good cheer and kindly fellow-
ship.
	The Savior came with Peace and Good
Will to all men. He brought life and
salvation to all who obey His command-
ments. It was the beginning of a new
time from which we date our years, now
numbering Nineteen Hundred in a few
more days, as time is now calculated. He
gave us the Golden Rule, “To do unto
others as we would have them do unto us.”
	In this century the Gospel he taught has
been restored again to earth by Joseph
Smith, whose mission was greater than any
other man or prophet since the days of the
Savior and His Apostles. And the name
and fame of the great Prophet of the
Latter-day dispensation should be kept be-
fore the children of the Latter-day Saints,
the name be spoken with reverence among
this people.
	The children old enough to appreciate the
fact, and thoughtful people generally, will
be sure to remember that this Christmas is
more unusual than any they have celebrated
heretofore, because it is the last in the cen-
tury and commences the last one of the two
thousand years since the birth of Christ.
	The Christmas seventy years ago, the
first one after the Church of the Latter-day
Saints was organized, though little thought
of then, was really an eventful one.
Sometimes we pass over almost uncon-
sciously the events that in after years prove
to have been the most important turning
points in history, and it is only in looking
backward that we recognize even afterwards
their significance.
						AUNT EM.

DOMESTIC SCIENCE DEPARTMENT.
		SUSA YOUNG GATES.
	It is with great pleasure that I greet the
readers of the EXPONENT here in this par-
ticular corner which shall be ours to discuss
some of the problems which interest women
in their homes. We may exchange
recipes and methods, and, too, we shall
consider many of those home topics which
just now are so popular and which engage
so much attention from the scientific and
economic world.
	But, bear with me, dear sister, we shall
not enter too deeply into the grave question
of economics, at least, not at present; but
we shall discuss some practical phases of
home life and home uplifting.
	I am very happy to present to you, as the
first paper in this Dept., one written by the
brilliant President of the N. H. E. A., who
herself is deeply interested in our Western
problems, while she as yet has never been
West of the Mississippi.

[Column 2]
	I commend your earnest attention to the
paper, and bid you good day for a season.
DOMESTIC SERVICE PROBLEM.
	We no longer try to dodge this question
which is perplexing all housekeepers at the
present time, for we would still have the
fact staring us in the face to the consterna- 
tion of all who desire to live in homes, that
there is certainly a movement going on
which some one has designated as “The
passing of the servant girl.” We have all
read columns of stuff on this subject, (some
of it funny and more of it almost tragic), in
our eager and determined desire to discover
a cure for this disease which has gathered
us all in its clutches like an epidemic.
Back of all this seething unrest in the house-
hold world may the cause not be found in
three stupendous facts? Is not one of them
due to the recently opened door in the busi-
ness world which has not only allowed all
women to enter, but has made the path so
easy that many have forced their way in, to
the sacrifice of their own abilities?
	Let us dwell for a moment upon this
peculiar phase of the present situation—the
woman in business and in consequence the
recognized depletion in the ranks of house-
keepers as well as household wage earners.
First, however, let us be perfectly clear on
this subject and not confound the women
who have forced themselves into the busi-
ness world needlessly and unfittingly, with
the business woman who has learned to
make herself so invaluable in whatever
niche of affairs she has secured for herself
by ability and perseverance for which she is
now receiving all due recompense. There
is always a good market for skill and brains
and tact in every department of wage earn-
ing. Therefore, we have to do with the
women who have rushed into business pur-
suits and pre-empted their claims with an
avidity worthy of our Oklahoma settlers, 
and not the reliable, capable, level-headed,
earnest business women which this pro-
gressive nineteenth century has brought
forth.
	If we may believe the signs of the times
as depicted in recent magazine and news-
paper articles, we need hardly trouble our-
selves a great deal about this large army of
incompetents. One writer facetiously claims
a speedy return of the prodigal daughter in-
to the safe but neglected harbor of home
life, which show that in the economic re-
lations of the world of business there is no
room for sentiment. Just as soon as the
supply of efficiency equals the demand, the
incompetents in business pursuits will
be crowded out, and they will then be forced
naturally with regret, to learn the sad decree,
some one has put so tersely, that “women
who are eager for a career must remember
that ignorance and inefficiency in the home
are not good recommendations for the busi-
ness life for which they long.” Nearly all
of the holes in commercial life are square and
we must admit that the average woman is
by education, heritage and environment so
well rounded that she has a natural fitness for
the many-sided phases of home life. Never-
theless we must emphasize the statement, at
the risk of repetition, that the average
woman seeking for a place in the world of
commerce must not be confounded with the
business woman, who perhaps has a family to
support and at least a pardonable desire for a
home to have and to hold. When our
young women show that degree of self-
reliance and capability which this business


[Column 1]
woman must possess in order to keep pace
with the competition which has always an
economic value in the world of affairs, she
will receive the compensation which is due
the skilled and thoroughly reliable employee,
utterly regardless of sex.
	Let us go back a generation or two if
we would gain a clear idea of another reason
for our domestic difficulties. In our great
army of domestic home makers we see a
constant struggle to make both ends meet
around a rapidly increasing surface of re-
sponsibility and expenditure, but this class
of women in our grandmother’s days were
quite content to spend at least a portion of
the time in looking after their own especial
business of household management, but now
the heads of these homes have found so
many interests outside of the home that it
has become necessary to increase the quality
and quantity of those who are to remain
under cover in order to keep the wheels of
all of the domestic industries in motion. We
rejoice, however, that women have these
varied and enlightened interests which have
come to us all in this latter end of a particu-
larly progressive century.
	In connection with this evolution in
household concerns, our public educators
have made it possible for all pupils, without
regard to fitness or ability to become imbned
with the idea that if the avenues of the so
called genteel pursuits for which they are
preparing themselves should be closed to
them, they would almost prefer to become
parasites upon their relatives, friends or
even the State than to bring their unused
hands into service. We are seeing the
effect of this mistaken step in the plethora of
mediocre seekers after positions of short
hours and light labor, and a corresponding
scarcity in the ranks of efficient artisans
with skill and competency which are so
closely related to all human happiness and
well being.
	Another snarl which perhaps seems the
most hopeless of disentanglement is the
rapidly increasing number of mills and
factories which furnish a great outlet for
purely mechanical labor. Women and
young girls have, in Europe as well as this
country, rushed into this easiest of all in-
dustries. The automaton-like methods
which are so quickly learned and more
easily repeated day after day, and the cor-
responding emoluments of such labor and
definite hours of release from supervision,
have been the means more than anything
else of depriving the household of its old
time servitors. When we realize that this
kind of employment gives to the employees
all that they most desire—their holidays,
their Sundays and their evenings, and the
unquestioned freedom of action so much
sought for by our liberty loving people,
we can readily understand why the majority
of housekeepers of today are in such
domestic turmoil.
	We may suggest numerous remedies for
this deploroble situation; perhaps the most
enticing is co-operative housekeeping which
always shows up so well on paper, but we
fear it will take generations of persistent

[Column 2]
educators to entirely extinguish the idiosyn-
cracies of the individual family.
	Even in these progressive projects there
must be rules and regulations and corps of
expert, qualified artisans, if there is to be the
harmonious system which its progenitors
promise in lieu of the independence of the
separate home life. While we are waiting
for these model “Homes” we must still
struggle with individual households and we
are forced to admit that no enterprising
combination of co-workers, or even sug-
gestions of any system of rules and regula-
tions will ever accomplish anything until
all women who are to enter into the business
of housekeeping, either as brain workers or
hand workers, have become thoroughly
educated in this most essential of all that
goes to make the home a perfect abiding
place.
	Therefore let us plead for this education
for our classes as well as our masses; then
we may safely follow with suggestions and
wise regulations of the laws which should
govern the management of households. We
should not lose sight of the pertinent fact
that for these laws to be successful, we must
grant a just estimation of the skill and
capability of experienced and educated
employees and demand thorough business
ability on the part of the employers. This
means that we must find ways and means to
enable us to sift out of the army of domestic
workers the shiftless, the incompetent and
the utterly hopeless cases and give just
treatment, leisure hours and good wages to
those who survive this ordeal. But no
housekeeper can do this unless she under-
stands her business, and then not without
co operation with all others of her kind.
	But, my sisters, let us strive to keep ever
before us in this great work of ours, that
Golden Rule which means forbearance to-
wards every dweller under the household
roof and which presupposes a wise tolerance
and appreciation for the individuality of the
workers as well as the idlers who
live therein.
			LINDA HULL LARNED.

RELIEF SOCIETY ANNUAL REPORTS.

	The time has come for annual reports and
it is important that reports be sent in as soon
as possible after the close of 1900. It is not
only necessary to have Stake reports, but
wherever a Relief Society has been organized
outside Stakes, whether in the United States,
British Empire, Scandinavia, Germany,
Switzerland, Holland, the islands of the sea,
reports for the year 1900, from January 1
to December 31, are expected at head-
quarters.
E. B. WELLS, General Secretary.


LADY SOMERSET'S COLONY.
	The Viscountess Horncastle gave a
garden party August 20, at Taymouth
House, Hackney Downs to acquaint her
friends with the work at Lady Somerset for
inebriate women at the colony she has es-


tablished at Duxhurst. Rev. C. F.
Wiliams was in the chair and introduced
Mrs. Graham, who is connected with the
work at Duxhurst. She said that there
was originally six cottages, each of which
could receive six patients. Two more have
been added, one by the voluntary sub-
scription of sailors, the other by the town of
Hastings.
	Under the Act of 1898 magistrates are
empowered to send inebriate women to a
home after a third conviction, and the good
that Duxhurst can do is only limited by the
number it can house. As many as three
thousand cases have had to be refused in
one year.
